MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Depolarisation initiated in the SA node gets sequentially conducted to the entire heart.

Which part of the heart is the LAST to receive depolarisation?

Uppermost portion of septum
Epicardial surface
Apex of the heart
Mid portion of the septum

Answer: (B) — Epicardial surface

Explanation:

The sequence of cardiac depolarization is a highly organized process that ensures efficient ventricular contraction. The electrical impulse originates in the sinoatrial (SA) node and follows a specific path:

  1. SA Node to AV Node: The impulse spreads from the SA node across the atria, causing them to depolarize (P wave on ECG).
  2. AV Node Delay: The impulse is briefly delayed at the atrioventricular (AV) node, allowing the ventricles to fill with blood.
  3. Ventricular Depolarization (QRS Complex):
    • The impulse travels down the Bundle of His and splits into the right and left bundle branches within the interventricular septum.
    • The first part of the ventricles to depolarize is the mid-portion of the interventricular septum, from left to right.
    • The impulse then rapidly travels via the Purkinje fibers to the apex of the heart.
    • The main wave of ventricular depolarization proceeds from the apex upwards towards the base and, crucially, from the endocardium (inner surface) to the epicardium (outer surface).

Because the wave of activation spreads from the inside out, the epicardial surface of the ventricles is the last layer to receive the depolarization signal. The last regions to be fully depolarized are the posterobasal walls of the left ventricle and the pulmonary conus.

Options:

  • (A) Uppermost portion of septum: This region is one of the last areas to depolarize, but the epicardial surface is the last layer to depolarize throughout the entire ventricular mass, making it a more fundamentally correct answer for the final point of activation.
  • (C) Apex of the heart: The apex is one of the earlier parts of the ventricular mass to depolarize, occurring soon after the septal activation.
  • (D) Mid portion of the septum: This is the very first part of the ventricles to depolarize.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 50-year-old female, on neurological examination, has loss of pain and temperature sensation, vibration and proprioception in both legs.

Which one of the following BEST explains these symptoms?

A large tumour affecting the posterior paracentral gyri
A large tumour affecting the sacral dorsal horn
A tumour on the medial lemniscal pathway in the sacral spinal cord
A peripheral neuropathy

Answer: (D) — A peripheral neuropathy

Explanation:

The patient presents with a loss of all major sensory modalities (pain, temperature, vibration, and proprioception) bilaterally in the legs. This specific pattern of sensory loss is best explained by a condition that affects all types of sensory nerve fibers supplying this region.

  1. Pain and Temperature Sensation: These are transmitted by small, thinly myelinated (A-delta) and unmyelinated (C) fibers that travel in the spinothalamic tract.
  2. Vibration and Proprioception: These are transmitted by large, heavily myelinated (A-beta) fibers that travel in the dorsal column-medial lemniscus (DCML) pathway.

A peripheral neuropathy is a condition characterized by damage to the peripheral nerves. Since peripheral nerves contain a mixture of all sensory fiber types (as well as motor fibers), a diffuse process like a polyneuropathy will affect all sensory modalities simultaneously. The "stocking" distribution (affecting the feet and lower legs) is a hallmark of length-dependent axonal neuropathies (e.g., due to diabetes, alcohol, vitamin deficiencies), where the longest nerves are affected first. This perfectly matches the patient's symptoms of losing all sensation in both legs.

Options:

  • (A) A large tumour affecting the posterior paracentral gyri: The posterior paracentral gyrus is the part of the primary somatosensory cortex that processes sensation from the contralateral leg and foot. A lesion here would cause sensory loss in only one leg (contralateral to the lesion). To cause bilateral symptoms, the tumor would have to be exceptionally large and midline, affecting both hemispheres, which is less common and would likely present with other significant signs.
  • (B) A large tumour affecting the sacral dorsal horn: The dorsal horn is where first-order sensory neurons for the spinothalamic tract (pain and temperature) synapse. A lesion here would affect pain and temperature but would spare the dorsal columns, which carry vibration and proprioception. Therefore, this option does not explain the loss of vibration and proprioception.
  • (C) A tumour on the medial lemniscal pathway in the sacral spinal cord: This option is anatomically incorrect. The medial lemniscus is located in the brainstem (medulla, pons, and midbrain), not the spinal cord. The pathway in the spinal cord that carries vibration and proprioception is the dorsal column. A lesion in the dorsal columns of the sacral cord would cause a loss of vibration and proprioception, but it would not affect the spinothalamic tracts, leaving pain and temperature sensation intact.

Confidence: 95%

3MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY Which one of the following statements is CORRECT regarding the Type IIB skeletal muscle fibres? Resistant to fatigue Have slow myosin-ATPase activity Characterised by high oxidative capacity Characterised by relatively large diameter

Answer: (D) — Characterised by relatively large diameter

Explanation:

Type IIB (also known as Type IIX in humans) skeletal muscle fibers are fast-twitch, glycolytic fibers adapted for producing rapid, powerful contractions for short durations. To generate this high level of force, they possess a large number of myofibrils packed together. This results in a significantly larger cross-sectional area, or diameter, compared to other fiber types. Force production in a muscle fiber is directly proportional to its cross-sectional area; therefore, the large diameter of Type IIB fibers is a key characteristic enabling their function in activities like sprinting or heavy weightlifting.

Options:

  • (A) Resistant to fatigue: This is incorrect. This characteristic describes Type I (slow-oxidative) fibers. Type IIB fibers rely on anaerobic glycolysis for ATP production, which is rapid but unsustainable, leading to quick accumulation of metabolic byproducts (like H+) and depletion of glycogen, causing them to fatigue very quickly.
  • (B) Have slow myosin-ATPase activity: This is incorrect. This is the defining feature of Type I (slow-twitch) fibers, resulting in a slow rate of cross-bridge cycling and contraction. Type IIB fibers have the fastest myosin-ATPase activity, which allows for the most rapid muscle contractions.
  • (C) Characterised by high oxidative capacity: This is incorrect. High oxidative capacity is characteristic of Type I fibers, which are rich in mitochondria, myoglobin, and capillaries to support aerobic respiration for sustained activity. Type IIB fibers have a low oxidative capacity, few mitochondria, and rely primarily on their large stores of glycogen for anaerobic energy production.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following statements regarding cardiac muscle is CORRECT?

Phases 0 to 2 is when the cardiac muscle is in its absolute refractory period
The contractile response lasts about 1.5 times as along as the action potential
Cardiac muscle has relatively high ATPase activity
Resting membrane potential is -70mV

Answer: (A) — Phases 0 to 2 is when the cardiac muscle is in its absolute refractory period

Explanation:

The cardiac action potential in a ventricular myocyte has several distinct phases. The absolute refractory period (ARP) is the time during which a new action potential cannot be elicited, regardless of the stimulus strength.

  • Phase 0 (Rapid Depolarization): Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels open, causing a rapid influx of Na⁺. These channels then quickly transition to an inactivated state.
  • Phase 1 (Initial Repolarization): Inactivation of Na⁺ channels and opening of transient K⁺ channels.
  • Phase 2 (Plateau): A balance between Ca²⁺ influx through L-type calcium channels and K⁺ efflux maintains a depolarized state.
  • Phase 3 (Rapid Repolarization): Ca²⁺ channels close, and K⁺ efflux dominates, returning the membrane potential to rest.

The ARP begins at the start of Phase 0 and extends through Phase 1 and Phase 2, ending in early Phase 3. Throughout this time, the voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are in an inactivated state and cannot be reopened until the membrane repolarizes sufficiently. This long ARP is a crucial protective mechanism for the heart, as it ensures that the mechanical contraction (systole) is nearly complete before another action potential can be generated. This prevents summation of contractions and tetanus, allowing the ventricles adequate time to fill with blood (diastole) between beats. Therefore, the statement that the ARP covers phases 0 to 2 is correct.

Options:

  • (B) The contractile response lasts about 1.5 times as long as the action potential: This is incorrect. A key feature of cardiac muscle is that the action potential duration is very long (approx. 250 ms) and is nearly as long as the mechanical contractile response or "twitch" (approx. 300 ms). The ratio is closer to 1.2, not 1.5. This close temporal relationship between the electrical and mechanical events is what prevents tetanus.
  • (C) Cardiac muscle has relatively high ATPase activity: This is incorrect. Cardiac muscle is designed for continuous, rhythmic contraction and is highly resistant to fatigue. It functions similarly to slow-twitch skeletal muscle. As such, its myosin ATPase activity is relatively low. This results in a slower rate of cross-bridge cycling and contraction, which is more energy-efficient compared to fast-twitch skeletal muscle that has high ATPase activity.
  • (D) Resting membrane potential is -70mV: This is incorrect. The resting membrane potential of a typical contractile cardiac myocyte (e.g., in the ventricles) is approximately -90 mV. This is more negative than that of skeletal muscle or neurons (which are typically around -70 mV) due to a higher resting permeability to potassium ions (K⁺) through inward rectifier K⁺ channels.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following hormonal polypeptides is NOT secreted by the pancreas?

Pancreatic polypeptide
Somatomedin
Insulin
Glucagon

Answer: (B) — Somatomedin x000D

Explanation:

Somatomedin, more commonly known as Insulin-like Growth Factor (IGF-1), is a polypeptide hormone that is crucial for growth and development. However, it is primarily synthesized and secreted by the liver in response to stimulation by Growth Hormone (GH) from the anterior pituitary gland. Somatomedin then acts on target tissues, such as bone and muscle, to mediate the growth-promoting effects of GH. While it shares some structural similarities with proinsulin, its primary site of production is not the pancreas.

The pancreas has both exocrine (secreting digestive enzymes) and endocrine functions. The endocrine function is carried out by clusters of cells called the islets of Langerhans, which secrete several key hormones directly into the bloodstream.

Options:

  • Pancreatic polypeptide: This hormone is secreted by the PP cells (or F cells) located in the islets of Langerhans within the pancreas. It plays a role in regulating pancreatic exocrine secretion and gastrointestinal motility.
  • Insulin: This is a well-known hormone secreted by the beta (β) cells of the pancreatic islets. It is the primary anabolic hormone responsible for lowering blood glucose levels by promoting glucose uptake and storage in cells.
  • Glucagon: This hormone is secreted by the alpha (α) cells of the pancreatic islets. It has effects opposite to insulin, raising blood glucose levels by stimulating glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis in the liver.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
What percentage of total body weight is the total blood volume?

15%
8%
5%
12%

Answer: (8%)

Explanation:

Total blood volume in a healthy adult is a relatively constant fraction of body weight. The widely accepted physiological value is approximately 7-8% of total body weight. For an average 70 kg (154 lb) adult, this calculates to a blood volume of about 5 to 6 liters (70 kg * 0.08 = 5.6 L).

Blood is composed of two main components: 1. Plasma: The liquid matrix, which makes up about 55% of blood volume. Plasma is approximately 92% water. 2. Formed Elements: These are the cells and cell fragments, making up about 45% of blood volume. This percentage is known as the hematocrit and consists primarily of red blood cells (erythrocytes), with a much smaller contribution from white blood cells (leukocytes) and platelets (thrombocytes).

This percentage can vary slightly based on factors like age (newborns have a higher percentage, ~10%), sex, and body composition (adipose tissue is less vascular than muscle, so obese individuals may have a lower percentage relative to their total weight).

Options:

  • 15%: This value is excessively high. A 15% blood volume in a 70 kg person would be 10.5 liters, a volume seen only in severe pathological conditions like massive fluid overload or extreme polycythemia vera.
  • 5%: This value is too low for a healthy, euhydrated adult. A 5% blood volume (3.5 L in a 70 kg person) might be seen in states of significant dehydration or following acute hemorrhage, but it does not represent the normal physiological baseline. This value is closer to the volume of plasma alone.
  • 12%: Similar to 15%, this is a pathologically high percentage. It would correspond to a blood volume of 8.4 liters in a 70 kg person, which is not consistent with normal physiology.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Regarding the process of diffusion and according to Fick’s Law of Diffusion, which one of the following statements is FALSE?

The time required for equilibrium by diffusion is proportional to the square of the diffusion distance
The permeability of the boundaries across which diffusion occurs in the body remains constant
The magnitude of diffusing tendency is directly proportional to the concentration gradient
Diffusion is a major force affecting the distribution of water and solutes

Answer: (B) — The permeability of the boundaries across which diffusion occurs in the body remains constant

Explanation:

This statement is false because the permeability of biological membranes is a highly regulated and dynamic variable, not a constant. Fick's Law, when applied to diffusion across a membrane, is often expressed as: J = P × A × (C₁ - C₂), where J is the rate of net diffusion, P is the permeability coefficient, A is the surface area, and (C₁ - C₂) is the concentration gradient.

In the body, the permeability (P) of cell membranes and epithelial layers can be altered significantly to meet physiological demands. For example: * Ion Channels: The permeability to ions like Na⁺, K⁺, and Ca²⁺ is controlled by the opening and closing of specific ion channels, which can be gated by voltage, ligands, or mechanical stress. * Aquaporins: The permeability of certain cells to water is regulated by the hormone ADH (antidiuretic hormone), which causes the insertion of aquaporin-2 water channels into the apical membrane of kidney collecting duct cells, dramatically increasing water reabsorption. * Transporters: The activity and number of carrier proteins, like GLUT4 transporters for glucose, can be increased in response to insulin, thereby changing the membrane's "permeability" to glucose.

Therefore, the ability to modulate permeability is a fundamental mechanism of physiological control.

Options:

  • (A) The time required for equilibrium by diffusion is proportional to the square of the diffusion distance: This statement is true. The relationship, derived from principles of random walk (Einstein's relation), is approximately t ∝ x²/2D, where 't' is time, 'x' is distance, and 'D' is the diffusion coefficient. This principle explains why diffusion is very rapid over microscopic distances (e.g., across a synapse) but extremely slow over macroscopic distances, necessitating a circulatory system for long-distance transport in large organisms.

  • (C) The magnitude of diffusing tendency is directly proportional to the concentration gradient: This statement is true. It is the fundamental principle of Fick's First Law of Diffusion. The net movement of particles (flux) occurs from a region of high concentration to a region of low concentration, and the rate of this movement is directly driven by the steepness of this concentration gradient. A larger difference in concentration results in a faster rate of diffusion.

  • (D) Diffusion is a major force affecting the distribution of water and solutes: This statement is true. Diffusion is a fundamental physical process responsible for the passive movement of most substances within the body. This includes the exchange of gases (O₂ and CO₂) in the lungs and tissues, the movement of nutrients and waste products across capillary walls and cell membranes, and the movement of water across membranes via osmosis (a specific case of diffusion).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Frusemide inhibits sodium reabsorption from which PART of the nephron?

Distal convoluted tubule
Thin descending limb of loop of Henle
Thin ascending limb of loop of Henle
Thick ascending limb of loop of Henle

Answer: (D) — Thick ascending limb of loop of Henle

Explanation:

Furosemide (also spelled frusemide) is a potent loop diuretic. Its primary site of action is the thick ascending limb of the loop of Henle. Here, it inhibits the Na⁺-K⁺-2Cl⁻ cotransporter (NKCC2) located on the apical (luminal) membrane of the tubular epithelial cells.

By blocking this transporter, furosemide prevents the reabsorption of sodium (Na⁺), potassium (K⁺), and chloride (Cl⁻) from the tubular fluid into the cells. This action disrupts the generation of the hypertonic medullary interstitium, which is crucial for the countercurrent multiplication mechanism. A less concentrated medullary interstitium impairs the ability of the collecting ducts to reabsorb water, leading to a significant increase in the excretion of water, sodium, and other electrolytes, resulting in a powerful diuretic effect.

Options:

  • Distal convoluted tubule: This is the primary site of action for thiazide diuretics (e.g., hydrochlorothiazide), which inhibit the Na⁺-Cl⁻ cotransporter (NCC). Furosemide has a weak effect here, but its main, potent action is not in this segment.
  • Thin descending limb of loop of Henle: This segment is highly permeable to water but has low permeability to solutes like sodium. Water is passively reabsorbed here due to the osmotic gradient created by the surrounding hypertonic medulla. There are no major active sodium transport mechanisms for furosemide to inhibit in this part.
  • Thin ascending limb of loop of Henle: This segment is impermeable to water but allows for the passive reabsorption of Na⁺ and Cl⁻ down their concentration gradients. It does not contain the Na⁺-K⁺-2Cl⁻ cotransporter that furosemide targets.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A 20-year old presents with a paracetamol overdose.

By which one of the following mechanisms do the toxic metabolites of paracetamol formed in the liver?

The cytochrome p450 mixed function oxidase system
Anaerobic glycolysis
Glucuronidation
Sulphation

Answer: (A) — The cytochrome p450 mixed function oxidase system Explanation: The toxic metabolite of paracetamol (acetaminophen) is N-acetyl-p-benzoquinone imine (NAPQI). This highly reactive compound is formed in the liver through a Phase I metabolic reaction catalysed by the cytochrome P450 (CYP) mixed-function oxidase system, primarily involving the isoenzyme CYP2E1.

In therapeutic doses, the vast majority of paracetamol is safely metabolised via Phase II conjugation reactions (glucuronidation and sulphation) into non-toxic, water-soluble compounds that are excreted by the kidneys. Only a small fraction is metabolised via the CYP450 pathway into NAPQI, which is then rapidly detoxified by conjugation with glutathione.

In an overdose, the primary glucuronidation and sulphation pathways become saturated. This shunts a much larger proportion of the paracetamol dose down the CYP450 pathway, leading to excessive production of the toxic NAPQI. The liver's stores of glutathione are quickly depleted, and the unbound NAPQI then covalently binds to essential hepatocellular proteins, causing oxidative damage and centrilobular liver necrosis.

Options: * Anaerobic glycolysis: This is a metabolic pathway for energy (ATP) production from glucose in the absence of oxygen. It is unrelated to drug metabolism. * Glucuronidation: This is a major Phase II detoxification pathway for paracetamol. It conjugates paracetamol with glucuronic acid to form a non-toxic, excretable metabolite. It does not form the toxic metabolite. * Sulphation: This is another major Phase II detoxification pathway for paracetamol. It conjugates the drug with a sulphate group, rendering it non-toxic and water-soluble for excretion. It does not form the toxic metabolite.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
In overdose and toxicity, which ONE of the following agent’s elimination is enhanced by dialysis?

Digoxin
Benzodiazepines
Aspirin
Organophosphates

Answer: (C) — Aspirin Explanation: The effectiveness of dialysis in removing a substance from the body depends on several key pharmacokinetic properties: a small molecular weight, a low volume of distribution (Vd), low protein binding, and high water solubility.

Aspirin (salicylates) is an ideal candidate for removal by hemodialysis in cases of severe overdose. * Molecular Weight: Salicylic acid, the active metabolite, has a low molecular weight (~138 g/mol), allowing it to pass easily through the dialysis membrane. * Volume of Distribution (Vd): It has a relatively low volume of distribution (0.1-0.2 L/kg), meaning a significant portion of the drug remains in the bloodstream where it can be accessed by the dialysis machine. * Protein Binding: While protein binding is high at therapeutic levels, it is saturable. In an overdose, a larger fraction of the drug is unbound and free in the plasma, making it available for dialysis. * Clinical Benefit: Severe aspirin toxicity causes profound metabolic acidosis and electrolyte disturbances. Hemodialysis is highly effective not only at removing the salicylate but also at correcting these life-threatening metabolic derangements. Therefore, it is a cornerstone of management in severe cases.

Options: * (A) Digoxin: Incorrect. Digoxin has a very large volume of distribution (Vd ≈ 5-7 L/kg) because it is extensively distributed into tissues, particularly skeletal and cardiac muscle. Consequently, only a very small fraction of the total body burden of digoxin is in the plasma at any given time, making dialysis ineffective for enhancing its elimination. The primary treatment for severe digoxin toxicity is digoxin-specific antibody fragments (DigiFab).

  • (B) Benzodiazepines: Incorrect. Benzodiazepines are highly lipid-soluble and have a large volume of distribution. They are also highly bound to plasma proteins (e.g., diazepam is >98% bound). These factors make them poorly amenable to removal by dialysis. Treatment for overdose is primarily supportive, with the specific antagonist flumazenil used in certain situations.

  • (D) Organophosphates: Incorrect. Organophosphates are highly lipid-soluble compounds with a large volume of distribution, sequestering them in adipose tissue. Furthermore, their primary toxicity stems from the irreversible inhibition of the enzyme acetylcholinesterase. Removing the small amount of unbound compound from the plasma via dialysis does not reverse this established enzyme inhibition. Treatment focuses on supportive care, atropine to manage muscarinic symptoms, and pralidoxime to reactivate the inhibited enzyme.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 42-year old female has lethargy, hyponatremia and hyperkalemia. She does not have any abnormal pigmentation on her skin. Her condition improves after she was started on ACTH.

She MOST likely has:

Primary abnormality in the hypothalamus leading to hyperaldosteronism
Primary abnormality in the pituitary gland leading to hyperaldosteronism
Primary abnormality in the adrenal gland leading to adrenal insufficiency
Primary abnormality in the pituitary gland leading to adrenal insufficiency

Answer: (D) — Primary abnormality in the pituitary gland leading to adrenal insufficiency

Explanation:

This patient presents with a classic triad of symptoms and lab findings that point towards a specific type of adrenal insufficiency. Let's break down the clinical picture:

  1. Lethargy, Hyponatremia, Hyperkalemia: These are hallmark signs of adrenal insufficiency.

    • Lethargy is caused by cortisol deficiency.
    • Hyponatremia (low sodium) and hyperkalemia (high potassium) are caused by aldosterone deficiency. Aldosterone normally promotes sodium reabsorption and potassium excretion in the kidneys.

  2. No Abnormal Skin Pigmentation: This is a crucial diagnostic clue. Hyperpigmentation is characteristic of primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's disease), where the adrenal glands fail. The lack of cortisol production removes negative feedback on the pituitary, leading to a massive increase in Adrenocorticotropic Hormone (ACTH) secretion. The precursor molecule for ACTH (POMC) also yields Melanocyte-Stimulating Hormone (MSH), causing the skin to darken. The absence of hyperpigmentation in this patient suggests that ACTH levels are low, not high, pointing to a problem with the pituitary or hypothalamus.

  3. Improvement with ACTH: This is the definitive test presented in the vignette. Administering exogenous ACTH stimulates the adrenal glands. The patient's improvement indicates that her adrenal glands are functional but are not receiving the signal (ACTH) to produce hormones. This rules out a primary adrenal problem (where the glands are damaged and would not respond) and confirms the issue is upstream in the pituitary or hypothalamus.

Combining these findings, the patient has adrenal insufficiency caused by a failure of the pituitary gland to secrete ACTH. This is known as secondary adrenal insufficiency.

Options:

  • (A) Primary abnormality in the hypothalamus leading to hyperaldosteronism: Incorrect. The patient's symptoms (hyponatremia, hyperkalemia) indicate hypoaldosteronism (part of adrenal insufficiency), not hyperaldosteronism.
  • (B) Primary abnormality in the pituitary gland leading to hyperaldosteronism: Incorrect. As with option A, the patient has signs of adrenal insufficiency, not hyperfunction (hyperaldosteronism).
  • (C) Primary abnormality in the adrenal gland leading to adrenal insufficiency: Incorrect. This describes primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's disease). This diagnosis is ruled out by two key findings: the absence of hyperpigmentation and the positive response to ACTH administration.
  • (D) Primary abnormality in the pituitary gland leading to adrenal insufficiency: Correct. This diagnosis (secondary adrenal insufficiency) perfectly aligns with all the clinical data: symptoms of adrenal insufficiency, low ACTH (inferred from the lack of hyperpigmentation), and functional adrenal glands (proven by the positive response to ACTH).

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 25-year-old female presents with dehydration.

The secretion of which ONE of the following hormones is least affected?

Vasopressin
Estrogens
Aldosterone
Corticotropin releasing hormone

Answer: (B) — Estrogens Explanation: Dehydration is a state of negative fluid balance characterized by a decrease in total body water, which leads to increased plasma osmolarity and decreased blood volume. The body mounts a robust neurohormonal response to counteract these changes.

  • Estrogens are steroid hormones primarily involved in the regulation of the female reproductive cycle and the development of secondary sexual characteristics. Their secretion is controlled by the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal (HPG) axis (GnRH, LH, FSH) and is cyclical in nature. While severe, chronic physiological stress can disrupt the HPG axis, the secretion of estrogens is not directly or acutely regulated by plasma volume or osmolarity. Therefore, among the choices provided, estrogen secretion would be the least affected by an acute state of dehydration.

Options: * (A) Vasopressin: Incorrect. Vasopressin, or Antidiuretic Hormone (ADH), is a key hormone in fluid balance. Its secretion from the posterior pituitary is powerfully stimulated by even small increases in plasma osmolarity (detected by osmoreceptors in the hypothalamus) and by significant decreases in blood volume/pressure (detected by baroreceptors). In dehydration, vasopressin levels rise sharply to increase water reabsorption in the kidneys. * (C) Aldosterone: Incorrect. Aldosterone secretion is primarily controlled by the Renin-Angiotensin-Aldosterone System (RAAS). Dehydration leads to decreased blood volume and renal perfusion, which triggers the release of renin from the kidneys. This activates the RAAS cascade, culminating in increased Angiotensin II levels, which is a potent stimulator of aldosterone secretion from the adrenal cortex. Aldosterone then acts to increase sodium and water retention by the kidneys. * (D) Corticotropin releasing hormone (CRH): Incorrect. Dehydration is a significant physiological stressor. The body's response to stress is mediated by the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis. The hypothalamus releases CRH in response to stress, which stimulates the pituitary to release ACTH, leading to cortisol secretion. Therefore, CRH secretion would be increased during dehydration.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Regarding the circulatory system, which ONE of the following has the largest total cross-sectional area?

Capillaries
Venules
Arterioles
Veins

Answer: (A) — Capillaries

Explanation:

The total cross-sectional area of a specific level of the circulatory system (e.g., all arteries, all arterioles, all capillaries) is the sum of the individual cross-sectional areas of all the vessels at that level. While a single capillary has the smallest diameter of any blood vessel, the sheer number of capillaries in the body is immense (billions). This massive number means that when their individual areas are summed up, they collectively have the largest total cross-sectional area in the entire circulatory system.

This large area has a critical physiological consequence. According to the principle of continuity in fluid dynamics (Flow = Velocity × Area), blood flow velocity is inversely proportional to the total cross-sectional area. Therefore, the blood flows slowest through the capillaries. This slow transit time is essential for maximizing the exchange of oxygen, carbon dioxide, nutrients, and waste products between the blood and the surrounding tissues.

Options:

  • (B) Venules: Capillaries converge to form venules. Although individual venules are larger than individual capillaries, there are far fewer of them. Consequently, the total cross-sectional area of all venules is significantly smaller than that of the capillaries.
  • (C) Arterioles: Arterioles branch from arteries and lead into the capillary beds. They are fewer in number and individually larger than capillaries. Therefore, their total cross-sectional area is much less than that of the vast capillary network they feed.
  • (D) Veins: Veins are formed by the convergence of venules. While veins (especially the large ones) have a large diameter and hold a significant portion of the blood volume (high capacitance), their total cross-sectional area is substantially less than that of the capillaries.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which ONE of the following substances penetrate the brain slowest?

Water
Carbondioxide
Glucose
Oxygen

Answer: (C) — Glucose Explanation: The rate at which substances penetrate the brain is determined by the properties of the blood-brain barrier (BBB). The BBB is a highly selective semipermeable border of endothelial cells. * Glucose is a large, polar molecule. It cannot passively diffuse across the lipid membranes of the BBB. Its entry into the brain is entirely dependent on a specific carrier-mediated transport system, namely the glucose transporter 1 (GLUT1). This process, known as facilitated diffusion, is saturable and rate-limited by the number and efficiency of these transporters. Compared to the simple diffusion of small, lipid-soluble molecules, this process is significantly slower.

Options: * (A) Water: Water is a small molecule that can cross the BBB relatively quickly. Its movement occurs both by diffusing across the lipid membrane and, more significantly, through specialized water channels called aquaporins (especially AQP1 and AQP4). * (B) Carbon dioxide: Carbon dioxide is a small, highly lipid-soluble gas. It can rapidly and freely diffuse across the BBB down its partial pressure gradient. This rapid diffusion is crucial for the central chemoreceptor-mediated control of respiration. * (D) Oxygen: Similar to carbon dioxide, oxygen is a small, lipid-soluble gas. It diffuses very rapidly across the BBB to meet the brain's high metabolic demand. The rate of diffusion is driven by the partial pressure gradient between the blood and the brain tissue.

In summary, the transport of gases (O2, CO2) and water is much faster than the carrier-mediated transport required for glucose. Therefore, glucose penetrates the brain the slowest among the given options.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Angiotensin II causes all of the following EXCEPT:

Increases thirst
Stimulates aldosterone release
Stimulate ADH release
Vasodilation

Answer: (D) — Vasodilation

Explanation:

Angiotensin II is a key hormone in the Renin-Angiotensin-Aldosterone System (RAAS), which plays a crucial role in regulating blood pressure and fluid balance. The primary overall effect of Angiotensin II is to increase blood pressure. It achieves this through several mechanisms. The option that contradicts this primary function is vasodilation. Angiotensin II is, in fact, one of the most potent vasoconstrictors in the body. It acts directly on the smooth muscle of arterioles, causing them to constrict. This constriction increases total peripheral resistance (TPR), which directly elevates systemic blood pressure.

Options:

  • (A) Increases thirst: This is a correct function of Angiotensin II. It acts centrally on the hypothalamus (specifically the subfornical organ) to stimulate the sensation of thirst, encouraging water intake, which helps to increase blood volume and pressure.
  • (B) Stimulates aldosterone release: This is a hallmark function of Angiotensin II. It acts on the zona glomerulosa of the adrenal cortex to stimulate the synthesis and secretion of aldosterone. Aldosterone, in turn, acts on the kidneys to increase the reabsorption of sodium and water, further increasing blood volume and pressure.
  • (C) Stimulates ADH release: This is another correct function. Angiotensin II acts on the posterior pituitary gland to stimulate the release of Antidiuretic Hormone (ADH, or vasopressin). ADH increases water reabsorption in the collecting ducts of the kidneys, which concentrates urine and conserves body water, thus increasing blood volume and pressure.
  • (D) Vasodilation: This is the incorrect statement and therefore the correct answer to the "EXCEPT" question. Vasodilation is the widening of blood vessels, which would decrease blood pressure. Angiotensin II causes the opposite effect: potent vasoconstriction.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
You are resuscitating a patient with profuse blood loss with massive transfusion. The patient inadvertently receives packed red blood cell transfusion of a different blood group than his own, however the patient does not have a transfusion reaction.

What is the MOST LIKELY blood group of the patient?

O positive
AB positive
O negative
AB negative

Answer: (B) — AB positive Explanation: The key to this question lies in understanding the basis of transfusion reactions. These reactions are typically caused by the recipient's plasma antibodies attacking antigens on the surface of the donor's red blood cells (RBCs).

A patient with blood group AB positive is known as the "universal recipient" for packed red blood cells (PRBCs). This is because: 1. ABO System: Individuals with type AB blood have both A and B antigens on their RBCs. Consequently, their immune system recognizes both antigens as "self," and they do not produce anti-A or anti-B antibodies in their plasma. The absence of these antibodies means they can receive RBCs from donors of type A, B, AB, or O without causing an ABO-related hemolytic reaction. 2. Rhesus (Rh) System: An Rh-positive individual has the RhD antigen on their RBCs. Therefore, their body will not mount an immune response against RhD-positive blood. They can safely receive blood from both Rh-positive and Rh-negative donors.

Given that the patient received a different blood group without a reaction, it is most likely they are a universal recipient who lacks the antibodies that would attack the foreign RBCs. An AB positive patient fits this description perfectly.

Options: * (A) O positive: Incorrect. A person with type O blood has both anti-A and anti-B antibodies. They would have a severe transfusion reaction if they received A, B, or AB blood. * (C) O negative: Incorrect. Similar to O positive, this patient has both anti-A and anti-B antibodies and would react to non-O blood groups. They are universal donors of RBCs, not universal recipients. * (D) AB negative: Incorrect. While a person with AB negative blood is a universal recipient within the ABO system (lacking anti-A and anti-B antibodies), they are Rh-negative. If they received Rh-positive blood (e.g., A positive, B positive, O positive), they could become sensitized or have a delayed hemolytic reaction, especially if previously exposed to the Rh antigen. Since AB positive patients can tolerate both Rh+ and Rh- blood, they are the most likely group to avoid a reaction from any randomly selected "different" blood type.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A patient presents with difficulty swallowing. You suspect a neurological cause for this.

Which ONE of the following nerves mediates the afferent and efferent impulses of the swallowing reflex response?

Trigeminal
Vagus
Glossopharyngeal
Facial

Answer: (C) — Glossopharyngeal

Explanation:

The swallowing reflex (deglutition) is a complex neuromuscular process with both voluntary and involuntary phases. The question focuses on the reflex response, which primarily refers to the involuntary pharyngeal phase. This reflex requires both a sensory (afferent) limb to detect the bolus and a motor (efferent) limb to execute the swallow.

The Glossopharyngeal nerve (CN IX) is the principal nerve mediating both of these functions for the initiation of the swallow reflex. * Afferent (Sensory) Limb: CN IX carries general sensory information from the posterior one-third of the tongue, the soft palate, the tonsils, and the pharyngeal walls. When a food bolus stimulates tactile receptors in these areas, CN IX transmits this signal to the swallowing center in the brainstem (medulla oblongata), thus triggering the reflex. * Efferent (Motor) Limb: CN IX also provides motor innervation to the stylopharyngeus muscle, which plays a role in elevating and widening the pharynx during swallowing.

Because the Glossopharyngeal nerve has a critical, well-defined role in both carrying the primary sensory trigger (afferent) and contributing to the motor response (efferent), it is the correct answer.

Options:

  • (A) Trigeminal: The trigeminal nerve (CN V) is primarily involved in the voluntary oral phase of swallowing. It provides general sensation to the anterior two-thirds of the tongue and oral cavity and motor innervation to the muscles of mastication. It is not the primary mediator of the involuntary pharyngeal reflex.
  • (B) Vagus: The vagus nerve (CN X) is the primary efferent (motor) nerve of the pharyngeal phase, innervating most of the muscles of the soft palate, pharynx, and larynx. It also has a sensory (afferent) role, particularly from the larynx and lower pharynx, which is crucial for airway protection (e.g., cough reflex). However, the glossopharyngeal nerve (CN IX) is considered the primary trigger for the swallowing reflex itself.
  • (D) Facial: The facial nerve (CN VII) is mainly involved in the voluntary oral phase. It provides motor innervation to the muscles of facial expression, such as the buccinator (cheek muscle) and orbicularis oris (lip muscle), which help contain food in the mouth. It does not mediate the primary swallowing reflex arc.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Gastroenteritis can lead to significant fluid losses.

Which ONE of the following organs contributes the most to endogenous gastrointestinal secretions?

Bile
Pancreas
Stomach
Salivary glands

Answer: (C) — Stomach

Explanation:

The gastrointestinal (GI) tract secretes a large volume of fluid daily to aid in digestion and lubrication. When comparing the major sources of endogenous secretions, the stomach produces the largest volume. Gastric juice, which is composed of hydrochloric acid, pepsinogen, intrinsic factor, and mucus, is secreted at a rate of approximately 2 to 3 liters per day. This substantial volume is crucial for sterilizing food, denaturing proteins, and initiating protein digestion.

Options:

  • (A) Bile: Bile is produced by the liver and stored in the gallbladder. Its daily secretion volume is approximately 0.5 to 1 liter. While essential for fat emulsification and absorption, its volume is significantly less than that of gastric secretions.
  • (B) Pancreas: The pancreas secretes a bicarbonate-rich fluid containing a wide array of digestive enzymes. The daily volume of pancreatic secretion is substantial, around 1.5 to 2 liters, but it is typically less than the volume secreted by the stomach.
  • (D) Salivary glands: The salivary glands produce saliva, which initiates carbohydrate digestion and lubricates food for swallowing. The total daily volume of saliva production is about 1.5 liters, which is less than the volume of gastric juice.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following statements about defecation is CORRECT?

Initiated by a stretch reflex which involves primarily the lumbar segments of the spinal cord
Is a spinal reflex
Ceases during starvation
Does not affect the venous return to the heart

Answer: (B) — Is a spinal reflex

Explanation: The defecation reflex is an autonomic process that results in the emptying of the bowels. The primary mechanism is a spinal reflex, specifically a parasympathetic reflex, mediated by the sacral segments of the spinal cord (S2, S3, S4). When feces enter and distend the rectum, stretch receptors in the rectal wall are activated. Afferent nerve signals are sent to the sacral spinal cord. In response, efferent parasympathetic signals travel back to the descending colon, sigmoid colon, and rectum via the pelvic splanchnic nerves. This causes powerful peristaltic contractions of the colon and rectum and relaxation of the internal anal sphincter, propelling feces towards the anus. While this reflex can be voluntarily inhibited or facilitated by higher brain centers through control of the external anal sphincter and abdominal muscles, the core reflex arc itself is located in the spinal cord.

Options: (A) Initiated by a stretch reflex which involves primarily the lumbar segments of the spinal cord: This statement is incorrect. While it is initiated by a stretch reflex, the parasympathetic nerve fibers that control the reflex originate from the sacral segments (S2-S4) of the spinal cord, not the lumbar segments.

(C) Ceases during starvation: This is incorrect. Even during prolonged fasting or starvation, the gastrointestinal tract continues to produce secretions, shed mucosal cells, and host a large population of bacteria. These components, along with indigestible material, form a small amount of fecal matter. Therefore, while the frequency and volume of defecation are significantly reduced, it does not completely cease.

(D) Does not affect the venous return to the heart: This is incorrect. The act of defecation, especially if it involves straining (the Valsalva maneuver), significantly impacts cardiovascular hemodynamics. Straining increases intrathoracic and intra-abdominal pressure, which compresses the large veins (like the vena cava) and impedes the flow of blood back to the heart, thereby decreasing venous return.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
In profuse diarrhoea, large volumes of water are lost.

In a normal state, what percentage of fluid does the colon reabsorb?

30%
70%
90%
50%

Answer: (C) — 90%

Explanation:

The gastrointestinal (GI) tract processes a large volume of fluid daily, approximately 9 liters. This includes ingested fluids (about 2 L) and endogenous secretions like saliva, gastric juice, bile, and pancreatic juice (about 7 L).

The vast majority of this fluid is absorbed in the small intestine. About 1.5 to 2 liters of fluid chyme pass from the ileum into the colon each day. The colon's primary function is to absorb the remaining water and electrolytes. It is highly efficient in this role, absorbing approximately 90% of the fluid it receives. This process concentrates the fecal matter, leaving only about 100-200 mL of water to be excreted in the feces daily.

Therefore, the colon reabsorbs about 90% of the fluid that enters it from the small intestine. In profuse diarrhea, this absorptive capacity is either overwhelmed by excessive fluid entering the colon or impaired by disease, leading to significant water loss.

Options:

  • (A) 30%: This is incorrect. If the colon only reabsorbed 30% of the 1.5 L it receives, approximately 1.05 L of water would be lost in the stool daily, which would be defined as diarrhea.
  • (B) 70%: This is also incorrect. While a significant amount, the colon is more efficient. Reabsorbing only 70% would still result in a fecal water content of around 450 mL, which is abnormally high.
  • (D) 50%: This is incorrect. This level of absorption is significantly lower than the colon's normal capacity and would lead to substantial daily fluid loss in the feces.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following is NOT a physiological effect of thyroid hormones?

Stimulate lipogenesis
Increased protein breakdown
Increased rate of carbohydrate absorption
Increased number of beta-adrenergic receptors

Answer: (A) — Stimulate lipogenesis

Explanation:

Thyroid hormones (T3 and T4) are crucial regulators of the body's basal metabolic rate (BMR). Their overall effect is catabolic, meaning they promote the breakdown of energy stores to increase energy availability and consumption. The primary effect of thyroid hormones on adipose tissue is to stimulate lipolysis, which is the breakdown of stored triglycerides into free fatty acids and glycerol. These fatty acids are then used by other tissues for energy through beta-oxidation. Stimulating lipogenesis (the synthesis of fatty acids and triglycerides for storage) would be counterintuitive to the primary function of thyroid hormones, which is to increase metabolism and energy expenditure. Therefore, stimulating lipogenesis is not a physiological effect of thyroid hormones; they stimulate its opposite process, lipolysis.

Options:

  • (B) Increased protein breakdown: This is a well-established effect of thyroid hormones. In hyperthyroid states, high levels of thyroid hormone lead to a net catabolic effect on proteins, causing muscle wasting, weakness, and a negative nitrogen balance.
  • (C) Increased rate of carbohydrate absorption: Thyroid hormones enhance the absorption of carbohydrates from the gastrointestinal tract. This effect, along with increased gluconeogenesis and glycogenolysis in the liver, helps to provide the necessary glucose to fuel the heightened metabolic rate.
  • (D) Increased number of beta-adrenergic receptors: Thyroid hormones have a significant permissive effect on catecholamines (epinephrine and norepinephrine). They achieve this by upregulating the number and sensitivity of beta-adrenergic receptors in various tissues, including the heart, skeletal muscle, and adipose tissue. This synergism explains many of the cardiovascular and metabolic symptoms of hyperthyroidism, such as tachycardia, palpitations, and increased lipolysis.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
You are about to start NSAID therapy for a patient who has had previous history of reflux disease.

Which ONE of the following has the lowest risk of serious gastrointestinal tract reactions?

Indomethacin
Naproxen
Ketorolac
Ibuprofen

Answer: (D) — Ibuprofen

Explanation

The risk of serious gastrointestinal (GI) tract reactions with Non-Steroidal Anti-Inflammatory Drugs (NSAIDs) is primarily related to the inhibition of the cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) enzyme. COX-1 is responsible for producing prostaglandins that protect the gastric mucosa by stimulating mucus and bicarbonate secretion and maintaining mucosal blood flow. The degree of GI toxicity generally correlates with the potency and duration of COX-1 inhibition.

Among the options provided, ibuprofen (particularly at lower doses, ≤1200 mg/day) is associated with one of the lowest relative risks of serious GI complications compared to other traditional, non-selective NSAIDs. While the risk increases with higher doses, its overall GI safety profile is considered more favorable than the other listed agents for initiating therapy.

Options

  • Indomethacin: This is a potent, non-selective COX inhibitor known for having a high incidence of GI side effects, including dyspepsia, ulceration, and bleeding. It is generally considered one of the more toxic NSAIDs in terms of GI adverse events.
  • Naproxen: While having a more favorable cardiovascular risk profile than some other NSAIDs, naproxen carries an intermediate to high risk of GI complications. Its risk is consistently shown to be higher than that of low-dose ibuprofen.
  • Ketorolac: This is a very potent NSAID with a particularly high risk of severe GI reactions, such as perforation, ulceration, and serious bleeding. Due to this high risk, its use is restricted to a maximum of 5 days for the management of acute, moderately severe pain. It has the highest GI toxicity among the choices.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
The analgesic and dependence effect of various opiates and related agents is dependent on their mu-receptor affinity.

Which one of the following has the WEAKEST mu-receptor affinity?

Tramadol
Codiene
Tapentadol
Buprenorphine

Answer: (A) — Tramadol

Explanation:

Tramadol is a centrally-acting synthetic analgesic with a dual mechanism of action. It is a very weak agonist at the mu-opioid receptor. Its affinity for the mu-receptor is approximately 1/6000th that of morphine. A significant portion of its analgesic effect comes from its second mechanism: the inhibition of serotonin and norepinephrine reuptake in the central nervous system, which enhances inhibitory descending pain pathways. While its primary metabolite, O-desmethyltramadol (M1), has a much higher affinity for the mu-receptor (about 200 times that of the parent drug), tramadol itself has the weakest affinity among the options provided.

Options:

  • (B) Codeine: Codeine is a prodrug that is metabolized into morphine by the enzyme CYP2D6. Codeine itself has a low affinity for the mu-opioid receptor (about 1/200th that of morphine), but this affinity is still considerably stronger than that of the parent drug tramadol.
  • (C) Tapentadol: Similar to tramadol, tapentadol has a dual mechanism of action, acting as both a mu-opioid receptor agonist and a norepinephrine reuptake inhibitor. However, its affinity for the mu-receptor is significantly higher than tramadol's (approximately 1/50th that of morphine), making it a more potent opioid agonist than tramadol.
  • (D) Buprenorphine: Buprenorphine is a partial agonist at the mu-opioid receptor and an antagonist at the kappa-opioid receptor. A key characteristic of buprenorphine is its extremely high affinity (strong binding) for the mu-receptor, which is even higher than that of morphine. This high affinity allows it to displace other full agonists like heroin or methadone from the receptor, which is why it is effective in the treatment of opioid use disorder. Therefore, it has the strongest affinity of the drugs listed, not the weakest.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Proton pump inhibitors are MOST effective when they are given:

During prolonged fasting periods
After meals
Shortly before meals
Along with H2 blockers

Answer: (C) — Shortly before meals

Explanation:

Proton pump inhibitors (PPIs) are a class of drugs that potently suppress gastric acid secretion. They are weak bases and are administered as inactive prodrugs. For them to be effective, they must undergo the following steps: 1. Absorption from the small intestine into the bloodstream. 2. Travel through the bloodstream to the parietal cells of the stomach lining. 3. Accumulation in the highly acidic secretory canaliculi of the parietal cells. 4. Conversion to their active form (a sulfenamide) by the acidic environment. 5. The active form then forms an irreversible covalent bond with the H⁺/K⁺-ATPase enzyme (the proton pump), inactivating it.

Crucially, PPIs can only bind to and inhibit active proton pumps, i.e., those that have been inserted into the canaliculus membrane to secrete acid. The greatest number of proton pumps are activated in response to a meal. Therefore, by administering the PPI 30 to 60 minutes before a meal (typically the first meal of the day), the peak plasma concentration of the drug coincides with the peak activation of the proton pumps, leading to maximum inhibition and therapeutic effect.

Options:

  • (A) During prolonged fasting periods: This is incorrect. During fasting, gastric acid secretion is at a basal level, and most proton pumps are inactive and sequestered within the cytoplasm of the parietal cells. Administering a PPI at this time would be ineffective as there are very few active pumps to inhibit.
  • (B) After meals: This is also incorrect and less effective. If a PPI is taken after a meal, the peak of meal-stimulated acid secretion may have already occurred by the time the drug reaches its peak concentration. This mismatch in timing results in suboptimal inhibition of the proton pumps.
  • (D) Along with H2 blockers: This is generally not recommended for maximizing PPI efficacy. H₂ receptor antagonists (like famotidine or cimetidine) work by blocking one of the key stimuli (histamine) for acid secretion. This reduces the activation of proton pumps. Giving an H₂ blocker concurrently would therefore reduce the number of active pumps available for the PPI to inhibit, potentially making the PPI less effective.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A 20-year old presents with sudden onset of palpitations. Her heart rate is 220 and her ECG shows regular narrow complex tachycardia. You decide to treat her with verapamil.

Which one of the following statements regarding verapamil is CORRECT?

Its cardiotoxic effects are dose-related
Enhances delayed afterpolarisation
Can cause diarrhea
Activates L-type calcium channels

Answer: (A) — Its cardiotoxic effects are dose-related

Explanation:

Verapamil is a non-dihydropyridine calcium channel blocker (CCB). Its primary mechanism of action is to block L-type voltage-gated calcium channels, particularly in cardiac and vascular smooth muscle tissue.

The therapeutic effects of verapamil on the heart include: * Negative Chronotropy: Decreased heart rate by slowing the rate of firing of the sinoatrial (SA) node. * Negative Dromotropy: Slowed conduction velocity through the atrioventricular (AV) node. This is the primary mechanism for its use in supraventricular tachycardias (SVT). * Negative Inotropy: Decreased myocardial contractility.

Cardiotoxicity is an extension of these therapeutic effects. As the dose of verapamil increases, these effects become more pronounced and can lead to adverse outcomes such as severe bradycardia, high-degree AV block, profound hypotension, and cardiogenic shock due to severely depressed contractility. Therefore, the cardiotoxic effects are directly related to the dose administered.

Options:

  • (B) Enhances delayed afterpolarisation: This is incorrect. Delayed afterdepolarizations (DADs) are caused by intracellular calcium overload. Verapamil, by blocking calcium influx into the cell, reduces intracellular calcium levels. Consequently, it suppresses DADs and is used to treat arrhythmias caused by this mechanism.
  • (C) Can cause diarrhea: This is incorrect. Verapamil relaxes smooth muscle, including that of the gastrointestinal tract. This effect slows down gut motility, leading to constipation, which is a very common side effect of the drug. Diarrhea is not a characteristic side effect.
  • (D) Activates L-type calcium channels: This is incorrect. Verapamil is a calcium channel blocker or antagonist. It inhibits the function of L-type calcium channels, preventing the influx of calcium into cells. Activation is the opposite of its mechanism of action.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which of the following is the BEST inotrope for use in right heart failure?

Dopamine
Milrinone
Digoxin
Dobutamine

Answer: (B) — Milrinone Explanation: Right heart failure (RHF) is characterized by the inability of the right ventricle (RV) to pump blood effectively into the pulmonary circulation. This is often exacerbated by high pulmonary vascular resistance (PVR), which increases the afterload the RV must pump against. The ideal inotrope for RHF should therefore not only increase RV contractility (positive inotropy) but also reduce the RV afterload by causing pulmonary vasodilation.

Milrinone is a phosphodiesterase-3 (PDE3) inhibitor. Its mechanism of action is ideal for treating RHF: 1. Positive Inotropy: By inhibiting PDE3 in cardiac myocytes, milrinone prevents the breakdown of cyclic AMP (cAMP). Increased cAMP levels lead to activation of protein kinase A, which phosphorylates calcium channels, resulting in increased intracellular calcium and enhanced myocardial contractility. 2. Pulmonary Vasodilation: In vascular smooth muscle (including the pulmonary arteries), inhibition of PDE3 also increases cAMP, leading to smooth muscle relaxation and potent vasodilation. This directly reduces PVR, decreasing the afterload on the failing right ventricle.

This dual action as an "inodilator" (inotrope + vasodilator) makes milrinone particularly effective for right heart failure, especially when it is associated with pulmonary hypertension.

Options: (A) Dopamine: Dopamine is a catecholamine with dose-dependent effects. While it provides inotropic support via β1-receptor stimulation at moderate doses, at higher doses, its α1-agonist effects cause systemic and pulmonary vasoconstriction. An increase in PVR is detrimental as it further strains the failing right ventricle, making dopamine a suboptimal choice for isolated RHF.

(C) Digoxin: Digoxin is a cardiac glycoside that acts by inhibiting the Na+/K+ ATPase pump. It is a relatively weak inotrope and is primarily used in chronic, stable heart failure (often with atrial fibrillation) rather than for acute decompensated heart failure. It has a slow onset of action and no significant effect on pulmonary vascular resistance, making it unsuitable for acute RHF management.

(D) Dobutamine: Dobutamine is a synthetic catecholamine that is a potent β1-adrenergic agonist, providing strong inotropic support. It also has mild β2-agonist effects, which can cause some peripheral and pulmonary vasodilation. However, its pulmonary vasodilating effect is generally less pronounced than that of milrinone. While dobutamine is a reasonable choice and is often used, milrinone's more potent effect on reducing RV afterload often makes it the superior agent for RHF.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Nitrates are unlikely to cause
Preload reduction
Increase in cardiac work
Decrease heart size
Dilation of cutaneous blood vessels

Answer: (B) — Increase in cardiac work

Explanation:

The primary therapeutic effect of nitrates (e.g., nitroglycerin, isosorbide dinitrate) in conditions like angina pectoris is to decrease cardiac work and myocardial oxygen demand. Nitrates are pro-drugs that are converted to nitric oxide (NO). NO activates guanylyl cyclase, leading to increased cGMP levels, which causes relaxation of vascular smooth muscle.

This vasodilation has two main effects: 1. Venodilation (Dominant Effect): Nitrates primarily dilate veins. This increases venous capacitance, causing blood to pool in the peripheral circulation. This reduces venous return to the heart, thereby decreasing the left ventricular end-diastolic volume and pressure (preload). 2. Arterial Dilation (Less Pronounced): At higher doses, nitrates also dilate arteries, which reduces systemic vascular resistance (afterload).

By reducing both preload and afterload, nitrates significantly decrease the workload of the heart. An increase in cardiac work is therefore the opposite of their intended and actual effect.

Options:

  • (A) Preload reduction: This is the principal mechanism of action for nitrates. Venodilation leads to decreased venous return and a subsequent reduction in preload. This is an incorrect option because nitrates are very likely to cause preload reduction.
  • (C) Decrease heart size: By reducing preload, nitrates decrease the volume of blood filling the ventricles during diastole. This leads to a temporary decrease in the end-diastolic size of the heart. This is an incorrect option because it's a direct consequence of nitrate's primary effect.
  • (D) Dilation of cutaneous blood vessels: Nitrates cause generalized vasodilation, which includes the blood vessels in the skin. This effect is responsible for the common side effects of flushing and warmth, particularly in the face and neck. This is an incorrect option because it's a known effect of nitrates.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
In which of the following circumstances, the actions of digoxin are least likely to be affected?
Drug interactions
Renal failure
Hepatic disease
Electrolyte disturbances

Answer: (C) — Hepatic disease

Explanation:

Digoxin's pharmacokinetics are characterized by its primary elimination through the kidneys. Approximately 70-80% of a digoxin dose is excreted unchanged by the kidneys via glomerular filtration and P-glycoprotein-mediated tubular secretion. While some hepatic metabolism does occur (to dihydrodigoxin and other metabolites), it represents a minor pathway for the drug's clearance. Therefore, in a patient with isolated hepatic disease but normal renal function, the clearance of digoxin is not significantly altered, and its actions are least likely to be affected compared to the other conditions listed.

Options:

  • (A) Drug interactions: This is incorrect. Digoxin is well-known for numerous clinically significant drug interactions. For example, drugs like amiodarone, verapamil, quinidine, and macrolide antibiotics can inhibit the P-glycoprotein transporter in the kidneys and gut, reducing digoxin's clearance and increasing its serum levels, thereby potentiating its effects and toxicity risk.
  • (B) Renal failure: This is incorrect. Because digoxin is predominantly cleared by the kidneys, renal failure or any significant decline in glomerular filtration rate (GFR) dramatically reduces its excretion. This leads to drug accumulation, a prolonged half-life, and a substantially increased risk of digoxin toxicity. Dose adjustment is almost always necessary in patients with renal impairment.
  • (D) Electrolyte disturbances: This is incorrect. The action of digoxin is highly sensitive to serum electrolyte levels. Digoxin works by inhibiting the Na+/K+-ATPase pump.
    • Hypokalemia (low potassium) reduces competition between potassium and digoxin for binding sites on the pump, thereby increasing digoxin's effect and the risk of arrhythmias.
    • Hypomagnesemia (low magnesium) also potentiates digoxin toxicity.
    • Hypercalcemia (high calcium) increases the risk of calcium overload in cardiac myocytes, which can also precipitate digoxin-induced arrhythmias.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A 58-year old patient presents with tearing central chest pain radiating through to her back. You diagnose an aortic dissection. Her heart rate is 60 and Blood pressure is 240/120. Which one of the following is your initial agent of choice in this situation?
Sodium nitroprusside
Verapamil
Captopril
Esmolol

Answer: (D) — Esmolol Explanation: The immediate management of an acute aortic dissection focuses on reducing aortic wall shear stress. This is achieved by lowering both the heart rate and the blood pressure. The most critical initial step is to decrease the rate of rise of the aortic pressure (dP/dt), which is primarily determined by left ventricular contractility.

Beta-blockers are the first-line agents because they directly reduce heart rate and myocardial contractility, thereby decreasing dP/dt. Esmolol is an ideal choice as it is a short-acting, titratable, intravenous, cardioselective beta-1 blocker, allowing for precise control in an emergent setting. The goal is to first achieve a target heart rate of less than 60 beats per minute. Although the patient's heart rate is already 60, initiating a beta-blocker is still the priority to reduce contractility. After adequate beta-blockade is established, if the blood pressure remains elevated (as it is here at 240/120 mmHg), a vasodilator like sodium nitroprusside is added to bring the systolic blood pressure down to a target of 100-120 mmHg.

Options: (A) Sodium nitroprusside: This is a potent vasodilator. If used alone, it will cause a reflex tachycardia and increase in myocardial contractility. This would increase dP/dt and aortic shear stress, potentially worsening or rupturing the dissection. It is only used after heart rate control with a beta-blocker has been achieved.

(B) Verapamil: This is a non-dihydropyridine calcium channel blocker. While it does lower heart rate and blood pressure, beta-blockers are considered superior and are the first-line choice for reducing dP/dt in aortic dissection. Verapamil may be considered in patients with contraindications to beta-blockers.

(C) Captopril: This is an ACE inhibitor. It is not used for the acute management of aortic dissection as it has a slower onset of action and does not directly control heart rate or myocardial contractility, which are the primary therapeutic targets. IV agents are required for rapid and titratable control.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A normal dose of a drug is injected to a healthy adult. The pupils dilate and the systolic blood pressure rises. Which one of the following drugs best explains this effect?
Morphine
Atropine
Diazepam
Adrenaline

Answer: (D) — Adrenaline

Explanation:

Adrenaline (also known as epinephrine) is a sympathomimetic catecholamine that acts as a non-selective agonist on both alpha (α) and beta (β) adrenergic receptors. Its effects are characteristic of a "fight-or-flight" response, which aligns perfectly with the signs described.

  1. Pupil Dilation (Mydriasis): Adrenaline stimulates α1-adrenergic receptors on the radial dilator muscle of the iris. Contraction of this muscle pulls the iris peripherally, leading to active dilation of the pupil.
  2. Rise in Systolic Blood Pressure: Adrenaline has powerful effects on the cardiovascular system.
    • It stimulates β1-receptors in the heart, increasing heart rate (positive chronotropy), contractility (positive inotropy), and cardiac output.
    • It stimulates α1-receptors in many vascular beds (e.g., skin, splanchnic circulation), causing vasoconstriction.
    • The combination of increased cardiac output and vasoconstriction leads to a significant increase in systolic blood pressure.

Therefore, the combination of pupil dilation and a rise in systolic blood pressure is a classic presentation of adrenaline administration.

Options:

  • (A) Morphine: This is an opioid agonist. Its classic effects on the autonomic nervous system are the opposite of what is described. Morphine characteristically causes miosis (pupil constriction) by stimulating the Edinger-Westphal nucleus and can cause hypotension due to histamine release and vasodilation.
  • (B) Atropine: This is a muscarinic antagonist (an anticholinergic drug). It blocks the parasympathetic nervous system. It does cause mydriasis (pupil dilation) by blocking muscarinic receptors on the pupillary sphincter muscle. It also causes tachycardia (increased heart rate) by blocking vagal tone on the heart's SA node, which can lead to a rise in systolic blood pressure. However, adrenaline is a better choice because it directly and potently causes both vasoconstriction and increased cardiac contractility, leading to a more pronounced and reliable increase in systolic blood pressure than atropine.
  • (C) Diazepam: This is a benzodiazepine, which acts as a CNS depressant by enhancing the effect of the neurotransmitter GABA. It does not characteristically cause pupil dilation or hypertension at a normal dose. Instead, it can cause sedation, muscle relaxation, and may lead to hypotension and respiratory depression, particularly when administered intravenously.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A 20-year old presents with sudden onset of palpitations. Her heart rate is 220 and her ECG shows regular narrow complex tachycardia. You decide to treat her with adenosine. Which one of the following statements regarding adenosine is CORRECT?
It has a half-life in blood of 60 seconds
Adenosine has an efficacy of 90-95% for conversion of SVT to sinus rhythm
It results in activation of calcium-dependent action potentials
Caffeine potentiates adenosine effect.

Answer: (B) — Adenosine has an efficacy of 90-95% for conversion of SVT to sinus rhythm

Explanation:

The patient's presentation of a regular, narrow-complex tachycardia at a rate of 220 bpm is characteristic of supraventricular tachycardia (SVT), most commonly AV-nodal re-entrant tachycardia (AVNRT) or atrioventricular re-entrant tachycardia (AVRT). Adenosine is the first-line pharmacologic agent for terminating stable SVT. It works by binding to A1 receptors in the AV node, which slows conduction velocity and interrupts the re-entrant circuit, allowing the SA node to regain control of the heart rhythm. Due to this highly effective mechanism, adenosine successfully converts SVT to sinus rhythm in approximately 90-95% of cases, making this statement correct.

Options:

  • (A) It has a half-life in blood of 60 seconds
    This is incorrect. Adenosine has an extremely short half-life, typically less than 10 seconds. This is why it must be administered as a rapid intravenous (IV) push through a large-bore cannula, followed immediately by a saline flush, to ensure it reaches the heart before being metabolized.

  • (C) It results in activation of calcium-dependent action potentials
    This is incorrect. Adenosine's mechanism involves activating A1 receptors, which are coupled to inhibitory G-proteins (Gi). This leads to two main effects: 1) Inhibition of adenylyl cyclase, reducing cAMP and subsequently decreasing calcium influx through L-type calcium channels. 2) Activation of potassium channels, causing potassium efflux and hyperpolarization of the cell membrane. Both effects make the AV nodal tissue less excitable and slow conduction, thus inhibiting rather than activating calcium-dependent action potentials.

  • (D) Caffeine potentiates adenosine effect.
    This is incorrect. Caffeine and other methylxanthines (like theophylline) are competitive antagonists at adenosine receptors. Therefore, caffeine blocks or inhibits the effect of adenosine. Patients who have recently consumed significant amounts of caffeine may require higher doses of adenosine to achieve a therapeutic effect.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
You plan to commence a 65-year old on antihypertensive medications. Which one of the following is likely to cause severe hypotension following the initial dose?
Verapamil
Clonidine
Ramipril
Metoprolol

Answer: (C) — Ramipril Explanation: Ramipril is an Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitor. ACE inhibitors are well-known for causing a "first-dose hypotension" phenomenon, which is a sharp and sometimes severe drop in blood pressure after the initial dose. This effect is most pronounced in patients with a highly activated renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS), such as those who are elderly, volume-depleted (e.g., on diuretics), or have congestive heart failure. The sudden inhibition of angiotensin II production leads to significant vasodilation and a fall in blood pressure. For this reason, it is standard practice to start ACE inhibitors at a low dose, often given at bedtime to mitigate the effects of any potential postural hypotension.

Options: * (A) Verapamil: This is a non-dihydropyridine calcium channel blocker. While it can cause hypotension by reducing cardiac contractility and causing some peripheral vasodilation, it is not characteristically associated with a severe first-dose hypotensive reaction like ACE inhibitors. Its most common side effects are constipation, bradycardia, and potential worsening of heart failure. * (B) Clonidine: This is a centrally-acting alpha-2 adrenergic agonist. It lowers blood pressure by reducing sympathetic outflow from the central nervous system. While hypotension is a side effect, severe first-dose hypotension is not its most prominent characteristic. More common side effects include drowsiness, dry mouth, and a significant risk of severe rebound hypertension if the medication is stopped abruptly. * (D) Metoprolol: This is a cardioselective beta-1 blocker. It lowers blood pressure primarily by reducing cardiac output and inhibiting renin release. The onset of its antihypertensive effect is generally gradual. While hypotension can occur, a severe reaction to the first dose is uncommon. Bradycardia is a more frequent initial side effect.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Salbutamol is preferred over adrenaline in treatment of asthma due to its:
Beta-1 selectivity
Alpha-1 selectivity
Beta-2 selectivity
Alpha-2 selectivity

Answer: (C) — Beta-2 selectivity

Explanation:

The primary goal in treating an acute asthma attack is to achieve rapid bronchodilation (widening of the airways) to relieve bronchospasm. This is mediated by stimulating the beta-2 adrenergic receptors located on the smooth muscle of the bronchioles.

  • Salbutamol (also known as albuterol) is a selective beta-2 adrenergic agonist. This means it has a high affinity for and primarily stimulates beta-2 receptors. By targeting these receptors, it effectively relaxes the bronchial smooth muscle, leading to bronchodilation and relief of asthma symptoms. Its selectivity minimizes its effects on other adrenergic receptors.

  • Adrenaline (epinephrine) is a non-selective adrenergic agonist. It stimulates alpha-1, alpha-2, beta-1, and beta-2 receptors. While its action on beta-2 receptors does cause bronchodilation, its stimulation of other receptors leads to significant, often undesirable, side effects:

    • Beta-1 stimulation: Increases heart rate (tachycardia), contractility, and can cause palpitations or arrhythmias.
    • Alpha-1 stimulation: Causes vasoconstriction, which increases blood pressure.

Therefore, salbutamol is preferred for the routine management of asthma because its beta-2 selectivity provides the desired therapeutic effect (bronchodilation) with a much lower risk of cardiovascular side effects compared to the non-selective adrenaline.

Options:

  • (A) Beta-1 selectivity: Incorrect. Beta-1 receptors are primarily located in the heart. A drug with beta-1 selectivity (like dobutamine) would primarily increase heart rate and contractility, which is an unwanted side effect in asthma treatment, not the therapeutic goal.
  • (B) Alpha-1 selectivity: Incorrect. Alpha-1 receptors are mainly found on vascular smooth muscle. A drug with alpha-1 selectivity (like phenylephrine) would cause vasoconstriction and is used as a decongestant or to raise blood pressure, but it does not cause bronchodilation.
  • (D) Alpha-2 selectivity: Incorrect. Alpha-2 receptors are primarily located on presynaptic nerve terminals and in the CNS. A drug with alpha-2 selectivity (like clonidine) is used as an antihypertensive and has no role in bronchodilation.

Confidence: 100%
