MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding pharmacodynamics of H1-receptor antagonist, all of the following are potential actions of various H1-receptor antagonists EXCEPT:
Antipsychotic effect
Local anaesthesia
Alpha adrenoceptor blocking actions
Sedation

Answer: (A) — Antipsychotic effect

Explanation:

H1-receptor antagonists, particularly the first-generation agents, are known for their lack of receptor selectivity. This means they can bind to and affect various other receptors in addition to the H1 histamine receptor, leading to a wide range of actions and side effects. The question asks to identify the action that is NOT associated with this drug class.

The primary mechanism of action for typical antipsychotic drugs is the blockade of dopamine D2 receptors in the central nervous system (CNS), particularly in the mesolimbic pathway. While some first-generation H1-antagonists, such as promethazine (a phenothiazine derivative), are structurally similar to antipsychotics and may exhibit very weak D2 receptor affinity, they do not produce a clinically significant antipsychotic effect. They are not used to treat psychosis (e.g., schizophrenia, mania), and this effect is not considered a property of the drug class.

Options:

  • (B) Local anaesthesia: This is an incorrect option because some H1-receptor antagonists, notably diphenhydramine and promethazine, possess local anaesthetic properties. They achieve this by blocking voltage-gated sodium channels in neuronal membranes, similar to conventional local anaesthetics like lidocaine. This makes them useful alternatives for patients with allergies to standard anaesthetic agents.
  • (C) Alpha adrenoceptor blocking actions: This is an incorrect option because several first-generation H1-antagonists, particularly those in the phenothiazine class like promethazine, also block alpha-1 adrenergic receptors. This action can lead to side effects such as orthostatic hypotension, dizziness, and reflex tachycardia.
  • (D) Sedation: This is an incorrect option because sedation is one of the most prominent and well-known effects of first-generation H1-receptor antagonists (e.g., diphenhydramine, hydroxyzine, chlorpheniramine). These agents are lipophilic and readily cross the blood-brain barrier, where they block H1 receptors in the CNS. Since histamine acts as a neurotransmitter promoting wakefulness, blocking its action leads to drowsiness and sedation. This effect is significant enough that these drugs are commonly used as over-the-counter sleep aids.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Benztropine can cause which one of the following?
Diarrhoea
Salivation
Confusion
Miosis

Answer: (C) — Confusion

Explanation:

Benztropine is an anticholinergic medication, specifically a centrally-acting antimuscarinic agent. It works by blocking acetylcholine receptors. Acetylcholine is a neurotransmitter involved in many bodily functions, including those of the parasympathetic nervous system ("rest and digest") and cognitive functions within the central nervous system (CNS).

By blocking acetylcholine's action in the brain, benztropine can interfere with cognitive processes like memory, learning, and attention. This disruption can lead to CNS side effects, most notably confusion. Other CNS effects can include delirium, hallucinations, and memory impairment. These effects are particularly common in elderly patients, who are more sensitive to the anticholinergic burden.

Options:

  • (A) Diarrhoea: This is incorrect. Anticholinergic drugs decrease gastrointestinal motility by blocking parasympathetic stimulation. This leads to constipation, which is the opposite of diarrhoea. Diarrhoea is a characteristic of cholinergic overstimulation.
  • (B) Salivation: This is incorrect. Anticholinergics block the parasympathetic signals that stimulate the salivary glands, leading to decreased saliva production. This results in xerostomia, or dry mouth, a very common side effect. Increased salivation is a cholinergic effect.
  • (D) Miosis: This is incorrect. Miosis is the constriction of the pupil, which is controlled by the parasympathetic nervous system. Benztropine blocks this action, leading to unopposed sympathetic stimulation of the iris, which causes mydriasis (pupil dilation) and can lead to blurred vision.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
50% dextrose solution for injection contains:
5mg of dextrose in 10ml of solution
50g of dextrose in 50ml of solution
50mg of dextrose in 1ml of solution
5g of dextrose in 10ml of solution

Answer: (D) — 5g of dextrose in 10ml of solution

Explanation:

In pharmacology and clinical medicine, solution concentrations are most commonly expressed as a weight/volume percentage (% w/v). This means the percentage represents the number of grams of solute per 100 milliliters (mL) of solution.

A 50% dextrose solution therefore contains 50 grams of dextrose per 100 mL of solution.

To find the correct option, we can evaluate each choice to see which one represents this concentration:

  • The ratio given in option (D) is 5g of dextrose in 10ml of solution.
  • To express this as a percentage (i.e., grams per 100 mL), we can set up a simple proportion: (5 g / 10 mL) = (X g / 100 mL)
  • Solving for X: X = (5 g * 100 mL) / 10 mL = 50 g.
  • This means that a solution containing 5g in 10mL is equivalent to 50g in 100mL, which is a 50% solution.

Clinically, 50% dextrose (D50W) is often supplied in 50 mL pre-filled syringes containing 25 grams of dextrose, which is another common way to express this same concentration (25g/50mL = 5g/10mL = 50g/100mL).

Options:

  • (A) 5mg of dextrose in 10ml of solution: This is equivalent to 0.005g in 10mL, or 0.05g in 100mL, which is a 0.05% solution. This is incorrect.
  • (B) 50g of dextrose in 50ml of solution: This is equivalent to 100g in 100mL, which would be a 100% solution. This is incorrect.
  • (C) 50mg of dextrose in 1ml of solution: This is equivalent to 0.05g in 1mL, or 5g in 100mL, which is a 5% solution (commonly known as D5W). This is incorrect.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
The agent of choice to treat kawasaki’s disease is:
Corticosteroids
Methotrexate
Aspirin
IV immunoglobulin

Answer: (D) — IV immunoglobulin

Explanation:

The cornerstone of treatment for Kawasaki's disease (KD) is the prompt administration of high-dose intravenous immunoglobulin (IVIG). KD is an acute systemic vasculitis of childhood that primarily affects medium-sized arteries, most notably the coronary arteries. The primary goal of treatment is to reduce the intense inflammation and, crucially, to prevent the development of coronary artery aneurysms, which are the most serious complication. A single infusion of IVIG (typically 2 g/kg over 8-12 hours) given within the first 10 days of illness has been shown to dramatically reduce the incidence of coronary artery aneurysms from approximately 25% in untreated children to less than 5%. While the exact mechanism is not fully understood, IVIG is believed to modulate the immune system and downregulate the widespread inflammatory response. IVIG is typically administered along with high-dose aspirin.

Options:

  • (A) Corticosteroids: Corticosteroids were historically controversial in the treatment of KD. However, current evidence supports their use as an adjunctive therapy, particularly for patients at high risk of developing coronary artery aneurysms or for those who are refractory (do not respond) to the initial IVIG treatment. They are not considered the first-line, primary agent of choice for all patients.
  • (B) Methotrexate: Methotrexate is a disease-modifying antirheumatic drug (DMARD) used to treat chronic inflammatory conditions like rheumatoid arthritis and psoriasis. It is not used in the acute management of Kawasaki's disease.
  • (C) Aspirin: High-dose aspirin is a standard and important part of the treatment regimen for acute Kawasaki's disease, given for its anti-inflammatory and antipyretic effects. After the acute phase, the dose is reduced for its antiplatelet effect to prevent thrombosis. However, IVIG is the single most effective agent for preventing coronary artery aneurysms, making it the most critical component of therapy and the best answer for "agent of choice."

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following vaccines contains live virus?
Hepatitis B
Hepatitis A
Rotavirus vaccine
Rabies vaccine

Answer: (C) — Rotavirus vaccine

Explanation:

The rotavirus vaccine is a live-attenuated oral vaccine. This means it contains a weakened (attenuated) form of the live rotavirus. When administered, the weakened virus replicates in the intestines, mimicking a natural infection without causing significant disease. This process stimulates a strong local (mucosal) and systemic immune response, providing effective protection against future rotavirus infections, which are a common cause of severe gastroenteritis in infants and young children.

Options:

  • (A) Hepatitis B: The Hepatitis B vaccine is a recombinant subunit vaccine. It does not contain any live or killed virus. Instead, it is produced by inserting a segment of the hepatitis B virus gene into the gene of a yeast cell. The yeast then produces the hepatitis B surface antigen (HBsAg), which is harvested, purified, and used as the vaccine antigen.
  • (B) Hepatitis A: The Hepatitis A vaccine is an inactivated (killed) vaccine. It contains whole hepatitis A viruses that have been rendered non-infectious, typically with a chemical like formalin. The body's immune system recognizes the antigens on the surface of the killed virus and mounts a protective response.
  • (D) Rabies vaccine: The rabies vaccine used in humans is an inactivated (killed) vaccine. It is prepared from rabies virus grown in cell culture and then inactivated. It is used for both pre-exposure and post-exposure prophylaxis.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Enzyme inactivation is the main mode of antimicrobial resistance for which group of antibiotics?
Aminoglycosides
Glycopeptides
Quinolones
Rifampicin

Answer: (A) — Aminoglycosides

Explanation:

The primary and most clinically significant mechanism of resistance to aminoglycosides is the production of aminoglycoside-modifying enzymes (AMEs) by bacteria. These enzymes inactivate the antibiotic by covalently adding chemical groups to it, such as an acetyl group (by acetyltransferases), a phosphate group (by phosphotransferases), or an adenyl group (by nucleotidyltransferases). This modification prevents the aminoglycoside from binding effectively to its target, the 30S ribosomal subunit, thereby inhibiting its ability to disrupt protein synthesis.

Options:

  • (B) Glycopeptides: The main mechanism of resistance to glycopeptides, such as vancomycin, is target site modification. Resistant bacteria (e.g., Vancomycin-Resistant Enterococci or VRE) alter the drug's binding target in the bacterial cell wall precursor from a D-alanyl-D-alanine (D-Ala-D-Ala) dipeptide to a D-alanyl-D-lactate (D-Ala-D-Lac) or D-alanyl-D-serine (D-Ala-D-Ser) dipeptide. This change significantly reduces the binding affinity of the glycopeptide antibiotic.

  • (C) Quinolones: The most common mechanism of resistance to quinolones is target site mutation. Bacteria develop mutations in the genes that code for the target enzymes, DNA gyrase (gyrA and gyrB) and topoisomerase IV (parC and parE). These mutations alter the enzymes' structure, preventing the quinolone from binding and inhibiting DNA replication. Efflux pumps are also a significant mechanism.

  • (D) Rifampicin: Resistance to rifampicin almost exclusively arises from target site mutation. A single point mutation in the rpoB gene, which encodes the beta-subunit of the bacterial DNA-dependent RNA polymerase, is sufficient to prevent rifampicin from binding to its target, thus rendering the drug ineffective.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A 24-year old male is intubated for aspiration and respiratory failure. He goes into cardiac arrest soon after administering drugs for rapid sequence induction. He gets diagnosed later on with a neuromuscular disorder. Which one of the following has caused the cardiac arrest?
Vecuronium
Suxamethonium
Atracurium
Rocuronium

Answer: (B) — Suxamethonium

Explanation:

Suxamethonium (also known as succinylcholine) is a depolarizing neuromuscular blocking agent. Its mechanism involves acting as an agonist at the nicotinic acetylcholine receptors (nAChR) at the neuromuscular junction. This causes a prolonged depolarization of the motor endplate, leading to initial muscle fasciculations followed by flaccid paralysis.

A key physiological effect of this depolarization is the efflux of potassium ions (K+) from the muscle cells. In a healthy individual, this results in a small, transient increase in serum potassium (approximately 0.5 mEq/L), which is usually clinically insignificant.

However, in patients with certain underlying conditions, including major burns, crush injuries, denervation (e.g., spinal cord injury, stroke), and neuromuscular disorders (like muscular dystrophies or myotonia), there is an upregulation and proliferation of extrajunctional acetylcholine receptors across the muscle cell membrane. When suxamethonium is administered to these patients, the widespread stimulation of both junctional and extrajunctional receptors leads to a massive, rapid, and uncontrolled release of potassium into the bloodstream. This can cause severe, life-threatening hyperkalemia, leading to cardiac arrhythmias (e.g., peaked T-waves, widened QRS, ventricular fibrillation) and culminating in cardiac arrest.

The patient in the question had an undiagnosed neuromuscular disorder, which created a state of hypersensitivity to the potassium-releasing effects of suxamethonium, leading to his cardiac arrest following rapid sequence induction.

Options:

  • (A) Vecuronium: This is a non-depolarizing neuromuscular blocker. It acts as a competitive antagonist at the nAChR, preventing acetylcholine from binding and thus causing paralysis without depolarization. It does not cause potassium release and is not associated with hyperkalemic cardiac arrest.
  • (C) Atracurium: Like vecuronium, atracurium is a non-depolarizing agent. It does not cause depolarization or hyperkalemia. Its primary side effect of concern is histamine release, which can cause hypotension and bronchospasm, but not hyperkalemic cardiac arrest.
  • (D) Rocuronium: This is another non-depolarizing agent, a competitive antagonist at the nAChR. It is a very common alternative to suxamethonium for rapid sequence induction, precisely because it lacks the risk of causing hyperkalemia. It does not cause the clinical picture described.

Confidence: 100%

41


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Local anesthetic agents have low affinity towards the Na channel in which one of the following states?
Activated state
Positive membrane potential state
Inactivated state
Resting state

Answer: (D) — Resting state

Explanation:

Local anesthetics (LAs) exert their effect by blocking voltage-gated sodium (Na⁺) channels, thereby preventing the propagation of action potentials along nerve fibers. The effectiveness of this blockade is highly dependent on the conformational state of the Na⁺ channel, a principle known as state-dependent blockade or use-dependence.

Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels exist in three main states: 1. Resting State: The channel is closed but capable of opening in response to a depolarizing stimulus. This is the predominant state in a resting neuron. 2. Activated (Open) State: Upon depolarization, the channel opens, allowing Na⁺ influx. 3. Inactivated State: Shortly after opening, the channel enters a closed, non-conducting state from which it cannot be immediately reopened.

Local anesthetics have a significantly higher affinity for the activated (open) and inactivated states compared to the resting state. When the channel is open or inactivated, the intracellular binding site for the local anesthetic is more accessible. Binding of the LA to these states stabilizes them, prolongs the refractory period, and prevents the channel from returning to the resting state, ready for the next action potential.

Conversely, in the resting state, the channel is closed, and access to the internal binding site is restricted. Therefore, local anesthetic agents have the lowest affinity for Na⁺ channels in the resting state.

Options:

  • (A) Activated state: Incorrect. Local anesthetics have a high affinity for the open, activated Na⁺ channel. The channel pore is accessible, allowing the drug to bind to its receptor site.
  • (B) Positive membrane potential state: Incorrect. A positive membrane potential corresponds to the depolarized phase of the action potential, during which Na⁺ channels are either in the activated (open) or inactivated state. LAs have a high affinity for both of these states.
  • (C) Inactivated state: Incorrect. Local anesthetics have a high affinity for the inactivated state. Binding to and stabilizing this state is a key part of their mechanism, preventing the channel from recovering to the resting state.
  • (D) Resting state: Correct. In the resting state, the Na⁺ channel is closed, and the internal binding site is poorly accessible. Consequently, local anesthetics exhibit their lowest affinity for the channel in this conformation.

Confidence: 100%

42


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
What dose would you have administered the patient if you infiltrated 10ml of 2% Lignocaine?
100mg
20mg
50mg
200mg

Answer: (200mg)

Explanation:

Calculating drug dosages from percentage solutions is a fundamental skill in medicine. A percentage concentration (weight/volume or w/v) indicates the number of grams of a drug per 100 milliliters (ml) of solution.

  1. Interpret the Concentration: "2% Lignocaine" means there are 2 grams (g) of Lignocaine in every 100 ml of the solution.
  2. Convert Grams to Milligrams: Since drug doses are typically measured in milligrams (mg), we convert grams to milligrams.
    • 1 gram = 1000 milligrams
    • Therefore, 2 grams = 2000 mg.
  3. Determine Concentration in mg/ml: We now know there are 2000 mg of Lignocaine in 100 ml. To find the concentration per ml, we divide:
    • 2000 mg / 100 ml = 20 mg/ml.
  4. Calculate the Total Dose: The patient received 10 ml of this solution. To find the total dose administered, we multiply the volume by the concentration:
    • 10 ml × 20 mg/ml = 200 mg.

A common clinical shortcut is to multiply the percentage by 10 to get the concentration in mg/ml. For a 2% solution, 2 x 10 = 20 mg/ml.

Options:

  • (100mg): This would be the correct dose if you administered 10ml of a 1% Lignocaine solution (10 mg/ml × 10 ml = 100 mg) or 5ml of a 2% solution.
  • (20mg): This is the amount of Lignocaine in only 1 ml of a 2% solution (20 mg/ml × 1 ml = 20 mg).
  • (50mg): This would be the dose if you administered 2.5 ml of a 2% Lignocaine solution (20 mg/ml × 2.5 ml = 50 mg).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A patient has open-angle glaucoma. Which one of the following drugs act to increase the outflow of aqueous humor?
Pilocarpine
Acetazolamide
Timolol
Apraclonidine

Answer: (A) — Pilocarpine

Explanation

Pilocarpine is a direct-acting cholinergic (muscarinic) agonist. In the treatment of open-angle glaucoma, it acts on the muscarinic M3 receptors of the ciliary muscle. Stimulation of these receptors causes the ciliary muscle to contract. This contraction pulls on the scleral spur, which in turn opens up the spaces within the trabecular meshwork. By increasing the pore size of the trabecular meshwork, pilocarpine facilitates the drainage of aqueous humor from the anterior chamber through the conventional outflow pathway (the canal of Schlemm), thereby lowering intraocular pressure.

Options

  • (B) Acetazolamide: This is a carbonic anhydrase inhibitor. Carbonic anhydrase is an enzyme in the ciliary epithelium essential for the production of aqueous humor. By inhibiting this enzyme, acetazolamide decreases the production of aqueous humor, rather than increasing its outflow.
  • (C) Timolol: This is a non-selective beta-adrenergic blocker. Beta-adrenergic receptors on the ciliary body epithelium are involved in stimulating the production of aqueous humor. By blocking these receptors, timolol decreases the secretion and production of aqueous humor.
  • (D) Apraclonidine: This is an alpha-2 adrenergic agonist. Its primary mechanism is to decrease the rate of aqueous humor production by the ciliary body. It may have a secondary, minor effect of increasing uveoscleral outflow, but its main therapeutic action is reducing aqueous humor synthesis.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding Diazepam, which one of the following statements about its pharmacokinetics is CORRECT?
Oral bioavailability is 50%
Half-life of 43 hours
Urinary excretion is 10%
25% bound in plasma

Answer: (B) — Half-life of 43 hours

Explanation:

Diazepam is a long-acting benzodiazepine with distinct pharmacokinetic properties. Its elimination half-life is biphasic, but the terminal elimination half-life is quite long, typically ranging from 20 to 50 hours. A value of 43 hours falls squarely within this accepted range. This long half-life is due to the slow hepatic metabolism of the parent drug and the formation of a major active metabolite, desmethyldiazepam (nordiazepam), which has an even longer half-life (up to 100 hours). The presence of active metabolites contributes significantly to the prolonged duration of action and potential for accumulation with repeated dosing, particularly in the elderly or those with hepatic impairment.

Options:

  • (A) Oral bioavailability is 50%: This is incorrect. Diazepam is rapidly and almost completely absorbed after oral administration. Its oral bioavailability is very high, typically exceeding 90%.
  • (C) Urinary excretion is 10%: This is incorrect. This statement likely refers to the excretion of the unchanged parent drug. Diazepam is extensively metabolized in the liver, primarily by CYP2C19 and CYP3A4 enzymes. Consequently, very little of the drug is excreted unchanged in the urine; the actual value is less than 2%. The majority of the dose is excreted in the urine as conjugated metabolites (e.g., glucuronides of its metabolites like temazepam and oxazepam).
  • (D) 25% bound in plasma: This is incorrect. Diazepam is a highly lipophilic (fat-soluble) drug. As is typical for such drugs, it is highly bound to plasma proteins, primarily albumin. The fraction bound is approximately 98-99%, not 25%. This high degree of protein binding influences its distribution and potential for drug-drug interactions.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
An 8-year-old child presents with acute severe asthma. You are about to adminster a nebulised dose of magnesium sulphate. What is the dose you would provide this child?
50mg/kg
500mg/kg
5g/kg
5mg/kg

Answer: (D) — 5mg/kg

Explanation:

The administration of nebulised magnesium sulphate is an adjunctive therapy for acute severe asthma, particularly in cases not responding adequately to initial treatment with beta-agonists and corticosteroids. The dosing can be a point of confusion, as it differs significantly from the intravenous (IV) route.

For nebulised magnesium sulphate in children, a common and safe approach is to use a dose that approximates the fixed-dose regimens studied in clinical trials. The UK's British Thoracic Society (BTS) / Scottish Intercollegiate Guidelines Network (SIGN) guidelines recommend a fixed dose of 150mg of nebulised magnesium sulphate (co-administered with salbutamol and ipratropium) for children with severe asthma symptoms of short duration (<2 hours).

For an 8-year-old child, the average weight is approximately 25-30 kg. Calculating the dose based on the 5mg/kg option: * 25 kg x 5 mg/kg = 125 mg. This calculated dose is very close to the recommended 150mg fixed dose, making it the most appropriate and safest choice among the options provided. This low dose aims to provide local bronchodilation within the airways while minimizing the risk of systemic absorption and hypermagnesemia.

Options:

  • (A) 50mg/kg: This is incorrect. This is the standard intravenous (IV) dose of magnesium sulphate for severe asthma in children (typically 40-50 mg/kg, up to a maximum of 2g, infused over 20 minutes). Confusing the nebulised and IV dose is a common error and a frequent subject of test questions.
  • (B) 500mg/kg: This is incorrect. This dose is excessively high and would be extremely toxic, leading to life-threatening hypermagnesemia (symptoms include respiratory depression, hypotension, loss of reflexes, and cardiac arrest). For a 25kg child, this would be 12.5 grams.
  • (C) 5g/kg: This is incorrect. This dose is astronomically high and would be rapidly fatal. It is equivalent to 5000 mg/kg.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A patient with vomiting is given metoclopramide. Which one of the following is an effect of metoclopramide?
Stimulates gastric emptying
Reduces jejunal peristalsis
Reduces lower oesophageal peristaltic amplitude
Dopaminergic agonist activity

Answer: Stimulates gastric emptying

Explanation:

Metoclopramide is a prokinetic and antiemetic agent. Its primary mechanism of action is as a dopamine D2 receptor antagonist. It exerts its effects both centrally and peripherally. * Central Effect: It blocks D2 receptors in the chemoreceptor trigger zone (CTZ) of the medulla, which produces its potent antiemetic (anti-vomiting) effect. * Peripheral (Prokinetic) Effect: In the gastrointestinal tract, metoclopramide's D2 antagonism increases the release of acetylcholine from myenteric neurons. It also has 5-HT4 receptor agonist activity. These actions enhance the response of upper GI smooth muscle to acetylcholine, leading to: * Increased lower esophageal sphincter (LES) tone. * Increased force and frequency of antral contractions in the stomach. * Relaxation of the pyloric sphincter. * Increased peristalsis in the duodenum and jejunum. Collectively, these effects result in accelerated gastric emptying and intestinal transit, making it useful for conditions like gastroparesis and gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD).

Options:

  • Reduces jejunal peristalsis: This is incorrect. Metoclopramide is a prokinetic agent that increases or accelerates peristalsis in the small intestine, including the jejunum.
  • Reduces lower oesophageal peristaltic amplitude: This is incorrect. Metoclopramide increases the tone of the lower esophageal sphincter and enhances the amplitude of esophageal peristaltic contractions, which helps prevent reflux.
  • Dopaminergic agonist activity: This is incorrect. Metoclopramide is a dopamine D2 receptor antagonist. Dopamine agonists, such as apomorphine, can actually induce vomiting by stimulating the CTZ. The antagonistic action is key to both its antiemetic and prokinetic effects.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
An 86-year-old man presents to ED after an overdose. He has symptoms over a 6-hour period of nausea, vomiting, tinnitus, loss of hearing and describes unsteadiness with the ‘room spinning’. He also describes blurred vision. His vital signs and neurological examination are normal.
Salicylism
Cinchonism
Anticholinergic syndrome
Serotonin syndrome

Answer: (B) — Cinchonism Explanation: The patient's constellation of symptoms—nausea, vomiting, tinnitus (ringing in the ears), hearing loss, vertigo ('room spinning'), and blurred vision—is the classic presentation of cinchonism. This toxidrome is caused by an overdose of cinchona alkaloids, most notably quinine (used for malaria and leg cramps) or its stereoisomer, quinidine (an antiarrhythmic drug). These drugs have direct toxic effects on the cochlear and vestibular apparatus, leading to the characteristic audiovestibular symptoms.

Options: (A) Salicylism: This is a strong distractor as salicylate (aspirin) toxicity also classically presents with tinnitus, nausea, vomiting, and vertigo. However, the complete syndrome presented, particularly the combination of tinnitus, hearing loss, vertigo, and visual disturbances, is more specifically and classically defined as cinchonism. Salicylism is also frequently associated with hyperventilation and a characteristic respiratory alkalosis followed by metabolic acidosis, which are not mentioned.

(C) Anticholinergic syndrome: This syndrome is caused by blocking acetylcholine receptors. The classic mnemonic is "blind as a bat (mydriasis), mad as a hatter (delirium), red as a beet (flushing), hot as a hare (hyperthermia), and dry as a bone (dry skin/mouth)". While the patient has blurred vision, the other key features like altered mental status, fever, flushing, and tachycardia are absent (vitals are normal). Tinnitus and hearing loss are not primary features.

(D) Serotonin syndrome: This is caused by excess serotonergic activity. It is characterized by a triad of altered mental status (e.g., agitation, confusion), autonomic hyperactivity (e.g., tachycardia, hypertension, fever), and neuromuscular abnormalities (e.g., tremor, clonus, hyperreflexia). The patient's presentation does not match this toxidrome; the vital signs and neurological exam are normal.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding toxidromes, their clinical presentations and differential diagnoses, match the best option with the stems that follow: A 5-year-old child was at the beach with family when it was noted she became 'drowsy and collapsed’. CPR was provided by lifesavers and she arrived in ED via ambulance with support of breathing via Bag valve mask ventilation. No skin markings or wounds are noted. Examination reveals a flaccid paralysis with poor respiratory effort.
Blue ringed Octopus envenomation
Chironex Fleckeri envenomation
Latrodectism
Irukandji syndrome

Answer: (A) — Blue ringed Octopus envenomation

Explanation:

The clinical presentation described is a classic toxidrome for envenomation by the blue-ringed octopus. The key features are: 1. Setting: Marine environment (beach). 2. Rapid Onset: The child became "drowsy and collapsed" suddenly. 3. Painless Bite: The absence of skin markings or wounds is a crucial feature, as the bite of a blue-ringed octopus is often painless and may go unnoticed. 4. Neuromuscular Paralysis: The presentation of flaccid paralysis with poor respiratory effort is the hallmark of tetrodotoxin (TTX) poisoning.

The venom of the blue-ringed octopus contains tetrodotoxin, a potent neurotoxin that blocks voltage-gated sodium channels in nerve tissue. This blockage prevents nerve impulse propagation, leading to a rapidly progressive, ascending flaccid paralysis. The paralysis affects the skeletal muscles, including the diaphragm and intercostal muscles, resulting in respiratory failure. Consciousness is typically preserved until hypoxia develops, which can be misinterpreted as drowsiness. The prompt need for CPR and bag-valve-mask ventilation aligns perfectly with this life-threatening respiratory muscle paralysis.

Options:

  • (B) Chironex Fleckeri envenomation: This is incorrect. Chironex fleckeri (the box jellyfish) causes immediate, excruciating pain at the sting site and leaves characteristic linear, erythematous, "frosted ladder" skin markings. While it can cause rapid cardiovascular collapse, the lack of severe pain and visible sting marks in this case makes it highly unlikely.
  • (C) Latrodectism: This is incorrect. Envenomation by a redback or widow spider (latrodectism) is caused by alpha-latrotoxin. This toxin leads to massive neurotransmitter release, causing a clinical picture of severe pain, muscle fasciculations, spasms, and rigidity (not flaccid paralysis). It also typically features autonomic signs like diaphoresis (sweating) and hypertension.
  • (D) Irukandji syndrome: This is incorrect. Irukandji syndrome, caused by the sting of small carybdeid jellyfish, has a characteristic delayed onset (typically 20-40 minutes after a minor, often unnoticed sting). It presents as a hyperadrenergic state with severe generalized pain (back, chest, abdomen), anxiety, profuse sweating, and life-threatening hypertension, not flaccid paralysis.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding Penicillin agents and their interactions with microbes, choose the best option to fit the stem that follows: Impaired penetration of drug to target Penicillin Binding Protein due to down regulation of porins in the outer cell membrane or the presence of efflux pumps is seen in:
Gram-negative organism
Anaerobic organisms
Gram positive organism
Aerobic organisms

Answer: (A) — Gram-negative organism

Explanation:

The question describes a specific mechanism of antibiotic resistance involving changes to the bacterial cell envelope. This mechanism is characteristic of Gram-negative bacteria due to their unique cell wall structure.

  • Gram-Negative Cell Wall: Gram-negative bacteria possess a complex cell envelope consisting of an inner cytoplasmic membrane, a thin layer of peptidoglycan located in the periplasmic space, and a crucial outer membrane.
  • Drug Penetration: For penicillin and other beta-lactam antibiotics to be effective, they must cross this outer membrane to reach their target, the Penicillin-Binding Proteins (PBPs), which are involved in synthesizing the peptidoglycan cell wall.
  • Porins and Efflux Pumps: Hydrophilic antibiotics like many penicillins traverse the outer membrane through water-filled channels called porins. Gram-negative bacteria can develop resistance by:
    1. Down-regulation of Porins: Decreasing the number of or altering the structure of these porin channels, thereby restricting the antibiotic's entry into the periplasmic space.
    2. Efflux Pumps: Utilizing protein pumps that actively transport the antibiotic out of the cell (either from the periplasm or cytoplasm) before it can reach its PBP target.

This combination of a restrictive outer membrane and the ability to modify porin channels or use efflux pumps is a hallmark of resistance in many Gram-negative organisms, such as Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Enterobacteriaceae.

Options:

  • (B) Anaerobic organisms: This is incorrect. This classification is based on oxygen metabolism, not cell wall structure. The group of anaerobic organisms includes both Gram-positive (e.g., Clostridium) and Gram-negative (e.g., Bacteroides) species. Therefore, it is not a universally applicable or precise description for this mechanism.
  • (C) Gram-positive organism: This is incorrect. Gram-positive bacteria lack an outer membrane. Their cell wall consists of a thick, external layer of peptidoglycan which is easily accessible to penicillins. Therefore, they do not have porin channels that can be down-regulated. Common resistance mechanisms in Gram-positive bacteria include producing beta-lactamase enzymes or altering the structure of the target PBP itself (e.g., PBP2a in MRSA).
  • (D) Aerobic organisms: This is incorrect. Similar to anaerobes, this is a metabolic classification. This group includes both Gram-negative organisms (which use this mechanism) and Gram-positive organisms (which do not), making it an imprecise answer.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which enzyme produced by pseudomonas and E coli hydrolyzes cephalosporins and penicillins only?
Carbapenamase
AmpC Beta Lactamase
AmpC Beta Lactamase and Carbapenamase
None of the options

Answer: (B) — AmpC Beta Lactamase Explanation: AmpC beta-lactamases are a group of clinically significant enzymes (Ambler Class C) that confer resistance to a wide variety of beta-lactam antibiotics. They are characteristically produced by several gram-negative bacteria, including Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Escherichia coli. The key feature of AmpC beta-lactamases is their substrate profile: they efficiently hydrolyze penicillins, cephalosporins (including first, second, and third-generation agents like ceftazidime), and cephamycins. However, they do not effectively hydrolyze carbapenems (like imipenem or meropenem) or fourth-generation cephalosporins (like cefepime). This specificity for penicillins and cephalosporins, but not carbapenems, directly matches the description in the question. Another important characteristic is their resistance to inhibition by common beta-lactamase inhibitors such as clavulanic acid.

Options: (A) Carbapenemase: This is incorrect. Carbapenemases are beta-lactamases (Ambler Class A, B, or D) that are defined by their ability to hydrolyze carbapenems, which are typically reserved for treating multidrug-resistant infections. While they also hydrolyze penicillins and cephalosporins, their activity is not limited to these classes, violating the "only" condition in the question.

(C) AmpC Beta Lactamase and Carbapenemase: This is incorrect because, as explained above, carbapenemases do not fit the specific substrate profile mentioned in the question. Their spectrum of activity is broader than what is described.

(D) None of the options: This is incorrect because AmpC Beta Lactamase is the correct answer, as it perfectly fits the description of an enzyme produced by Pseudomonas and E. coli that hydrolyzes penicillins and cephalosporins but not carbapenems.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding Penicillin agents and their interactions with microbes, choose the best option to fit the stem that follows: This drug has greatest activity against gram-positive organisms, gram-negative cocci, and non-β-lactamase producing anaerobes. However, little activity against gram-negative rods, and they are susceptible to hydrolysis by β-lactamases. 
Penicillin G
Dicloxacillin
Carbapenems
Ampicillin

Answer: (A) — Penicillin G Explanation: The description provided is the classic microbiological spectrum for natural penicillins, such as Penicillin G and Penicillin V. * Greatest activity against gram-positive organisms, gram-negative cocci, and non-β-lactamase producing anaerobes: Penicillin G is highly effective against many species of Streptococcus, Neisseria (gram-negative cocci), and anaerobes like Clostridium perfringens. It is also the drug of choice for the spirochete Treponema pallidum (syphilis). * Little activity against gram-negative rods: Natural penicillins cannot effectively penetrate the outer membrane of most gram-negative rods (e.g., E. coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas), rendering them ineffective. * Susceptible to hydrolysis by β-lactamases: Penicillin G is readily destroyed by penicillinases (a type of β-lactamase), which are produced by many strains of Staphylococcus aureus and various gram-negative bacteria. This enzymatic degradation is a major mechanism of resistance.

Options: * (B) Dicloxacillin: This is an anti-staphylococcal penicillin (penicillinase-resistant penicillin). Its primary feature is its resistance to staphylococcal β-lactamases, which directly contradicts the last part of the stem ("susceptible to hydrolysis by β-lactamases"). Its spectrum is primarily focused on Methicillin-Sensitive Staphylococcus aureus (MSSA) and streptococci. * (C) Carbapenems: This class of β-lactam antibiotics (e.g., imipenem, meropenem) has an extremely broad spectrum of activity, including excellent coverage of gram-negative rods. Furthermore, they are highly resistant to most β-lactamases. This contradicts two key points in the stem. * (D) Ampicillin: This is an aminopenicillin, also known as an extended-spectrum penicillin. While it is susceptible to β-lactamases like Penicillin G, it was specifically developed to have improved activity against certain gram-negative rods (e.g., H. influenzae, E. coli, Listeria). Therefore, the description "little activity against gram-negative rods" fits Penicillin G more accurately than Ampicillin.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following is an intracellular second messenger that mediates such hormonal responses as mentioned below? - the mobilization of stored energy (the breakdown of carbohydrates in liver or triglycerides in fat cells stimulated by β-adrenomimetic catecholamines) - conservation of water by the kidney (mediated by vasopressin) - Ca2+ homeostasis (regulated by parathyroid hormone), and - increased rate and contractile force of heart muscle (β-adrenomimetic catecholamines)
Tyrosine
Cyclic Adenosine Monophosphate
Phosphoinositide
cGMP

Answer: (B) — Cyclic Adenosine Monophosphate

Explanation:

Cyclic Adenosine Monophosphate (cAMP) is a ubiquitous intracellular second messenger that plays a critical role in mediating the cellular responses to a wide variety of hormones and neurotransmitters. The synthesis of cAMP is catalyzed by the enzyme adenylyl cyclase, which is activated when a hormone or neurotransmitter binds to its specific G-protein coupled receptor (GPCR), particularly one linked to a stimulatory G-protein (Gs). The elevated intracellular cAMP then typically activates Protein Kinase A (PKA), which phosphorylates various target proteins, enzymes, and transcription factors to elicit the final physiological response.

The examples provided in the question are all classic illustrations of the cAMP signaling pathway:

  1. Mobilization of stored energy: Catecholamines (like epinephrine) bind to β-adrenergic receptors on liver and fat cells. This activates the Gs/adenylyl cyclase/cAMP/PKA pathway. In the liver, PKA activation leads to glycogenolysis (breakdown of glycogen to glucose). In fat cells, it leads to lipolysis (breakdown of triglycerides to fatty acids and glycerol).
  2. Conservation of water: Vasopressin (antidiuretic hormone, ADH) binds to V2 receptors in the principal cells of the kidney's collecting ducts. These are Gs-coupled receptors, and their activation increases cAMP, which promotes the insertion of aquaporin-2 water channels into the apical membrane, increasing water reabsorption.
  3. Ca2+ homeostasis: Parathyroid hormone (PTH) binds to its receptor (PTH1R) in bone and kidney cells. This receptor is also coupled to Gs, leading to increased cAMP. This pathway mediates PTH's effects on increasing calcium reabsorption in the kidney and stimulating bone resorption to release calcium into the blood.
  4. Increased cardiac function: Catecholamines binding to β1-adrenergic receptors in the heart activate the Gs/cAMP/PKA pathway. This leads to the phosphorylation of key proteins like L-type calcium channels and phospholamban, resulting in an increased heart rate (chronotropy) and force of contraction (inotropy).

Options:

  • (A) Tyrosine: This is an amino acid. It serves as a precursor for the synthesis of catecholamines (dopamine, norepinephrine, epinephrine) and thyroid hormones. While critical for signaling, it is not an intracellular second messenger itself. It can be phosphorylated in signaling cascades (e.g., receptor tyrosine kinases), but it doesn't function as a diffusible signaling molecule like cAMP.
  • (C) Phosphoinositide: This refers to the phosphoinositide signaling pathway. The key second messengers in this system are inositol trisphosphate (IP3) and diacylglycerol (DAG), which are generated by the action of phospholipase C on PIP2. This pathway is typically activated by Gq-coupled receptors (e.g., α1-adrenergic, M1/M3 muscarinic, V1 vasopressin receptors) and is not the primary mediator for the specific β-adrenergic and V2 receptor-mediated effects mentioned.
  • (D) cGMP: Cyclic Guanosine Monophosphate is another important second messenger, but it is involved in different signaling pathways. It is synthesized by guanylyl cyclase and is the key mediator for nitric oxide (NO)-induced smooth muscle relaxation (vasodilation) and the actions of natriuretic peptides. It is not the second messenger for the hormonal responses listed in the question.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A Protein tyrosine kinase from the Janus-kinase (JAK) family, binds noncovalently to the receptor. The receptors then dimerize allowing the bound JAKs to phosphorylate tyrosine residues on the receptor which then bind another set of proteins, called STATs (signal transducers and activators of transcription). The bound STATs are themselves phosphorylated by the JAKs - finally the STAT/STAT dimer dissociates from the receptor and travels to the nucleus, where it regulates transcription of specific genes.  The statement best describes:
Cytokine receptors
Ligand and Voltage Gated channels
Phosphoinositide signaling pathway
G protein-coupled receptors

Answer: (A) — Cytokine receptors Explanation: The passage describes the Janus kinase-signal transducer and activator of transcription (JAK-STAT) pathway. This is the characteristic signaling mechanism for cytokine receptors. These receptors lack intrinsic enzymatic activity. Upon ligand (e.g., cytokine, interferon, growth hormone) binding, the receptor monomers dimerize. This brings the associated JAK tyrosine kinases into close proximity, allowing them to trans-phosphorylate and activate each other. The activated JAKs then phosphorylate tyrosine residues on the intracellular domain of the receptor. These phosphorylated sites serve as docking points for STAT proteins. The STATs are then phosphorylated by the JAKs, causing them to dimerize, dissociate from the receptor, translocate to the nucleus, and act as transcription factors to regulate gene expression.

Options: (B) Ligand and Voltage Gated channels: These are ion channels that open or close in response to a specific ligand or a change in membrane potential, respectively. They directly alter ion flux across the cell membrane, leading to rapid changes in membrane potential or intracellular ion concentration. Their mechanism does not involve JAKs or STATs for gene transcription.

(C) Phosphoinositide signaling pathway: This pathway is typically initiated by G protein-coupled receptors or receptor tyrosine kinases. It involves the activation of phospholipase C (PLC), which cleaves PIP2 into second messengers, inositol trisphosphate (IP3) and diacylglycerol (DAG). These second messengers then activate downstream pathways, such as calcium release and protein kinase C (PKC) activation. This is a distinct pathway from the one described.

(D) G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs): These receptors activate intracellular G proteins upon ligand binding. The activated G protein then modulates the activity of an effector enzyme (like adenylyl cyclase) or an ion channel. This leads to the production of second messengers (like cAMP or IP3/DAG), which propagate the signal. This mechanism is fundamentally different from the direct kinase-mediated phosphorylation cascade of the JAK-STAT pathway.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
In the presence of spare receptors, which of the following is correct?
For a given pharmacological response, it is not possible to elicit maximal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist
For a given pharmacological response, it is possible to elicit maximal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist
For a given pharmacological response, it is only possible to elicit a sub-maximal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist
For a given pharmacological response, it is only possible to elicit minimal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist

Answer: (B) — For a given pharmacological response, it is possible to elicit maximal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist

Explanation:

The concept of spare receptors (or receptor reserve) is a fundamental principle in pharmacology. It describes a situation where the maximal biological effect (Emax) can be achieved even when only a fraction of the total available receptors are occupied by an agonist.

In such systems, there is a very efficient coupling between receptor activation and the downstream signaling cascade. This amplification means that activating a relatively small number of receptors is sufficient to trigger the cell's maximum possible response. Consequently, the concentration of the agonist required to produce a 50% maximal effect (the EC50) is significantly lower than the concentration required to occupy 50% of the receptors (the KD). The presence of spare receptors increases the sensitivity of the system to the agonist.

Option (B) is the correct definition of this phenomenon.

Options:

  • (A) For a given pharmacological response, it is not possible to elicit maximal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist: This statement describes a system with no spare receptors, where 100% receptor occupancy is required to achieve a maximal response. In this case, the EC50 would be equal to the KD.
  • (C) For a given pharmacological response, it is only possible to elicit a sub-maximal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist: This is incorrect. While it is true that at very low concentrations of agonist the response will be sub-maximal, the defining characteristic of a system with spare receptors is the ability to reach a maximal response without full receptor occupancy. This option wrongly negates that possibility.
  • (D) For a given pharmacological response, it is only possible to elicit minimal biological response at a concentration of agonist that does not result in occupancy of the full complement of available receptors for the agonist: This is an even more extreme and incorrect version of option (C). A graded dose-response relationship exists, and responses greater than minimal can certainly be achieved without occupying every available receptor.

Confidence: 100%

55


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
The use of insulin to manage hyperglycaemia in the setting of cortisol excess is an example of
Irreversible antagonism
Competitive antagonism
None of the options
Physiological antagonism

Answer: (D) — Physiological antagonism

Explanation:

This scenario is a classic example of physiological antagonism. This type of antagonism occurs when two substances produce opposing physiological effects on the body by acting on different receptors or through different mechanisms.

  • Cortisol: A glucocorticoid hormone, cortisol increases blood glucose levels (hyperglycemia). It achieves this by promoting gluconeogenesis (the production of new glucose) in the liver and decreasing glucose uptake and utilization by peripheral tissues, thereby inducing a state of insulin resistance. It acts via intracellular glucocorticoid receptors.
  • Insulin: A peptide hormone, insulin lowers blood glucose levels. It promotes the uptake of glucose into muscle and adipose tissue, stimulates glycogen synthesis in the liver and muscle, and inhibits gluconeogenesis. It acts via cell-surface insulin receptors.

In the setting of cortisol excess (e.g., Cushing's syndrome or high-dose steroid therapy), the resulting hyperglycemia is managed with insulin. Insulin counteracts the hyperglycemic effect of cortisol by its own glucose-lowering actions. Since these two substances have opposite physiological outcomes but act on entirely different receptor systems, their interaction is defined as physiological (or functional) antagonism.

Options:

  • (A) Irreversible antagonism: This occurs when an antagonist binds to a receptor, often covalently, and dissociates very slowly or not at all. The effect cannot be overcome by increasing the agonist concentration. This is incorrect because insulin and cortisol do not act on the same receptor.
  • (B) Competitive antagonism: This involves an antagonist that reversibly binds to the same receptor site as an agonist, competing with it for binding. The effect of a competitive antagonist can be overcome by increasing the concentration of the agonist. This is incorrect as insulin and cortisol have distinct and separate receptors.
  • (C) None of the options: This option is incorrect because "Physiological antagonism" accurately describes the interaction between insulin and cortisol in regulating blood glucose.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding toxidromes, their clinical presentations and differential diagnoses, match the best option with the stems that follow: A 40-year-old man with a history of suicidal ideation presents with confusion, vomiting and diarrhoea. Examination reveals miosis, bradycardia, hypertension, tachypnea and excessive salivation. He is also noted to have muscle fasciculations and weakness.
Anticholinergic syndrome
Neuroleptic malignant syndrome
Cholinergic syndrome
Sympathomimetic toxidrome

Answer: (C) — Cholinergic syndrome

Explanation:

The patient's clinical presentation is a classic example of a cholinergic toxidrome, which results from excessive stimulation of acetylcholine receptors. This is often caused by exposure to organophosphates or carbamates (found in pesticides), nerve agents, or certain medications (e.g., donepezil, physostigmine). The history of suicidal ideation makes intentional ingestion of a pesticide a likely cause.

  • Muscarinic effects (DUMBELS):

    • Diarrhoea/Diaphoresis (sweating)
    • Urination
    • Miosis (pinpoint pupils)
    • Bradycardia (slow heart rate), Bronchorrhea (excessive bronchial secretions), Bronchospasm
    • Emesis (vomiting)
    • Lacrimation (tearing)
    • Salivation (drooling)

    • Mydriasis (pupil dilation - less common than miosis but possible)
    • Tachycardia
    • Weakness
    • tHypertension
    • Fasciculations (muscle twitching)
    • Seizures

This patient exhibits confusion, vomiting, diarrhoea, miosis, bradycardia, excessive salivation (muscarinic effects), as well as muscle fasciculations, weakness, and hypertension (nicotinic effects), which is the complete picture of a severe cholinergic crisis.

Caused by stimulants like cocaine or amphetamines, this toxidrome presents with agitation, mydriasis (dilated pupils), tachycardia, hypertension, and diaphoresis (sweating). This patient has miosis and bradycardia, which are inconsistent with a sympathomimetic toxidrome.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
In cardiac muscle cell, Na+ influx through rapidly opening Na+ channels (the Na+current, INa) corresponds to
Phase 2 Plateau phase in cardiac muscle cell
Phase 0 rapid depolarisation in cardiac muscle cell
Phase 1 rapid repolarisation in cardiac muscle cell
Phase 3 Slow repolarisation phase in cardiac muscle cell

Answer: (B) — Phase 0 rapid depolarisation in cardiac muscle cell

Explanation:

The action potential of a contractile cardiac muscle cell (myocyte) is divided into five phases (0-4). The event described in the question, a massive and rapid influx of sodium ions (Na+) through fast voltage-gated Na+ channels, is the defining characteristic of Phase 0.

  • Phase 0 (Rapid Depolarization/Upstroke): When a cardiac myocyte is stimulated and its membrane potential reaches a threshold (approximately -70 mV), voltage-gated fast Na+ channels open. This allows a large and rapid influx of positively charged Na+ ions into the cell, causing the membrane potential to rapidly depolarize from its resting state of about -90 mV to a positive value of about +20 mV. This event is responsible for the steep upstroke of the action potential curve.

Options:

  • (A) Phase 2 Plateau phase in cardiac muscle cell: This is incorrect. The plateau phase is characterized by a balance between an inward calcium current (Ca2+ influx through L-type Ca2+ channels) and an outward potassium current (K+ efflux through delayed rectifier K+ channels). The fast Na+ channels are inactivated during this phase.
  • (C) Phase 1 rapid repolarisation in cardiac muscle cell: This is incorrect. Phase 1 is a brief, initial repolarization that occurs immediately after the upstroke. It is caused by the inactivation of the fast Na+ channels and a transient efflux of potassium ions (K+) through transient outward K+ channels (Ito).
  • (D) Phase 3 Slow repolarisation phase in cardiac muscle cell: This is incorrect. Phase 3 is the final, rapid repolarization phase where the membrane potential returns to its resting state. This is primarily caused by the closure of L-type Ca2+ channels and a dominant efflux of K+ ions through delayed rectifier potassium channels (IKr and IKs).

Confidence: 100%

58


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Match the following statement with the best option from the list: Which of the following explains why CO2 diffuses 20 times more rapidly than O2 across tissues?
Alveolar ventilation
Henry’s Law
Fick’s Law
Oxygen saturation of hemoglobin

Answer: (C) — Fick’s Law

Explanation:

Fick's Law of diffusion is the principle that governs the rate of gas transfer across a membrane. The law is described by the equation:

V̇_gas = (A / T) × D × (P₁ - P₂)

Where: * V̇_gas is the rate of gas diffusion. * A is the surface area of the membrane. * T is the thickness of the membrane. * P₁ - P₂ is the partial pressure difference of the gas across the membrane. * D is the diffusion coefficient of the gas.

The diffusion coefficient (D) is specific to each gas and is determined by its physical properties. It is directly proportional to the gas's solubility (S) and inversely proportional to the square root of its molecular weight (MW).

D ∝ S / √MW

When comparing CO₂ and O₂: * Solubility (S): CO₂ is approximately 24 times more soluble in the fluid of the respiratory membrane and tissues than O₂. * Molecular Weight (MW): CO₂ (MW ≈ 44) is heavier than O₂ (MW ≈ 32).

Although O₂ is slightly lighter, the vastly higher solubility of CO₂ is the dominant factor. The combination of these properties means that the diffusion coefficient (D) for CO₂ is about 20 times greater than that for O₂. Therefore, according to Fick's Law, CO₂ diffuses across tissues about 20 times more rapidly than O₂ for the same partial pressure gradient.

Options:

  • (A) Alveolar ventilation: This is the process of moving air into and out of the alveoli. While it is crucial for maintaining the partial pressure gradients (P₁ - P₂) necessary for diffusion, it does not explain the intrinsic physical properties of the gases that determine their relative diffusion rates.
  • (B) Henry’s Law: This law states that the amount of a gas dissolved in a liquid is proportional to its partial pressure. It explains the concept of solubility, which is a key component of the diffusion coefficient in Fick's Law. While Henry's law explains why CO₂ is more soluble, Fick's Law is the overarching principle that integrates solubility with other factors to describe the overall rate of diffusion. Therefore, Fick's Law is the more complete answer.
  • (D) Oxygen saturation of hemoglobin: This refers to the percentage of hemoglobin binding sites occupied by oxygen in red blood cells. It is a measure of oxygen transport in the blood, not the physical process of gas diffusion across the tissue membranes.

Confidence: 99%

59


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Match the following statement with the best option from the list: (1.39 x Hb x Sat/100) + 0.003 x PO2 
Oxygen concentration of blood
Oxygen saturation of hemoglobin
Alveolar ventilation
Alveolar gas equation

Answer: (A) — Oxygen concentration of blood

Explanation:

The equation provided, (1.39 x Hb x Sat/100) + (0.003 x PO2), calculates the total oxygen content or concentration of blood (CaO2). Oxygen is transported in the blood in two forms:

  1. Bound to Hemoglobin (Hb): This is the primary method of oxygen transport. This portion of the equation is represented by (1.39 x Hb x Sat/100).

    • Hb: Concentration of hemoglobin in the blood (in g/dL).
    • 1.39: Hüfner's constant, which is the maximum amount of oxygen (in mL) that can bind to one gram of hemoglobin. (Note: Values from 1.34 to 1.39 are used in different texts).
    • Sat/100: The oxygen saturation of hemoglobin, expressed as a decimal (e.g., 98% saturation is 0.98).

  2. Dissolved in Plasma: A small amount of oxygen is dissolved directly in the blood plasma. This is represented by (0.003 x PO2).

    • PO2: The partial pressure of oxygen in the blood (in mmHg).
    • 0.003: The solubility coefficient for oxygen in plasma (mL of O2 per dL of blood per mmHg of PO2).

By summing these two components—the oxygen bound to hemoglobin and the oxygen dissolved in plasma—the equation yields the total oxygen concentration in the blood, typically expressed in mL of O2 per dL of blood.

Options:

  • Oxygen saturation of hemoglobin: This is incorrect. Oxygen saturation (Sat) is a variable within the equation used to calculate the amount of oxygen bound to hemoglobin. It represents the percentage of hemoglobin binding sites occupied by oxygen, not the total oxygen concentration.
  • Alveolar ventilation: This is incorrect. Alveolar ventilation is the volume of fresh air that reaches the alveoli per minute. It is calculated as (Tidal Volume - Dead Space) x Respiratory Rate and is unrelated to the given formula.
  • Alveolar gas equation: This is incorrect. The alveolar gas equation is used to estimate the partial pressure of oxygen in the alveoli (PAO2). The simplified equation is PAO2 = FiO2 x (Patm - PH2O) - (PaCO2 / R). This is a different formula used for a different physiological calculation.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The function of the GP IIb/IIIa complex - the most abundant receptor on the platelet surface is
Platelet secretion and aggregation
Platelet adhesion and aggregation
Platelet aggregation and activation
Platelet aggregation

Answer: (D) — Platelet aggregation

Explanation:

The Glycoprotein (GP) IIb/IIIa complex is a critical integrin receptor found in high abundance on the surface of platelets. Its primary and essential function is to mediate platelet aggregation.

Upon platelet activation by various agonists (such as thrombin, ADP, or collagen), the GP IIb/IIIa receptor undergoes a conformational change. This change allows it to bind with high affinity to its main ligand, fibrinogen. Fibrinogen is a dimeric molecule, meaning it can bind to GP IIb/IIIa receptors on two different platelets simultaneously. This cross-linking of platelets by fibrinogen bridges is the fundamental mechanism of platelet aggregation, leading to the formation of a primary hemostatic plug. Therefore, the GP IIb/IIIa complex serves as the final common pathway for platelet aggregation, regardless of the initial activating stimulus.

A clinical correlate is Glanzmann thrombasthenia, a rare genetic bleeding disorder caused by a deficiency or dysfunction of the GP IIb/IIIa complex, which leads to a severe impairment of platelet aggregation.

Options:

  • (A) Platelet secretion and aggregation: Incorrect. Platelet secretion (degranulation) is a component of platelet activation, a process that leads to the activation of the GP IIb/IIIa receptor. The receptor itself does not mediate secretion.
  • (B) Platelet adhesion and aggregation: Incorrect. While GP IIb/IIIa is essential for aggregation, initial platelet adhesion to the injured blood vessel wall (specifically, to subendothelial von Willebrand factor) is primarily mediated by a different receptor, the GP Ib-IX-V complex.
  • (C) Platelet aggregation and activation: Incorrect. Platelet activation is the upstream process that causes the GP IIb/IIIa receptor to become functional. The receptor is the target of inside-out signaling from an activated platelet; it does not cause the activation itself.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Drug that binds to platelet GP IIb/IIIa receptors inhibiting their function
Aspirin
Abciximab
Ibuprofen
Clopidogrel

Answer: (B) — Abciximab

Explanation:

Abciximab is a monoclonal antibody fragment (Fab) that functions as a potent antiplatelet agent. Its mechanism of action involves directly binding to the glycoprotein (GP) IIb/IIIa receptor on the surface of platelets. The GP IIb/IIIa receptor is crucial for platelet aggregation; upon platelet activation, this receptor undergoes a conformational change that allows it to bind to fibrinogen. Fibrinogen then acts as a bridge, cross-linking adjacent platelets. By binding to the GP IIb/IIIa receptor, abciximab sterically hinders the binding of fibrinogen, thereby blocking the final common pathway of platelet aggregation. This makes it a very effective inhibitor of platelet plug formation.

Options:

  • (A) Aspirin: Incorrect. Aspirin is an antiplatelet agent that works by irreversibly inhibiting the cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) enzyme in platelets. This action prevents the synthesis of thromboxane A2 (TXA2), a potent platelet activator and vasoconstrictor. This mechanism is upstream of GP IIb/IIIa receptor activation; it does not directly bind to the receptor itself.

  • (C) Ibuprofen: Incorrect. Ibuprofen is a non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drug (NSAID) that, like aspirin, inhibits COX enzymes. However, its inhibition is reversible and less potent for antiplatelet purposes compared to aspirin. Its mechanism does not involve direct binding to the GP IIb/IIIa receptor.

  • (D) Clopidogrel: Incorrect. Clopidogrel is a P2Y12 receptor antagonist. It works by irreversibly blocking the P2Y12 ADP receptor on the platelet surface. ADP is another key activator of platelets. By blocking this receptor, clopidogrel prevents the ADP-mediated signaling cascade that leads to the activation of the GP IIb/IIIa receptor. This action is also upstream and does not involve direct binding to the GP IIb/IIIa receptor.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The intact endothelium produces nitric oxide and prostacyclin. Their principal action is:
Prevents platelet adhesion
Platelet activation
Platelet adhesion
Prevent platelet activation

Answer: (D) — Prevent platelet activation

Explanation:

The intact vascular endothelium plays a crucial role in preventing thrombosis (unwanted blood clot formation) by creating an anti-thrombotic surface. It achieves this by producing several key molecules, including nitric oxide (NO) and prostacyclin (PGI₂).

The principal action of both NO and PGI₂ is to inhibit platelet function. They achieve this primarily by preventing platelet activation.

  • Prostacyclin (PGI₂): Binds to receptors on the platelet surface, activating adenylyl cyclase, which increases the intracellular concentration of cyclic AMP (cAMP).
  • Nitric Oxide (NO): Diffuses into the platelet and activates guanylyl cyclase, which increases the intracellular concentration of cyclic GMP (cGMP).

Both increased cAMP and cGMP act as powerful second messengers that inhibit platelet activation. They do so by reducing intracellular calcium (Ca²⁺) levels. Low intracellular Ca²⁺ prevents the critical steps of platelet activation, which include: 1. Change in platelet shape. 2. Degranulation (release of pro-aggregatory molecules like ADP and Thromboxane A₂). 3. Conformational change and activation of the glycoprotein IIb/IIIa receptor, which is essential for platelet aggregation.

By preventing activation, NO and PGI₂ effectively block subsequent platelet adhesion and aggregation, thus maintaining blood fluidity and preventing clot formation on the healthy endothelial surface.

Options:

  • (A) Prevents platelet adhesion: This is a correct statement, but it is a consequence of preventing platelet activation. The primary mechanism is the inhibition of the activation process itself, which then prevents firm adhesion and aggregation. Therefore, "prevent platelet activation" is a more fundamental and accurate description of their principal action.
  • (B) Platelet activation: This is incorrect. This is the opposite of the function of NO and PGI₂. Platelet activation is triggered by endothelial injury and exposure to subendothelial collagen, not by substances from an intact endothelium.
  • (C) Platelet adhesion: This is incorrect. This is also the opposite of their function. NO and PGI₂ work to prevent, not promote, platelet adhesion.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which of the following results in increased intracellular Na as well as calcium?
Digoxin
Calcium channel blockers
Beta-blockers
Glyceryl trinitrate

Answer: (A) — Digoxin

Explanation: Digoxin is a cardiac glycoside used primarily in the treatment of heart failure and atrial fibrillation. Its mechanism of action directly leads to an increase in both intracellular sodium (Na⁺) and calcium (Ca²⁺) concentrations in cardiac myocytes.

  1. Inhibition of Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase: Digoxin selectively and reversibly inhibits the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase pump located in the sarcolemma (cell membrane) of cardiac muscle cells. This pump is responsible for actively transporting 3 Na⁺ ions out of the cell in exchange for 2 potassium (K⁺) ions entering the cell.
  2. Increased Intracellular Na⁺: By inhibiting this pump, digoxin causes Na⁺ to accumulate inside the cell, leading to an increased intracellular Na⁺ concentration.
  3. Increased Intracellular Ca²⁺: The rise in intracellular Na⁺ alters the electrochemical gradient for the Na⁺/Ca²⁺ exchanger (NCX). This exchanger normally uses the strong Na⁺ gradient (low intracellular Na⁺) to pump Ca²⁺ out of the cell. With the gradient diminished, the NCX's ability to extrude Ca²⁺ is reduced. This results in a net increase in the intracellular Ca²⁺ concentration.
  4. Positive Inotropy: The higher intracellular Ca²⁺ concentration leads to greater uptake of Ca²⁺ into the sarcoplasmic reticulum (SR). During subsequent action potentials, more Ca²⁺ is released from the SR, which enhances the binding of Ca²⁺ to troponin C and results in a more forceful contraction of the heart muscle (a positive inotropic effect).

Options:

  • (B) Calcium channel blockers: These drugs (e.g., verapamil, diltiazem) block L-type calcium channels in the heart and vascular smooth muscle. This action reduces the influx of Ca²⁺ into the cell, leading to decreased intracellular Ca²⁺, which results in negative inotropy (reduced contractility) and vasodilation.
  • (C) Beta-blockers: These drugs (e.g., metoprolol, propranolol) antagonize beta-adrenergic receptors. Activation of these receptors normally increases intracellular Ca²⁺ via the cAMP pathway. Therefore, beta-blockers lead to a decrease in intracellular Ca²⁺ levels, reducing heart rate and contractility.
  • (D) Glyceryl trinitrate: Also known as nitroglycerin, this drug is converted to nitric oxide (NO). NO primarily acts on vascular smooth muscle, causing vasodilation (especially of veins) by increasing cGMP levels. This reduces the preload and afterload on the heart, decreasing myocardial oxygen demand. It does not directly act on cardiac myocytes to increase intracellular Na⁺ or Ca²⁺.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to cardiac electrical conduction, choose the option that best matches stem to follow. Which phase is due to net K+ efflux through multiple types of K+ channel?
Phase 3 Slow repolarisation phase in cardiac muscle cell
Phase 1 rapid repolarisation in cardiac muscle
Phase 0 rapid depolarisation in cardiac muscle
Phase 2 Plateau phase in cardiac muscle cell

Answer: (A) — Phase 3 Slow repolarisation phase in cardiac muscle cell

Explanation:

The cardiac action potential in a ventricular myocyte is divided into five phases (0-4). Phase 3 is the main repolarization phase, where the membrane potential returns from the plateau level (around 0 mV) back to the resting membrane potential (around -90 mV).

This repolarization is driven by a large, net efflux of potassium ions (K+) out of the cell. This occurs because the L-type calcium channels, which were open during Phase 2, inactivate and close, while several types of voltage-gated potassium channels, known as delayed rectifier channels, are fully open. These include the rapid (IKr) and slow (IKs) delayed rectifier K+ channels. The combined efflux of K+ through these multiple channel types makes the inside of the cell progressively more negative, leading to repolarization.

Options:

  • (B) Phase 1 rapid repolarisation in cardiac muscle: This is the brief, initial repolarization phase following the peak of depolarization. It is primarily caused by the inactivation of fast Na+ channels and the opening of a specific type of K+ channel called the transient outward K+ channel (Ito). While it involves K+ efflux, it is a short-lived phase dominated by a single major K+ current, not the sustained efflux through multiple channel types that characterizes Phase 3.
  • (C) Phase 0 rapid depolarisation in cardiac muscle: This phase is caused by a massive and rapid influx of sodium ions (Na+) through fast voltage-gated Na+ channels, not an efflux of K+. This is the "upstroke" of the action potential.
  • (D) Phase 2 Plateau phase in cardiac muscle cell: This phase is characterized by a balance between an inward current of calcium ions (Ca2+) through L-type Ca2+ channels and an outward current of potassium ions (K+) through delayed rectifier channels. Because these currents are roughly equal and opposite, there is no significant change in membrane potential, creating a plateau. There is no large net K+ efflux during this phase.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Regarding the use of special diagnostic techniques to detect certain types of organisms, match the best option with the stem that follows. A 22-year-old HIV positive patient presenting with acute onset shortness of breath, non-productive cough, fever and interstitial infiltrate on CXR:
Anaerobic blood culture
Periodic Acid Schiff stain
Silver stains
Giemsa Stain

Answer: (C) — Silver stains Explanation: The clinical presentation of a 22-year-old HIV-positive patient with acute shortness of breath, non-productive cough, fever, and bilateral interstitial infiltrates on chest X-ray is classic for Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia (PCP). Pneumocystis jirovecii is a fungus that causes opportunistic infections in immunocompromised individuals, particularly those with a low CD4 count (typically <200 cells/µL).

Definitive diagnosis of PCP requires visualization of the organism in a respiratory specimen, such as induced sputum or, more commonly, bronchoalveolar lavage (BAL) fluid. The most widely used and specific special stain for identifying P. jirovecii is a silver stain, such as the Grocott-Gomori methenamine-silver (GMS) stain. This stain impregnates the cyst walls of the fungus with silver, causing them to appear black or dark brown against a light green background. The cysts often have a characteristic crushed, cup-shaped, or "crushed ping-pong ball" appearance.

Options: * (A) Anaerobic blood culture: This technique is used to isolate anaerobic bacteria from the bloodstream. Pneumocystis is a fungus primarily located in the alveoli of the lungs, not an anaerobic bacterium that causes bacteremia. This test would not be useful for diagnosing PCP. * (B) Periodic Acid Schiff stain: The PAS stain can visualize the polysaccharide-rich cell walls of fungi, including Pneumocystis, staining them magenta. However, it is generally considered less sensitive and specific than the GMS stain for this particular purpose and can produce more background staining. * (D) Giemsa Stain: This stain is useful for visualizing the trophozoite forms of Pneumocystis by staining their nuclei purple/blue. However, it does not stain the thick, characteristic cyst walls, which are the primary target for diagnosis with stains like GMS. While sometimes used in conjunction with other stains, the silver stain is superior for identifying the cysts.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
This pathogen can cause a destructive orchitis if primary infection occurs in adults
Echovirus
Mumps
Enterovirus
Epstein Barr Virus

Answer: (B) — Mumps Explanation: Mumps virus, a member of the paramyxovirus family, is the classic cause of destructive orchitis (inflammation of the testes) when primary infection occurs in post-pubertal males. While mumps is often a self-limited disease in children, primarily causing parotitis (swelling of the salivary glands), infection in adolescents and adults carries a higher risk of complications. Orchitis develops in approximately 15-30% of post-pubertal males with mumps. The condition is typically unilateral but can be bilateral. It presents with abrupt onset of fever, severe testicular pain, and swelling. This inflammatory process can be destructive, leading to testicular atrophy in about 30-50% of affected cases and, although rare, can result in impaired fertility or sterility, especially if both testes are involved.

Options: (A) Echovirus: Echoviruses are a type of enterovirus. While they can cause a wide range of illnesses, including aseptic meningitis and rashes, orchitis is a very rare complication and is not the pathogen classically associated with severe, destructive orchitis.

(C) Enterovirus: This is a broad genus of viruses that includes echoviruses and coxsackieviruses. Coxsackie B virus is a known, but uncommon, cause of orchitis. As a group, enteroviruses are not the primary cause of destructive orchitis in adults; this distinction belongs to the Mumps virus.

(D) Epstein-Barr Virus (EBV): EBV is the cause of infectious mononucleosis. While it can have various systemic manifestations, orchitis is an extremely rare complication of EBV infection and is not considered a characteristic feature of the disease.
