MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to disorders of brain, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: Multiple, bilateral, gray and white matter lesions may result in a clinical syndrome characterized by memory deficit, gait abnormalities, pseudobulbar signs and superimposed focal neurologic deficits. The pathology that best matches the description is:
Alzheimer’s dementia
Hypertensive encephalopathy
Vascular dementia
Friedrichs Ataxia

Answer: (C) — Vascular dementia Explanation: The clinical and pathological description provided is a classic presentation of vascular dementia, specifically the multi-infarct subtype. This condition results from the cumulative effect of multiple strokes (infarcts) over time. These infarcts are areas of dead brain tissue caused by a lack of blood flow.

  • Multiple, bilateral, gray and white matter lesions: This directly describes the underlying pathology of multiple strokes occurring in various locations throughout the brain, affecting both the cortex (gray matter) and the underlying connecting fibers (white matter).
  • Memory deficit: This is a core criterion for any dementia. In vascular dementia, it is often accompanied by deficits in executive function.
  • Gait abnormalities: Damage to subcortical motor circuits and white matter tracts frequently leads to gait disturbances, which can be apraxic ("magnetic gait"), spastic, or ataxic. This is a very common early sign.
  • Pseudobulbar signs: Lesions that bilaterally affect the corticobulbar tracts (which control muscles of the face, mouth, and throat) lead to pseudobulbar palsy. This is characterized by dysarthria (slurred speech), dysphagia (difficulty swallowing), and emotional lability (inappropriate or exaggerated laughing or crying).
  • Superimposed focal neurologic deficits: Each new stroke can cause a new, distinct neurological problem (e.g., weakness in one arm, a visual field cut). This leads to a characteristic "stepwise" decline, where the patient's condition is stable for a period and then suddenly worsens with each new event.

Options: * (A) Alzheimer’s dementia: This is a neurodegenerative disease primarily affecting cortical gray matter, caused by amyloid plaques and neurofibrillary tangles. The onset is typically insidious with a gradual, smooth decline in memory. Significant gait abnormalities, pseudobulbar signs, and focal deficits are not early or characteristic features, usually appearing only in the late stages of the disease. * (B) Hypertensive encephalopathy: This is an acute or subacute medical emergency caused by a rapid, severe elevation in blood pressure. It presents with headache, confusion, seizures, and visual disturbances due to cerebral edema. It does not present as a chronic, progressive dementia with multiple, discrete old lesions. * (D) Friedreichs Ataxia: This is an inherited, autosomal recessive neurodegenerative disorder that typically begins in childhood or adolescence. It primarily affects the spinal cord (spinocerebellar tracts) and cerebellum, leading to progressive ataxia, loss of reflexes, and cardiomyopathy. Dementia is not a primary feature, and the pathology is a specific system degeneration, not multiple scattered cerebral infarcts.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 68-year-old suffers from congestive cardiac failure. He has elevated levels of BNP (B-type natriuretic peptide). Regarding BNP, which of the following statements is CORRECT?
BNP decreases capillary permeability
BNP secretion is increased when ventricles are stretched
BNP constricts the afferent arterioles in the kidney
BNP increases sodium absorption from renal tubules

Answer: (B) — BNP secretion is increased when ventricles are stretched

Explanation:

B-type natriuretic peptide (BNP) is a hormone primarily synthesized and secreted by cardiac ventricular myocytes in response to myocardial wall stress and stretch. In conditions like congestive cardiac failure, the ventricles are often dilated and experience increased pressure due to volume overload. This physical stretching of the ventricular muscle cells is the main physiological trigger for the release of BNP into the bloodstream. The body uses BNP as a counter-regulatory hormone to combat the effects of heart failure by promoting vasodilation, natriuresis (sodium excretion), and diuresis (water excretion), thereby reducing cardiac preload and afterload. Therefore, elevated BNP levels are a key clinical marker for the diagnosis and severity of heart failure.

Options:

  • BNP decreases capillary permeability: This is incorrect. Natriuretic peptides, including BNP, are known to increase capillary permeability. This action facilitates the movement of fluid from the intravascular compartment to the interstitial space, which helps to reduce blood volume and vascular congestion.

  • BNP constricts the afferent arterioles in the kidney: This is incorrect. To promote natriuresis and diuresis, BNP needs to increase the glomerular filtration rate (GFR). It achieves this by dilating the afferent (incoming) arterioles and constricting the efferent (outgoing) arterioles of the glomerulus. This combination increases the hydrostatic pressure within the glomerular capillaries, thus enhancing filtration.

  • BNP increases sodium absorption from renal tubules: This is incorrect. The term "natriuretic" literally means promoting the excretion of sodium in the urine. BNP's primary renal function is to inhibit sodium reabsorption in the distal tubules and collecting ducts, primarily by antagonizing the effects of aldosterone and directly inhibiting sodium channels (like ENaC). This leads to increased sodium and water excretion.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
When the barometric pressure is half the normal atmospheric pressure, the PO2 of inspired air is
60 mm Hg
70 mmHg
80 mm Hg
50 mm Hg

Answer: (B) — 70 mmHg

Explanation:

The partial pressure of inspired oxygen (PiO2) is calculated using the following formula, which accounts for the humidification of air in the upper airways:

PiO2 = FIO2 × (Pb - PH2O)

Where: * PiO2 is the partial pressure of inspired oxygen. * FIO2 is the fraction of inspired oxygen, which is approximately 21% (or 0.21) and remains constant regardless of altitude. * Pb is the total barometric (atmospheric) pressure. * PH2O is the partial pressure of water vapor in the airways, which is constant at 47 mm Hg at a body temperature of 37°C.

Step-by-step calculation: 1. Determine the new barometric pressure (Pb): Normal atmospheric pressure at sea level is 760 mm Hg. The question states the pressure is half of this. * Pb = 760 mm Hg / 2 = 380 mm Hg. 2. Apply the formula: Substitute the known values into the equation. * PiO2 = 0.21 × (380 mm Hg - 47 mm Hg) * PiO2 = 0.21 × (333 mm Hg) * PiO2 ≈ 69.93 mm Hg

This calculated value is approximately 70 mm Hg.

Options:

  • (A) 60 mm Hg: This value is incorrect. It would result from an error in the calculation or assuming a lower barometric pressure or higher water vapor pressure.
  • (B) 70 mmHg: This is the correct answer, derived from the accurate calculation of PiO2 at a barometric pressure of 380 mm Hg.
  • (C) 80 mm Hg: This value is incorrect. It is a common distractor that arises from forgetting to subtract the water vapor pressure (PH2O) from the barometric pressure before multiplying by FIO2 (i.e., 0.21 × 380 mm Hg ≈ 80 mm Hg). This would be the PO2 in dry ambient air, not inspired, humidified air.
  • (D) 50 mm Hg: This value is incorrect and does not correspond to the physiological calculation under the given conditions.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The typical value of minute ventilation in normal quiet breathing is
6L
15L
12L
4L

Answer: (A) — 6L Explanation: Minute ventilation (V̇E) is the volume of air inhaled or exhaled from the lungs per minute. It is calculated by multiplying the tidal volume (VT) by the respiratory rate (RR).

V̇E = VT × RR

In a typical, healthy adult during normal quiet breathing (eupnea): * Tidal Volume (VT), the volume of air per breath, is approximately 500 mL (or 0.5 L). * Respiratory Rate (RR), the number of breaths per minute, is typically around 12-15 breaths/minute.

Using these standard values: V̇E = 0.5 L/breath × 12 breaths/minute = 6 L/minute

This value represents the total volume of air moving in and out of the respiratory tract each minute.

Options: * (B) 15L: This value is significantly higher than that of quiet breathing. A minute ventilation of 15 L/min would be seen during light to moderate exercise or in states of hyperventilation. * (C) 12L: This value is double the typical resting minute ventilation. It would be characteristic of a state with increased respiratory demand, such as light exercise. * (D) 4L: While this value could be seen in smaller individuals or those with a very low respiratory rate, 6 L/min is the widely accepted typical value for an average-sized adult at rest.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The amount of air that keeps the alveoli inflated between breaths and mixes with fresh air on next inspiration is:
Tidal volume
Residual volume
Inspiratory reserve volume
Expiratory reserve volume

Answer: (B) — Residual volume

Explanation:

The Residual Volume (RV) is the volume of air that remains in the lungs after a maximal, forceful expiration. This trapped air is crucial because it prevents the alveoli (the tiny air sacs where gas exchange occurs) and small airways from collapsing completely. By keeping the alveoli partially inflated at all times, it reduces the effort required to inflate the lungs during the next inspiration. Furthermore, this residual air allows for continuous gas exchange to occur between the blood and the alveolar air, even between breaths. When fresh air is inhaled (Tidal Volume), it mixes with this residual volume, which helps to buffer and stabilize the concentrations of oxygen and carbon dioxide in the blood.

The volume of air in the lungs at the end of a normal, quiet expiration is called the Functional Residual Capacity (FRC), which is the sum of the Residual Volume (RV) and the Expiratory Reserve Volume (ERV). While the FRC is the volume present "between breaths" in a resting state, the Residual Volume is the fundamental component that can never be exhaled and is primarily responsible for preventing alveolar collapse. Given the options, Residual Volume is the most accurate answer describing the air that always keeps the alveoli inflated.

Options:

  • (A) Tidal volume: This is the amount of air inhaled or exhaled during a single normal, quiet breath. It represents the volume exchanged with the environment, not the volume that remains in the lungs.
  • (C) Inspiratory reserve volume: This is the extra amount of air that can be forcibly inhaled after a normal tidal inspiration. It is a measure of additional inspiratory capacity, not the air remaining after exhalation.
  • (D) Expiratory reserve volume: This is the extra amount of air that can be forcibly exhaled after a normal tidal expiration. This volume is part of the air in the lungs between quiet breaths (as part of the FRC), but it can be expelled and does not represent the air that always remains to prevent collapse.

Confidence: 95%

74


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which of the following causes relaxation of bronchial smooth muscle?
Vasoactive intestinal polypeptide
Acetylcholine
Sulphur dioxide
Leukotrienes

Answer: (A) — Vasoactive intestinal polypeptide

Explanation:

Bronchial smooth muscle tone is regulated by a complex interplay of neural, hormonal, and inflammatory signals. Vasoactive intestinal polypeptide (VIP) is a neuropeptide that plays a key role in bronchodilation. It is the principal neurotransmitter of the non-adrenergic, non-cholinergic (NANC) inhibitory nervous system in the airways. When released from nerve endings, VIP binds to its receptors (VPAC1 and VPAC2) on bronchial smooth muscle cells. This binding activates the adenylyl cyclase pathway, leading to an increase in intracellular cyclic adenosine monophosphate (cAMP). Elevated cAMP levels activate protein kinase A (PKA), which phosphorylates various proteins, ultimately resulting in the sequestration of intracellular calcium and the inhibition of myosin light chain kinase, causing smooth muscle relaxation and bronchodilation.

Options:

  • (B) Acetylcholine: This is incorrect. Acetylcholine is the primary neurotransmitter of the parasympathetic nervous system. It acts on muscarinic (M3) receptors on bronchial smooth muscle, leading to an increase in intracellular calcium and causing potent bronchoconstriction (contraction).
  • (C) Sulphur dioxide: This is incorrect. Sulphur dioxide is an airway irritant. Inhalation triggers a vagally-mediated reflex that causes the release of acetylcholine, leading to bronchoconstriction. It does not cause relaxation.
  • (D) Leukotrienes: This is incorrect. Leukotrienes (specifically cysteinyl leukotrienes LTC4, LTD4, and LTE4) are powerful inflammatory mediators, particularly important in the pathophysiology of asthma. They bind to CysLT1 receptors on airway smooth muscle, causing profound and sustained bronchoconstriction.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
In a healthy person, what is the partial pressure of oxygen in the alveoli when breathing air, if the respiratory quotient is 0.8 and the alveolar ventilation is halved?

49
54
69
34

Answer: (A) — 49

Explanation:

This question requires the use of the Alveolar Gas Equation to determine the partial pressure of oxygen in the alveoli (PAO2) after a physiological change.

  1. The Alveolar Gas Equation: The simplified form is: PAO2 = PiO2 - (PACO2 / R) Where:

    • PAO2: Alveolar partial pressure of O2.
    • PiO2: Partial pressure of inspired O2.
    • PACO2: Alveolar partial pressure of CO2.
    • R: Respiratory quotient (CO2 produced / O2 consumed).

  2. Determine Baseline Values for a Healthy Person:

    • PiO2: First, we calculate the partial pressure of inspired oxygen. At sea level (atmospheric pressure ≈ 760 mmHg), the air is humidified in the airways, exerting a water vapor pressure (PH2O) of 47 mmHg. PiO2 = (Atmospheric Pressure - PH2O) * FiO2 PiO2 = (760 mmHg - 47 mmHg) * 0.21 = 713 * 0.21 ≈ 150 mmHg
    • PACO2: In a healthy person, the normal alveolar PCO2 is approximately 40 mmHg.
    • R: The question provides R = 0.8.

  3. Analyze the Effect of Halving Alveolar Ventilation (VA):

    • Alveolar PCO2 is inversely proportional to alveolar ventilation (PACO2 ∝ 1/VA), assuming a constant rate of CO2 production.
    • If alveolar ventilation is halved, the PACO2 will double.
    • New PACO2 = Normal PACO2 * 2 = 40 mmHg * 2 = 80 mmHg.

  4. Calculate the New PAO2:

    • Now, we plug the new PACO2 value into the Alveolar Gas Equation.
    • PAO2 = 150 mmHg - (80 mmHg / 0.8)
    • PAO2 = 150 mmHg - 100 mmHg
    • PAO2 = 50 mmHg

The calculated value is 50 mmHg. The closest option provided is 49 mmHg. This slight difference is likely due to rounding of the PiO2 value (149.73 mmHg vs 150 mmHg). Therefore, 49 mmHg is the correct answer.

Options:

  • (A) 49: Correct. This value is the closest to the calculated PAO2 of 50 mmHg after accounting for the doubling of PACO2 due to halved alveolar ventilation.
  • (B) 54: Incorrect. This value would be obtained if there was a smaller increase in PACO2 or an error in the calculation. For example, if PACO2 only rose to ~77 mmHg.
  • (C) 69: Incorrect. This value is too high. A decrease in ventilation (hypoventilation) causes hypercapnia (high PACO2) and subsequent hypoxemia (low PAO2). A PAO2 of 69 would imply a much smaller rise in PACO2 (to about 65 mmHg), which is inconsistent with halving ventilation.
  • (D) 34: Incorrect. This value is too low. It would imply a PACO2 of approximately 93 mmHg, which is a greater increase than the simple doubling expected from halving ventilation.

Confidence: 100%

76


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following reduces airway resistance?
Forced expiration
Stimulation of adrenergic receptors
Breathing through the nose instead of the mouth
Low lung volumes

Answer: (B) — Stimulation of adrenergic receptors Explanation: Airway resistance is primarily determined by the radius of the airways. Stimulation of β2-adrenergic receptors, which are part of the sympathetic nervous system, causes relaxation of the bronchial smooth muscle. This leads to an increase in the diameter of the airways (bronchodilation), which significantly reduces airway resistance according to Poiseuille's law (Resistance ∝ 1/radius⁴). This is the mechanism by which medications like albuterol (a β2-agonist) work to relieve bronchoconstriction in conditions like asthma.

Options: (A) Forced expiration: During forced expiration, the contraction of expiratory muscles (e.g., abdominal muscles) generates high positive intrapleural pressure. This pressure can exceed the pressure inside the smaller, non-cartilaginous airways, causing them to compress (dynamic compression). This narrowing of the airways markedly increases resistance.

(C) Breathing through the nose instead of the mouth: The nasal passages are narrower and have a more complex, turbulent airflow path compared to the mouth. The nose is responsible for approximately 50% of the total airway resistance during quiet breathing. Therefore, breathing through the nose increases airway resistance compared to breathing through the mouth.

(D) Low lung volumes: The airways are embedded within the lung parenchyma. The surrounding lung tissue exerts an outward pulling force, known as radial traction, on the airways, helping to keep them open. At low lung volumes, the lungs are less inflated, and this radial traction is reduced. The decrease in outward pull allows the airways to become narrower, which increases airway resistance. Conversely, at high lung volumes, radial traction is high, pulling the airways open and decreasing resistance.

Confidence: 100%

77


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Auscultation of heart sounds is a vital component of the cardiovascular examination. Regarding the heart sounds, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
Second heart sound – closure of aortic and pulmonary valves just after end of ventricular systole
First heart sound – sudden closure of AV valves at the start of ventricular diastole
Third heart sound – Rapid ventricular filling, at the end of diastole
Fourth heart sound – Sudden opening of the AV valves at the end of systole

Answer: (A) — Second heart sound – closure of aortic and pulmonary valves just after end of ventricular systole

Explanation:

The second heart sound (S2), often described as "dub," marks the end of ventricular systole and the beginning of ventricular diastole. During ventricular systole, blood is ejected from the ventricles into the aorta and pulmonary artery. As the ventricles relax at the end of systole, the pressure within them falls rapidly. When ventricular pressure drops below the pressure in the aorta and pulmonary artery, the back pressure from these great vessels causes the aortic and pulmonary (semilunar) valves to snap shut. This closure produces the S2 sound. This event corresponds to the dicrotic notch on the aortic pressure waveform and signals the start of isovolumetric ventricular relaxation.

Options:

  • (B) First heart sound – sudden closure of AV valves at the start of ventricular diastole: This is incorrect. The first heart sound (S1 or "lub") is caused by the closure of the atrioventricular (AV) valves (mitral and tricuspid). This event occurs at the start of ventricular systole, not diastole, when ventricular pressure rises above atrial pressure.
  • (C) Third heart sound – Rapid ventricular filling, at the end of diastole: This is incorrect. The third heart sound (S3), when present, is a low-frequency sound that occurs during the early, rapid ventricular filling phase of diastole, just after S2. It is caused by the vibration of the ventricular walls as blood rushes in. The end of diastole is associated with atrial contraction, which can cause a fourth heart sound (S4).
  • (D) Fourth heart sound – Sudden opening of the AV valves at the end of systole: This is incorrect. The fourth heart sound (S4) is a pathological sound caused by the forceful contraction of the atria pushing blood into a stiff, non-compliant ventricle. This occurs in late diastole, just before S1. Furthermore, the opening of heart valves is normally a silent event; it is the closure that produces the primary heart sounds.

Confidence: 100%

78


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which of the following causes increase in coronary blood flow?
Hypokalemia
Alkalosis
Hypocarbia
Adenosine

Answer: (D) — Adenosine

Explanation:

Coronary blood flow is tightly regulated to match the metabolic demands of the myocardium. The primary mechanism for this regulation is metabolic hyperemia, where byproducts of metabolism act as local vasodilators. Adenosine is the most significant of these metabolic mediators. When myocardial oxygen consumption increases or oxygen supply is limited (ischemia), ATP is catabolized to ADP, AMP, and ultimately adenosine. Adenosine diffuses out of the cardiomyocytes and binds to A2A receptors on the vascular smooth muscle cells of the coronary arterioles. This activation leads to an increase in intracellular cAMP, which causes smooth muscle relaxation, vasodilation, and a subsequent increase in coronary blood flow to restore the oxygen supply-demand balance.

Options:

  • (A) Hypokalemia: Low extracellular potassium (hypokalemia) generally causes vasoconstriction by hyperpolarizing the vascular smooth muscle cells, which can close some K+ channels and facilitate Ca2+ entry. Conversely, hyperkalemia causes vasodilation. Therefore, hypokalemia would decrease, not increase, coronary blood flow.
  • (B) Alkalosis: A decrease in hydrogen ion concentration (increased pH or alkalosis) leads to vasoconstriction in the coronary and cerebral circulations. Its opposite, acidosis (increased H+), is a potent vasodilator. Therefore, alkalosis would decrease coronary blood flow.
  • (C) Hypocarbia: A low partial pressure of carbon dioxide (PCO2) in the blood causes vasoconstriction. CO2 is a vasodilator, partly through its conversion to carbonic acid, which lowers pH (acidosis). Therefore, hypocarbia (a state of low CO2) would lead to a decrease in coronary blood flow.

Confidence: 100%

79


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
By which one of the following mechanisms does ethanol induce diuresis?
Inhibiting vasopressin secretion
Increasing GFR
Osmotic diuresis
Inhibiting Na+ reabsorptions

Answer: (A) — Inhibiting vasopressin secretion Explanation: The primary mechanism by which ethanol (alcohol) induces diuresis is by inhibiting the secretion of antidiuretic hormone (ADH), also known as vasopressin, from the posterior pituitary gland. ADH normally acts on the collecting ducts of the kidneys to increase their permeability to water by promoting the insertion of aquaporin-2 water channels into the apical membrane. This action allows for the reabsorption of water from the filtrate back into the bloodstream, thus concentrating the urine. By suppressing ADH release, ethanol reduces the permeability of the collecting ducts to water. Consequently, less water is reabsorbed, leading to the excretion of a larger volume of dilute urine (a state known as water diuresis).

Options: * (B) Increasing GFR: Ethanol is a vasodilator, but its effect on the glomerular filtration rate (GFR) is not the primary mechanism of its diuretic effect. The net effect on GFR can be variable and is not significant enough to account for the pronounced diuresis observed after alcohol consumption. * (C) Osmotic diuresis: Osmotic diuresis occurs when a non-reabsorbable solute in the renal tubules (like glucose in uncontrolled diabetes) exerts an osmotic force, drawing water into the tubule and preventing its reabsorption. While ethanol is filtered at the glomerulus, it is a small, lipid-soluble molecule that is readily reabsorbed along the nephron. Therefore, it does not remain in the tubular fluid in high enough concentrations to act as a significant osmotic diuretic. * (D) Inhibiting Na+ reabsorption: Many diuretic drugs (e.g., loop diuretics, thiazides) work by inhibiting sodium (Na+) reabsorption, which in turn reduces water reabsorption. However, this is not the principal mechanism for ethanol-induced diuresis. Ethanol's main effect is on water balance regulation via ADH, not on the primary transport of electrolytes like sodium.

Confidence: 100%

80


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
In normal physiological state, what proportion of filtered Na+ is reabsorbed?
50-55%
96-99%
80-85%
65-70%

Answer: (B) — 96-99%

Explanation:

The kidneys play a crucial role in maintaining sodium and water balance in the body. A large volume of plasma is filtered through the glomeruli each day (approximately 180 L), resulting in a massive filtered load of sodium (around 25,200 mEq/day). To prevent life-threatening dehydration and electrolyte imbalance, the renal tubules must reabsorb the vast majority of this filtered sodium.

The reabsorption of sodium occurs along the entire nephron in a segmented fashion: 1. Proximal Convoluted Tubule (PCT): This is the site of bulk reabsorption. Approximately 65-70% of the filtered sodium is reabsorbed here, along with water, glucose, and amino acids. 2. Thick Ascending Limb (TAL) of the Loop of Henle: About 20-25% of the filtered sodium is reabsorbed here via the Na⁺-K⁺-2Cl⁻ cotransporter (NKCC2). This segment is impermeable to water, which is critical for creating a hypertonic medullary interstitium. 3. Distal Convoluted Tubule (DCT): Roughly 5% of the filtered sodium is reabsorbed here through the Na⁺-Cl⁻ cotransporter (NCC). This site is important for fine-tuning sodium excretion and is the target of thiazide diuretics. 4. Collecting Duct (CD): The final 1-3% of filtered sodium is reabsorbed in the collecting duct via the epithelial sodium channel (ENaC). This process is tightly regulated by the hormone aldosterone, allowing for precise control of sodium balance to match dietary intake.

Summing these percentages (67% + 25% + 5% + ~2% = 99%), it is clear that over 99% of the initially filtered sodium is reabsorbed back into the bloodstream. The final amount excreted in the urine is typically less than 1% of the filtered load, a value that is adjusted to maintain homeostasis. Therefore, the physiological range for total sodium reabsorption is 96-99%.

Options:

  • (A) 50-55%: This is incorrect. Reabsorbing only half of the filtered sodium would lead to massive urinary losses of sodium and water, causing rapid dehydration, hypovolemia, and circulatory collapse.
  • (C) 80-85%: This is incorrect. This value significantly underestimates the total reabsorption, failing to account for the crucial contributions of the distal convoluted tubule and the collecting duct in reclaiming sodium.
  • (D) 65-70%: This is incorrect. This value represents the proportion of sodium reabsorbed in the proximal convoluted tubule alone, not the total reabsorption along the entire nephron.

Confidence: 100%

81


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to cerebrovascular disease, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: This pathology is most often found in the anterior circulation which usually results in cerebral infarction. 
Mycotic aneurysm
Arteriovenous malformation
Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy
Berry aneurysm

Answer: (A) — Mycotic aneurysm

Explanation:

A mycotic aneurysm is an aneurysm that results from an infection of the arterial wall. This condition is most commonly caused by septic emboli, which are infected clots that travel through the bloodstream, often originating from infective endocarditis. These emboli tend to lodge in distal branches of the cerebral arteries, with a strong predilection for the Middle Cerebral Artery (MCA) territory. The MCA is a major part of the brain's anterior circulation.

The septic embolus itself can block the artery, causing a cerebral infarction (ischemic stroke). Subsequently, the infection within the embolus weakens the arterial wall, leading to the formation of an aneurysm. Therefore, a cerebral infarction is a very common, and often the initial, clinical manifestation of the process that leads to a mycotic aneurysm. This aligns perfectly with the statement that the pathology is found in the anterior circulation and usually results in cerebral infarction.

Options:

  • (B) Arteriovenous malformation (AVM): This is a congenital tangle of abnormal blood vessels. While AVMs are most commonly located in the territory of the MCA (anterior circulation), their most frequent clinical presentation is intracerebral or subarachnoid hemorrhage, not infarction. Infarction can occur due to a "steal phenomenon" where blood is shunted away from adjacent brain tissue, but this is less common than hemorrhage.
  • (C) Cerebral Amyloid Angiopathy (CAA): This condition involves the deposition of amyloid protein in the walls of small-to-medium cortical and leptomeningeal arteries. It typically affects older adults and has a predilection for the occipital and parietal lobes. The primary clinical manifestation of CAA is recurrent lobar hemorrhage, not infarction.
  • (D) Berry aneurysm (Saccular aneurysm): This is the most common type of intracranial aneurysm. While over 85% of berry aneurysms are located in the anterior circulation (at branch points of the Circle of Willis), their classic and most devastating result is rupture leading to subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH). Cerebral infarction can be a severe complication of SAH due to secondary vasospasm, but it is not the primary event or the pathology's most direct result. Therefore, it does not fit the description as well as a mycotic aneurysm.

Confidence: 90%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to cerebrovascular disease, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: In some instances, this pathology is associated with minute aneurysms, termed Charcot-Bouchard microaneurysms which occur in vessels that are less than 300 µm in diameter, most commonly within the basal ganglia. 
Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy
Hypertensive intracerebral haemorrhage
Mycotic aneurysms
Lacunar infarcts

Answer: (B) — Hypertensive intracerebral haemorrhage Explanation: The statement describes the classic pathophysiology of hypertensive intracerebral hemorrhage. Chronic systemic hypertension leads to degenerative changes in the walls of small, penetrating arteries and arterioles, a process known as hyaline arteriolosclerosis. This process weakens the vessel walls, making them prone to forming minute aneurysms, which were first described by Charcot and Bouchard. Rupture of these Charcot-Bouchard microaneurysms is the most common cause of intracerebral hemorrhage, which characteristically occurs in deep brain structures like the basal ganglia, thalamus, pons, and cerebellum—regions supplied by these small penetrating vessels.

Options: (A) Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy: This condition involves the deposition of amyloid-β protein in the walls of small to medium-sized cortical and leptomeningeal vessels. This weakens the vessel walls and predisposes them to rupture, but it typically causes superficial, lobar hemorrhages, not the deep hemorrhages associated with Charcot-Bouchard aneurysms.

(C) Mycotic aneurysms: These are aneurysms resulting from an infection of the arterial wall, most commonly from septic emboli in the setting of infective endocarditis. The term "mycotic" is a misnomer, as most are caused by bacteria. They are pathogenetically distinct from the degenerative Charcot-Bouchard aneurysms caused by hypertension.

(D) Lacunar infarcts: These are small, ischemic infarcts (<15 mm) in the deep brain structures, caused by the occlusion of a single penetrating artery. While the underlying vessel disease (lipohyalinosis and microatheroma from chronic hypertension) is the same as that which causes Charcot-Bouchard aneurysms, a lacunar infarct is a result of vessel blockage and ischemia, not vessel rupture and hemorrhage.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to cerebrovascular disease, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: This pathology may involve blood vessels in the subarachnoid space, brain parenchyma or both and usually has high blood flow with pulsatile enlarged vessels.
Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy
Cavernous malformation
Saccular aneurysm
Arteriorvenous malformation

Answer: (D) — Arteriovenous malformation

Explanation: An Arteriovenous Malformation (AVM) is a congenital vascular anomaly characterized by a tangle of abnormal blood vessels, known as a nidus, where arteries connect directly to veins without an intervening capillary bed. This direct connection creates a high-pressure, high-flow shunt. The key features described in the question align perfectly with the pathophysiology of an AVM: * Location: AVMs can be located anywhere in the central nervous system, including deep within the brain parenchyma, on the surface of the brain extending into the subarachnoid space, or both. * High Blood Flow: The lack of a high-resistance capillary bed results in a low-resistance pathway, causing a large volume of blood to be shunted directly from the arterial to the venous system. * Pulsatile Enlarged Vessels: The high flow and pressure cause the feeding arteries and draining veins to become dilated, tortuous, and engorged. The draining veins become "arterialized" and the entire lesion can be pulsatile on examination or imaging.

Options: * (A) Cerebral Amyloid Angiopathy (CAA): This is a condition where amyloid-β protein deposits in the walls of small and medium-sized arteries and arterioles of the cerebral cortex and leptomeninges. This weakens the vessel walls, leading to lobar hemorrhages, but it does not create a high-flow shunt or a tangle of pulsatile vessels. * (B) Cavernous malformation: Also known as a cavernoma, this is a low-flow vascular malformation composed of a cluster of dilated, thin-walled sinusoidal vessels. They are characterized by slow blood flow and are not associated with large feeding arteries or early draining veins, and therefore are not pulsatile or high-flow lesions. * (C) Saccular aneurysm: This is a localized, sac-like outpouching of an arterial wall, typically occurring at a bifurcation in the Circle of Willis within the subarachnoid space. While it is on a high-flow artery and is pulsatile, it is a single focal lesion, not a tangle of vessels, and does not represent a shunt like an AVM.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Intracranial pressure is a function of the volume and compliance of each component of the intracranial compartment that includes brain parenchyma, CSF and blood. The statement describes:
Fick's Law
The Monroe Kellie Doctrine
Frank-starling effect
Gay-Lussac's law

Answer: (B) — The Monroe Kellie Doctrine

Explanation:

The Monro-Kellie doctrine is a fundamental principle in neurophysiology that describes the pressure-volume relationship within the rigid, non-expansile cranium. It states that the skull encloses a fixed total volume, which is occupied by three components: brain parenchyma (approximately 80%), cerebrospinal fluid (CSF, approximately 10%), and intracranial blood (approximately 10%).

According to this doctrine, the sum of the volumes of these three components must remain constant. If the volume of one component increases (e.g., due to a tumor, hemorrhage, or hydrocephalus), there must be a compensatory decrease in the volume of one or both of the other components to maintain normal intracranial pressure (ICP). Initially, CSF can be displaced into the spinal canal, and venous blood volume can be reduced. However, once these compensatory mechanisms are exhausted, any further small increase in volume will cause a sharp and dangerous rise in ICP. The statement in the question perfectly describes this principle.

Options:

  • (A) Fick's Law: This is incorrect. Fick's laws of diffusion describe the rate of transfer of a substance across a membrane, which is proportional to the concentration gradient. It is relevant to processes like gas exchange in the lungs but does not describe the pressure-volume dynamics within the skull.
  • (C) Frank-Starling effect: This is incorrect. The Frank-Starling law of the heart states that the stroke volume of the heart increases in response to an increase in the volume of blood filling the ventricles (the end-diastolic volume). This is a principle of cardiac physiology, not neurocranial dynamics.
  • (D) Gay-Lussac's law: This is incorrect. Gay-Lussac's law is a gas law from physics which states that the pressure of a given mass of gas varies directly with the absolute temperature when the volume is kept constant. It is not applicable to the biological tissues and fluids within the intracranial compartment.

Confidence: 100%

85


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regards to types of brain haemorrhage and brain trauma, match the stem below with the option that fits best. This pathology may affect the corpus callosum, paraventricular, and hippocampal areas in the supratentorial compartment, cerebral peduncles, brachium conjunctivum, superior colliculi, and deep reticular formation in the brainstem. 50% of trauma victims with coma may be affected by this; victims of non accidental injury under 6 months of age may have this pathology. 
Cerebral contusions
Diffuse axonal injury
Subarachnoid Haemorrhage
Cerebral Oedema

Answer: (B) — Diffuse axonal injury Explanation: The question describes the key features of Diffuse Axonal Injury (DAI). DAI is a type of traumatic brain injury caused by shearing forces, typically from rapid acceleration-deceleration or rotational movements of the head. These forces stretch and damage axons, the long extensions of nerve cells. The damage is characteristically widespread (diffuse) and occurs at the junctions between tissues of different densities, such as the grey-white matter junction.

The locations mentioned in the stem are classic sites for DAI: * Corpus Callosum: The largest white matter tract connecting the cerebral hemispheres. * Paraventricular and Hippocampal areas: Deep white matter structures. * Brainstem structures (cerebral peduncles, brachium conjunctivum, superior colliculi, deep reticular formation): These are critical white matter tracts and nuclei in the brainstem.

The clinical correlation provided also strongly supports DAI. It is a major cause of unconsciousness and persistent vegetative state after head trauma, affecting up to 50% of comatose trauma victims. Furthermore, the violent shaking involved in non-accidental injury (shaken baby syndrome) in infants is a classic mechanism for producing DAI.

Options: * (A) Cerebral contusions: These are bruises on the surface of the brain, typically located at the crests of the gyri. They are focal injuries resulting from the brain striking the inside of the skull (coup-contrecoup mechanism), not the widespread, deep white matter injury described. * (C) Subarachnoid Haemorrhage: This is bleeding into the subarachnoid space, which is the area between the arachnoid mater and the pia mater that contains cerebrospinal fluid. While it can be caused by trauma, it represents bleeding around the brain rather than injury within the deep white matter tracts. * (D) Cerebral Oedema: This refers to brain swelling due to an increase in fluid content. It is a common secondary response to many types of brain injury, including trauma, stroke, and infection. While it can be diffuse, it is a consequence of the primary injury, not the specific pattern of axonal damage described in the question.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
In cases of trauma to brain, the most common locations for this pathology correspond to the frontal lobes along the orbital ridges and the temporal lobes.
Subarachnoid Haemorrhage
Cerebral Oedema
Cerebral contusions
Axonal injury

Answer: (C) — Cerebral contusions Explanation: Cerebral contusions are bruises of the brain tissue, typically involving the crests of the gyri. They are caused by the brain impacting the hard, irregular surfaces of the interior of the skull during traumatic acceleration-deceleration events. The locations mentioned in the question—the frontal lobes along the orbital ridges and the tips of the temporal lobes—are the most common sites for contusions. This is because these areas of the brain are adjacent to the rough and bony prominences of the anterior and middle cranial fossae (e.g., the orbital plates of the frontal bone and the sphenoid wing). When the head suddenly stops or changes direction, the brain continues to move and strikes these surfaces, leading to bruising, hemorrhage, and tissue damage in these specific locations.

Options: (A) Subarachnoid Haemorrhage: This is bleeding into the subarachnoid space. While it can be caused by trauma, it is not defined by parenchymal bruising in the specific locations mentioned. Traumatic subarachnoid hemorrhage is often more diffuse, and the most common non-traumatic cause is a ruptured berry aneurysm, typically located at the branch points of the Circle of Willis.

(B) Cerebral Oedema: This refers to brain swelling due to an increase in water content. It is a common secondary response to many forms of brain injury, including trauma. However, it is a consequence of the primary injury and can be focal or diffuse, rather than being the primary pathology defined by these specific anatomical locations.

(D) Axonal injury: This refers to Diffuse Axonal Injury (DAI), which involves widespread shearing damage to axons in the brain's white matter. It is caused by rotational and acceleration-deceleration forces. While it is a common pathology in trauma, it is characterized by its diffuse nature, affecting white matter tracts like the corpus callosum and brainstem, not by focal bruises (contusions) on the cortical surface in the locations described.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regards to types of brain haemorrhage and brain trauma, match the stem below with the option that fits best. A 42-year-old man fell down while intoxicated and lost consciousness. His non-contrast CT brain image slice is reproduced. (Click on image to enlarge)
Epidural hemorrhage
Subarachnoid Haemorrhage
Subdural hemorrhage
Cerebral contusions

Answer: (C) — Subdural hemorrhage

Explanation:

The non-contrast CT image displays a crescent-shaped, hyperdense (bright white) collection of blood overlying the cerebral hemisphere. This collection is located in the potential space between the dura mater and the arachnoid mater. It is seen to cross suture lines but is limited by dural reflections (like the falx cerebri). This crescentic appearance is the hallmark radiological sign of a subdural hemorrhage (SDH).

The clinical history is also highly consistent with this diagnosis. SDHs are typically caused by the tearing of bridging veins that cross the subdural space. This often occurs due to acceleration-deceleration injuries, such as falls, which is the mechanism of injury in this 42-year-old intoxicated man. Intoxication increases the risk of falls, and chronic alcoholism can lead to cerebral atrophy, which stretches the bridging veins and makes them more susceptible to tearing. The loss of consciousness further supports a significant intracranial injury.

Options:

  • (A) Epidural hemorrhage: This is incorrect. An epidural hemorrhage (EDH) is bleeding between the dura mater and the inner table of the skull, typically from an arterial source like the middle meningeal artery, often associated with a skull fracture. On CT, an EDH classically appears as a biconvex or lentiform (lens-shaped) collection that does not cross suture lines. The image clearly shows a crescent-shaped bleed.
  • (B) Subarachnoid Haemorrhage: This is incorrect. A subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH) is bleeding into the subarachnoid space (between the arachnoid and pia mater). On CT, this appears as hyperdense material filling the cerebral sulci, fissures, and basal cisterns. While trauma can cause SAH, the image shows a distinct, localized extra-axial collection rather than blood diffusely outlining the brain's surface.
  • (D) Cerebral contusions: This is incorrect. Cerebral contusions are bruises of the brain parenchyma itself (intraparenchymal injury). On CT, they appear as areas of mixed hyperdensity (hemorrhage) and hypodensity (edema) within the brain tissue, often described as a "salt-and-pepper" appearance. The hemorrhage in the provided image is clearly located outside the brain parenchyma (extra-axial).

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
In the absence of vasopressin, the greatest fraction of filtered water is reabsorbed in which part of the nephron?
Proximal tubule
Loop of Henle
Distal tubule
Collecting tubule

Answer: (A) — Proximal tubule

Explanation:

The proximal tubule is the primary site for the reabsorption of the glomerular filtrate. Approximately 65-70% of the filtered water is reabsorbed in this segment. This reabsorption is considered obligatory, meaning it occurs regardless of the body's hydration status or hormonal signals like vasopressin (also known as Antidiuretic Hormone, ADH). Water reabsorption in the proximal tubule is a passive process that follows the active reabsorption of solutes, such as sodium, glucose, and amino acids, to maintain iso-osmotic balance.

In the absence of vasopressin, the distal convoluted tubule and the collecting tubule (or duct) become largely impermeable to water. Vasopressin is the hormone responsible for inserting aquaporin-2 water channels into the apical membranes of these later segments, allowing for facultative water reabsorption. Without vasopressin, this facultative reabsorption does not occur, and the dilute fluid from the loop of Henle passes through to be excreted. Therefore, the proximal tubule remains the site where the greatest fraction of filtered water is reabsorbed, as its function is independent of vasopressin.

Options:

  • (B) Loop of Henle: The descending limb of the loop of Henle is highly permeable to water and reabsorbs about 15% of the filtered water. However, this is significantly less than the amount reabsorbed in the proximal tubule. The ascending limb is impermeable to water.
  • (C) Distal tubule: Water reabsorption in the distal tubule is facultative and depends on the presence of vasopressin. In the absence of vasopressin, this segment is nearly impermeable to water, and very little reabsorption occurs.
  • (D) Collecting tubule: Similar to the distal tubule, the collecting tubule's permeability to water is regulated by vasopressin. Without vasopressin, it is largely impermeable to water, and minimal water reabsorption takes place. This leads to the excretion of a large volume of dilute urine (diuresis).

Confidence: 100%

89


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following is the principle buffer in the interstitial fluid?
Plasma proteins
Hemoglobin
H2PO4
Carbonic acid

Answer: (D) — Carbonic acid

Explanation:

The principal buffer system in the extracellular fluid (ECF), which includes both interstitial fluid and plasma, is the bicarbonate/carbonic acid buffer system (HCO₃⁻/H₂CO₃). This system is represented by the equilibrium:

CO₂ + H₂O ⇌ H₂CO₃ ⇌ H⁺ + HCO₃⁻

This system is the most important for several reasons: 1. High Concentration: The components, particularly bicarbonate (HCO₃⁻), are present in relatively high concentrations in the ECF. 2. Physiological Regulation: The two components of the system are precisely and independently regulated. The concentration of carbonic acid (H₂CO₃) is controlled by the respiratory system through the exhalation of CO₂, and the concentration of bicarbonate (HCO₃⁻) is controlled by the renal system through reabsorption and secretion. This open system allows for powerful buffering capacity against both metabolic and respiratory acid-base disturbances.

Since interstitial fluid is the main component of the ECF, the carbonic acid/bicarbonate system is its most important buffer. The option "Carbonic acid" represents this key physiological system.

Options:

  • (A) Plasma proteins: Plasma proteins, like albumin, act as important buffers, but they are primarily confined to the plasma (the intravascular component of the ECF). Their concentration in the interstitial fluid is very low because they are too large to easily cross the capillary endothelium. Therefore, they are a major buffer in plasma but not in the interstitial fluid.
  • (B) Hemoglobin: Hemoglobin is an extremely effective buffer, but it is located exclusively inside red blood cells. It is the most important non-bicarbonate buffer in the blood but is absent from the interstitial fluid.
  • (C) H₂PO₄⁻ (Phosphate buffer system): The phosphate buffer system (H₂PO₄⁻/HPO₄²⁻) is a significant buffer, but its primary importance is within the intracellular fluid and in the renal tubules for titrating urinary acid. Its concentration in the extracellular fluid is much lower than that of the bicarbonate system, making its contribution to ECF buffering minor.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
In which one of the following conditions, the cardiac output decreases?
Sitting or standing from lying position
Sleep
Change in environmental temperature
Eating

Answer: (B) — Sleep Explanation: During sleep, particularly non-REM (non-rapid eye movement) sleep, the body's overall metabolic rate is significantly reduced. This leads to decreased oxygen demand from the tissues. To match this lower metabolic need, the cardiovascular system adjusts by reducing its work. There is an increase in parasympathetic (vagal) nervous system activity and a decrease in sympathetic activity. This combined autonomic shift results in a lower heart rate (bradycardia) and a slight decrease in myocardial contractility and stroke volume. The net effect is a sustained decrease in cardiac output, typically falling by 10-25% compared to the resting awake state. This is a regulated physiological state where a lower cardiac output is the appropriate baseline.

Options: * (A) Sitting or standing from lying position: This postural change causes a transient decrease in cardiac output. When moving to an upright position, gravity causes blood to pool in the veins of the legs and abdomen, which decreases venous return (preload) to the heart. According to the Frank-Starling mechanism, reduced preload leads to a reduced stroke volume and thus a fall in cardiac output. However, this is a temporary event. Baroreceptors detect the drop in blood pressure and trigger a reflex response that increases heart rate and vasoconstriction to quickly compensate and restore blood pressure. Because this is a transient change that is immediately counteracted, it is a less suitable answer than sleep, which is a sustained condition of lower cardiac output. * (C) Change in environmental temperature: An increase in environmental temperature necessitates heat dissipation. The body achieves this through cutaneous vasodilation (widening of blood vessels in the skin), which requires an increase in cardiac output to supply the extra blood flow to the skin while maintaining overall blood pressure. Conversely, exposure to cold may induce shivering, which increases metabolic rate and oxygen consumption, also requiring an increase in cardiac output. * (D) Eating: After a meal, the gastrointestinal tract becomes highly active to digest and absorb nutrients. This process requires a significant increase in blood flow to the splanchnic circulation (the arteries supplying the digestive organs). To meet this demand, the heart pumps more blood, leading to an increase in cardiac output.

Confidence: 90%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 25-year-old has a sudden twisting injury to his knee while playing soccer. His knee is in full extension and is unable to flex. In normal circumstances, which nerve innervates the muscle responsible for initiating the flexion of knee?
Deep peroneal nerve
Sciatic nerve
Common peroneal nerve
Tibial nerve

Answer: (D) — Tibial nerve

Explanation:

The question describes a "locked" knee, which is in full extension and cannot be flexed. The initiation of knee flexion from a fully extended, weight-bearing position requires the "unlocking" of the knee joint. This action is primarily performed by the popliteus muscle. The popliteus muscle achieves this by laterally rotating the femur on the fixed tibia, which slackens the collateral ligaments and allows flexion to begin. The major knee flexors (hamstrings) can then take over. The popliteus muscle is located in the posterior compartment of the leg and is innervated by the tibial nerve (L4-S1), which is a terminal branch of the sciatic nerve.

Options:

  • (A) Deep peroneal nerve: This nerve, also known as the deep fibular nerve, innervates the muscles of the anterior compartment of the leg, which are responsible for dorsiflexion of the ankle and extension of the toes. It has no role in knee flexion.
  • (B) Sciatic nerve: The sciatic nerve is the large nerve in the posterior thigh that branches into the tibial and common peroneal nerves. While the sciatic nerve itself (specifically its tibial division) innervates the main knee flexors (hamstrings), the tibial nerve is the more specific and direct nerve that innervates the popliteus muscle, which is responsible for the specific action of initiating flexion. Therefore, the tibial nerve is the most precise answer.
  • (C) Common peroneal nerve: This nerve, also known as the common fibular nerve, innervates the short head of the biceps femoris muscle (a knee flexor) and then divides to supply the lateral and anterior compartments of the leg. It does not innervate the popliteus muscle.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 65 year old presents with an injury to the upper arm. On examination the patient has loss of sensation to the dorsal first web space. Which part of the humerus is likely to have fractured?
Surgical neck
Distal end
Shaft
Medial epicondyle

Answer: (C) — Shaft Explanation: The key clinical finding is the loss of sensation to the dorsal first web space (the area between the thumb and index finger on the back of the hand). This area is exclusively supplied by the superficial sensory branch of the radial nerve. The radial nerve, along with the deep brachial artery (profunda brachii), courses directly on the bone within the radial (or spiral) groove on the posterior aspect of the humeral shaft. A mid-shaft fracture of the humerus is therefore the most common cause of radial nerve palsy, leading to motor deficits (e.g., wrist drop) and the specific sensory loss described.

Options: (A) Surgical neck: A fracture of the surgical neck of the humerus is classically associated with an injury to the axillary nerve and the posterior circumflex humeral artery. An axillary nerve injury would result in paralysis of the deltoid and teres minor muscles, and sensory loss over the "regimental badge" area of the shoulder, not the hand.

(B) Distal end: A fracture of the distal humerus (supracondylar fracture) is most commonly associated with injury to the median nerve and brachial artery as they pass anteriorly. While the radial nerve can be injured in a supracondylar fracture, the mid-shaft is the most classic and common location for this specific injury.

(D) Medial epicondyle: A fracture or injury to the medial epicondyle is classically associated with an injury to the ulnar nerve, which passes posteriorly in the ulnar groove ("funny bone"). This would cause motor and sensory deficits in the ulnar distribution of the hand (e.g., weakness of intrinsic hand muscles and numbness/tingling in the 4th and 5th digits).

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 45-year-old female presents with features suspicious for carpal tunnel sydrome. Which one of the following features on examination suggests median nerve compression?
Weakness of the 1st and 2nd lumbricals
Loss of sensation in the central palm
Loss of sensation in the medial three and a half digits
Weakness of the abductor pollicis longus

Answer: (A) — Weakness of the 1st and 2nd lumbricals

Explanation:

Carpal tunnel syndrome is a condition caused by the compression of the median nerve as it passes through the carpal tunnel at the wrist. The median nerve provides motor and sensory innervation to parts of the hand.

The motor branches of the median nerve that arise within or distal to the carpal tunnel supply the thenar muscles (Opponens pollicis, Abductor pollicis brevis, and the superficial head of Flexor pollicis brevis) and the 1st and 2nd lumbricals. Therefore, compression of the median nerve at the wrist can lead to weakness and eventually atrophy of these muscles. Weakness of the 1st and 2nd lumbricals, which are responsible for flexing the metacarpophalangeal (MCP) joints and extending the interphalangeal (IP) joints of the index and middle fingers, is a classic motor finding in carpal tunnel syndrome.

Options:

  • (B) Loss of sensation in the central palm: This is incorrect. The sensation to the skin over the thenar eminence and the central palm is supplied by the palmar cutaneous branch of the median nerve. This branch arises from the median nerve proximal to the carpal tunnel and travels superficially to the transverse carpal ligament. Consequently, sensation in this area is typically spared in carpal tunnel syndrome, a key diagnostic clue that helps differentiate it from more proximal median nerve lesions.
  • (C) Loss of sensation in the medial three and a half digits: This is incorrect. This sensory distribution is not supplied by the median nerve. The ulnar nerve supplies sensation to the medial one and a half digits (the little finger and the medial half of the ring finger). The median nerve supplies sensation to the palmar aspect of the lateral three and a half digits (the thumb, index, middle, and lateral half of the ring finger).
  • (D) Weakness of the abductor pollicis longus: This is incorrect. The abductor pollicis longus (APL) muscle is located in the posterior compartment of the forearm and is innervated by the posterior interosseous nerve, a deep branch of the radial nerve. Its function is unrelated to the median nerve.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 20 year old presents with an anterior shoulder dislocation. Examination reveals loss of sensation over the skin of the superolateral arm. Which one of the following movements is least likely to be affected?

Abduction of arm
Lateral rotation
Adduction of arm
Medial rotation

Answer: (C) — Adduction of arm

Explanation:

The clinical presentation of an anterior shoulder dislocation accompanied by loss of sensation over the superolateral arm (the "regimental badge" or "sergeant's patch" area) is classic for an injury to the axillary nerve. The axillary nerve (C5, C6) courses around the surgical neck of the humerus and is vulnerable to stretching or damage during this type of dislocation.

The axillary nerve provides motor innervation to two key muscles: 1. Deltoid muscle: The primary abductor of the arm from 15 to 90 degrees. It also assists in flexion and extension of the arm. 2. Teres minor muscle: One of the four rotator cuff muscles, which assists in lateral (external) rotation of the arm.

Given the axillary nerve injury, movements performed by these muscles will be weakened. The question asks which movement is least likely to be affected.

Adduction of the arm (moving the arm towards the body's midline) is primarily performed by the pectoralis major, latissimus dorsi, and teres major muscles. These muscles are innervated by the pectoral nerves, thoracodorsal nerve, and lower subscapular nerve, respectively. Since none of these muscles are supplied by the axillary nerve, adduction strength will be preserved, making it the least affected movement.

Options:

  • (A) Abduction of arm: This is incorrect. The deltoid muscle is the main abductor of the arm (from 15-90 degrees). Paralysis of the deltoid due to axillary nerve injury will cause significant weakness in arm abduction.
  • (B) Lateral rotation: This is incorrect. The teres minor, innervated by the axillary nerve, is a lateral rotator of the arm. While the infraspinatus (innervated by the suprascapular nerve) is the more powerful lateral rotator, loss of teres minor function will still result in notable weakness of this movement.
  • (D) Medial rotation: This is incorrect. While the primary medial rotators (subscapularis, pectoralis major, latissimus dorsi) are not innervated by the axillary nerve, anterior shoulder dislocations are frequently associated with tears of the subscapularis tendon (a primary medial rotator). This associated structural damage would weaken medial rotation. Therefore, adduction is less likely to be affected than medial rotation in the overall context of this injury.

Confidence: 95%

Myotome corresponding to the lateral rotation of shoulder
C6
C4
C5
C7

Answer: (C) — C5 Explanation: The myotome corresponding to a specific spinal nerve root represents the group of muscles innervated by that single root. Lateral (or external) rotation of the shoulder is a key movement used to test the integrity of the C5 nerve root.

This action is primarily carried out by two muscles of the rotator cuff: 1. Infraspinatus: The main lateral rotator, innervated by the suprascapular nerve. 2. Teres Minor: Assists in lateral rotation, innervated by the axillary nerve.

Both the suprascapular nerve and the axillary nerve derive their fibers predominantly from the C5 and C6 spinal nerve roots. However, in clinical practice, C5 is considered the primary myotome for both shoulder abduction (via the deltoid muscle, also innervated by the axillary nerve) and lateral rotation.

Options: * (A) C6: The C6 myotome is primarily associated with elbow flexion (along with C5) and, more distinctly, wrist extension. While C6 contributes to the nerves that perform lateral rotation, C5 is the principal root tested for this movement. * (B) C4: The C4 myotome is mainly responsible for the function of the diaphragm (via the phrenic nerve, C3-C5) and elevation of the scapula (shoulder shrug). It is not involved in shoulder rotation. * (D) C7: The C7 myotome is primarily tested by assessing elbow extension (triceps), wrist flexion, and finger extension. It is not associated with lateral rotation of the shoulder.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 19-year-old male was involved in a motor vehicle accident. He had x-rays of his entire upper limb to exclude fractures. Which one of his long bone is likely to still have epiphysis?
Ulna
Clavicle
Humerus
Radius

Answer: (B) — Clavicle

Explanation:

The question asks which long bone in the upper limb of a 19-year-old male is most likely to have an unfused epiphysis. This relates to the timeline of skeletal maturation and the fusion of epiphyseal growth plates.

The clavicle is unique among long bones. Its medial (sternal) end has a secondary ossification center that is the last in the entire body to appear and fuse. This medial clavicular epiphysis typically appears on radiographs between the ages of 17 and 20 and does not completely fuse with the shaft of the clavicle until approximately age 22 to 25, and sometimes even as late as 31. Therefore, in a 19-year-old male, it is highly probable that the medial epiphysis of the clavicle will still be unfused and visible as a separate entity on an x-ray.

Options:

  • (A) Ulna: The distal epiphysis of the ulna typically fuses with the shaft between the ages of 18 and 20. While it's possible it could be unfused at 19, it is more likely to be fused or in the final stages of fusion compared to the clavicle. The proximal (olecranon) epiphysis fuses much earlier, around age 14-16.
  • (C) Humerus: The epiphyses at the distal end of the humerus fuse with the shaft around age 14-16. The proximal epiphysis (head of the humerus) fuses later, around age 18-21. At 19, the proximal humeral epiphysis might be unfused, but the clavicle's fusion is consistently later, making it the more likely answer.
  • (D) Radius: The distal epiphysis of the radius fuses between the ages of 18 and 20. Similar to the ulna, it is likely to be fused or nearing complete fusion in a 19-year-old. The proximal (radial head) epiphysis fuses earlier, at about age 14-16.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Regarding the visual pathways of the brain, match the best option below with the stem that follows. A 17-year-old female with Temporal lobe epilepsy presents with auditory hallucinations and a complex partial seizure that has resolved. Examination reveals a visual field defect.
Homonymous upper quadrant defect
Homonymous lower quadrant defect
Homonymous Sectoranopia
Homonymous upper quadranopsia with macular sparing

Answer: (A) — Homonymous upper quadrant defect

Explanation:

The clinical presentation of temporal lobe epilepsy, including auditory hallucinations (as the auditory cortex is in the superior temporal gyrus) and complex partial seizures, strongly localizes the pathology to the temporal lobe. The visual pathway fibers that pass through the temporal lobe are the inferior optic radiations, also known as Meyer's loop.

Meyer's loop carries fibers from the inferior retina, which corresponds to the superior visual field. These fibers loop anteriorly and inferiorly within the temporal lobe before projecting to the inferior bank of the calcarine fissure in the occipital lobe. A lesion (such as the epileptogenic focus, or post-ictal changes) in one temporal lobe will damage the ipsilateral Meyer's loop, resulting in a loss of the contralateral superior visual field. This specific visual field defect is called a contralateral superior homonymous quadrantanopia, or more simply, a homonymous upper quadrant defect. It is often colloquially referred to as a "pie in the sky" defect.

Options:

  • (A) Homonymous upper quadrant defect: Correct. This is the classic visual field defect associated with a lesion in the temporal lobe affecting Meyer's loop.
  • (B) Homonymous lower quadrant defect: Incorrect. This defect, also known as a "pie on the floor," is caused by a lesion in the parietal lobe. The parietal lobe contains the superior optic radiations (Baum's loop), which carry information from the superior retina corresponding to the inferior visual field.
  • (C) Homonymous Sectoranopia: Incorrect. This is a wedge-shaped visual field defect. It is most characteristically caused by a lesion of the lateral geniculate nucleus (LGN) of the thalamus, which has a specific vascular supply to different sectors. It is not the typical defect for a temporal lobe lesion.
  • (D) Homonymous upper quadranopsia with macular sparing: Incorrect. While this is a homonymous upper quadrant defect, the presence of macular sparing specifically points to a lesion in the primary visual cortex (occipital lobe). Macular sparing occurs because the macula has a large cortical representation and often a dual blood supply (from both the posterior and middle cerebral arteries), making it resistant to infarction from a single vessel occlusion. The clinical signs in this case point to the temporal lobe, not the occipital lobe.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
About 80% of optic tract nerve fibers project to which structure in the ipsilateral thalamus.
Periventricular Nucleus
Ventral Posterior Nucleus
Medial Geniculate Nucleus
Lateral Geniculate Nucleus

Answer: (D) — Lateral Geniculate Nucleus

Explanation:

The visual pathway is a complex neural circuit that transmits information from the retina to the brain. After light is detected by photoreceptors in the retina, the signal is processed and transmitted by retinal ganglion cells, whose axons form the optic nerve. The optic nerves from both eyes converge at the optic chiasm, where fibers from the nasal half of each retina cross to the opposite side. The reorganized fibers then form the optic tracts.

Each optic tract carries visual information from the contralateral visual field and projects primarily to the thalamus. The main thalamic relay center for the visual system is the Lateral Geniculate Nucleus (LGN). Approximately 80-90% of the nerve fibers in the optic tract terminate and synapse in the LGN of the ipsilateral thalamus. From the LGN, visual information is relayed via the optic radiations to the primary visual cortex (V1) in the occipital lobe for conscious perception of vision.

The remaining 10-20% of optic tract fibers bypass the LGN and project to other midbrain structures, including the superior colliculus (for controlling eye movements) and the pretectal nucleus (for the pupillary light reflex).

Options:

  • Periventricular Nucleus: This is a collection of nuclei lining the walls of the third ventricle, primarily involved in neuroendocrine regulation, autonomic functions, and pain modulation, not the primary visual pathway.
  • Ventral Posterior Nucleus (VPN): This thalamic nucleus is the main relay station for somatosensory information (touch, pain, temperature, proprioception) from the body and face, projecting to the somatosensory cortex. It is not involved in vision.
  • Medial Geniculate Nucleus (MGN): This is the thalamic relay center for the auditory system. It receives input from the inferior colliculus and projects to the primary auditory cortex. A common mnemonic is "Medial for Music, Lateral for Light."

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Manubrio-sternal joint is a
Fibrous joint
Primary cartilaginous joint
Secondary cartilaginous joint
Synovial joint -Plane

Answer: (C) — Secondary cartilaginous joint

Explanation:

The manubriosternal joint, also known as the sternal angle or angle of Louis, is the articulation between the manubrium and the body of the sternum. This joint is classified as a secondary cartilaginous joint, also called a symphysis.

A symphysis is characterized by: 1. The articulating bony surfaces being covered by a thin layer of hyaline cartilage. 2. These hyaline cartilage layers being connected by a pad of fibrocartilage. 3. Being located in the midline of the body. 4. Allowing for a small amount of movement (amphiarthrosis), which in the case of the manubriosternal joint, facilitates the "pump-handle" movement of the chest wall during respiration.

This joint typically persists throughout life, although in some older individuals (around 10%), it may undergo fusion (synostosis).

Options:

  • (A) Fibrous joint: Incorrect. Fibrous joints (e.g., sutures of the skull) connect bones with dense fibrous connective tissue. The manubriosternal joint is united by cartilage, not fibrous tissue.
  • (B) Primary cartilaginous joint: Incorrect. A primary cartilaginous joint (synchondrosis) is where bones are joined by hyaline cartilage only (e.g., the epiphyseal growth plate or the first sternocostal joint). The manubriosternal joint contains a fibrocartilaginous disc in addition to hyaline cartilage, which is the defining feature of a secondary cartilaginous joint.
  • (D) Synovial joint - Plane: Incorrect. Synovial joints are characterized by a synovial cavity, a joint capsule, and synovial fluid, allowing for significant movement. The manubriosternal joint lacks these features. While some sternocostal joints (2nd-7th) are plane synovial joints, the manubriosternal joint is not.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
You are about to place an arterial catheter into the radial artery in the distal forearm. The structure that is present immediately medial to the artery is:
Brachioradialis tendon
Median nerve
Flexor carpi radialis tendon
Palmaris longus tendon

Answer: (C) — Flexor carpi radialis tendon

Explanation:

The radial artery in the distal forearm runs in a specific anatomical location that is crucial for procedures like arterial blood gas sampling and arterial line placement. The key landmarks are the tendons of the anterior forearm muscles.

At the wrist, the radial artery pulse is palpated just lateral to the tendon of the Flexor Carpi Radialis (FCR) muscle and medial to the tendon of the Brachioradialis muscle. Therefore, the structure that lies immediately medial to the radial artery is the Flexor carpi radialis tendon.

The anatomical arrangement from lateral to medial at the wrist crease is: 1. Brachioradialis tendon 2. Radial Artery 3. Flexor Carpi Radialis (FCR) tendon 4. Median Nerve (lies deep to the palmaris longus or between the FCR and flexor digitorum superficialis) 5. Palmaris Longus tendon (if present)

Options:

  • (A) Brachioradialis tendon: This tendon forms the lateral border of the space where the radial artery is palpated. It is located lateral, not medial, to the artery.
  • (B) Median nerve: The median nerve is located medial to the Flexor carpi radialis tendon. While it is medial to the radial artery, the FCR tendon is situated between the artery and the nerve. Thus, it is not immediately medial.
  • (D) Palmaris longus tendon: This tendon is medial to the Flexor carpi radialis tendon. It is therefore not immediately adjacent to the radial artery.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 25 year old presents with an anterior shoulder dislocation. Regarding the glenhumeral joint, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
The humeral head is held in the glenoid cavity by the tone of the rotator cuff muscles
It is a pivot type synovial joint
The glenoid cavity accepts one-fourth of the humeral head and thus making the joint relatively unstable
The coracohumeral ligament is a strong band that prevents superior displacement of humeral head

Answer: (A) — The humeral head is held in the glenoid cavity by the tone of the rotator cuff muscles

Explanation:

The glenohumeral (shoulder) joint is the most mobile joint in the human body, but this mobility comes at the cost of inherent instability. The stability of this joint relies on a combination of static and dynamic stabilizers. The rotator cuff muscles (supraspinatus, infraspinatus, teres minor, and subscapularis) are the primary dynamic stabilizers. They form a musculotendinous cuff around the humeral head. The resting tone of these muscles creates a compressive force that actively pulls the large humeral head into the small, shallow glenoid cavity, keeping it centered. This "concavity compression" mechanism is crucial for maintaining joint stability during both rest and movement. In an anterior dislocation, these muscles are often stretched or torn.

Options:

  • (B) It is a pivot type synovial joint: This is incorrect. The glenohumeral joint is a multiaxial ball-and-socket (spheroid) type synovial joint. This structure allows for a wide range of motion, including flexion/extension, abduction/adduction, internal/external rotation, and circumduction. A pivot joint, such as the proximal radioulnar joint, only allows for uniaxial rotation.
  • (C) The glenoid cavity accepts one-fourth of the humeral head and thus making the joint relatively unstable: This statement is incorrect due to the specific fraction mentioned. While the concept that the shallow glenoid contributes to instability is correct, the glenoid cavity articulates with approximately one-third of the humeral head, not one-fourth. This significant size mismatch is a key reason for the joint's instability.
  • (D) The coracohumeral ligament is a strong band that prevents superior displacement of humeral head: This is incorrect. The coracohumeral ligament is a strong ligament that runs from the coracoid process to the humeral tubercles. Its primary functions are to reinforce the superior part of the joint capsule, resist the pull of gravity (preventing inferior displacement), and limit external rotation. The structure that prevents superior displacement of the humeral head is the coracoacromial arch, which is formed by the acromion, the coracoid process, and the coracoacromial ligament.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 44-year-old female presents with swelling and redness to the front of the knee. Assessment suggests a diagnosis of bursitis. Which one of the following bursae communicates to the knee joint?
Deep infrapatella
Pre patellar
Subcutaneous infrapatellar
Suprapatellar

Answer: (D) — Suprapatellar

Explanation

The knee joint is surrounded by several bursae, which are fluid-filled sacs that reduce friction between tissues like tendons, muscles, and bones. The suprapatellar bursa is unique among the options provided because it consistently communicates with the synovial cavity of the knee joint. It is located superior to the patella, between the femur and the quadriceps femoris tendon. Functionally, it is considered an extension of the knee joint's synovial space. This communication is clinically significant because an infection or inflammation within the suprapatellar bursa can easily spread to the entire knee joint (causing septic arthritis), and a knee joint effusion will often present as swelling in the suprapatellar region.

Options

  • (A) Deep infrapatella: The deep infrapatellar bursa is located between the posterior aspect of the patellar ligament and the anterior surface of the tibia. It is separated from the knee joint cavity by the infrapatellar fat pad and does not communicate with the joint.
  • (B) Pre patellar: The prepatellar bursa is located superficially, between the skin and the anterior surface of the patella. It is not connected to the knee joint. Inflammation of this bursa is commonly known as "housemaid's knee."
  • (C) Subcutaneous infrapatellar: Also known as the superficial infrapatellar bursa, this bursa lies between the skin and the tibial tuberosity, just below the patella. It does not communicate with the knee joint. Inflammation here is sometimes called "clergyman's knee."
