MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 44-year-old sustains a fracture of the neck of fibula. Which one of the following is an unlikely complication from the above injury?
Inability to dorsiflex the foot
Loss of sensation to the sole of the foot
Weakness of foot eversion
Loss of sensation to the first web space of the foot

Answer: (B) — Loss of sensation to the sole of the foot

Explanation:

A fracture of the neck of the fibula can directly damage the common peroneal nerve (also known as the common fibular nerve), which winds superficially around this part of the bone. The common peroneal nerve divides into the superficial peroneal nerve and the deep peroneal nerve.

Injury to the common peroneal nerve and its branches would lead to: * Deep peroneal nerve palsy: This nerve supplies the anterior compartment of the leg (e.g., tibialis anterior), which is responsible for dorsiflexion of the foot. Damage causes an inability to dorsiflex the foot, a condition known as "foot drop." The deep peroneal nerve also provides sensation to the first web space of the foot. * Superficial peroneal nerve palsy: This nerve supplies the lateral compartment of the leg (peroneus longus and brevis), which is responsible for eversion of the foot. Damage causes weakness of foot eversion.

The sole (plantar surface) of the foot is innervated by the medial and lateral plantar nerves, which are branches of the tibial nerve. The tibial nerve travels down the posterior compartment of the leg, far from the neck of the fibula, and is therefore not at risk from this specific injury. Consequently, loss of sensation to the sole of the foot is an unlikely complication.

Options:

  • (A) Inability to dorsiflex the foot: This is incorrect because it is a likely complication. It is a classic sign of deep peroneal nerve injury (foot drop), a branch of the common peroneal nerve which is vulnerable at the fibular neck.
  • (B) Loss of sensation to the sole of the foot: This is the correct answer because it is an unlikely complication. Sensation to the sole is provided by the tibial nerve, which is not located near the fibular neck.
  • (C) Weakness of foot eversion: This is incorrect because it is a likely complication. It results from injury to the superficial peroneal nerve, a branch of the common peroneal nerve.
  • (D) Loss of sensation to the first web space of the foot: This is incorrect because it is a likely complication. This specific sensory area is supplied by the deep peroneal nerve, a branch of the common peroneal nerve.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 70-year-old male presents with intermittent claudication. You examine various arterial pulses of the lower limb. Which one of the following statements about Dorsalis Pedis artery is CORRECT?
Is a continuation of the anterior tibial artery
Run superficial to the inferior extensor retinaculum
Congenitally non-palpable dorsalis pedis is usually unilateral
Is palpated medial to the extensor hallucis longus tendon

Answer: (A) — Is a continuation of the anterior tibial artery

Explanation:

The dorsalis pedis artery is the principal artery supplying the dorsum of the foot. It arises as a direct continuation of the anterior tibial artery as it crosses the ankle joint. The anterior tibial artery originates from the popliteal artery in the popliteal fossa, passes into the anterior compartment of the leg, and descends to the ankle, where its name changes to the dorsalis pedis artery. This pulse is crucial for assessing vascular supply to the distal lower limb, especially in conditions like peripheral artery disease, which causes intermittent claudication.

Options:

  • (B) Run superficial to the inferior extensor retinaculum: This is incorrect. The anterior tibial artery and its continuation, the dorsalis pedis artery, run deep to the inferior extensor retinaculum, along with the tendons of the extensor muscles. The retinaculum serves to hold these structures in place.
  • (C) Congenitally non-palpable dorsalis pedis is usually unilateral: This is incorrect. The dorsalis pedis artery is congenitally absent or in an anomalous position in approximately 10-12% of the population, making it non-palpable. However, this anatomical variation is more often bilateral than unilateral. A new, unilateral absence of this pulse is clinically significant and suggests pathology like an embolism or thrombosis.
  • (D) Is palpated medial to the extensor hallucis longus tendon: This is incorrect. For a reliable pulse check, the dorsalis pedis artery is palpated on the dorsum of the foot, just lateral to the tendon of the extensor hallucis longus muscle. It lies between the extensor hallucis longus tendon and the tendon of the extensor digitorum longus to the second toe.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Biceps femoris is one of the flexors of the knee joint. It has dual innervation from:
Tibial nerve and common peroneal nerve
Femoral and common peroneal nerve
Tibial and obturator nerve
Femoral and obturator nerve

Answer: (A) — Tibial nerve and common peroneal nerve

Explanation:

The biceps femoris muscle is located in the posterior compartment of the thigh and is one of the three muscles that form the hamstring group. A key anatomical feature of the biceps femoris is that it has two heads, a long head and a short head, which have different origins and, consequently, dual innervation from the two major divisions of the sciatic nerve.

  1. Long Head of Biceps Femoris: This head originates from the ischial tuberosity, sharing a common tendon with the semitendinosus muscle. It is considered a "true" hamstring muscle because it crosses both the hip and knee joints. It is innervated by the tibial division of the sciatic nerve (L5, S1, S2).
  2. Short Head of Biceps Femoris: This head originates from the linea aspera and the lateral supracondylar line of the femur. It only crosses the knee joint. It is innervated by the common peroneal (also called common fibular) division of the sciatic nerve (L5, S1, S2).

Therefore, the biceps femoris muscle as a whole receives dual innervation from both the tibial and common peroneal nerves, which are the two components of the sciatic nerve.

Options:

  • (B) Femoral and common peroneal nerve: Incorrect. The femoral nerve is the primary motor nerve for the anterior compartment of the thigh, innervating muscles like the quadriceps femoris, which extend the knee. It does not innervate any part of the biceps femoris.
  • (C) Tibial and obturator nerve: Incorrect. The obturator nerve is the primary motor nerve for the medial (adductor) compartment of the thigh, innervating muscles like the adductor longus, brevis, and magnus. It does not innervate the biceps femoris.
  • (D) Femoral and obturator nerve: Incorrect. As explained above, the femoral nerve innervates the anterior compartment and the obturator nerve innervates the medial compartment of the thigh. The biceps femoris is in the posterior compartment.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 20-year-old sustains fracture of his tibia and fibula. He has ongoing pain and you suspect a compartment syndrome affecting the anterior compartment. Which one of the following is UNLIKELY to be associated with a compartment syndrome of the anterior compartment?
Weakness of ankle inversion
Loss of sensation over most of the dorsum of foot
Loss of dorsalis pedis pulse
Weakness of fibularis tertius

Answer: (B) — Loss of sensation over most of the dorsum of foot

Explanation:

Anterior compartment syndrome of the leg occurs when swelling and/or bleeding increase pressure within the inextensible fascial boundaries of the anterior compartment. This elevated pressure compromises the function of the neurovascular structures within it.

The contents of the anterior compartment are: * Muscles: Tibialis anterior, extensor hallucis longus, extensor digitorum longus, and fibularis (peroneus) tertius. These muscles primarily act to dorsiflex the ankle and extend the toes. * Nerve: Deep fibular (peroneal) nerve. This nerve innervates all the muscles of the anterior compartment. Its cutaneous sensory distribution is limited to the skin of the first web space (between the first and second toes). * Artery: Anterior tibial artery, which continues as the dorsalis pedis artery on the foot.

Compression of the deep fibular nerve would lead to motor weakness in the anterior compartment muscles and sensory loss in the first web space only. The sensation over most of the dorsum of the foot is supplied by the superficial fibular (peroneal) nerve, which runs in the lateral compartment. Therefore, widespread sensory loss on the dorsum of the foot is not a characteristic sign of an isolated anterior compartment syndrome.

Options:

  • (A) Weakness of ankle inversion: This is incorrect because ankle inversion is performed by the tibialis anterior (in the anterior compartment) and the tibialis posterior (in the deep posterior compartment). Weakness of the tibialis anterior due to anterior compartment syndrome would therefore contribute to weakness of ankle inversion.
  • (C) Loss of dorsalis pedis pulse: This is incorrect because the anterior tibial artery runs through the anterior compartment and becomes the dorsalis pedis artery at the ankle. Increased intracompartmental pressure can compress this artery, leading to a diminished or absent pulse. This is a classic, though often late, sign of compartment syndrome.
  • (D) Weakness of fibularis tertius: This is incorrect because the fibularis tertius muscle is located within the anterior compartment and is innervated by the deep fibular nerve. It would therefore be one of the muscles weakened by anterior compartment syndrome.

These muscles are mainly innervated by the obturator nerve, which arises from the anterior divisions of the lumbar spinal nerve roots L2, L3, and L4. Therefore, the myotomes corresponding to hip adduction are L2, L3, and L4.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
As part of your neurological examination, you are testing the cranial nerve XI. Which one of the following movements of the shoulder cannot be used to assess the function of cranial nerve XI?
Depression
Protraction
Retraction
Elevation

Answer: (B) — Protraction

Explanation:

Cranial nerve XI, the spinal accessory nerve, is a motor nerve that innervates two key muscles: the sternocleidomastoid (SCM) and the trapezius. The trapezius muscle is a large, flat muscle of the upper back responsible for several movements of the scapula (shoulder blade).

The trapezius muscle has three functional parts: 1. Upper fibers: Responsible for elevation of the scapula (shrugging the shoulders). 2. Middle fibers: Responsible for retraction of the scapula (pulling the shoulder blades together). 3. Lower fibers: Responsible for depression of the scapula (pulling the shoulder blades down).

Therefore, testing elevation, retraction, and depression of the shoulder all assess the integrity of the trapezius muscle and, by extension, the spinal accessory nerve (CN XI).

Protraction of the scapula (moving it forward and away from the spine, as in pushing or reaching forward) is primarily performed by the serratus anterior muscle, which is innervated by the long thoracic nerve (C5, C6, C7), and the pectoralis minor muscle, innervated by the medial pectoral nerve. Since CN XI does not innervate the muscles responsible for protraction, this movement cannot be used to assess its function.

Options:

  • (A) Depression: This movement is incorrect because the lower fibers of the trapezius muscle, innervated by CN XI, cause depression of the scapula. Therefore, testing depression can assess CN XI function.
  • (C) Retraction: This movement is incorrect because the middle fibers of the trapezius muscle, innervated by CN XI, are the primary retractors of the scapula. This is a key movement to test for CN XI integrity.
  • (D) Elevation: This movement is incorrect because this is the classic test for CN XI function. The patient is asked to shrug their shoulders against resistance, which directly tests the upper fibers of the trapezius muscle.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
The 22-year old sustained a fall on to this out-stretched hand while skateboarding. He complains of wrist pain. You assess for tenderness in the anatomical snuffbox. Which one of the following statements regarding structures and contents of the anatomical snuffbox is CORRECT?
Scaphoid and trapezium form the floor
The extensor pollicis longus tendon forms the lateral boundary
The radial artery passes along the ulnar side
The abductor pollicis brevis tendon forms the lateral boundary

Answer: (A) — Scaphoid and trapezium form the floor

Explanation:

The anatomical snuffbox is a triangular depression on the radial, dorsal aspect of the hand, best visualized when the thumb is extended. Its name originates from the historical practice of placing powdered tobacco (snuff) in this depression before inhalation. The "floor" of the snuffbox is formed by the carpal bones that lie deep to the extensor tendons. From proximal to distal, these bones are the scaphoid and the trapezium. Palpating the floor is clinically crucial, as tenderness here, especially after a fall on an outstretched hand (FOOSH), is a classic sign of a scaphoid fracture.

Options:

  • (B) The extensor pollicis longus tendon forms the lateral boundary

    • This is incorrect. The tendon of the extensor pollicis longus (EPL) forms the medial (ulnar) boundary of the snuffbox. The lateral (radial) boundary is formed by the tendons of the extensor pollicis brevis (EPB) and the abductor pollicis longus (APL).

  • (C) The radial artery passes along the ulnar side

    • This is incorrect. The radial artery is a key content of the snuffbox, but it crosses the floor of the snuffbox (deep to the tendons) as it travels from the anterior forearm to the deep palm. It is located on the radial side of the wrist, not the ulnar side.

  • (D) The abductor pollicis brevis tendon forms the lateral boundary

    • This is incorrect. The lateral boundary is formed by the tendons of the abductor pollicis longus (APL) and extensor pollicis brevis (EPB). The abductor pollicis brevis (APB) is a short, intrinsic muscle of the thumb (a thenar muscle) located on the palmar side of the hand; its tendon does not contribute to the boundaries of the anatomical snuffbox.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding changes related to ageing that affect pharmacokinetics of drugs, compared to a 30-year-old, an 85-year-old is likely to have increased amount of:

Body water
Body fat
Hepatic blood flow
Serum albumin

Answer: (B) — Body fat

Explanation:

One of the most significant physiological changes with ageing is the alteration in body composition. Compared to a younger adult, an older adult (e.g., 85 years old) has a relative increase in the proportion of body fat and a decrease in both total body water and lean body mass. This change has important pharmacokinetic consequences. The increased body fat increases the volume of distribution (Vd) for lipophilic (fat-soluble) drugs, such as diazepam and amiodarone. This can lead to a longer half-life and accumulation of the drug, increasing the risk of toxicity.

Options:

  • (A) Body water: This is incorrect. Total body water, both intracellular and extracellular, decreases with age. This leads to a smaller volume of distribution for hydrophilic (water-soluble) drugs, resulting in higher initial plasma concentrations for a given dose.
  • (C) Hepatic blood flow: This is incorrect. Hepatic (liver) blood flow decreases by up to 40% in the elderly. This reduces the clearance of drugs that undergo extensive first-pass metabolism (high-extraction ratio drugs like propranolol and morphine), leading to increased bioavailability and higher plasma concentrations.
  • (D) Serum albumin: This is incorrect. Serum albumin levels tend to decrease with age, especially in individuals with malnutrition or chronic illness. Since many acidic drugs (e.g., warfarin, phenytoin) are highly bound to albumin, a decrease in albumin leads to a higher fraction of unbound, pharmacologically active drug, increasing the risk of adverse effects.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Intercarpal joint is a
Synovial joint - Hinge
Synovial joint - Saddle
Synovial joint – Plane
Synovial joint – Pivot

Answer: (C) — Synovial joint – Plane Explanation: The intercarpal joints are the articulations between the individual carpal bones of the wrist. These joints are classified as plane-type synovial joints (also known as gliding or arthrodial joints). This classification is based on the shape of their articular surfaces, which are typically flat or slightly curved. This morphology allows for limited gliding or sliding movements between the bones, contributing to the overall complex movements of the wrist, such as flexion, extension, and ulnar/radial deviation.

Options: (A) Synovial joint - Hinge: A hinge joint (ginglymus) allows movement in only one plane, like a door hinge (uniaxial), permitting flexion and extension. Examples include the elbow joint (humeroulnar articulation) and interphalangeal joints. The intercarpal joints have gliding motion, not the hinge-like motion characteristic of this joint type.

(B) Synovial joint - Saddle: A saddle joint (sellar) has reciprocally concave and convex articular surfaces, allowing for biaxial movement (flexion/extension and abduction/adduction). The classic example in the hand is the first carpometacarpal joint (between the trapezium and the first metacarpal), which allows the thumb's unique opposable movement. The intercarpal articular surfaces are not saddle-shaped.

(D) Synovial joint – Pivot: A pivot joint (trochoid) allows for rotation around a central axis (uniaxial). It involves a rounded process of one bone rotating within a ring formed by another bone and a ligament. Examples include the proximal radioulnar joint (allowing pronation/supination) and the atlantoaxial joint (allowing the head to turn side-to-side). This type of rotational movement is not the primary function of the intercarpal joints.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
First carpometacarpal joint is a
Synovial joint - Plane
Synovial joint - Saddle
Synovial joint – Pivot
Synovial joint - Hinge

Answer: (B) — Synovial joint - Saddle Explanation: The first carpometacarpal (CMC) joint, also known as the trapeziometacarpal joint, is the articulation between the trapezium (a carpal bone) and the base of the first metacarpal bone (the thumb). The articular surface of the trapezium is convex in the palmar-dorsal direction and concave in the medial-lateral direction. The base of the first metacarpal has a reciprocally shaped surface. This unique, interlocking "saddle" shape classifies it as a synovial saddle (sellar) joint. This biaxial joint structure is crucial as it permits a wide range of motion, including flexion/extension, abduction/adduction, circumduction, and the critically important movement of opposition, which allows the thumb to touch the tips of the other fingers.

Options: * (A) Synovial joint - Plane: This is incorrect. Plane (or gliding) joints, such as the intercarpal joints, have flat or slightly curved articular surfaces that allow for simple sliding movements. The first CMC joint has distinctly curved, saddle-shaped surfaces that allow for complex, multi-axial motion. * (C) Synovial joint – Pivot: This is incorrect. A pivot (trochoid) joint allows for rotation around a single axis, where a bony process rotates within a ring of bone and ligament. Examples include the proximal radioulnar joint (for pronation/supination) and the atlanto-axial joint. The first CMC joint's movements are not limited to uniaxial rotation. * (D) Synovial joint - Hinge: This is incorrect. A hinge (ginglymus) joint allows for movement in only one plane, like the hinge of a door, primarily permitting flexion and extension. The elbow and interphalangeal joints are examples. The first CMC joint is biaxial, allowing movement in two primary planes, which is more complex than a hinge joint.

The dermatome corresponding to palmar aspect of Index finger is:

C8
C6
C7
C5

Answer: (C) — C7 Explanation: A dermatome is an area of skin that is primarily supplied by sensory fibers from a single spinal nerve root. The dermatomes of the upper limb follow a logical sequence from superior and lateral to inferior and medial. The C7 dermatome classically provides sensation to the middle finger. However, its territory also includes the index finger and the radial aspect of the ring finger, as well as the central portion of the palm and dorsum of the hand. The sensory nerves supplying the palmar aspect of the index finger are branches of the median nerve, which is composed of nerve fibers primarily from the C6, C7, C8, and T1 spinal roots, with the C7 root being the principal contributor to the sensation of the index and middle fingers.

The C8 dermatome supplies the little finger, the ulnar aspect of the ring finger, and the medial (ulnar) border of the hand and forearm. * (B) C6: This is incorrect. The C6 dermatome supplies the lateral aspect of the forearm, the thumb (first digit), and sometimes the radial aspect of the index finger. The thumb is the most reliable landmark for the C6 dermatome. * (D) C5: This is incorrect. The C5 dermatome supplies the skin over the lateral aspect of the upper arm, including the deltoid region.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A drug’s therapeutic index is an assessment of:

Duration of action
The margin of safety
Onset of action
Potency of drug

Answer: (B) — The margin of safety Explanation: The therapeutic index (TI) is a quantitative measurement of a drug's relative safety. It is calculated as the ratio of the dose that produces toxicity in 50% of the population (TD₅₀) to the dose that produces a clinically desired or effective response in 50% of the population (ED₅₀).

TI = TD₅₀ / ED₅₀

A high therapeutic index indicates that a much larger dose is needed to evoke a toxic response than to cause a therapeutic effect, signifying a wide margin of safety. Conversely, a low or narrow therapeutic index (e.g., for drugs like warfarin, lithium, and digoxin) means that the effective and toxic doses are close, indicating a small margin of safety and requiring careful dose monitoring to avoid adverse effects.

Options: (A) Duration of action: This refers to the length of time a drug has a therapeutic effect. It is primarily determined by the drug's pharmacokinetic properties, such as its half-life and rate of elimination, not its therapeutic index.

(C) Onset of action: This is the time required after administration for a drug to elicit a therapeutic response. It is influenced by the route of administration, absorption, and distribution of the drug to its site of action. It is unrelated to the therapeutic index.

(D) Potency of drug: Potency refers to the amount of a drug required to produce a specific effect. It is measured by the ED₅₀. While the ED₅₀ is a component of the therapeutic index calculation, the TI itself is a ratio that assesses safety, not just the dose required for an effect. A drug can be highly potent (low ED₅₀) but have either a low or high therapeutic index.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which of the following PROPERTIES OF A DRUG will enable it to be used in low concentrations?

High affinity
High stability
High specificity
Low specificity

Answer: (A) — High affinity

Explanation: The ability of a drug to be effective at low concentrations is a measure of its potency. Potency is primarily determined by the drug's affinity for its target receptor. Affinity describes the strength of the binding interaction between a drug and its receptor.

A drug with high affinity binds tightly to its receptors. Consequently, even at low concentrations, a sufficient number of receptors can be occupied by the drug to produce a significant biological or therapeutic effect. This relationship is often quantified by the dissociation constant (Kd), where a lower Kd value signifies higher affinity and greater potency.

Options:

  • (B) High stability: This property refers to the drug's resistance to chemical or physical degradation. While a stable drug is desirable for shelf-life and ensuring it reaches its target intact, stability does not determine the concentration required to elicit a pharmacological effect. A very stable drug might still have low affinity and require high concentrations to be effective.
  • (C) High specificity: This refers to a drug's ability to bind selectively to one type of receptor or target, minimizing interactions with other targets. High specificity is crucial for reducing side effects and having a targeted action. However, a drug can be highly specific but have low affinity, meaning it would still require a high concentration to work, even if it only acts at the desired site.
  • (D) Low specificity: This is an undesirable property where a drug binds to multiple different receptors or targets throughout the body. This leads to a wide range of effects, often including significant side effects. It does not enable use at low concentrations; in fact, it makes high concentrations more dangerous.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following statements BEST CHARACTERISES the first order kinetics?

Rate of elimination increases as the plasma concentration increases
Rate of clearance decreases as the plasma concentration increases
There is no relationship between the rate of elimination and plasma concentration
It is a dose dependent elimination

Answer: (A) — Rate of elimination increases as the plasma concentration increases

Explanation:

First-order kinetics describes the elimination of most drugs. In this process, the rate of drug elimination is directly proportional to the drug's plasma concentration. This means that a constant fraction or percentage of the drug is eliminated per unit of time, regardless of the total amount of drug in the body.

The mathematical relationship is: Rate of Elimination = Clearance (CL) × Plasma Concentration (C)

As you can see from the formula, if the plasma concentration (C) increases, the rate of elimination will also increase proportionally, because clearance (CL) is a constant value in first-order kinetics. For example, if the plasma concentration doubles, the amount of drug eliminated per hour also doubles. This ensures that the drug's half-life (the time it takes for the plasma concentration to decrease by 50%) remains constant.

Options:

  • (B) Rate of clearance decreases as the plasma concentration increases: This is incorrect. In first-order kinetics, clearance is a constant. It represents the theoretical volume of plasma from which the drug is completely removed per unit of time. A changing clearance with concentration is characteristic of non-linear (Michaelis-Menten) kinetics.
  • (C) There is no relationship between the rate of elimination and plasma concentration: This statement describes zero-order kinetics. In zero-order kinetics, a constant amount of drug is eliminated per unit of time, irrespective of the plasma concentration. This occurs when the elimination pathways (e.g., metabolic enzymes) are saturated.
  • (D) It is a dose dependent elimination: This term is more accurately used to describe non-linear (Michaelis-Menten) kinetics, where the elimination process can shift from first-order at low doses to zero-order at high, saturating doses. In pure first-order kinetics, the elimination parameters (like half-life and clearance) are independent of the dose, even though the elimination rate is dependent on the concentration resulting from that dose. Therefore, this option is not the best characterization.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - AORTIC DISSECTION
A 59-year-old man presents to the Emergency Department with severe chest pain. You suspect an aortic dissection.

CT aortogram of the patient shows aortic dissection to be involving the descending aorta.

As per the Stanford classification, which one of the following BEST DESCRIBES this dissection?

Type C
Type B
Type D
Type A

Answer: (B) — Type B Explanation: The Stanford classification is a widely used system to categorize aortic dissections based on the involvement of the ascending aorta. This classification has significant implications for management. * Type A (A for Ascending): Involves the ascending aorta, regardless of the site of the primary intimal tear. These are considered surgical emergencies due to the high risk of catastrophic complications like aortic rupture into the pericardium (causing cardiac tamponade), acute aortic regurgitation, or coronary artery occlusion (causing myocardial infarction). * Type B (B for Beyond/Below): Involves the descending aorta only (distal to the origin of the left subclavian artery) and does not involve the ascending aorta. These are typically managed medically with aggressive blood pressure and heart rate control, unless complications such as malperfusion, intractable pain, or rapid aortic expansion occur.

In this clinical scenario, the CT aortogram confirms the dissection is confined to the descending aorta, which correctly classifies it as a Stanford Type B dissection.

Options: * (A) Type C: This is not a category within the Stanford classification for aortic dissection. * (C) Type D: This is not a category within the Stanford classification for aortic dissection. * (D) Type A: This classification is incorrect because it is used for dissections that involve the ascending aorta. The patient's dissection is limited to the descending aorta.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - AORTIC DISSECTION
A 59-year-old man presents to the Emergency Department with severe chest pain. You suspect an aortic dissection.

Which ONE of the following is a MAJOR RISK FACTOR for aortic dissection?

Marfans syndrome
Vasculitis
Atheroscleosis
Hypertension

Answer: (D) — Hypertension

Explanation:

Hypertension is the single most important and prevalent risk factor for aortic dissection, being present in over 75% of cases. Chronic high blood pressure exerts constant hemodynamic stress on the wall of the aorta. This sustained stress leads to degenerative changes within the aortic media (the middle layer of the vessel wall), a process often referred to as cystic medial necrosis or degeneration. This degeneration weakens the aortic wall, making the intima (the inner layer) more susceptible to tearing. Once an intimal tear occurs, the high pressure of the blood forces its way between the layers of the aortic wall, creating a false lumen and propagating the dissection.

Options:

  • (A) Marfan's syndrome: This is a significant risk factor for aortic dissection, especially in younger patients. It is a genetic connective tissue disorder that weakens the aortic wall due to a defect in the fibrillin-1 protein, leading to cystic medial necrosis. However, Marfan's syndrome is a relatively rare condition and accounts for a much smaller percentage of total aortic dissection cases compared to hypertension.
  • (B) Vasculitis: Certain large-vessel vasculitides, such as Giant Cell Arteritis and Takayasu arteritis, can cause inflammation of the aortic wall (aortitis). This inflammation can weaken the wall and lead to aneurysm formation and dissection. However, vasculitis is a rare cause of aortic dissection.
  • (C) Atherosclerosis: While atherosclerosis is a major risk factor for many cardiovascular diseases (e.g., myocardial infarction, stroke), its role in typical aortic dissection is less direct than that of hypertension. Atherosclerosis primarily affects the intima. While a penetrating atherosclerotic ulcer can be a cause of dissection, the primary underlying pathology for the majority of dissections is the medial degeneration caused by chronic hypertension.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - AORTIC DISSECTION
A 59-year-old man presents to the Emergency Department with severe chest pain. You suspect an aortic dissection.

Which of the following statements regarding the relations of the arch of aorta is CORRECT?

The arch of aorta ends by becoming the thoracic aorta posterior to the 4th left sternocostal joint
The arch of aorta is crossed on its right side by the phrenic nerve and vagus nerves
The left recurrent laryngeal nerve hooks around the arch of aorta and ascends in the tracheo-oesophageal groove
The ascending aorta arches forwards to reach the body of the fourth thoracic vertebra

Answer: (C) — The left recurrent laryngeal nerve hooks around the arch of aorta and ascends in the tracheo-oesophageal groove

Explanation:

This statement accurately describes the anatomical course of the left recurrent laryngeal nerve. The left vagus nerve (CN X) descends into the superior mediastinum, crossing the left side of the aortic arch. As it passes the arch, it gives off the left recurrent laryngeal nerve, which loops inferiorly around the arch of the aorta (just lateral to the ligamentum arteriosum) and then ascends towards the larynx in the groove between the trachea and the esophagus. This anatomical relationship is clinically significant; an aneurysm of the aortic arch can compress this nerve, leading to hoarseness of the voice (Ortner's syndrome).

Options:

  • (A) The arch of aorta ends by becoming the thoracic aorta posterior to the 4th left sternocostal joint: This is incorrect. The arch of the aorta ends and becomes the descending (thoracic) aorta at the level of the sternal angle (the articulation between the manubrium and the body of the sternum). This corresponds to the level of the T4/T5 intervertebral disc, not the 4th left sternocostal joint, which is significantly lower.
  • (B) The arch of aorta is crossed on its right side by the phrenic nerve and vagus nerves: This is incorrect. The aortic arch is crossed on its left side by the left phrenic nerve and the left vagus nerve. The right phrenic and vagus nerves pass through the superior mediastinum to the right of the great vessels and do not cross the aortic arch.
  • (D) The ascending aorta arches forwards to reach the body of the fourth thoracic vertebra: This is incorrect. The aortic arch curves posteriorly (backwards) and to the left, moving from its anterior origin (ascending aorta) to a more posterior position (descending aorta) just anterior to the vertebral column at the T4 level. It does not arch forwards.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - AORTIC DISSECTION
A 59-year-old man presents to the Emergency Department with severe chest pain. You suspect an aortic dissection.

The flow of blood in a long narrow tube is directly proportional to:

Pressure difference between the two ends of the tube
Viscosity of blood
Length of the tube
Resistance of the tube

Answer: (A) — Pressure difference between the two ends of the tube

Explanation:

This question addresses the fundamental principles of hemodynamics, which are described by Poiseuille's Law. This law models the flow of a fluid (like blood) through a cylindrical tube (like a blood vessel).

The formula for Poiseuille's Law is: Q = (ΔP * π * r⁴) / (8 * η * L)

Where: * Q = Flow rate * ΔP = Pressure difference (or gradient) between the two ends of the tube * r = Radius of the tube * η (eta) = Viscosity of the fluid * L = Length of the tube

From this equation, we can see the following relationships: * Flow (Q) is directly proportional to the pressure difference (ΔP). A larger pressure gradient will drive a greater flow of blood. This is the correct answer. * Flow (Q) is also directly proportional to the radius to the fourth power (r⁴), making the vessel's radius the most significant factor in determining flow.

Options:

  • Viscosity of blood: Flow is inversely proportional to viscosity (η). This means that as blood becomes more viscous (thicker), as in conditions like polycythemia or dehydration, the flow rate decreases.
  • Length of the tube: Flow is inversely proportional to the length of the tube (L). A longer vessel offers more surface area for friction, which increases resistance and decreases the flow rate.
  • Resistance of the tube: Flow is inversely proportional to resistance. The relationship is often expressed in a form analogous to Ohm's Law: Flow (Q) = Pressure (ΔP) / Resistance (R). Therefore, as resistance increases, flow must decrease. The factors of viscosity, length, and radius all contribute to the overall resistance.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - AORTIC DISSECTION
A 59-year-old man presents to the Emergency Department with severe chest pain. You suspect an aortic dissection.

The patient is diagnosed with an aortic dissection. The heart rate is 100 and blood pressure is 240/120.

Which ONE of the following is your initial agent of choice?

Verapamil
Metoprolol
Glyceryl trinitrate
Captopril

Answer: (B) — Metoprolol Explanation: The initial management of an acute aortic dissection focuses on aggressively reducing aortic wall stress to prevent the propagation of the dissection. This is achieved by lowering both the heart rate (HR) and the systolic blood pressure (SBP). The most critical initial step is to decrease the rate of rise of aortic pressure (dP/dt), which is primarily determined by the heart rate and cardiac contractility.

Intravenous beta-blockers are the first-line agents because they directly address this by reducing the heart rate and the force of left ventricular contraction, thereby decreasing dP/dt. Metoprolol is a beta-1 selective blocker that effectively lowers the heart rate and provides some blood pressure reduction. The target heart rate is typically <60 beats per minute, and the target SBP is 100-120 mmHg. Only after adequate heart rate control is achieved with a beta-blocker should a vasodilator be added if the blood pressure remains elevated.

Options: (A) Verapamil: This is a non-dihydropyridine calcium channel blocker. While it does lower heart rate and blood pressure, beta-blockers are considered superior as first-line agents for reducing dP/dt. Verapamil is typically reserved as a second-line option for patients with contraindications to beta-blockers (e.g., severe asthma or cocaine-induced dissection).

(C) Glyceryl trinitrate: This is a vasodilator. If administered alone before heart rate control, it will cause reflex tachycardia. An increased heart rate would increase the shear stress (dP/dt) on the aortic wall, potentially worsening or extending the dissection. Therefore, it is contraindicated as an initial, sole agent but can be used for blood pressure control after adequate beta-blockade has been established.

(D) Captopril: This is an oral Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitor. Its onset of action is too slow, and it is not titratable, making it unsuitable for the emergent management of a hypertensive crisis in aortic dissection, which requires rapid-acting intravenous medications.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - AORTIC DISSECTION
A 59-year-old man presents to the Emergency Department with severe chest pain. You suspect an aortic dissection.

Esmolol is an ultra-short acting beta-blocker that can be used in the treatment of aortic dissection.

The MECHANISM RESPONSIBLE for its short half-life is:

Rapid redistribution
Rapid elimination by kidney
Rapid protein binding
Hydrolysis by blood esters

Answer: (D) — Hydrolysis by blood esters

Explanation:

Esmolol is a cardioselective (β1-selective) beta-blocker characterized by its ultra-short duration of action, with a half-life of approximately 9 minutes. This unique pharmacokinetic profile is due to its chemical structure, which contains an ester linkage. This ester bond is rapidly hydrolyzed by esterases located in the cytosol of red blood cells. This metabolic process converts esmolol into its inactive carboxylic acid metabolite and methanol. Because this metabolism occurs rapidly within the bloodstream and is independent of liver (hepatic) or kidney (renal) function, esmolol's effects are quickly terminated upon discontinuation of the infusion. This makes it an ideal agent for situations requiring precise, titratable control of heart rate and blood pressure, such as in an acute aortic dissection, where minimizing aortic wall shear stress is critical.

Options:

  • Rapid redistribution: This mechanism describes the termination of a drug's effect as it moves from the central compartment (blood and highly perfused organs like the brain) to peripheral compartments (like muscle and fat). While redistribution contributes to the initial decline in plasma concentration for many drugs (e.g., propofol, thiopental), it is not the primary reason for esmolol's ultra-short half-life. Its rapid metabolic clearance is the dominant factor.
  • Rapid elimination by kidney: This would imply that the unchanged, active drug is quickly filtered and secreted by the kidneys. Esmolol itself is not significantly cleared by the kidneys. Its inactive metabolite is renally excreted, but the rapid termination of the drug's action is due to its metabolism in the blood, not excretion by the kidney. Beta-blockers like atenolol are primarily cleared by the kidney and have much longer half-lives.
  • Rapid protein binding: High or rapid protein binding would prolong a drug's half-life. Only the unbound (free) fraction of a drug is available for metabolism and excretion. Therefore, extensive binding to plasma proteins like albumin sequesters the drug in the bloodstream, reducing its clearance and extending its duration of action. Esmolol has relatively low protein binding (around 55%).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
The dermatome corresponding to umbilicus is:
T10
T12
T6
T8

Answer: T10

Explanation:

A dermatome is a specific area of skin that is supplied by sensory neurons that arise from a single spinal nerve root. These are essential landmarks in neurology and clinical medicine for localizing the level of a spinal cord lesion or determining the extent of a spinal anesthetic block.

The dermatome corresponding to the umbilicus (belly button) is T10 (the tenth thoracic spinal nerve). This is a key anatomical landmark that is consistently taught and used in clinical practice. A simple way to remember key thoracic dermatomes is: * T4 at the nipple line. * T6 at the xiphoid process (the tip of the sternum). * T10 at the umbilicus.

Options:

  • T12: This dermatome is located in the suprapubic region, just superior to the inguinal ligament and pubic symphysis. It is inferior to the umbilicus.
  • T6: This dermatome corresponds to the level of the xiphoid process, which is the inferior tip of the sternum. It is superior to the umbilicus.
  • T8: This dermatome is located approximately halfway between the xiphoid process (T6) and the umbilicus (T10). It often corresponds to the lower costal (rib) margin.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which ONE of the following pairs of agents is an example of physiological antagonism?

Propronalol and Isoprenaline
Adrenaline and histamine
Adrenaline and Isoprenaline
Isoprenaline and salbutamol

Answer: (B) — Adrenaline and histamine

Explanation:

Physiological (or functional) antagonism occurs when two drugs produce opposing physiological effects by acting on different receptors or through different mechanisms. The net effect is a cancellation of their individual actions.

Adrenaline and histamine are a classic example of physiological antagonists. * Histamine, released during an allergic reaction, acts on H1 receptors on bronchial smooth muscle and blood vessels, causing bronchoconstriction and vasodilation (leading to a drop in blood pressure). * Adrenaline counteracts these effects by acting on different receptors: it stimulates β2-adrenergic receptors to cause bronchodilation and α1-adrenergic receptors to cause vasoconstriction (raising blood pressure).

Because they produce opposite effects on the body (e.g., on airway diameter and blood pressure) by binding to their own distinct receptor types (histamine receptors vs. adrenergic receptors), they are physiological antagonists.

Options:

  • (A) Propranolol and Isoprenaline: This is an example of pharmacological antagonism. Isoprenaline is a non-selective β-adrenergic agonist, and propranolol is a non-selective β-adrenergic antagonist. They both compete for the same beta-adrenergic receptors. Propranolol blocks the receptor, preventing isoprenaline from binding and eliciting its effect.

  • (C) Adrenaline and Isoprenaline: These are not antagonists. Both are adrenergic agonists. Adrenaline is an agonist at α and β receptors, while isoprenaline is an agonist primarily at β receptors. They would have additive or synergistic effects on beta-adrenergic systems (e.g., increasing heart rate).

  • (D) Isoprenaline and salbutamol: These are not antagonists. Both are β-adrenergic agonists. Isoprenaline is non-selective (β1 and β2), while salbutamol is selective for β2 receptors. They both cause effects like bronchodilation and would have additive effects at the β2 receptor.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY

S1
L5
L3
L4

Answer: (B) — L5 Explanation: The L5 dermatome provides sensory innervation to the skin over the anterolateral aspect of the lower leg, the dorsum (top) of the foot, and specifically, the great toe (hallux). Clinically, testing sensation on the dorsal aspect of the great toe or the webspace between the first and second toes is a key part of assessing the integrity of the L5 spinal nerve root. Compression or injury to the L5 nerve root can lead to sensory loss in this distribution, as well as weakness in ankle dorsiflexion and great toe extension.

Options: * (A) S1: Incorrect. The S1 dermatome supplies the lateral aspect of the foot (including the little toe), the sole of the foot, and the posterior calf. A common test for S1 integrity is assessing the Achilles tendon reflex. * (C) L3: Incorrect. The L3 dermatome provides sensation to the anteromedial aspect of the thigh and the area around the knee. * (D) L4: Incorrect. The L4 dermatome covers the medial aspect of the lower leg and the medial malleolus (the bony prominence on the inner side of the ankle). While there can be some overlap, the great toe is classically associated with L5. The patellar tendon reflex is used to test the L4 nerve root.

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - CVA
A 72-year-old patient presents to the Emergency Department with acute onset of left sided weakness.

Which ONE of the following antiplatelet agents, that may be used in treatment of CVA, is a prodrug?

Clopidogrel
Tirofiban
Dipyridamole
Aspirin

Answer: (A) — Clopidogrel

Explanation:

A prodrug is a medication that is administered in an inactive or less active form, which is then converted to its active form through metabolic processes in the body (in vivo).

Clopidogrel is a classic example of a prodrug. It requires a two-step oxidation process by hepatic cytochrome P450 (CYP) enzymes, most notably CYP2C19, to be converted into its active thiol metabolite. This active metabolite then irreversibly binds to the P2Y12 subtype of the ADP receptor on the surface of platelets. By blocking this receptor, clopidogrel prevents ADP-mediated activation of the GPIIb/IIIa receptor complex, thereby inhibiting platelet aggregation. The fact that clopidogrel is a prodrug has significant clinical implications, including genetic variability in CYP2C19 function (poor metabolizers have a reduced antiplatelet effect and higher risk of thrombotic events) and drug-drug interactions with CYP2C19 inhibitors (e.g., omeprazole).

Options:

  • (B) Tirofiban: This is incorrect. Tirofiban is a direct-acting, non-peptide glycoprotein (GP) IIb/IIIa receptor inhibitor. It is administered intravenously in its active form and works by directly blocking the final common pathway of platelet aggregation, without requiring metabolic activation.
  • (C) Dipyridamole: This is incorrect. Dipyridamole is administered in its active form. It exerts its antiplatelet effect directly by inhibiting phosphodiesterase (which increases intracellular cAMP) and by blocking the cellular reuptake of adenosine, both of which lead to inhibition of platelet function. It does not need to be metabolized to become active.
  • (D) Aspirin: This is incorrect. Aspirin (acetylsalicylic acid) is a direct-acting drug. Upon absorption, it irreversibly inhibits the cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) enzyme in platelets. This action prevents the synthesis of thromboxane A2, a potent promoter of platelet aggregation. Although aspirin is rapidly metabolized to salicylic acid (which has some anti-inflammatory properties but is a much weaker antiplatelet agent), the primary, potent antiplatelet effect is exerted by the parent molecule, acetylsalicylic acid, itself. Therefore, it is not considered a prodrug.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - CVA
A 72-year-old patient presents to the Emergency Department with acute onset of left sided weakness.

On examination, the patient has left-sided homonymous hemianopia with macular sparing.

What is the LOCATION of lesion?

Right occipital lobe
Left optic tract
Right optic tract
Left occipital lobe

Answer: (A) — Right occipital lobe Explanation: The clinical findings point to a lesion in the right occipital lobe. Let's break down the reasoning:

  1. Contralateral Control: The brain controls the opposite side of the body. The patient's left-sided weakness indicates a lesion in the right cerebral hemisphere.
  2. Visual Pathway: The visual pathway is also organized contralaterally after the optic chiasm. The left visual field from both eyes is processed by the right side of the brain. A "left-sided homonymous hemianopia" (loss of the left half of the visual field in both eyes) therefore localizes the lesion to the right side of the brain, posterior to the optic chiasm (i.e., in the right optic tract, right optic radiations, or right occipital lobe).
  3. Macular Sparing: This is the key localizing sign. The macula is responsible for central, high-acuity vision. The primary visual cortex, located in the occipital lobe, is primarily supplied by the posterior cerebral artery (PCA). However, the very posterior tip of the occipital cortex, where macular vision is represented, often has a dual blood supply from both the PCA and the middle cerebral artery (MCA). In the event of a PCA occlusion (a common cause of stroke), the bulk of the visual cortex is infarcted, causing the hemianopia, but the macular area may be "spared" due to collateral blood flow from the MCA. This phenomenon specifically points to a cortical lesion in the occipital lobe.

Combining these points, the left homonymous hemianopia with macular sparing is a classic sign of a lesion, most commonly a PCA stroke, affecting the right occipital lobe.

Options: * (B) Left optic tract: A lesion here would cause a right homonymous hemianopia because it affects fibers carrying information from the right visual field. * (C) Right optic tract: A lesion here would cause a left homonymous hemianopia. However, lesions of the optic tract typically cause a complete hemianopia without macular sparing because the nerve fibers are tightly bundled, and the dual blood supply phenomenon does not apply here. Macular sparing is a cortical sign. * (D) Left occipital lobe: A lesion here would cause a right homonymous hemianopia with or without macular sparing, which contradicts the patient's left-sided visual field defect.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - CVA
A 72-year-old patient presents to the Emergency Department with acute onset of left sided weakness.

Lacunar infarcts are found MOST COMMONLY in which ONE of the following parts of the brain?

Internal capsule
Thalamus
Lenticular nucleus
Caudate nucleus

Answer: (C) — Lenticular nucleus

Explanation:

Lacunar infarcts are small (<15 mm in diameter) ischemic strokes that occur in the deep, non-cortical areas of the brain. They result from the occlusion of a single, small, penetrating artery, most commonly due to lipohyalinosis or microatheroma, which are strongly associated with chronic hypertension and diabetes.

These small perforating arteries, such as the lenticulostriate arteries (from the middle cerebral artery), thalamoperforator arteries (from the posterior cerebral artery), and paramedian branches of the basilar artery, supply deep structures. Epidemiological and radiological studies have shown that the lenticular nucleus (specifically the putamen) is the most common site for lacunar infarcts. The putamen alone accounts for approximately 40-50% of all lacunar strokes.

Options:

  • (A) Internal capsule: The posterior limb of the internal capsule is a very common site for lacunar infarcts (around 10-20% of cases), classically causing a pure motor hemiparesis. However, it is not as frequent as the lenticular nucleus.
  • (B) Thalamus: The thalamus is the second most common location for lacunar infarcts (around 15-30% of cases). An infarct here can lead to a pure sensory stroke or a sensorimotor stroke.
  • (D) Caudate nucleus: Lacunar infarcts can occur in the caudate nucleus, but this location is significantly less common than the lenticular nucleus, thalamus, or internal capsule.

Confidence: 95%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - CVA
A 72-year-old patient presents to the Emergency Department with acute onset of left sided weakness.

The peak incidence for spontaneous (nontraumatic) intraparenchymal hemorrhages occurs at the age of:

50
60
70
80

Answer: (B) — 60

Explanation:

Spontaneous (nontraumatic) intraparenchymal hemorrhage (IPH) is a subtype of stroke with a strong correlation with age. The incidence of IPH rises steadily with increasing age. The two most common underlying causes are chronic hypertension and cerebral amyloid angiopathy (CAA), both of which are age-related pathologies.

  • Hypertensive hemorrhage: This is the most common cause, resulting from the rupture of small, deep penetrating arteries (e.g., lenticulostriate, thalamoperforating) that have been damaged by long-standing high blood pressure (lipohyalinosis). This type of hemorrhage typically presents in middle-to-older age.
  • Cerebral Amyloid Angiopathy (CAA): This condition involves the deposition of amyloid protein in the walls of small- and medium-sized cerebral arteries, making them fragile and prone to rupture. CAA is a disease of the elderly, with its prevalence increasing significantly after the age of 70-75.

While the incidence rate (cases per 100,000 people) continues to increase into the 8th and 9th decades, large-scale epidemiological studies and major clinical trials (e.g., INTERACT2, ATACH-2) have consistently found that the mean age of presentation and the peak absolute number of cases occur in the seventh decade of life (i.e., the 60s). Therefore, for the purposes of a standardized medical question, the peak incidence is most commonly cited as being around 60 years of age. The 72-year-old patient in the clinical scenario represents a very typical age for this event.

Options:

  • (A) 50: This is generally considered too young for the peak incidence. While hypertensive hemorrhages can occur in this age group, the frequency is substantially lower than in the subsequent decades.
  • (C) 70: This is also a very high-risk decade, and the incidence is significantly elevated compared to younger ages. As populations age and CAA becomes more prevalent, the average age of IPH is increasing. However, many major datasets still place the peak number of cases in the 60s.
  • (D) 80: In this age group, the incidence rate (risk per person) is the highest. However, because the total population size is smaller in the 80s compared to the 60s and 70s, the absolute number of IPH events (the "peak incidence") is often lower than in the preceding decades.

Confidence: 90%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - CVA
A 72-year-old patient presents to the Emergency Department with acute onset of left sided weakness.

Which ONE of the following arteries supplies the temporal lobe?

Middle cerebral artery
Anterior cerebral artery
Basilar artery
Posterior cerebral artery

Answer: (A) — Middle cerebral artery

Explanation:

The brain receives its blood supply from two main systems: the anterior circulation (from the internal carotid arteries) and the posterior circulation (from the vertebral arteries). The temporal lobe, a key area for hearing, language comprehension, and memory, receives a dual blood supply from branches of both systems.

The Middle Cerebral Artery (MCA), a terminal branch of the internal carotid artery, is the largest cerebral artery and supplies the majority of the lateral surface of the cerebral hemispheres. This includes the lateral aspect of the temporal lobe, where crucial language centers like Wernicke's area (in the dominant hemisphere) are located. The clinical scenario of acute contralateral weakness (hemiparesis) affecting the face and arm more than the leg is the classic presentation of a stroke in the MCA territory.

Options:

  • (B) Anterior cerebral artery: Incorrect. The anterior cerebral artery (ACA) supplies the medial aspects of the frontal and parietal lobes. An ACA stroke typically causes contralateral weakness and sensory loss predominantly in the leg.
  • (C) Basilar artery: Incorrect. The basilar artery is formed by the union of the two vertebral arteries. It supplies the brainstem (pons, midbrain) and cerebellum before bifurcating into the posterior cerebral arteries. It does not directly supply the cerebral cortex of the temporal lobe.
  • (D) Posterior cerebral artery: Incorrect, although it does contribute. The posterior cerebral artery (PCA) supplies the occipital lobe and the inferior and medial aspects of the temporal lobe. While a PCA stroke can affect the temporal lobe (e.g., causing memory issues if the hippocampus is involved), the MCA supplies the larger lateral portion and is more classically associated with the symptoms described in the vignette. Given the options, the MCA is the most significant and primary supplier to the temporal lobe as a whole.

Confidence: 95% (The question is slightly imprecise as both the MCA and PCA supply parts of the temporal lobe. However, the MCA supplies the largest and most functionally prominent lateral portion, making it the best answer.)

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - CVA
A 72-year-old patient presents to the Emergency Department with acute onset of left sided weakness.

Which ONE of the following arteries supplies the medial and superior surface of the frontal lobe?

Anterior cerebral artery
Middle cerebral artery
Basilar artery
Posterior cerebral artery

Answer: (A) — Anterior cerebral artery

Explanation:

The anterior cerebral artery (ACA) is a branch of the internal carotid artery. It travels superiorly in the interhemispheric fissure and arches posteriorly over the corpus callosum. Its cortical branches supply the medial and superior surfaces of the frontal and parietal lobes. This area includes the primary motor and somatosensory cortices for the contralateral leg and foot. Therefore, an occlusion of the ACA would characteristically lead to contralateral weakness and sensory loss predominantly affecting the lower limb.

In the provided clinical scenario, the patient's left-sided weakness could be due to a stroke in the right cerebral hemisphere. While a Middle Cerebral Artery (MCA) stroke is more common, an ACA stroke is a possible cause, especially if the weakness is more pronounced in the leg than the arm and face.

Options:

  • (B) Middle cerebral artery: This is incorrect. The middle cerebral artery (MCA) is the largest branch of the internal carotid artery and supplies the majority of the lateral surface of the cerebral hemisphere, including the lateral frontal, parietal, and temporal lobes. It also supplies deep structures like the basal ganglia and internal capsule. A stroke in this territory typically causes contralateral weakness and sensory loss of the face and arm more than the leg.
  • (C) Basilar artery: This is incorrect. The basilar artery is part of the posterior circulation, formed by the union of the two vertebral arteries. It supplies the brainstem (pons and midbrain) and the cerebellum before bifurcating into the posterior cerebral arteries. It does not supply the frontal lobe.
  • (D) Posterior cerebral artery: This is incorrect. The posterior cerebral artery (PCA) is also part of the posterior circulation, arising from the basilar artery. It primarily supplies the occipital lobe (including the primary visual cortex) and the inferior aspect of the temporal lobe.

Confidence: 100%

136


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
The most sensitive test in the diagnosis of a person with suspected myxoedema:
TSH
T4
TRH
T3

Answer: (A) — TSH

Explanation:

Myxedema is a term used to describe the severe clinical manifestations of hypothyroidism, which results from a deficiency of thyroid hormones. The diagnosis of hypothyroidism relies on assessing the function of the hypothalamic-pituitary-thyroid (HPT) axis.

The most common cause of hypothyroidism (over 95% of cases) is primary hypothyroidism, where the thyroid gland itself is failing. In this situation, the following occurs: 1. The failing thyroid gland produces insufficient thyroxine (T4) and triiodothyronine (T3). 2. The pituitary gland, sensing the low levels of thyroid hormones, increases its production of Thyroid-Stimulating Hormone (TSH) in an attempt to stimulate the thyroid gland. 3. This relationship is logarithmic: a small decrease in free T4 leads to a large, exponential increase in TSH.

Because of this robust and logarithmic response, an elevated TSH level is the earliest and most reliable indicator of primary hypothyroidism, even before the T4 levels fall below the normal range (a state known as subclinical hypothyroidism). In a patient with suspected myxedema (severe overt hypothyroidism), the TSH level will be markedly elevated, making it the most sensitive test for diagnosis.

Options:

  • (B) T4: While the free T4 (thyroxine) level will be low in a patient with myxedema, it is a less sensitive marker than TSH. In early or subclinical hypothyroidism, T4 levels can still be within the normal reference range while TSH is already significantly elevated. Therefore, measuring TSH is a more sensitive screening and diagnostic test.
  • (C) TRH: Thyrotropin-Releasing Hormone (TRH) is produced by the hypothalamus to stimulate the pituitary to release TSH. Measuring blood TRH levels is not a routine clinical test for diagnosing hypothyroidism. The TRH stimulation test is a specialized test sometimes used to differentiate pituitary (secondary) from hypothalamic (tertiary) causes of hypothyroidism, but it is not the primary or most sensitive test for suspected myxedema, which is almost always due to primary thyroid failure.
  • (D) T3: Measuring T3 (triiodothyronine) is generally not recommended for the diagnosis of hypothyroidism. In a hypothyroid state, the body compensates by increasing the peripheral conversion of T4 to the more biologically active T3. Consequently, T3 levels often remain in the normal range until hypothyroidism becomes very severe. This makes T3 a very insensitive marker for diagnosing hypothyroidism.

Confidence: 100%

137


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
The best described etiology for the formation of Berry aneurysms is which one of the following?
Degeneration of tunica media
Degeneration of internal elastic lamina
Low grade inflammation in the vessel wall
Defect in the muscular layer

Answer: (D) — Defect in the muscular layer

Explanation:

The formation of Berry (saccular) aneurysms is most accurately attributed to a congenital or developmental defect in the arterial wall. These aneurysms characteristically occur at the bifurcation points of arteries in the circle of Willis. At these junctions, there is a focal gap or absence in the tunica media (the muscular layer) of the vessel wall. This inherent structural weakness creates a point susceptible to the constant hemodynamic stress of pulsatile blood flow. Over years, this pressure, often exacerbated by conditions like hypertension, causes the unsupported layers (tunica intima and adventitia) to bulge outward, forming a saccular aneurysm. While other degenerative changes occur over time, the fundamental predisposing factor is this muscular defect.

Options:

  • (A) Degeneration of tunica media: While the tunica media does undergo secondary degenerative changes within the aneurysm wall due to chronic stress, the primary etiology is a pre-existing defect or gap in this layer, not a generalized degenerative process as the initiating event.
  • (B) Degeneration of internal elastic lamina: The internal elastic lamina is also fragmented and lost at the site of the aneurysm, contributing to the weakness. However, the most significant structural component missing at the branch point is the muscular layer, making the defect in the media the more fundamental cause.
  • (C) Low grade inflammation in the vessel wall: This describes vasculitis, which is a cause of inflammatory or mycotic aneurysms. This is a distinct and much less common etiology for intracranial aneurysms compared to the developmental defect responsible for typical berry aneurysms. Inflammation may play a secondary role in the growth and rupture of an established aneurysm but is not the primary cause.

Confidence: 100%

138


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
The core of an atherosclerotic plaque contains which one of the following cells:
Platelets
Macrophages
Neutrophils
Eosinophils

Answer: (B) — Macrophages Explanation: The core of an atherosclerotic plaque, also known as an atheroma, is primarily composed of lipids, cellular debris, and a specific type of inflammatory cell. The process begins with endothelial injury, allowing low-density lipoproteins (LDL) to enter the arterial intima. These LDLs become oxidized, triggering a chronic inflammatory response. Monocytes are recruited from the bloodstream, migrate into the intima, and differentiate into macrophages. These macrophages engulf the oxidized LDL, becoming lipid-laden "foam cells." The accumulation and subsequent death (apoptosis) of these foam cells release lipids and cellular debris, forming the central necrotic, lipid-rich core of the plaque. Therefore, macrophages are the quintessential cell type found within the core.

Options: (A) Platelets: Platelets are primarily involved in thrombosis, a complication that occurs when an atherosclerotic plaque ruptures or erodes. While they adhere to the exposed subendothelial collagen at a site of rupture, they are not a primary cellular component of the stable plaque's core.

(C) Neutrophils: Neutrophils are key cells in acute inflammation. While they may be present in small numbers, especially in unstable or ruptured plaques, the inflammation in atherosclerosis is predominantly a chronic process dominated by macrophages and lymphocytes, not neutrophils.

(D) Eosinophils: Eosinophils are associated with allergic reactions, parasitic infections, and certain specific inflammatory conditions. They are not a characteristic or significant component of a typical atherosclerotic plaque.

Confidence: 100%

139


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 2-year-old child is found to have a low pitched murmur on routine examination. An echocardiogram shows the presence of ostium secundum, with a 1-cm defect. Which of the following abnormalities is most likely to be found in this child?
Left-to-right shunt
Mural thrombosis
Pulmonary hypertension
Cyanosis

Answer: (A) — Left-to-right shunt

Explanation:

An ostium secundum atrial septal defect (ASD) is a congenital heart defect characterized by a hole in the interatrial septum at the location of the fossa ovalis. In postnatal life, the pressure in the left atrium is normally higher than the pressure in the right atrium. This pressure gradient causes oxygenated blood to flow from the left atrium, through the defect, into the right atrium. This abnormal flow of blood from the left side of the heart to the right side is known as a left-to-right shunt. This increased volume of blood then passes through the right ventricle and into the pulmonary circulation, which is the direct cause of the low-pitched murmur described (a flow murmur across the tricuspid or pulmonic valve) and the classic fixed, wide splitting of the second heart sound (S2) associated with ASDs.

Options:

  • (B) Mural thrombosis: This refers to a blood clot forming on the wall of a heart chamber. While a large, long-standing ASD can lead to right atrial enlargement and eventually atrial fibrillation (an arrhythmia that predisposes to thrombosis), this is a late complication and is highly unlikely to be the primary finding in a 2-year-old child.
  • (C) Pulmonary hypertension: This is an increase in blood pressure within the pulmonary arteries. It can be a serious long-term consequence of a large, uncorrected left-to-right shunt. The chronic volume overload on the pulmonary vasculature leads to remodeling and increased resistance. However, this process typically takes several decades to become clinically significant and would be very rare in a 2-year-old.
  • (D) Cyanosis: This is a bluish discoloration of the skin caused by deoxygenated blood entering the systemic circulation. Cyanosis occurs with a right-to-left shunt. In an uncomplicated ASD, the shunt is left-to-right, so cyanosis is absent. Cyanosis can develop later in life if severe pulmonary hypertension causes the pressures on the right side of the heart to exceed those on the left, reversing the shunt (a condition called Eisenmenger syndrome). This is a very late complication and would not be present in a 2-year-old with a simple ASD.

Confidence: 100%

140


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
The most common site of atherosclerotic aneurysm is:
Abdominal aorta
Arch of aorta
Renal artery
Coronary artery

Answer: (A) — Abdominal aorta

Explanation:

An atherosclerotic aneurysm is a localized dilation of an artery caused by the weakening of its wall due to atherosclerosis. The most common site for this to occur is the abdominal aorta, specifically the segment below the origin of the renal arteries (infrarenal aorta).

The pathophysiology involves several factors that make this location particularly susceptible: 1. Atherosclerosis: This is the primary underlying cause. Plaque buildup in the intima disrupts the integrity of the vessel wall. 2. Inflammation and Degradation: Atherosclerotic plaques contain inflammatory cells (macrophages) that release matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) and other enzymes. These enzymes degrade elastin and collagen, the key structural components of the aortic media, leading to weakening and subsequent dilation. 3. Hemodynamic Stress: The aorta is a high-pressure vessel. The bifurcation of the aorta into the iliac arteries creates turbulent blood flow, which contributes to endothelial injury and atherosclerotic plaque formation. 4. Vasa Vasorum: The abdominal aorta has a less extensive network of vasa vasorum (small vessels that supply the outer aortic wall) compared to the thoracic aorta. This makes the outer media more vulnerable to ischemia and degeneration when intimal plaques impede nutrient diffusion from the lumen.

Due to this combination of factors, over 90% of atherosclerotic aortic aneurysms occur in the infrarenal abdominal aorta.

Options:

  • (B) Arch of aorta: While aneurysms can form in the aortic arch (a type of thoracic aortic aneurysm), they are significantly less common than abdominal aortic aneurysms (AAAs). Furthermore, aneurysms in the thoracic aorta are more frequently associated with genetic conditions (e.g., Marfan syndrome, Loeys-Dietz syndrome) and hypertension, although atherosclerosis can also be a cause.
  • (C) Renal artery: Renal artery aneurysms are relatively rare. The most common pathology affecting the renal artery due to atherosclerosis is stenosis (narrowing), which can lead to renovascular hypertension, rather than aneurysm formation.
  • (D) Coronary artery: Atherosclerosis is extremely common in the coronary arteries, but it characteristically leads to stenosis and occlusion, causing coronary artery disease, angina, and myocardial infarction. While coronary artery aneurysms can occur, they are a much rarer complication of atherosclerosis compared to the formation of aneurysms in the abdominal aorta.

Confidence: 100%

141


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following conditions can lead to vitamin B12 deficiency?
Left hemi-colectomy
Right hemi-colectomy
Fat malabsorption
Autoimmune gastritis

Answer: (D) — Autoimmune gastritis

Explanation:

Vitamin B12 (cobalamin) absorption is a multi-step process that is critically dependent on intrinsic factor (IF), a glycoprotein produced by the parietal cells of the stomach. In autoimmune gastritis, the body's immune system mistakenly attacks these parietal cells, leading to their destruction (atrophic gastritis). The loss of parietal cells results in a lack of intrinsic factor production. Without intrinsic factor, dietary vitamin B12 cannot be absorbed in its designated site, the terminal ileum. This specific cause of vitamin B12 deficiency is known as pernicious anemia.

Options:

  • (A) Left hemi-colectomy: This surgical procedure involves the removal of the left side of the colon (large intestine). The large intestine is primarily responsible for water absorption and stool formation. Since vitamin B12 is absorbed in the terminal ileum (the final part of the small intestine), removing the left colon does not affect B12 absorption.
  • (B) Right hemi-colectomy: This is a plausible cause but is less fundamentally correct than autoimmune gastritis. The terminal ileum, the site of B12 absorption, is anatomically close to the right colon (cecum). Surgical resection of the right colon (right hemi-colectomy), often for cancer, frequently includes the removal of the terminal ileum to ensure clear margins and remove associated lymph nodes. In such cases, B12 deficiency would occur. However, autoimmune gastritis represents a primary pathological process that directly targets the mechanism (intrinsic factor production) required for B12 absorption, making it a more direct and classic answer.
  • (C) Fat malabsorption: This option is incorrect as a direct cause. Vitamin B12 is a water-soluble vitamin, and its absorption is not directly linked to fat digestion or absorption pathways. While certain conditions, such as Crohn's disease or celiac disease, can cause both fat malabsorption and vitamin B12 deficiency, the cause of the B12 deficiency in those cases is damage to the absorptive surface of the terminal ileum, not the fat malabsorption itself.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Two days after eating a chicken salad sandwich, a 25-year old develops abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting and profuse diarrhoea. Which one of the following infectious organisms is most likely to produce these findings?
Staphylococcus
Salmonella
Rotavirus
Bacillus

Answer: (B) — Salmonella Explanation: The key features in this clinical scenario are the incubation period of two days (48 hours) and the food vehicle (chicken salad). Salmonella species are a common cause of foodborne gastroenteritis, classically associated with contaminated poultry, eggs, and dairy products. The incubation period for non-typhoidal Salmonella is typically 6 to 72 hours, with the onset of symptoms at 48 hours falling squarely within this range. The infection is caused by the ingestion of the bacteria, which then colonize the intestine and elicit an inflammatory response, leading to the characteristic symptoms of nausea, vomiting, abdominal pain, and inflammatory diarrhea.

Options: (A) Staphylococcus: Staphylococcus aureus food poisoning is caused by the ingestion of a pre-formed, heat-stable enterotoxin. This results in a very rapid onset of symptoms, typically within 1 to 6 hours. The 48-hour incubation period in this case makes S. aureus highly unlikely.

(C) Rotavirus: Rotavirus is a common cause of viral gastroenteritis, particularly in young children. While the incubation period is approximately 1 to 3 days, which aligns with the case, it is primarily transmitted via the fecal-oral route. While foodborne transmission can occur, Salmonella has a much stronger and more classic association with chicken-containing products.

(D) Bacillus: Bacillus cereus causes two distinct types of food poisoning. The emetic (vomiting) form, associated with reheated rice, has a short incubation period of 1 to 6 hours. The diarrheal form has a longer incubation period of 6 to 15 hours. Neither of these timeframes fits the 48-hour onset of symptoms described in the question.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 25-year old presents to ED with 5-day history of fever, dry cough, muscle aches and headache. A chest x-ray reveals prominent interstitial markings bilaterally. Which of the following is the most likely organism responsible for this condition?
Chlamydia pneumoniae
Respiratory syncytial virus
Influenza A
Mycoplasma pneumoniae

Answer: (D) — Mycoplasma pneumoniae

Explanation:

The clinical presentation described is a classic case of atypical pneumonia, also known as "walking pneumonia." The key features are the patient's age (young adult), the subacute onset of constitutional symptoms (fever, muscle aches, headache), and respiratory symptoms (dry, non-productive cough). The chest x-ray finding of bilateral interstitial markings, rather than a focal lobar consolidation, is the hallmark of atypical pneumonia.

Mycoplasma pneumoniae is the most common cause of community-acquired atypical pneumonia in school-aged children, military recruits, and young adults. It is a small bacterium that lacks a cell wall, which makes it resistant to penicillins and cephalosporins. The presentation in the question is the textbook description for Mycoplasma pneumoniae infection.

Options:

  • (A) Chlamydia pneumoniae: This is another common cause of atypical pneumonia, particularly in young adults. While it presents similarly, it is generally considered a less frequent cause than Mycoplasma pneumoniae in this demographic.
  • (B) Respiratory syncytial virus (RSV): RSV is the leading cause of lower respiratory tract infections (bronchiolitis and pneumonia) in infants and very young children. In healthy adults, it typically causes a mild upper respiratory infection (a common cold) and is an infrequent cause of pneumonia.
  • (C) Influenza A: Influenza A virus is a very common cause of seasonal flu and can certainly lead to primary viral pneumonia with identical symptoms and chest x-ray findings. It is a strong differential diagnosis. However, epidemiologically, Mycoplasma pneumoniae is the single most common pathogen responsible for the specific syndrome of community-acquired atypical pneumonia in a 25-year-old.

Confidence: 95%
