MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 54-year old male with known diagnosis of small cell lung carcinoma presents with puffiness of the face, pedal edema and hypertension. Immunohistochemical staining of the tumour cells is most likely to be positive for which of the following?
Corticotropin
Parathyroid hormone-related peptide
Gastrin
Erythropoietin

Answer: (A) — Corticotropin

Explanation:

The patient's clinical presentation is highly suggestive of a paraneoplastic syndrome associated with his known diagnosis of small cell lung carcinoma (SCLC). The symptoms of facial puffiness (moon facies), pedal edema, and hypertension are classic signs of Cushing's syndrome, which results from an excess of cortisol.

Small cell lung carcinoma is a neuroendocrine tumor notorious for causing various paraneoplastic syndromes. One of the most common is the ectopic production of Adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH), also known as corticotropin. This ectopic ACTH stimulates the adrenal glands to produce excessive amounts of cortisol, leading to the development of Cushing's syndrome. Therefore, immunohistochemical (IHC) staining of the tumor cells would be expected to be positive for corticotropin, confirming its production by the cancer cells.

Options:

  • (B) Parathyroid hormone-related peptide (PTHrP): This peptide causes hypercalcemia of malignancy by mimicking the effects of parathyroid hormone. While it is a common paraneoplastic product of lung cancer, it is most frequently associated with squamous cell carcinoma, not small cell carcinoma. The clinical presentation would be that of hypercalcemia (e.g., confusion, constipation, bone pain, kidney stones), not Cushing's syndrome.
  • (C) Gastrin: Ectopic production of gastrin by a tumor (gastrinoma) leads to Zollinger-Ellison syndrome, characterized by severe peptic ulcer disease and diarrhea. This is not associated with SCLC or the patient's symptoms.
  • (D) Erythropoietin: Ectopic production of erythropoietin (EPO) causes secondary polycythemia (an abnormally high red blood cell count). This is most commonly seen with renal cell carcinoma and hepatocellular carcinoma, and it is not a typical feature of SCLC. The patient's symptoms are not consistent with polycythemia.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 46-year old male receives polyvalent antivenom for snakebite envenomation. He returns 2 weeks later with low-grade fever, joint pains, lethargy and muscle aches. Which one of the following is the most likely pathological process?
Release of vasoactive amines
Complement activation by antigen-antibody complexes
T-lymphocyte mediated cytotoxicity
IgG and IgG mediated phagocytosis

Answer: (B) — Complement activation by antigen-antibody complexes

Explanation:

The patient's clinical presentation is classic for serum sickness, which is a Type III hypersensitivity reaction.

  1. The Trigger: The patient received polyvalent antivenom, which is derived from animal (often horse) serum. This introduces a large amount of foreign protein (antigen) into the body.
  2. Immune Response: Over the next 7-14 days, the patient's immune system mounts a response, producing its own IgG antibodies against these foreign proteins.
  3. Pathophysiology: These newly formed IgG antibodies bind to the circulating foreign antigens (the antivenom proteins), forming antigen-antibody complexes (immune complexes). These complexes are deposited in the walls of small blood vessels in various tissues, including the joints, skin, and kidneys.
  4. Inflammation: The deposited immune complexes activate the complement cascade, which generates inflammatory mediators (like C3a and C5a). These mediators increase vascular permeability and recruit neutrophils to the site. The neutrophils attempt to clear the complexes, releasing lysosomal enzymes and causing vasculitis (inflammation of blood vessels) and tissue damage.
  5. Clinical Manifestations: This systemic inflammation results in the characteristic symptoms of fever, joint pain (arthralgia), muscle aches (myalgia), and often a rash, which typically appear 1-2 weeks after the initial exposure.

Options:

  • (A) Release of vasoactive amines: This is the primary mechanism of a Type I hypersensitivity reaction (e.g., anaphylaxis). It is mediated by IgE antibodies on mast cells and occurs within minutes to hours of exposure, not two weeks later.
  • (C) T-lymphocyte mediated cytotoxicity: This describes a Type IV (delayed-type) hypersensitivity reaction. It is cell-mediated, not antibody-mediated. While the onset is delayed, the underlying mechanism involves T-cells, not immune complexes. Examples include contact dermatitis or the tuberculin skin test.
  • (D) IgG and IgG mediated phagocytosis: This phrasing likely refers to a Type II hypersensitivity reaction, where IgG antibodies bind to antigens on the surface of a cell, marking it for phagocytosis (opsonization) or destruction. This process targets specific cells (e.g., red blood cells in autoimmune hemolytic anemia), rather than causing systemic disease from deposition of circulating immune complexes.

Confidence: 100%

146


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
All of the following are features of haemolytic anaemia, EXCEPT:
Increased haptoglobin
Elevated reticulocytes
Jaundice
Urine hemosiderin

Answer: (A) — Increased haptoglobin

Explanation:

Haemolytic anaemia is characterized by the premature destruction of red blood cells (RBCs), either within the blood vessels (intravascular) or in the reticuloendothelial system (extravascular). This destruction leads to a classic set of clinical and laboratory findings.

The correct answer is Increased haptoglobin because haptoglobin levels decrease in significant haemolytic anaemia. Haptoglobin is a protein produced by the liver that binds to free haemoglobin released into the plasma from lysed RBCs. This haptoglobin-haemoglobin complex is then rapidly cleared by macrophages in the liver and spleen. In haemolytic anaemia, the rate of haptoglobin consumption exceeds the rate of its synthesis by the liver, leading to low or undetectable levels of free haptoglobin in the serum. Therefore, a decreased haptoglobin level is a sensitive marker for haemolysis.

Options:

  • (B) Elevated reticulocytes: This is incorrect because elevated reticulocytes (reticulocytosis) are a hallmark of haemolytic anaemia. The bone marrow responds to the anaemia (low RBC count) by increasing the production of new RBCs. Reticulocytes are immature RBCs that are released into the circulation in increased numbers as a compensatory measure.
  • (C) Jaundice: This is incorrect because jaundice is a common feature of haemolytic anaemia. The breakdown of haemoglobin from destroyed RBCs releases heme, which is metabolized into unconjugated bilirubin. The rapid production of bilirubin can overwhelm the liver's capacity to conjugate and excrete it, leading to a build-up of unconjugated bilirubin in the blood (hyperbilirubinaemia) and causing jaundice.
  • (D) Urine hemosiderin: This is incorrect because hemosiderinuria can be a feature of chronic intravascular haemolysis. In this type of haemolysis, free haemoglobin is filtered by the kidneys. It is reabsorbed by the renal tubular epithelial cells, where the iron is stored as hemosiderin. When these cells are shed into the urine, hemosiderin can be detected, indicating chronic intravascular RBC destruction.

Confidence: 100%

147


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
The primary defect that leads to sickle cell anaemia is:
A nonsense mutation of the beta-chain of HbA
Abnormality in porphyrin part of hemoglobin
Replacement of glutamate by valine in beta-chain of HbA
Substitution of valine by glutamate in the alpha-chain of HbA

Answer: (C) — Replacement of glutamate by valine in beta-chain of HbA

Explanation:

Sickle cell anemia is a classic example of a monogenic, autosomal recessive disorder known as a hemoglobinopathy. The fundamental defect is a specific point mutation in the beta-globin gene (HBB) located on chromosome 11. This mutation is a single nucleotide substitution (A to T) in the DNA, which changes the codon for the sixth amino acid of the beta-globin polypeptide chain from GAG (coding for glutamic acid) to GTG (coding for valine).

Glutamic acid is a hydrophilic, negatively charged amino acid, while valine is a hydrophobic, nonpolar amino acid. This single amino acid substitution dramatically alters the properties of the hemoglobin molecule, creating an abnormal form called Hemoglobin S (HbS). Under conditions of low oxygen tension (deoxygenation), the hydrophobic valine on the surface of one HbS molecule interacts with a hydrophobic pocket on an adjacent HbS molecule. This interaction causes the HbS molecules to polymerize into long, rigid, insoluble fibers. This polymerization distorts the red blood cell, forcing it into the characteristic rigid, crescent or "sickle" shape. These sickled cells are less flexible, leading to vaso-occlusion (blockage of small blood vessels), chronic hemolytic anemia, and ischemic tissue damage, which are the hallmarks of the disease.

Options:

  • A) A nonsense mutation of the beta-chain of HbA: This is incorrect. The mutation in sickle cell disease is a missense mutation, where one amino acid is substituted for another. A nonsense mutation would introduce a premature stop codon, leading to the synthesis of a truncated, and likely non-functional, protein. This is the mechanism for some forms of beta-thalassemia, but not sickle cell anemia.
  • B) Abnormality in porphyrin part of hemoglobin: This is incorrect. The porphyrin ring, complexed with iron, forms the heme group. The defect in sickle cell anemia lies in the globin protein chains, not the heme group. Disorders of heme synthesis are known as porphyrias and present with a different set of clinical and laboratory findings.
  • D) Substitution of valine by glutamate in the alpha-chain of HbA: This is incorrect for two reasons. First, the substitution is the reverse of what actually occurs (it's glutamate replaced by valine, not valine by glutamate). Second, the mutation affects the beta-chain, not the alpha-chain of hemoglobin. Genetic defects in the alpha-chain lead to alpha-thalassemias.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following is the most common site for pancreatic carcinoma?
Head
Tail
Body
Equal incidence at all sites

Answer: (A) — Head

Explanation: The vast majority of pancreatic carcinomas, specifically pancreatic ductal adenocarcinomas (PDAC), arise in the head of the pancreas. Approximately 60-70% of these tumors are located in the head. This anatomical location is clinically significant because tumors in the head of the pancreas often compress the common bile duct as they grow. This compression obstructs bile flow, leading to classic symptoms such as painless obstructive jaundice (yellowing of the skin and eyes), dark urine, and pale stools. These relatively early and noticeable symptoms can lead to a diagnosis sooner than tumors in other parts of the pancreas.

Options:

  • (B) Tail: The tail of the pancreas is a less common site, accounting for about 10-15% of pancreatic carcinomas. Tumors in the tail often do not cause specific symptoms until they are very large or have metastasized, leading to a later diagnosis and generally a poorer prognosis.
  • (C) Body: The body of the pancreas is the location for about 5-10% of these cancers. Similar to tumors in the tail, they tend to present late with non-specific symptoms like weight loss, abdominal pain, or new-onset diabetes.
  • (D) Equal incidence at all sites: This is incorrect. There is a clear and well-documented predilection for pancreatic carcinomas to develop in the head of the pancreas.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
What is the mode of transmission of Hepatitis E?
Blood
Fecal-oral
Sexual
Percutaneous exposure

Answer: (B) — Fecal-oral

Explanation:

Hepatitis E virus (HEV) is primarily transmitted via the fecal-oral route. This most commonly occurs through the ingestion of water or food that has been contaminated with fecal matter containing the virus. This mode of transmission is particularly responsible for large-scale epidemics in developing countries where sanitation and access to clean water may be limited (associated with HEV genotypes 1 and 2).

In developed countries, HEV (typically genotypes 3 and 4) is often a zoonotic infection, transmitted through the consumption of undercooked or raw meat from infected animals, such as pork, wild boar, and deer. This is still considered a form of oral transmission.

A key clinical feature of HEV is its potential to cause fulminant hepatic failure in pregnant women, especially during the third trimester, with a mortality rate as high as 20-30%. While typically an acute, self-limiting illness, HEV can establish chronic infection in immunocompromised individuals (e.g., organ transplant recipients).

Options:

  • (A) Blood: This is an incorrect primary mode. While HEV transmission through blood transfusion has been documented (particularly genotype 3 in endemic areas), it is a much less common route compared to fecal-oral transmission. Bloodborne transmission is the primary route for Hepatitis C (HCV) and a major route for Hepatitis B (HBV).
  • (C) Sexual: This is incorrect. Sexual transmission is a significant route for Hepatitis B but is not a recognized mode of transmission for Hepatitis E.
  • (D) Percutaneous exposure: This is incorrect. Percutaneous exposure, such as through needlestick injuries or intravenous drug use, is a form of bloodborne transmission. This is the hallmark transmission route for Hepatitis C and is also significant for Hepatitis B, but not for Hepatitis E.

Mnemonic: Hepatitis viruses transmitted via the fecal-oral route are those with vowels: Hepatitis A and Hepatitis E. ("Vowels go with the bowels").

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 25-year-old patient who has hepatitis B infection is least likely to develop this as a complication:
Chronic active hepatitis
Fulminant hepatitis
Hepatocellular carcinoma
Cholangiocarcinoma

Answer: (D) — Cholangiocarcinoma

Explanation:

Hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection is a well-known cause of several liver pathologies. The virus can lead to acute hepatitis, which can then resolve, progress to chronic hepatitis, or, rarely, cause fulminant hepatic failure. Chronic HBV infection significantly increases the risk of developing cirrhosis and hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC).

Cholangiocarcinoma, a malignancy of the biliary duct epithelium, is not strongly associated with Hepatitis B infection. The primary risk factors for cholangiocarcinoma include conditions that cause chronic inflammation of the bile ducts, such as primary sclerosing cholangitis (PSC), parasitic infections with liver flukes (Clonorchis sinensis, Opisthorchis viverrini), choledochal cysts, and hepatolithiasis (stones in the bile ducts). While some studies have suggested a possible weak association between chronic viral hepatitis and intrahepatic cholangiocarcinoma, it is not considered a primary or common complication, especially when compared to the very strong link between HBV and hepatocellular carcinoma.

Options:

  • (A) Chronic active hepatitis: This is an incorrect option. Following an acute HBV infection in adulthood, approximately 5-10% of patients will fail to clear the virus and develop chronic hepatitis. This is a common and significant complication.
  • (B) Fulminant hepatitis: This is an incorrect option. Although rare (occurring in <1% of cases of acute HBV), fulminant hepatitis is a recognized and devastating complication. It involves massive hepatic necrosis leading to acute liver failure and has a high mortality rate.
  • (C) Hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC): This is an incorrect option. Chronic HBV infection is one of the leading causes of HCC worldwide. The virus is directly oncogenic, meaning it can promote cancer development by integrating its DNA into the host hepatocyte genome, even in the absence of cirrhosis. Therefore, HCC is a major, long-term complication of HBV.

Confidence: 100%

151


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 50-year old man has sudden onset of massive hemetemesis. His heart rate is 120 and BP 80/50. His previous serological test of HBsAg is positive. The hematemesis is most likely to be a consequence of which of the following?
Squamous cell carcinoma
Reflux oesophagitis
Barrett’s oesophagus
Oesophageal varices

Answer: (D) — Oesophageal varices Explanation: The patient's clinical presentation of sudden, massive hematemesis leading to hemodynamic instability (tachycardia and hypotension) is highly suggestive of a ruptured esophageal varix. The key piece of information is the positive HBsAg test, which indicates an active Hepatitis B infection.

The pathophysiology is as follows: 1. Chronic Hepatitis B infection (indicated by HBsAg+) is a major cause of chronic liver disease. 2. Chronic liver disease can progress to liver cirrhosis, which involves extensive scarring and disruption of the normal liver architecture. 3. This scarring obstructs blood flow through the liver's portal venous system, leading to a significant increase in pressure, a condition known as portal hypertension. 4. To bypass the high-pressure liver circulation, blood is shunted through collateral vessels (portosystemic anastomoses). One of the most critical sites for these collaterals is at the gastroesophageal junction, where they form dilated, tortuous submucosal veins called oesophageal varices. 5. These varices are thin-walled and under high pressure, making them prone to rupture, which results in massive, often life-threatening, hemorrhage.

Options: * (A) Squamous cell carcinoma: While esophageal cancer can bleed, it typically causes more chronic, slower bleeding leading to anemia or melena. Massive, acute hemorrhage leading to shock is a less common presentation compared to variceal rupture. Furthermore, there is no direct link between squamous cell carcinoma and Hepatitis B. * (B) Reflux oesophagitis: This is inflammation of the esophagus due to acid reflux. It can cause mucosal erosions and bleeding, but the bleeding is almost always mild and chronic (e.g., streaks of blood in vomitus) and does not cause massive hematemesis and shock. * (C) Barrett’s oesophagus: This is a complication of chronic reflux disease and a precursor to adenocarcinoma. Ulcers can form within the Barrett's segment and bleed, but like reflux esophagitis, the bleeding is typically not massive enough to cause hemodynamic collapse.

Confidence: 100%

152


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following conditions has the highest potential for colorectal malignancy?
Familial polyposis
Infantile polyp
Crohn’s disease
Ulcerative colitis

Answer: (A) — Familial polyposis

Explanation:

Familial polyposis, more specifically Familial Adenomatous Polyposis (FAP), is an autosomal dominant inherited disorder caused by mutations in the adenomatous polyposis coli (APC) tumor suppressor gene. This condition is characterized by the development of hundreds to thousands of adenomatous polyps throughout the colon and rectum, typically beginning in adolescence. Adenomatous polyps are considered precancerous lesions. Due to the sheer number of polyps, the cumulative lifetime risk of developing colorectal adenocarcinoma in individuals with untreated FAP approaches 100% by the age of 40-50. This near-certainty of malignant transformation gives it the highest potential for colorectal malignancy among the choices. Prophylactic colectomy is the standard of care to prevent cancer.

Options:

  • (B) Infantile polyp: This is incorrect. An infantile polyp, also known as a juvenile polyp, is a type of hamartomatous polyp. Hamartomas are benign, disorganized growths of tissues native to the site of origin. A solitary juvenile polyp carries no increased risk of malignancy. While a rare condition called Juvenile Polyposis Syndrome involves multiple polyps and does carry an increased cancer risk, a single infantile polyp does not, and its risk is negligible compared to FAP.
  • (C) Crohn’s disease: This is incorrect. Crohn's disease is a form of inflammatory bowel disease (IBD) that increases the risk of colorectal cancer due to chronic inflammation. The risk depends on the duration and extent of colonic involvement. While the risk is significantly elevated compared to the general population (approximately 2-3 times higher), the lifetime risk is estimated to be around 2-8% after 20-30 years of disease, which is substantially lower than the near 100% risk in FAP.
  • (D) Ulcerative colitis: This is incorrect. Like Crohn's disease, ulcerative colitis is a form of IBD that predisposes to colorectal cancer through chronic inflammation. The risk is also related to the duration and extent of the disease (pancolitis carries the highest risk). The cumulative risk of cancer can be as high as 10-30% after 30 years of extensive disease. Although this risk is significant and generally considered slightly higher than in Crohn's disease, it is still much lower than the almost inevitable progression to cancer seen in untreated FAP.

Confidence: 100%

153


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
What is the percentage of pulmonary emboli proceed to cause lung infarction?
0-5%
5-15%
20-30%
30-40%

Answer: (B) — 5-15%

Explanation:

Pulmonary infarction, which is the ischemic necrosis (death) of lung tissue, is a relatively uncommon complication of pulmonary embolism (PE). This is primarily due to the lung's unique dual blood supply:

  1. Pulmonary Arteries: These vessels carry deoxygenated blood from the right ventricle to the lungs for gas exchange. This is the circulation that is obstructed during a PE.
  2. Bronchial Arteries: These arteries branch from the systemic circulation (usually the aorta) and supply oxygenated blood to the supporting structures of the lung, including the bronchi, bronchioles, and visceral pleura.

In most cases of PE, the bronchial circulation provides sufficient oxygenated blood to keep the lung parenchyma viable, thus preventing infarction even when a pulmonary artery branch is blocked. Therefore, infarction occurs in only a minority of cases, estimated to be around 10%.

Infarction is more likely to develop when there is a pre-existing compromise of the systemic circulation, such as in patients with left-sided heart failure or shock. In these conditions, the bronchial artery flow is already reduced and cannot adequately compensate for the loss of pulmonary arterial flow. Infarction is also more common with smaller, more peripheral emboli where collateral circulation is less robust.

Options:

  • (A) 0-5%: This range is too low. While infarction is not the most common outcome, it is not this rare.
  • (C) 20-30%: This range overestimates the incidence of pulmonary infarction. The dual blood supply is protective in the majority of PE cases.
  • (D) 30-40%: This is a significant overestimation. If this were the case, pulmonary infarction would be a very common sequela of PE, which is not clinically observed.

Confidence: 95%

154


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following conditions is NOT an indication for lung transplantation?
Chronic reversible airways disease
Cystic fibrosis
Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis
End-stage emphysema

Answer: (A) — Chronic reversible airways disease

Explanation:

Lung transplantation is a major surgical procedure reserved for patients with advanced, life-limiting, and irreversible lung diseases who have exhausted all other medical therapies. The fundamental principle for considering transplantation is that the underlying disease must be irreversible and progressive, leading to end-stage respiratory failure.

Chronic reversible airways disease, such as asthma, is characterized by bronchoconstriction and inflammation that is, by definition, reversible with appropriate medical treatment (e.g., bronchodilators, inhaled or systemic corticosteroids). While severe, refractory asthma can be debilitating, the underlying pathological process is not one of fixed, progressive parenchymal destruction like the other conditions listed. Therefore, it is not a standard indication for the high-risk, definitive procedure of lung transplantation.

Options:

  • (B) Cystic fibrosis: This is a common and well-established indication for lung transplantation. Cystic fibrosis leads to progressive, irreversible lung damage from chronic infections and inflammation, resulting in severe bronchiectasis and end-stage respiratory failure. For many young adults with CF, transplantation is a life-saving option.
  • (C) Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis (IPF): This is a leading indication for lung transplantation. IPF is a progressive, fatal disease characterized by irreversible scarring (fibrosis) of the lung tissue. There is no cure for IPF, and lung transplantation is the only treatment known to prolong survival.
  • (D) End-stage emphysema: This condition, a major type of Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD), is the most common indication for lung transplantation worldwide. Emphysema involves the irreversible destruction of the alveoli, leading to severe airflow limitation and gas exchange abnormalities. When the disease reaches its end stage, transplantation is a valid therapeutic option.

Confidence: 100%

155


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following is the most common site for metastasis of lung carcinoma?
Adrenal
Kidney
Testes
Brain

Answer: (A) — Adrenal

Explanation:

Lung carcinoma is known for its propensity for early hematogenous (blood-borne) dissemination to distant sites. While several organs are common targets, autopsy studies have consistently identified the adrenal glands as the single most common site of metastasis. Metastases can be found in the adrenal glands in up to 50% of patients with lung cancer at the time of post-mortem examination. This high frequency is thought to be related to the rich sinusoidal blood supply of the adrenal cortex. Despite this high incidence, adrenal metastases are often clinically silent and discovered incidentally during staging imaging (e.g., CT or PET scans).

Options:

  • (B) Kidney: Metastasis to the kidneys from lung cancer can occur, but it is significantly less common than metastasis to the adrenal glands, brain, bone, or liver.
  • (C) Testes: Metastasis to the testes from any primary cancer, including lung cancer, is exceedingly rare. Most testicular neoplasms are primary tumors.
  • (D) Brain: The brain is a very common and clinically significant site for lung cancer metastasis, particularly from small cell lung cancer and adenocarcinoma. Brain metastases are often symptomatic, leading to headaches, seizures, or focal neurological deficits, and thus are frequently diagnosed clinically. However, based on comprehensive autopsy series, the adrenal glands are involved with slightly higher frequency, even though they are less likely to cause symptoms.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 55-year old female who has been a heavy cigarette smoker presents with gradually worsening dyspnea. Assessment and investigations reveal that she has emphysema. What pattern would to expect to see in her CT scan?
Panacinar
Paraseptal
Centriacinar
Bullous

Answer: (C) — Centriacinar

Explanation:

Emphysema is pathologically defined as the permanent enlargement of airspaces distal to the terminal bronchioles, accompanied by the destruction of their walls. The pattern of this destruction within the lung acinus helps classify the type of emphysema.

Centriacinar (or centrilobular) emphysema is the most common type, accounting for over 95% of clinically significant cases. It is overwhelmingly associated with heavy cigarette smoking. In this pattern, the central or proximal parts of the acinus, formed by the respiratory bronchioles, are selectively damaged and dilated, while the distal alveoli are initially spared. The inflammatory response to inhaled cigarette smoke, involving neutrophils and macrophages, leads to the release of proteases (like elastase) that destroy the elastic tissue in the walls of these central airways. These lesions are typically more severe in the upper lobes and the superior segments of the lower lobes. The patient's history of heavy smoking makes centriacinar emphysema the most expected finding.

Options:

  • (A) Panacinar: This pattern involves the uniform enlargement and destruction of the entire acinus, from the respiratory bronchiole to the terminal alveoli. It is classically associated with a genetic deficiency of alpha-1 antitrypsin (A1AT). A1AT is a crucial protease inhibitor, and its absence leads to unchecked destruction of lung tissue. Panacinar emphysema is typically more severe in the lower lobes. While smoking can accelerate panacinar emphysema in A1AT deficient individuals, it is not the primary cause of this specific pattern.
  • (B) Paraseptal: Also known as distal acinar emphysema, this pattern primarily affects the distal part of the acinus, adjacent to the pleura and along the lobular connective tissue septa. It is more pronounced in the upper half of the lungs. While it can be seen in smokers, it is most notably associated with the formation of large airspaces (bullae) and is a common underlying cause of spontaneous pneumothorax in young adults. It is not the most common pattern associated with long-term heavy smoking.
  • (D) Bullous: This is a descriptive term rather than a primary pathological pattern. Bullous emphysema refers to the presence of large, subpleural air-filled spaces greater than 1 cm in diameter called bullae. Bullae can be a complication of any type of emphysema, particularly paraseptal and severe centriacinar emphysema. While a CT scan in this patient might show bullae, the fundamental underlying pattern of parenchymal destruction would be centriacinar.

Confidence: 100%

157


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 34-year old female presents with increasing muscle weakness over the last 3 months. The weakness is most pronounced in her eyelids causing difficulty in vision towards the end of the day. On examination her muscle strength diminishes on repetitive movement. Which one of the following is the most likely pathological process responsible for her condition?
Antibody-mediated dysfunction of the neuromuscular junction
Immune complex deposition in muscle capillaries
Secretion of cytokines by activated macrophages
Lysis of muscle cells by CD8+ lymphocytes

Answer: (A) — Antibody-mediated dysfunction of the neuromuscular junction

Explanation:

The clinical presentation described is highly characteristic of Myasthenia Gravis (MG). This is an autoimmune disorder defined by: 1. Fatiguable muscle weakness: Strength decreases with repetitive use and improves with rest. 2. Diurnal variation: Symptoms, particularly ptosis (eyelid drooping) and diplopia (double vision), are typically worse at the end of the day. 3. Specific muscle involvement: Ocular muscles are commonly affected first, leading to ptosis and diplopia. Proximal limb, facial, and bulbar muscles can also be involved.

The underlying pathophysiology of Myasthenia Gravis involves the production of autoantibodies against components of the postsynaptic membrane at the neuromuscular junction (NMJ). In approximately 85% of patients, these are anti-acetylcholine receptor (AChR) antibodies. These antibodies cause dysfunction by: * Blocking acetylcholine from binding to its receptor. * Promoting endocytosis and degradation of the receptors. * Activating complement-mediated damage to the postsynaptic membrane.

This reduction in functional acetylcholine receptors leads to inefficient neuromuscular transmission. With repeated nerve stimulation, the amount of acetylcholine released naturally declines, and in an MG patient, the end-plate potential fails to reach the threshold for muscle fiber contraction, resulting in clinical weakness and fatigue.

Options:

  • (B) Immune complex deposition in muscle capillaries: This is the characteristic pathological mechanism of Dermatomyositis. In this condition, immune complexes deposit in the microvasculature of muscle and skin, leading to complement activation, endothelial cell damage, and ischemic injury to muscle fibers, typically in a perifascicular pattern. While it causes muscle weakness, it does not typically present with the prominent fatiguability and diurnal variation described.
  • (C) Secretion of cytokines by activated macrophages: While macrophages and cytokines are involved in the inflammatory response in many muscle diseases, this is not the primary or specific initiating mechanism for Myasthenia Gravis. It is a more prominent feature in inflammatory myopathies like polymyositis or inclusion body myositis. The core defect in MG is the antibody-mediated attack on the NMJ, not a primary macrophage-driven process.
  • (D) Lysis of muscle cells by CD8+ lymphocytes: This describes the cell-mediated cytotoxicity that is the hallmark of Polymyositis and Inclusion Body Myositis. In these diseases, cytotoxic T-lymphocytes (CD8+) directly invade and destroy muscle fibers that aberrantly express MHC class I molecules. This leads to endomysial inflammation and muscle necrosis, but not the specific NMJ transmission defect seen in the patient.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
An 80-year-old female presents with fever and dysuria. Urine culture performed by her GP grew E.coli. Her vital signs on arrival to ED are Temp 38.9, HR 120, BP 70/50. Which of the following has most likely initiated her cardiovascular collapse?
Lipopolysaccharide
Tumour necrosis factor
Nitric oxide
Platelet activating factor

Answer: (A) — Lipopolysaccharide

Explanation:

The patient is presenting with signs and symptoms of septic shock (fever, tachycardia, and profound hypotension) secondary to a urinary tract infection caused by E. coli. The question asks for the most likely initiator of this process.

E. coli is a gram-negative bacterium. A key component of the outer membrane of gram-negative bacteria is lipopolysaccharide (LPS), also known as endotoxin. When these bacteria enter the bloodstream or their components are released during infection, LPS acts as a potent pathogen-associated molecular pattern (PAMP). It binds to Toll-like receptor 4 (TLR4) on the surface of immune cells, particularly macrophages and monocytes. This binding is the primary initiating event that triggers a massive, systemic inflammatory response. This response involves the release of numerous pro-inflammatory cytokines and mediators, which ultimately lead to the widespread vasodilation, increased vascular permeability, and myocardial depression characteristic of septic shock and cardiovascular collapse.

Options:

  • (B) Tumour necrosis factor (TNF): TNF-α is a critical pro-inflammatory cytokine that plays a central role in the pathophysiology of sepsis. However, it is an endogenous mediator that is released by macrophages after they have been activated by an initial trigger, such as LPS. Therefore, while crucial to the process, it is not the initiator.
  • (C) Nitric oxide (NO): NO is a potent vasodilator and a key downstream effector molecule responsible for the profound hypotension seen in septic shock. Its production is induced by cytokines like TNF-α and IL-1. It is a critical mediator of cardiovascular collapse, but it is produced late in the inflammatory cascade, not at the beginning.
  • (D) Platelet activating factor (PAF): PAF is another important inflammatory mediator that contributes to vasodilation, increased vascular permeability, and platelet aggregation during sepsis. Like TNF, its release is part of the host's inflammatory response and occurs downstream from the initial recognition of the pathogen's components by the immune system.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 24-year-old male sustains fractures of the femur and tibia in a motorbike accident. On day 2 of admission, he suddenly becomes severly dyspneic. Which of the following complications is the most likely cause of this sudden respiratory difficulty?
Fat embolism
Pulmonary infarction
Pulmonary edema
Cardiac tamponade

Answer: (A) — Fat embolism Explanation: The patient's presentation is a classic scenario for Fat Embolism Syndrome (FES). Following severe trauma, particularly fractures of long bones like the femur and tibia, fat globules from the bone marrow can enter the venous circulation through torn intramedullary veins. These fat globules travel to the lungs, where they cause mechanical obstruction of the pulmonary capillaries. Additionally, the breakdown of these fat globules into free fatty acids leads to a toxic endothelial injury, causing increased capillary permeability, inflammation, and a state similar to Acute Respiratory Distress Syndrome (ARDS). The clinical syndrome typically manifests 24 to 72 hours after the initial injury, with sudden onset of respiratory distress (dyspnea, tachypnea, hypoxemia), neurological symptoms (confusion, agitation, coma), and a petechial rash (often on the chest, axillae, and conjunctiva). Given the patient's long bone fractures and the timing of his sudden dyspnea on day 2, fat embolism is the most likely diagnosis.

Options: * Pulmonary infarction: This is tissue death in the lung, most commonly caused by a large pulmonary thromboembolism (a blood clot, usually from a deep vein thrombosis in the leg). While trauma and immobility are risk factors for DVT and subsequent PE, fat embolism is a more direct and classic complication specifically associated with long bone fractures in this timeframe. * Pulmonary edema: This refers to fluid accumulation in the alveoli and interstitium of the lungs. While fat embolism does cause a non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema (as part of ARDS), pulmonary edema is the resulting pathological state rather than the primary etiological event. Fat embolism is the specific cause of the edema and respiratory distress in this context. * Cardiac tamponade: This is the compression of the heart due to fluid accumulation in the pericardial sac. It typically results from direct chest trauma (e.g., myocardial rupture) or conditions like pericarditis or aortic dissection. It is not a typical complication of isolated lower limb fractures and would present with signs of obstructive shock (e.g., muffled heart sounds, jugular venous distension, hypotension).

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 74-year old patient with known lung cancer presents with new onset of hoarness of voice. You suspect involvement of the nerves in the superior mediastinum. Regarding the various nerves in the superior mediastinum, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
The right recurrent laryngeal nerve loops around the subclavian artery
The left vagus nerve passes in front of the left lung root
The left phrenic nerve passes behind the arch of aorta
The left recurrent laryngeal nerve hooks around the ligamentous arteriosum

Answer: (A) — The right recurrent laryngeal nerve loops around the subclavian artery

Explanation:

This question assesses knowledge of the anatomical relationships of major nerves within the superior mediastinum. The clinical presentation of hoarseness suggests pathology affecting a recurrent laryngeal nerve, which innervates the intrinsic muscles of the larynx.

The correct statement is that the right recurrent laryngeal nerve, a branch of the right vagus nerve, hooks posteriorly under the right subclavian artery before ascending in the tracheoesophageal groove to reach the larynx. This is a key anatomical distinction from its left-sided counterpart.

Options:

  • (A) The right recurrent laryngeal nerve loops around the subclavian artery: This is the correct statement. The right vagus nerve descends into the superior mediastinum, and at the level of the right subclavian artery, it gives off the right recurrent laryngeal nerve which loops under this artery to ascend towards the larynx.
  • (B) The left vagus nerve passes in front of the left lung root: This is incorrect. Both the right and left vagus nerves pass posterior to the root (hilum) of their respective lungs. The phrenic nerves pass anterior to the lung roots. A helpful mnemonic is "Phrenic is in front, Vagus is behind."
  • (C) The left phrenic nerve passes behind the arch of aorta: This is incorrect. The left phrenic nerve descends through the mediastinum passing anterior to the arch of the aorta and anterior to the root of the left lung. It runs between the mediastinal pleura and the fibrous pericardium.
  • (D) The left recurrent laryngeal nerve hooks around the ligamentous arteriosum: This is incorrect and a common point of confusion. The left recurrent laryngeal nerve hooks under the arch of the aorta, just posterolateral to the ligamentum arteriosum (a remnant of the fetal ductus arteriosus). It does not loop around the ligamentum arteriosum itself. The much longer course of the left recurrent laryngeal nerve makes it more vulnerable to compression from mediastinal pathologies like tumors or aortic aneurysms, which is a classic cause of hoarseness (Ortner's syndrome).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Regarding the muscles and neurovascular structures of the chest wall, which of the following statements is TRUE?
The neurovascular bundle lies between the internal and innermost intercostal muscles
The intercostal artery is more superficial than the vein
The transversus muscle lies between the external and internal intercostal muscles
The intercostal nerve lies between the intercostal artery and vein

Answer: (A) — The neurovascular bundle lies between the internal and innermost intercostal muscles

Explanation:

The thoracic wall is composed of several layers of muscle. From superficial to deep, the three main intercostal muscle layers are the external intercostal, internal intercostal, and innermost intercostal muscles. The primary intercostal neurovascular bundle, which consists of the intercostal vein, artery, and nerve (arranged from superior to inferior in the order V-A-N), travels in the costal groove on the inferior border of the rib. This neurovascular plane is located specifically between the internal intercostal muscle layer and the innermost intercostal muscle layer. This anatomical arrangement protects the bundle while allowing it to supply the chest wall structures.

Options:

  • (B) The intercostal artery is more superficial than the vein: This is incorrect. The arrangement of the neurovascular bundle in the costal groove from superior to inferior is Vein, Artery, Nerve (mnemonic: VAN). Therefore, the vein is superior (and in that plane, slightly more superficial) to the artery, not the other way around.
  • (C) The transversus muscle lies between the external and internal intercostal muscles: This is incorrect. The transversus thoracis muscle is part of the deepest muscle layer of the anterior chest wall, functionally continuous with the innermost intercostal muscles. It lies deep to the internal intercostal muscles, not between the external and internal layers.
  • (D) The intercostal nerve lies between the intercostal artery and vein: This is incorrect. As described by the VAN mnemonic, the intercostal nerve is the most inferior structure in the bundle within the costal groove, located below both the intercostal artery and vein.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 54-year old male presents with a food bolus (piece of meat) obstruction in his oesophagus. Which of the following is the narrowest part of the oesophagus where the food bolus has a high chance of obstruction?
Where it is crossed by the left main bronchus
Where it is crossed by the aortic arch
Where it passes through the diaphragm
At the level of the cricopharyngeus muscle

Answer: (D) — At the level of the cricopharyngeus muscle

Explanation:

The oesophagus has three main physiological constrictions, which are common sites for foreign bodies or food boluses to become impacted. The narrowest of these is the cervical constriction at the pharyngo-oesophageal junction, located at the level of the cricoid cartilage (approximately C6 vertebral level). This narrowing is caused by the cricopharyngeus muscle, which acts as the upper oesophageal sphincter (UES). Due to it being the tightest point in the entire oesophagus (and in fact, the entire gastrointestinal tract except for the appendix), it is the most common site for a food bolus obstruction.

Options:

  • (A) Where it is crossed by the left main bronchus: This is the broncho-aortic constriction, one of the three physiological narrowings of the oesophagus. While it is a common site for obstruction, it is not as narrow as the cricopharyngeal constriction.
  • (B) Where it is crossed by the aortic arch: This is also part of the broncho-aortic constriction, located just superior to the crossing of the left main bronchus. It represents a point of indentation and narrowing but is wider than the UES.
  • (C) Where it passes through the diaphragm: This is the diaphragmatic constriction, where the oesophagus passes through the oesophageal hiatus of the diaphragm (at T10). This point functions as the lower oesophageal sphincter (LES). Although it is a site of narrowing, it is generally wider than the cricopharyngeal sphincter.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 60-year old man presents with central chest pain. ECG shows ST elevation in the lateral leads. You suspect ischemia to the region supplied by the circumflex artery. The circumflex artery usually supplies
Anterior two thirds of the interventricular septum
AV node in approximately 60% of people
Right ventricle
The Left atrium

Answer: (D) — The Left atrium

Explanation:

The left circumflex artery (LCx) is a branch of the left main coronary artery. It courses along the left atrioventricular (coronary) sulcus, between the left atrium and the left ventricle. As it travels in this groove, it gives off several important branches: 1. Atrial branches: These supply the left atrium. 2. Obtuse marginal (OM) branches: These descend over the lateral wall of the left ventricle, supplying the anterolateral and posterolateral aspects of this chamber.

The clinical vignette describes a lateral wall myocardial infarction (ST elevation in lateral leads I, aVL, V5, V6), which is the classic presentation for an occlusion of the circumflex artery or its obtuse marginal branches. The question asks what the circumflex artery usually supplies from the given options. The LCx is the primary blood supply to the left atrium.

Options:

  • (A) Anterior two-thirds of the interventricular septum: This region is supplied by the septal perforator branches of the Left Anterior Descending (LAD) artery.
  • (B) AV node in approximately 60% of people: The Atrioventricular (AV) node is supplied by the AV nodal artery, which arises from the Posterior Descending Artery (PDA). The PDA originates from the Right Coronary Artery (RCA) in about 85% of individuals (right-dominant circulation). The LCx gives rise to the PDA and thus supplies the AV node in only about 15% of individuals (left-dominant circulation). The SA node is supplied by the RCA in ~60% and the LCx in ~40% of cases, which may be a source of confusion.
  • (C) Right ventricle: The majority of the right ventricle is supplied by the Right Coronary Artery (RCA), particularly via its acute marginal branches.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
The parasympathetic nervous system controls lacrimation and salivation via the cranial parasympathetic outflow. Which one of the following cranial nerves does NOT carry the fibres of parasympathetic outflow?
II
X
III
VII

Answer: (A) — II Explanation: The parasympathetic nervous system has a craniosacral outflow, meaning its preganglionic neurons originate in the brainstem (cranial) and the sacral spinal cord. The cranial outflow is carried by four specific cranial nerves: III, VII, IX, and X. * Cranial Nerve III (Oculomotor): Innervates the sphincter pupillae (causing pupillary constriction) and the ciliary muscle (for lens accommodation). * Cranial Nerve VII (Facial): Innervates the lacrimal gland (for lacrimation) and the submandibular and sublingual glands (for salivation). * Cranial Nerve IX (Glossopharyngeal): Innervates the parotid gland (for salivation). * Cranial Nerve X (Vagus): Provides parasympathetic innervation to the thoracic and abdominal viscera (e.g., heart, lungs, and gastrointestinal tract up to the splenic flexure).

Cranial nerve II, the optic nerve, is a purely special sensory nerve responsible for transmitting visual information from the retina to the brain. It contains no motor or autonomic (parasympathetic) fibers.

Options: * (B) X: The vagus nerve (CN X) is the primary parasympathetic nerve for the thoracic and abdominal organs. It is incorrect because it is a major carrier of parasympathetic fibers. * (C) III: The oculomotor nerve (CN III) carries parasympathetic fibers to control the pupil and lens. It is incorrect because it is part of the cranial parasympathetic outflow. * (D) VII: The facial nerve (CN VII) carries parasympathetic fibers controlling lacrimation and salivation, as mentioned in the question stem. It is incorrect because it is a key component of the cranial parasympathetic outflow.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 40-year old presents to ED with fall onto her face with swollen right cheek and double vision. A CT scan confirms a fracture to the roof of the maxillary sinus. This might result in sensory loss to
Upper molar teeth
Skin over the Zygomatic bone
Tip of the nose
Upper incisors

Answer: (D) — Upper incisors Explanation: The roof of the maxillary sinus is also the floor of the orbit. A key structure that runs through a canal within this bone is the infraorbital nerve, which is a terminal branch of the maxillary division (V2) of the trigeminal nerve (CN V). A fracture of the orbital floor, as described, can compress or damage this nerve.

The infraorbital nerve gives off several branches within its canal before emerging onto the face. One of these is the anterior superior alveolar (ASA) nerve. The ASA nerve is responsible for providing sensory innervation to the upper incisor and canine teeth, as well as the corresponding labial gingiva. Therefore, an injury to the infraorbital nerve in this location would lead to sensory loss (numbness) in the upper incisors. The patient would also likely experience numbness of the lower eyelid, the side of the nose, and the upper lip, as these areas are also supplied by terminal branches of the infraorbital nerve.

Options: (A) Upper molar teeth: These teeth are primarily innervated by the posterior superior alveolar (PSA) nerve, which branches from the maxillary nerve (V2) in the pterygopalatine fossa, before it becomes the infraorbital nerve and enters the orbit. Thus, an isolated orbital floor fracture would not affect the PSA nerve. The premolar teeth are supplied by the middle superior alveolar (MSA) nerve, which also branches from the infraorbital nerve in its canal and could be affected.

(B) Skin over the Zygomatic bone: This area is innervated by the zygomaticofacial nerve, a branch of the zygomatic nerve. While the zygomatic nerve is also a branch of the maxillary nerve (V2), it travels along the lateral wall of the orbit, not the floor, and would be spared in a fracture confined to the orbital floor.

(C) Tip of the nose: Sensory innervation to the tip of the nose is provided by the external nasal branch of the anterior ethmoidal nerve. This nerve is a branch of the nasociliary nerve, which originates from the ophthalmic division (V1) of the trigeminal nerve, not the maxillary division (V2).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 12-year old boy present with epistaxis. Examination reveals a bleeding vessel from the Kiesselbach’s area. Which one of the following blood vessels does not directly contribute to blood supply to Kiesselbach’s area?
Posterior Ethmoidal Artery
Anteior branch of Sphenopalatine Artery
Ascending branch of Greater Palatine Artery
Maxillary Artery

Answer: (D) — Maxillary Artery

Explanation:

Kiesselbach's area (also known as Little's area) is a region in the anteroinferior part of the nasal septum where a rich anastomosis of blood vessels, known as Kiesselbach's plexus, is located. This plexus is the most common site for anterior epistaxis (nosebleeds). The question asks which of the listed vessels does not directly contribute to this plexus.

The maxillary artery is a major artery of the deep face and a terminal branch of the external carotid artery. While it is the source for two of the key arteries that supply Kiesselbach's plexus (the sphenopalatine artery and the greater palatine artery), the maxillary artery itself is a large parent vessel located in the infratemporal and pterygopalatine fossae. It does not send a direct branch to anastomose in the plexus. Its contribution is therefore indirect, via its own branches.

The arteries that directly contribute to Kiesselbach's plexus are: 1. Anterior Ethmoidal Artery (from the ophthalmic artery, a branch of the internal carotid artery). 2. Septal Branch of the Sphenopalatine Artery (a terminal branch of the maxillary artery). 3. Septal Branch of the Greater Palatine Artery (which is also a branch of the maxillary artery). 4. Septal Branch of the Superior Labial Artery (from the facial artery, a branch of the external carotid artery).

Options:

  • (A) Posterior Ethmoidal Artery: This is incorrect. The posterior ethmoidal artery (a branch of the ophthalmic artery) supplies the superior part of the nasal septum. While its contribution to Kiesselbach's plexus is considered minor compared to the anterior ethmoidal artery, it is a terminal vessel that directly supplies the septal mucosa and is often included as a contributor.
  • (B) Anterior branch of Sphenopalatine Artery: This is incorrect. The septal branch of the sphenopalatine artery is a major and direct contributor to the posterior aspect of the plexus. The option's wording "Anterior branch" likely refers to the septal branch supplying the anterior septum.
  • (C) Ascending branch of Greater Palatine Artery: This is incorrect. The greater palatine artery gives off an ascending branch that travels through the incisive canal to directly anastomose with other vessels in Kiesselbach's area.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 24-year old female presents with chronic headaches. You plan to perform a lumbar puncture to exclude benign intracranial hypertension. What is the normal rate of production of Cerebrospinal fluid per day?
100-150 ml
1500-1800 ml
1000-1200 ml
500-700 ml

Answer: (4) — 500-700 ml

Explanation:

Cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) is a clear, colorless fluid that surrounds the brain and spinal cord, providing mechanical protection, buoyancy, and a stable chemical environment. It is primarily produced by the choroid plexus located within the ventricles of the brain. The rate of CSF production in a healthy adult is relatively constant, at approximately 0.35 ml per minute, which translates to about 20-25 ml per hour. Over a 24-hour period, this totals approximately 500-700 ml.

Given that the total CSF volume in the system at any one time is only about 150 ml, this high production rate means the entire CSF volume is replaced 3-4 times every day. This rapid turnover is crucial for clearing metabolic waste products from the central nervous system.

Options:

  • 100-150 ml: This is the approximate total volume of CSF present in the ventricles and subarachnoid space at any given moment, not the daily production rate. It is a common distractor.
  • 1500-1800 ml: This volume is excessively high and is more in line with daily urine output (typically 800-2000 ml) rather than CSF production.
  • 1000-1200 ml: This value is also significantly higher than the actual physiological rate of CSF production.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A patient presents with a stab wound to the neck between the angle of mandible and the cricoid cartilage (Zone 2). The wound is in the carotid triangle. Which of the following structures may be involved?
Trunks of brachial plexus
Submandibular gland
External jugular vein
Hypoglossal nerve

Answer: (D) — Hypoglossal nerve

Explanation: The carotid triangle is a subdivision of the anterior triangle of the neck. Its boundaries are the posterior belly of the digastric muscle (superiorly), the superior belly of the omohyoid muscle (anteroinferiorly), and the anterior border of the sternocleidomastoid muscle (posteriorly). The question describes an injury in Zone 2 of the neck, which encompasses the carotid triangle. The contents of the carotid triangle include: * The common carotid artery and its bifurcation into the internal and external carotid arteries. * The internal jugular vein. * Cranial nerves X (Vagus), XI (Accessory, passing through), and XII (Hypoglossal). The hypoglossal nerve (cranial nerve XII) passes through the carotid triangle as it loops forward, crossing the external and internal carotid arteries superficially before passing deep to the posterior belly of the digastric to innervate the muscles of the tongue. Therefore, an injury to the carotid triangle can damage the hypoglossal nerve.

Options: * (A) Trunks of brachial plexus: The roots and trunks of the brachial plexus are located in the posterior triangle of the neck, specifically in the interscalene triangle (between the anterior and middle scalene muscles). They are not found within the carotid triangle. * (B) Submandibular gland: This gland is located primarily in the submandibular (or digastric) triangle, which is superior to the carotid triangle and separated from it by the posterior belly of the digastric muscle. * (C) External jugular vein: This vein runs superficially, crossing the sternocleidomastoid muscle. It lies within the investing layer of deep cervical fascia and is considered a structure of the posterior triangle, not the carotid triangle, which contains the deep vessels and nerves of the neck.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 30 year old presents with one-day history of right lower abdominal pain. You suspect appendicitis. The most common position of appendix is:
Post-ileal
Sub-cecal
Pelvic
Retro-cecal

Answer: (D) — Retro-cecal Explanation: The vermiform appendix is a small, finger-like blind-ended tube that arises from the posteromedial aspect of the cecum. Its position is highly variable, but the most common anatomical location is retrocecal, meaning it is situated behind the cecum. Anatomical studies indicate that the retrocecal position occurs in approximately 65% of the population. The clinical presentation of appendicitis can vary depending on the appendix's location. A retrocecal appendix, for instance, may cause less intense, poorly localized pain and more pronounced back or flank pain due to its distance from the anterior parietal peritoneum.

Options: (A) Post-ileal: In this position, the appendix lies posterior to the terminal ileum. This is an uncommon position, found in only about 1-5% of individuals. (B) Sub-cecal: Here, the appendix is located inferior to the cecum. This is also an uncommon position, occurring in approximately 2-3% of cases. (C) Pelvic: This is the second most common position, where the appendix descends over the pelvic brim into the true pelvis. It is found in about 30% of the population. Inflammation of a pelvic appendix can irritate adjacent structures like the bladder or rectum, leading to urinary symptoms or tenesmus.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 30 year old presents with right upper abdominal pain. Gallbladder generally has limited mobility, however in some people the gallbladder is suspended from liver by a short mesentery. Mobile gallbladders are subject to torsion and infraction. What percentage of people have a mobile gallbladder?
15%
8%
1%
4%

Answer: (4) — 4%

Explanation:

A mobile gallbladder, also known as a "floating gallbladder" or "gallbladder with a mesentery," is an anatomical variant where the gallbladder is not closely adherent to the visceral surface of the liver in the gallbladder fossa. Instead, it is suspended from the liver by a complete peritoneal fold, or mesentery. This mesentery contains the cystic artery and duct. While the gallbladder's mobility is usually asymptomatic, this anatomical configuration predisposes it to torsion (twisting) around its pedicle. Gallbladder torsion is a rare but serious surgical emergency as it can lead to ischemia, infarction, and gangrene of the gallbladder. The prevalence of this anatomical variant is estimated to be approximately 4-5% in the general population.

Options:

  • 1%: This value is too low. While the clinical manifestation of gallbladder torsion is very rare, the underlying anatomical predisposition (a mobile gallbladder) is more common than 1%.
  • 8%: This figure is too high. Most anatomical and surgical studies place the prevalence in a lower range, typically not exceeding 7%.
  • 15%: This is a significant overestimation. A prevalence of 15% would mean this variant is quite common, which is not supported by anatomical studies or clinical experience.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 74-year-old male presents with a two-hour history of central chest pain. His ECG shows inferior ST segment elevation. Coronary angiography shows occlusion of right coronary artery. Which of the following mechanisms is the most likely cause of thrombosis in this patient?
Hypercoagulability
Endothelial injury
Venous stasis
Turbulent blood flow

Answer: (B) — Endothelial injury

Explanation:

The clinical scenario describes an acute ST-segment elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) of the inferior wall, confirmed by coronary angiography to be caused by an occlusion of the right coronary artery. The underlying pathophysiology of the vast majority of acute coronary syndromes, including STEMI, is the rupture or erosion of an atherosclerotic plaque.

Virchow's triad describes the three main factors that contribute to thrombosis: endothelial injury, abnormal blood flow, and hypercoagulability.

In the context of a myocardial infarction: 1. Atherosclerosis: Over years, an atherosclerotic plaque develops within the coronary artery wall. 2. Plaque Rupture: The fibrous cap overlying the plaque can become unstable and rupture. This event constitutes a severe and acute endothelial injury. 3. Thrombosis: The rupture exposes the highly thrombogenic lipid core and subendothelial collagen to the bloodstream. This triggers a rapid cascade of platelet adhesion, activation, and aggregation, along with the activation of the coagulation cascade, leading to the formation of a fibrin-rich thrombus that occludes the artery.

Therefore, endothelial injury (in the form of plaque rupture) is the primary and most critical event that initiates the acute thrombosis leading to the patient's myocardial infarction.

Options:

  • (A) Hypercoagulability: This refers to a systemic prothrombotic state (e.g., due to genetic factors like Factor V Leiden or acquired conditions like malignancy). While it can be a contributing factor, it is not the primary initiating event for most arterial thromboses in the setting of atherosclerosis. The trigger is a local event at the plaque site.
  • (C) Venous stasis: This is a key factor in the formation of thrombi in the venous system (e.g., deep vein thrombosis), where blood flow is slow. It is not a primary mechanism for thrombosis in the high-flow, high-pressure arterial system like the coronary arteries.
  • (D) Turbulent blood flow: While turbulent blood flow (often occurring at arterial bifurcations or around existing plaques) contributes to the chronic development and progression of atherosclerosis and can promote platelet activation, the acute, catastrophic event of occlusive thrombus formation is most directly precipitated by the plaque rupture (endothelial injury), not the pre-existing turbulence itself.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 62-year old male undergoes a midline laparotomy for perforated diverticulitis. He develops post-operative wound infection and dehiscence. The infection was treated with antibiotics and the wound was allowed to heal by secondary intention. At 6 weeks there is decrease in the size of wound. Which of the following processes best accounts for the change in the size of the wound?
Increased synthesis of collagen
Resolution of subcutaneous edema and infection.
Myofibroblast contraction
Elaboration of adhesive glycoprotein

Answer: (C) — Myofibroblast contraction

Explanation:

The patient's wound is healing by secondary intention, which is typical for wounds with significant tissue loss, infection, and separated edges (like a dehisced surgical wound). A key feature of healing by secondary intention is wound contraction. This process begins approximately one week after injury and continues for several weeks, peaking around 2-3 weeks but still being highly active at 6 weeks.

Wound contraction is primarily mediated by myofibroblasts. These are modified fibroblasts that possess characteristics of smooth muscle cells, containing abundant actin and myosin filaments. They align themselves within the granulation tissue, attach to the extracellular matrix at the wound margins, and exert a centripetal (pulling inwards) force. This action effectively pulls the edges of the wound together, significantly decreasing its surface area. This process is the most significant contributor to the reduction in the size of a large, open wound over time.

Options:

  • (A) Increased synthesis of collagen: Collagen synthesis (primarily Type III initially, later remodeled to Type I) by fibroblasts is crucial for filling the wound defect with granulation tissue and forming a scar. However, collagen deposition primarily provides tensile strength and fills the volume of the wound; it does not, by itself, cause the significant reduction in the wound's surface area. The process of contraction is distinct from simple filling.
  • (B) Resolution of subcutaneous edema and infection: The resolution of edema and the control of infection occur during the initial inflammatory phase of wound healing, typically within the first week. While this would lead to some initial reduction in swelling and apparent size, it is not the primary mechanism for the progressive decrease in the actual wound defect size seen at 6 weeks.
  • (D) Elaboration of adhesive glycoprotein: Adhesive glycoproteins, such as fibronectin and laminin, are essential components of the extracellular matrix. They form a scaffold that facilitates cell adhesion and migration (e.g., of fibroblasts and endothelial cells) into the wound bed. While critical for the overall process of granulation tissue formation, they are not the direct cause of the contractile force that shrinks the wound.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 64-year old presents with central chest pain. ECG shows ST elevation in anterior leads. Investigations show grossly elevated troponin. Which one of the following pathological findings would most likely be seen in the left ventricle 1 month later?
Coagulative necrosis
Complete resolution
Fibrous scar
Nodular regeneration

Answer: (C) — Fibrous scar Explanation: The clinical presentation of central chest pain, anterior ST elevation on ECG, and elevated troponins is characteristic of an acute anterior wall myocardial infarction (MI). Myocardial tissue is composed of cardiomyocytes, which are permanent cells with virtually no regenerative capacity. When these cells die due to ischemia, they undergo a predictable healing process that culminates in scar formation.

The timeline of healing after an MI is as follows: * 0-24 hours: Early coagulative necrosis, edema, and hemorrhage. * 1-3 days: Worsening coagulative necrosis and an acute inflammatory infiltrate, primarily neutrophils. * 3-10 days: Macrophages infiltrate to clear the necrotic debris. Granulation tissue begins to form at the margins. * 10-14 days: Well-established granulation tissue with neovascularization, fibroblasts, and collagen deposition. * 2-8 weeks: Progressive collagen deposition and maturation of the granulation tissue into a dense fibrous scar.

At 1 month (approximately 4 weeks), the area of infarction will have been replaced by a well-formed, dense, non-contractile fibrous scar.

Options: * (A) Coagulative necrosis: This is the characteristic form of cell death in an MI, but it is an acute finding, most prominent in the first few days. By 1 month, the necrotic tissue would have been cleared by macrophages and replaced by scar tissue. * (B) Complete resolution: This is incorrect because cardiomyocytes are permanent cells and cannot regenerate. Therefore, the damaged heart muscle cannot heal back to its original, functional state. * (D) Nodular regeneration: This type of healing occurs in organs with regenerative capacity, such as the liver (forming regenerative nodules in cirrhosis). This process does not occur in the heart.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 30 year old presents with burns to the lower legs from a gasoline fire that got out of control. Examination reveals large areas of erythema and blistering over both lower legs. Which of the following best describes the pathological process in response to thermal injury?
Granulomatous inflammation
Serous inflammation
Purulent inflammation
Fibrinous inflammation

Answer: (B) — Serous inflammation

Explanation:

Serous inflammation is a form of acute inflammation characterized by the exudation of a thin, watery, protein-poor fluid (serous exudate). This fluid can be derived from either blood plasma or the secretions of mesothelial cells lining the peritoneal, pleural, and pericardial cavities. In the context of a skin burn, the thermal injury causes damage to endothelial cells and increased vascular permeability, allowing this thin fluid to leak from the vessels and accumulate. When this fluid collects within or beneath the epidermis, it forms a blister (vesicle or bulla), which is the hallmark of a second-degree burn as described in the question. The erythema (redness) is due to vasodilation, another component of the acute inflammatory response.

Options:

  • (A) Granulomatous inflammation: This is a chronic inflammatory pattern characterized by collections of activated macrophages (epithelioid histiocytes). It is typically seen in response to persistent, indigestible agents such as Mycobacterium tuberculosis, fungi, or foreign bodies. It is not characteristic of the acute response to a thermal burn.
  • (C) Purulent inflammation: Also known as suppurative inflammation, this is characterized by the production of pus, which is an exudate rich in neutrophils and necrotic cellular debris. It is typically caused by pyogenic (pus-forming) bacteria. While a burn wound can become secondarily infected and develop purulent inflammation, the initial, uncomplicated response described (blistering) is serous, not purulent.
  • (D) Fibrinous inflammation: This occurs with more severe increases in vascular permeability that allow large molecules, such as fibrinogen, to pass out of the blood and form fibrin deposits. This is characteristic of inflammation in the lining of body cavities, like fibrinous pericarditis or pleuritis. While some fibrin may be present in a severe burn, the prominent feature of blistering with clear fluid is best described as serous inflammation.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 24-year old patient with acute upper respiratory infection takes Aspirin for pain and fever. Which of the following inflammatory responses is most affected by Aspirin?
Vasodilation
Migration of leucocytes
Chemotaxis
Phagocytosis

Answer: (A) — Vasodilation

Explanation:

Aspirin is a non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drug (NSAID) that exerts its effects by irreversibly inhibiting the cyclooxygenase (COX) enzymes, COX-1 and COX-2. These enzymes are critical for the conversion of arachidonic acid into prostaglandins and thromboxanes.

In the acute inflammatory response, certain prostaglandins, particularly PGE₂ and PGI₂ (prostacyclin), are potent mediators of vasodilation. Vasodilation is the widening of arterioles, which increases blood flow to the site of injury or infection. This process is responsible for two of the cardinal signs of inflammation: redness (rubor) and heat (calor). By inhibiting COX enzymes, Aspirin blocks the synthesis of these prostaglandins, thereby significantly reducing vasodilation and its associated inflammatory signs. Aspirin also reduces pain by preventing prostaglandins from sensitizing nerve endings and reduces fever by inhibiting PGE₂ synthesis in the hypothalamus.

Options:

  • (B) Migration of leucocytes: This process, also known as extravasation, involves the rolling, adhesion, and transmigration of leukocytes from blood vessels into the tissue. It is primarily mediated by adhesion molecules (selectins and integrins) on leukocytes and endothelial cells, whose expression is upregulated by cytokines like TNF and IL-1. While prostaglandins can play a minor role, this process is not the one most affected by Aspirin.
  • (C) Chemotaxis: This is the directed migration of leukocytes toward the site of injury along a chemical gradient. The major chemoattractants include bacterial products, complement component C5a, chemokines (like IL-8), and the leukotriene LTB₄. Aspirin inhibits the COX pathway, which can shunt arachidonic acid metabolism towards the lipoxygenase pathway, potentially increasing the production of leukotrienes like LTB₄. Therefore, Aspirin does not inhibit chemotaxis and may even enhance it.
  • (D) Phagocytosis: This is the process by which phagocytes (e.g., neutrophils and macrophages) engulf and destroy pathogens or cellular debris. This is a complex cellular function that is not directly dependent on prostaglandin synthesis and is therefore not significantly inhibited by Aspirin.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 24-year old sustains a tibial fracture in a motorbike accident. His leg was placed in a plaster cast. Several weeks later the size of his left calf has decreased. Which of the following processes best explains this change in size?
Aplasia
Atrophy
Hypoplasia
Dystrophy

Answer: (B) — Atrophy Explanation: The decrease in the size of the patient's calf muscle is a classic example of disuse atrophy. Atrophy is a cellular adaptation characterized by a decrease in the size of a pre-existing tissue or organ, resulting from a decrease in the size and/or number of its constituent cells. When a limb is immobilized in a cast, the skeletal muscles are not used. This lack of physical stress and neural stimulation leads to a decrease in protein synthesis and an increase in protein degradation within the muscle cells (myocytes), causing them to shrink. This is a reversible process; once the cast is removed and the muscle is used again, it will typically return to its original size (hypertrophy).

Options: A) Aplasia is incorrect. Aplasia refers to the complete failure of an organ or tissue to develop. This is a congenital condition, meaning the tissue was never present from birth. In this case, the patient had a normally developed calf muscle before the fracture.

C) Hypoplasia is incorrect. Hypoplasia is the incomplete or underdevelopment of an organ or tissue, resulting in a smaller-than-normal size from birth due to an insufficient number of cells. Like aplasia, it is a developmental abnormality, not an acquired change in a previously normal tissue.

D) Dystrophy is incorrect. While dystrophy involves the "wasting away" of tissue, in a medical context, it typically refers to a specific group of diseases, most notably muscular dystrophies. These are primary, often genetic, disorders characterized by progressive degeneration of muscle fibers, which are then replaced by fibrous and fatty tissue. The patient's muscle wasting is a secondary, adaptive response to a clear external factor (immobilization), not an intrinsic disease process. Atrophy is the precise term for the pathological process occurring.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 70-year old man presents with difficulty urinating, including hesitancy, poor flow and dribbling. Regarding the most common cause for this man’s symptoms, which of the following pathological processes has most likely occurred in the prostate gland?
Metaplasia
Dysplasia
Hyperplasia
Hypertrophy

Answer: (C) — Hyperplasia

Explanation:

The patient, a 70-year-old man, presents with classic lower urinary tract symptoms (LUTS): hesitancy, poor flow, and dribbling. The most common cause for these symptoms in this demographic is Benign Prostatic Hyperplasia (BPH).

The pathological process underlying BPH is hyperplasia, which is an increase in the number of cells. In the case of BPH, there is a proliferation of both the stromal (fibromuscular) and epithelial (glandular) cells within the transitional zone of the prostate gland. This proliferation leads to the formation of large, fairly discrete nodules, which enlarge the prostate and compress the prostatic urethra, causing obstruction and the resulting urinary symptoms. This process is driven by androgens, particularly dihydrotestosterone (DHT).

Options:

  • (A) Metaplasia: This is the reversible transformation of one differentiated cell type into another. For example, the change from columnar to squamous epithelium in the airways of smokers. This is not the primary pathological process in BPH.
  • (B) Dysplasia: This refers to disordered growth and maturation of cells, which is considered a pre-malignant condition. An example in the prostate is Prostatic Intraepithelial Neoplasia (PIN). Dysplasia does not cause the significant glandular enlargement seen in BPH.
  • (D) Hypertrophy: This is an increase in the size of individual cells, not the number of cells, leading to an increase in organ size. A classic example is the enlargement of cardiac muscle cells in response to chronic hypertension. While the term "Benign Prostatic Hypertrophy" is a common misnomer, the actual pathological process is predominantly hyperplasia (increase in cell number).

Confidence: 100%
