MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 25-year old woman presents to ED with severe acute abdominal pain. You suspect ovarian torsion. The ovarian artery arises from
The common iliac artery
The abdominal aorta
The external iliac artery
The internal iliac artery

Answer: (B) — The abdominal aorta

Explanation:

The ovarian arteries are paired vessels that supply blood to the ovaries. They arise directly from the anterolateral aspect of the abdominal aorta, typically at the vertebral level of L2, just inferior to the origin of the renal arteries.

This high origin is a result of embryological development. The gonads (ovaries in females, testes in males) initially develop high on the posterior abdominal wall, near the kidneys. As they descend to their final positions (the pelvis for ovaries, the scrotum for testes), they drag their neurovascular supply with them. Consequently, the primary arterial supply, the ovarian artery, maintains its origin from the abdominal aorta.

The ovarian artery travels inferiorly within the retroperitoneum, crossing anterior to the ureter. It then enters the suspensory ligament of the ovary (infundibulopelvic ligament) to reach the ovary and fallopian tube. In cases of ovarian torsion, this suspensory ligament, containing the ovarian artery and vein, twists, compromising blood flow and leading to ischemia, which causes severe pain and can result in ovarian necrosis if not treated emergently.

It is important to note that the ovary has a dual blood supply, also receiving blood from the ovarian branch of the uterine artery (which originates from the internal iliac artery). These two arteries (ovarian and uterine) form an anastomosis near the ovary. However, the question specifically asks for the origin of the ovarian artery.

Options:

  • (A) The common iliac artery: This is incorrect. The abdominal aorta bifurcates into the right and left common iliac arteries at the L4 vertebral level. The ovarian artery arises superior to this bifurcation.
  • (C) The external iliac artery: This is incorrect. The external iliac artery is a branch of the common iliac artery and primarily supplies the lower limb. It does not give rise to the ovarian artery.
  • (D) The internal iliac artery: This is a common distractor and is incorrect. The internal iliac artery supplies the pelvic viscera, gluteal region, and perineum. It gives rise to the uterine artery, which in turn provides an ovarian branch that anastomoses with the main ovarian artery. However, the primary ovarian artery itself originates from the aorta, not the internal iliac artery.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 35-year old male presents to ED with acute urinary retention. You plan to place an indwelling urinary catheter. Regarding the male urethra, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
The membranous part of the urethra is the narrowest part
The male urethra is approximately 10-12cm in length
The intramural part of the urethra is invested by the external urethral sphincter
The prostatic part of the urethra is the shortest and least distensible portion of urethra

Answer: (A) — The membranous part of the urethra is the narrowest part

Explanation:

The male urethra is divided into four parts: intramural (pre-prostatic), prostatic, membranous, and spongy (penile). The membranous urethra is the segment that passes through the deep perineal pouch, surrounded by the external urethral sphincter. It is the shortest (approx. 1-2 cm), least distensible, and narrowest part of the urethra (excluding the external meatus). This anatomical feature makes it the most common site of iatrogenic injury during instrumentation like catheterization or cystoscopy.

Options:

  • (B) The male urethra is approximately 10-12cm in length: This is incorrect. The average length of the adult male urethra is significantly longer, typically around 18-22 cm. The female urethra is much shorter, approximately 4 cm long.
  • (C) The intramural part of the urethra is invested by the external urethral sphincter: This is incorrect. The intramural (pre-prostatic) part of the urethra, located at the neck of the bladder, is surrounded by the internal urethral sphincter, which is composed of smooth muscle and is under involuntary (autonomic) control. The external urethral sphincter (skeletal muscle, voluntary control) surrounds the membranous part of the urethra.
  • (D) The prostatic part of the urethra is the shortest and least distensible portion of urethra: This is incorrect. The prostatic urethra, which traverses the prostate gland, is the widest and most dilatable portion of the urethra. It is approximately 3-4 cm long, making it longer than both the intramural and membranous parts.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A complete transection of the sciatic nerve is unlikely to cause
Impaired flexion of hip
Impaired flexion of knee
Loss of movement at ankle and foot
Impaired extension of hip

Answer: (A) — Impaired flexion of hip

Explanation:

The sciatic nerve is the largest nerve in the body, originating from the sacral plexus (spinal roots L4, L5, S1, S2, S3). It provides motor innervation to the muscles of the posterior thigh, the entire leg, and the foot. A complete transection of this nerve would paralyze these muscles.

The primary muscles responsible for hip flexion are the iliopsoas (innervated by the femoral nerve and direct branches from the lumbar plexus L1-L3) and the rectus femoris (innervated by the femoral nerve, L2-L4). Since the sciatic nerve does not innervate the primary hip flexors, a transection of the sciatic nerve is unlikely to cause impaired flexion of the hip.

Options:

  • (B) Impaired flexion of knee: This is incorrect. The primary muscles for knee flexion are the hamstring muscles (semitendinosus, semimembranosus, and biceps femoris). These muscles are located in the posterior thigh and are directly innervated by the sciatic nerve. Therefore, a sciatic nerve transection would cause significant impairment of knee flexion.
  • (C) Loss of movement at ankle and foot: This is incorrect. The sciatic nerve bifurcates into the tibial and common fibular (peroneal) nerves. These two branches innervate all the muscles in the anterior, posterior, and lateral compartments of the leg, which are responsible for all movements at the ankle and foot (dorsiflexion, plantarflexion, inversion, eversion). Transection would lead to a "flail foot" with complete loss of voluntary movement.
  • (D) Impaired extension of hip: This is incorrect. The hamstring muscles, innervated by the sciatic nerve, are powerful hip extensors, working alongside the gluteus maximus (innervated by the inferior gluteal nerve). Loss of hamstring function due to sciatic nerve transection would significantly weaken or impair hip extension.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The typical value of the amount of air that can be exhaled with maximum effort after maximum inspiration is:
5900ml
1200ml
4700ml
2400ml

Answer: (C) — 4700ml

Explanation:

The question asks for the volume of air that can be exhaled with maximum effort after a maximum inspiration. This is the definition of Vital Capacity (VC).

Vital Capacity is the sum of three lung volumes: 1. Tidal Volume (TV): The amount of air moved during normal, quiet breathing (~500 ml). 2. Inspiratory Reserve Volume (IRV): The additional volume of air that can be forcibly inhaled after a normal inspiration (~3100 ml). 3. Expiratory Reserve Volume (ERV): The additional volume of air that can be forcibly exhaled after a normal exhalation (~1200 ml).

Therefore, VC = TV + IRV + ERV. Using typical values for a healthy young adult male: VC ≈ 500 ml + 3100 ml + 1200 ml = 4800 ml.

The value of 4700 ml is the closest and most commonly cited typical value for Vital Capacity among the given options.

Options:

  • (A) 5900ml: This value is a close approximation of the Total Lung Capacity (TLC). TLC is the maximum volume of air the lungs can hold (TLC = Vital Capacity + Residual Volume). For example, 4700 ml (VC) + 1200 ml (RV) = 5900 ml.
  • (B) 1200ml: This is the typical value for the Residual Volume (RV), which is the amount of air remaining in the lungs after a maximal exhalation. It is also the approximate value for the Expiratory Reserve Volume (ERV).
  • (D) 2400ml: This value represents the Functional Residual Capacity (FRC), which is the volume of air remaining in the lungs after a normal, passive exhalation (FRC = Expiratory Reserve Volume + Residual Volume). For example, 1200 ml (ERV) + 1200 ml (RV) = 2400 ml.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A patient presents with difficulty swallowing. You suspect a neurological cause for this. Which one of the following nerves does NOT mediate the afferent impulses of the swallowing reflex response?
Facial
Trigeminal
Glossopharyngeal
Vagus

Answer: (A) — Facial

Explanation:

The swallowing reflex (deglutition) is a complex process involving a coordinated sequence of voluntary and involuntary actions. The involuntary pharyngeal phase is triggered when a bolus of food stimulates sensory receptors in the oropharynx. This sensory information is the afferent (incoming) limb of the reflex. The central processing occurs in the swallowing center of the medulla and pons, which then sends out motor commands via the efferent (outgoing) limb.

The afferent signals that initiate the pharyngeal phase of swallowing are primarily carried by three cranial nerves: 1. Glossopharyngeal nerve (CN IX): This is the principal afferent nerve. It carries sensory information (touch, pressure) from the posterior one-third of the tongue, the soft palate, the fauces, and the walls of the oropharynx. 2. Vagus nerve (CN X): The internal branch of the superior laryngeal nerve (a branch of the vagus) provides sensory innervation to the laryngopharynx and the laryngeal mucosa down to the vocal folds. This is crucial for triggering the reflex and protecting the airway. 3. Trigeminal nerve (CN V): The maxillary (V2) and mandibular (V3) divisions provide general sensation from the soft palate, hard palate, and anterior two-thirds of the tongue, which also contributes to the afferent limb.

The Facial nerve (CN VII) is primarily involved in the efferent (motor) part of swallowing. It innervates the buccinator muscle (keeping food between the teeth), the orbicularis oris (lip seal), and muscles that elevate the hyoid bone (stylohyoid and posterior belly of the digastric). While the facial nerve does have a sensory component (taste from the anterior two-thirds of the tongue via the chorda tympani), it does not carry the general somatic afferent signals from the pharynx that are responsible for triggering the swallowing reflex.

Options:

  • (B) Trigeminal: Incorrect. The trigeminal nerve (CN V) carries afferent sensory information from the palate and anterior tongue, which contributes to initiating the swallowing reflex.
  • (C) Glossopharyngeal: Incorrect. The glossopharyngeal nerve (CN IX) is the primary afferent nerve for the swallowing reflex, innervating the key trigger zones in the oropharynx.
  • (D) Vagus: Incorrect. The vagus nerve (CN X), via its superior laryngeal branch, carries critical afferent information from the laryngopharynx and larynx, contributing to both the reflex and airway protection.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 30-year-old is diagnosed with peptic ulcer disease. Which one of the following components of the gastric juice protects against gastric and duodenal ulceration?
Gastric lipase
Mucus
Intrinsic factor
Pepsin

Answer: (B) — Mucus Explanation: The stomach lining is protected from its own harsh acidic environment and digestive enzymes by the gastric mucosal barrier. This barrier has two key components: a thick layer of mucus secreted by surface mucous cells and mucus neck cells, and bicarbonate (HCO₃⁻) which is trapped within the mucus layer. The mucus provides a physical barrier, while the bicarbonate neutralizes the hydrochloric acid near the surface of the cells, maintaining a near-neutral pH at the epithelial surface. This prevents the acid and the proteolytic enzyme, pepsin, from digesting the stomach wall. A breakdown in this protective mucus-bicarbonate barrier is a primary cause of peptic ulcer disease.

Options: (A) Gastric lipase: This is an enzyme secreted by gastric chief cells that aids in the digestion of triglycerides (fats). It does not have a protective function for the gastric mucosa.

(C) Intrinsic factor: This is a glycoprotein secreted by the parietal cells of the stomach. Its essential function is to bind to vitamin B12, facilitating its absorption in the terminal ileum. It has no role in protecting the gastric lining from ulceration.

(D) Pepsin: This is the primary proteolytic (protein-digesting) enzyme in the stomach. It is an aggressive factor that contributes to the formation of ulcers by digesting the protein structure of the gastric wall if the protective mucosal barrier is compromised. It is the opposite of a protective factor.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Gastric emptying is fastest when food rich in which substances is ingested?
Hyperosmolar substances
Fat
Protein
Carbohydrate

Answer: (D) — Carbohydrate Explanation: The rate of gastric emptying is primarily regulated by feedback mechanisms originating from the duodenum. The goal of this regulation is to ensure that chyme enters the small intestine at a rate that does not overwhelm its digestive and absorptive capacities. Of the major macronutrients, carbohydrates are processed the most rapidly and have the least inhibitory effect on gastric emptying. Liquids containing carbohydrates empty from the stomach faster than solids, and among the macronutrients, carbohydrate-rich meals empty the fastest, followed by protein-rich meals, and finally fat-rich meals, which empty the slowest.

Options: (A) Hyperosmolar substances: Incorrect. Osmoreceptors in the duodenal mucosa are sensitive to the osmolarity of chyme. When hyperosmolar chyme enters the duodenum, it triggers the enterogastric reflex, a neural pathway that inhibits gastric motility and slows down gastric emptying. This allows time for the chyme to be diluted to an isotonic state, preventing rapid fluid shifts in the intestine.

(B) Fat: Incorrect. Fat is the most potent inhibitor of gastric emptying. The presence of fatty acids and monoglycerides in the duodenum stimulates the release of cholecystokinin (CCK) from I-cells. CCK strongly inhibits gastric motility and slows the emptying process. This delay is crucial because the digestion and absorption of fat are complex and time-consuming processes requiring emulsification by bile and breakdown by pancreatic lipase.

(C) Protein: Incorrect. The products of protein digestion (amino acids and peptides) also slow gastric emptying, but less potently than fats. They stimulate the release of CCK and gastrin, and trigger neural reflexes that inhibit gastric motility to allow adequate time for protein digestion by pancreatic proteases in the small intestine.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A child swallows multiple coloured beads. X-ray shows the location of the beads in the stomach. The parents ask how long it might take for the child to pass all the beads. Regarding colonic transit, how long might it take for a total passage of all the beads?
Approximately 24 hours
One week or more
Approximately 3 days
Approximately 5 days

Answer: (D) — Approximately 5 days

Explanation:

The total time for an object to travel through the gastrointestinal (GI) tract is known as the total transit time. This process can be divided into three main phases: gastric emptying, small intestinal transit, and colonic transit.

  1. Gastric Emptying: Indigestible solids, like beads, are emptied from the stomach more slowly than liquids or digestible food. This can take several hours.
  2. Small Intestinal Transit: This phase is relatively rapid, typically taking 3-5 hours.
  3. Colonic Transit: This is the longest and most variable part of the journey. The average colonic transit time in a healthy adult is approximately 30-40 hours but can vary significantly.

The question asks for the time it might take for all the beads to pass. This is an important distinction from the average transit time. While the first bead might pass within 2-3 days, the passage of multiple objects will be spread out over time.

Clinical studies using radiopaque markers (which are analogous to the beads) are used to measure colonic transit time. A common protocol considers it normal for a person to have passed more than 80% of the ingested markers by day 5 (120 hours). This implies that in a healthy individual, the complete clearance of all markers can take up to 5 days. Therefore, telling the parents that it could take approximately 5 days for all beads to pass is a physiologically sound and safe estimate.

Options:

  • (A) Approximately 24 hours: This is incorrect. This timeframe is too short. The average colonic transit time alone is often longer than 24 hours. This might represent the transit time for the fastest components of a liquid meal, but not for multiple indigestible solids.
  • (B) One week or more: This is incorrect for a child with normal GI motility. A transit time of 7 days or more would be considered significantly delayed and is suggestive of a motility disorder, such as constipation or slow-transit constipation. While possible, it is not the expected timeframe.
  • (C) Approximately 3 days: This is a plausible timeframe for the average transit time or for the passage of the majority of the beads. However, it may not be sufficient for the complete clearance of all the ingested objects, as the last few can take longer to pass.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 20 year old with no history of diabetes accidentally receives a dose of insulin. A number of different hormones help combat hypoglycemia. The return of a low blood sugar level to normal is delayed in
Adrenal medullary insuffiency
Growth hormone excess
Thyroid hormone excess
Somatostatin deficiency

Answer: (A) — Adrenal medullary insuffiency

Explanation:

When an individual experiences hypoglycemia (low blood sugar), the body initiates a counter-regulatory hormonal response to restore normal blood glucose levels (euglycemia). The primary hormones involved are glucagon, epinephrine, cortisol, and growth hormone.

  1. Glucagon and Epinephrine are the most important rapid-acting hormones.
    • Glucagon, secreted by pancreatic alpha cells, is the first line of defense. It acts on the liver to stimulate glycogenolysis (breakdown of glycogen into glucose) and gluconeogenesis (synthesis of new glucose).
    • Epinephrine (adrenaline), secreted by the adrenal medulla, is the second line of rapid defense. It has similar effects to glucagon, promoting hepatic glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis. It also inhibits insulin secretion and stimulates lipolysis, which provides substrates for gluconeogenesis.

In a person with adrenal medullary insufficiency, the ability to secrete epinephrine is impaired. While the glucagon response remains the primary defense, the absence of the redundant and potent epinephrine response significantly weakens the body's ability to rapidly counteract hypoglycemia. This loss of a key counter-regulatory hormone delays the return of blood sugar levels to normal.

Options:

  • (B) Growth hormone excess: Growth hormone (GH) is a counter-regulatory hormone itself. It has an anti-insulin effect, decreasing glucose uptake by peripheral tissues. An excess of GH would therefore aid in the correction of hypoglycemia, not delay it.
  • (C) Thyroid hormone excess: Thyroid hormones can increase hepatic glucose production and potentiate the effects of other counter-regulatory hormones like epinephrine. Therefore, an excess would likely accelerate or have a neutral effect on the recovery from hypoglycemia, but it would not delay it.
  • (D) Somatostatin deficiency: Somatostatin is an inhibitory hormone that suppresses the secretion of both insulin and glucagon. In a state of hypoglycemia, glucagon secretion is critical for recovery. A deficiency of somatostatin would lead to disinhibition (i.e., increased secretion) of glucagon, which would accelerate the return to normal blood glucose levels.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which enzyme is primarily responsible for the conversion of T4 to T3 in the peripheral tissues?
D1 thyroid deiodinase
D2 thyroid deiodinase
D3 thyroid deiodinase
D4 thyroid deiodinase

Answer: (B) — D2 thyroid deiodinase

Explanation:

The conversion of thyroxine (T4) to the more biologically active triiodothyronine (T3) occurs mainly in peripheral tissues through the action of enzymes called deiodinases. There are three types of deiodinases (D1, D2, and D3) with distinct locations and functions.

D2 (Type 2) deiodinase is considered the primary enzyme responsible for generating most of the circulating T3 from T4 in euthyroid (normal thyroid function) individuals. It is found in tissues like the brain, pituitary gland, brown adipose tissue, and skeletal muscle. D2 performs outer ring deiodination, a process that specifically converts T4 into the active T3. This local production of T3 within tissues like the pituitary is crucial for the negative feedback regulation of the hypothalamic-pituitary-thyroid (HPT) axis.

Options:

  • (A) D1 thyroid deiodinase: Incorrect. While D1, located primarily in the liver, kidneys, and thyroid gland, also converts T4 to T3, it is not considered the primary source in the euthyroid state. Its contribution to circulating T3 becomes more significant during hyperthyroidism. D1 can perform both outer ring (activating) and inner ring (inactivating) deiodination.
  • (C) D3 thyroid deiodinase: Incorrect. D3 is an inactivating enzyme. It primarily performs inner ring deiodination, which converts T4 to reverse T3 (rT3) and T3 to T2, both of which are biologically inactive. Its role is to protect tissues from excessive thyroid hormone action, which is particularly important in the placenta and developing fetal brain.
  • (D) D4 thyroid deiodinase: Incorrect. A D4 deiodinase has not been identified or characterized in human physiology. This option is a distractor.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 24-year-old gets confirmation of her pregnancy by a serum bHCG. Regarding human chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
hCG is absolutely specific for pregnancy
It can be detected in blood as early as 3 days after conception
hCG primarily has luteinizing and luteotropic activity.
It can be detected in urine as early as 6 days after conception

Answer: (C) — hCG primarily has luteinizing and luteotropic activity.

Explanation:

Human chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) is a glycoprotein hormone produced by the syncytiotrophoblast cells of the placenta following implantation. Its primary and most critical role in early pregnancy is to "rescue" the corpus luteum, the structure that remains in the ovary after ovulation.

  • Structural Similarity to LH: hCG is structurally very similar to Luteinizing Hormone (LH), sharing the same alpha subunit and having a beta subunit that closely resembles that of LH.
  • Luteotropic Activity: Due to this similarity, hCG binds to LH receptors on the corpus luteum. This action prevents the corpus luteum from its normal process of degradation (luteolysis).
  • Progesterone Production: By maintaining the corpus luteum, hCG ensures the continued and increasing production of progesterone. Progesterone is essential for maintaining the uterine lining (endometrium) and creating a supportive environment for the developing embryo.
  • This function continues until the placenta is sufficiently developed to take over as the primary source of progesterone, which occurs around the 8th to 10th week of gestation. Therefore, hCG's main function is luteotropic (supporting the corpus luteum) and luteinizing (acting like LH).

Options:

  • (A) hCG is absolutely specific for pregnancy: This is incorrect. While hCG is the primary marker for pregnancy, elevated levels can also be caused by pathological conditions. These include gestational trophoblastic diseases (like molar pregnancies and choriocarcinoma), certain germ cell tumors (e.g., testicular or ovarian cancer), and some non-trophoblastic cancers (e.g., lung, breast). Low levels can also be detected in perimenopausal and postmenopausal women due to pituitary production.

  • (B) It can be detected in blood as early as 3 days after conception: This is incorrect. hCG production begins only after the blastocyst implants into the uterine wall. Implantation typically occurs 6 to 12 days after conception (fertilization). Therefore, hCG is usually detectable in maternal serum approximately 8 to 11 days after conception, not as early as 3 days.

  • (D) It can be detected in urine as early as 6 days after conception: This is incorrect. For hCG to be detected in urine, it must first reach a sufficient concentration in the bloodstream to be filtered by the kidneys. This occurs after it is detectable in the blood. Given that implantation happens around day 6-12, and blood detection follows, urine detection is not possible as early as 6 days post-conception. Most sensitive home urine tests can detect hCG around the time of the first missed period (about 14 days after conception).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Among the conditions that increase aldosterone secretion, which one of the following does not affect glucocorticoid secretion
Anxiety
Low sodium intake
Surgery
Hemorrhage

Answer: (B) — Low sodium intake Explanation: The secretion of aldosterone and glucocorticoids (primarily cortisol) is regulated by different primary mechanisms. Aldosterone secretion from the zona glomerulosa of the adrenal cortex is mainly controlled by the Renin-Angiotensin-Aldosterone System (RAAS) and plasma potassium concentration. Glucocorticoid secretion from the zona fasciculata is primarily controlled by the Hypothalamic-Pituitary-Adrenal (HPA) axis via ACTH.

Low sodium intake leads to a decrease in extracellular fluid volume and reduced sodium delivery to the macula densa in the kidneys. This is a powerful stimulus for the release of renin, which activates the RAAS, leading to the production of angiotensin II. Angiotensin II then potently stimulates the adrenal cortex to secrete aldosterone. This is a specific physiological response to maintain sodium balance and blood volume and does not typically activate the HPA axis. Therefore, low sodium intake increases aldosterone secretion without a significant effect on glucocorticoid secretion.

Options: (A) Anxiety: Anxiety is a psychological stressor. Stress is a potent activator of the HPA axis, leading to the release of CRH, then ACTH, which stimulates glucocorticoid secretion. The stress response also activates the sympathetic nervous system, which can increase renin release and thus aldosterone secretion. Therefore, anxiety increases both.

(C) Surgery: Surgery is a major physical stressor. It causes a robust activation of the HPA axis, leading to a significant increase in ACTH and glucocorticoids. The stress, potential fluid loss, and inflammatory response during surgery also activate the RAAS, thereby increasing aldosterone secretion. Therefore, surgery increases both.

(D) Hemorrhage: Hemorrhage (blood loss) causes a drop in blood volume and blood pressure. This is a very strong stimulus for the RAAS, leading to high levels of aldosterone to promote sodium and water retention. Hemorrhage is also a severe physiological stressor that strongly activates the HPA axis, resulting in increased glucocorticoid secretion. Therefore, hemorrhage increases both.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A patient has localised spinal pathology causing impingement onto the spinal cord. Examination reveals a unilateral paresis, hyperreflexia and hypertonia. Based on this information, which part of the spinal cord is affected?
Contralateral posterolateral quadrant
Contralateral anterolateral quadrant
Ipsilateral posterolateral quadrant
Ipsilateral anterolateral quadrant

Answer: (C) — Ipsilateral posterolateral quadrant

Explanation:

The patient's clinical presentation of unilateral paresis (weakness), hyperreflexia (exaggerated reflexes), and hypertonia (increased muscle tone) are classic signs of an Upper Motor Neuron (UMN) lesion.

The primary UMN pathway that controls voluntary motor function is the corticospinal tract. This tract originates in the cerebral cortex, descends through the brainstem, and travels down the spinal cord. Crucially, about 85-90% of the corticospinal fibers decussate (cross over to the opposite side) at the level of the medullary pyramids. These crossed fibers then descend in the posterolateral quadrant of the spinal cord as the lateral corticospinal tract.

Because the decussation occurs above the spinal cord, a lesion within the spinal cord that impinges on the lateral corticospinal tract will cause motor deficits on the same side (ipsilateral) as the lesion, but below the level of the impingement. Therefore, an ipsilateral posterolateral quadrant lesion explains the patient's ipsilateral UMN signs.

Options:

  • (A) Contralateral posterolateral quadrant: Incorrect. A lesion in this quadrant would affect the corticospinal tract, but because the fibers have already crossed in the medulla, the resulting motor signs would be on the same side (ipsilateral) as the lesion, not the opposite (contralateral) side.
  • (B) Contralateral anterolateral quadrant: Incorrect. This quadrant primarily contains the ascending spinothalamic tract, which carries pain and temperature sensation. A lesion here would cause a contralateral loss of pain and temperature sensation. While it also contains some motor tracts (like the anterior corticospinal tract), a lesion would not produce the profound UMN signs described, which are characteristic of a lateral corticospinal tract lesion.
  • (D) Ipsilateral anterolateral quadrant: Incorrect. As mentioned above, this quadrant contains the spinothalamic tract. A lesion here would primarily cause a contralateral loss of pain and temperature sensation because the spinothalamic tract decussates at the level of the spinal cord. It does not contain the major motor pathway responsible for the observed signs.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A patient with a blood group of AB positive is undergoing plasma exchange. Which one of the following donor plasma type is compatible?
B positive
O positive
O negative
AB negative

Answer: (D) — AB negative

Explanation:

The key principle in plasma transfusion is that the donor's plasma must not contain antibodies that will react with the recipient's red blood cell (RBC) antigens. This is the reverse of red blood cell transfusion, where the recipient's plasma antibodies must not react with the donor's RBC antigens.

A patient with AB positive blood has the following characteristics: * RBC Antigens: A, B, and Rh(D) antigens. * Plasma Antibodies: No anti-A, anti-B, or anti-Rh(D) antibodies.

Since the recipient has A and B antigens on their RBCs, the donor plasma must not contain anti-A or anti-B antibodies.

  • Donor AB Plasma: Individuals with blood group AB do not produce anti-A or anti-B antibodies. Therefore, their plasma is safe to give to recipients of any ABO group (A, B, AB, or O). This makes group AB the universal plasma donor.
  • Rh Factor: The patient is Rh positive, meaning they have the D antigen. The donor is Rh negative. Rh-negative plasma is safe for an Rh-positive recipient.

Therefore, plasma from an AB negative donor is compatible.

Options:

  • (A) B positive: Incorrect. A person with group B blood has anti-A antibodies in their plasma. Transfusing this plasma into an AB patient would cause the donor's anti-A antibodies to attack the recipient's A antigens on their RBCs, leading to a hemolytic transfusion reaction.
  • (B) O positive: Incorrect. A person with group O blood has both anti-A and anti-B antibodies in their plasma. Transfusing this plasma would cause a severe hemolytic reaction as both antibody types would attack the recipient's A and B antigens.
  • (C) O negative: Incorrect. Similar to O positive, a person with group O negative blood has both anti-A and anti-B antibodies in their plasma, making it incompatible.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The penetration of substances into the brain is most rapid for:
Oxygen, carbodioxide and water
Glucose
Urea
Na+ and K+

Answer: (A) — Oxygen, carbon dioxide and water

Explanation:

The blood-brain barrier (BBB) is a highly selective semipermeable membrane that regulates the passage of substances from the bloodstream into the central nervous system. The rate of penetration is primarily determined by the substance's molecular size and lipid solubility.

Oxygen (O₂), carbon dioxide (CO₂), and water (H₂O) cross the BBB with extreme rapidity. * O₂ and CO₂: These are small, lipid-soluble gases. They can easily and rapidly diffuse across the lipid cell membranes of the brain's capillary endothelial cells, following their partial pressure gradients. This rapid exchange is crucial for maintaining brain metabolism and pH. * Water (H₂O): Although a polar molecule, water is very small and can move freely across the BBB through both simple diffusion across the lipid bilayer and via specific water channels called aquaporins (especially Aquaporin-4).

Options:

  • (B) Glucose: Glucose is the brain's primary energy source, but it is a large, polar molecule that cannot diffuse freely across the BBB. It requires a specific carrier-mediated transport system, the glucose transporter 1 (GLUT1), to enter the brain via facilitated diffusion. While this process is efficient and vital, it is slower than the simple diffusion of gases and water and can be saturated.
  • (C) Urea: Urea is a small, water-soluble molecule and a metabolic waste product. Its passage across the BBB is very slow because it has low lipid solubility and lacks specific transporters. The BBB effectively restricts its entry to protect the brain.
  • (D) Na+ and K+: Sodium (Na⁺) and potassium (K⁺) are ions. Due to their electrical charge, they cannot diffuse across the lipid membranes of the BBB. Their transport is tightly regulated by specific ion channels and active transport pumps (e.g., Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase) to maintain the precise ionic environment necessary for neuronal function. This transport is slow and meticulously controlled, not rapid.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The amount of air exhaled in one breath during relaxed quiet breathing.is:
Residual volume
Tidal volume
Inspiratory reserve volume
Expiratory reserve volume

Answer: (B) — Tidal volume

Explanation: Tidal volume (TV or VT) is the volume of air that is inspired or expired during a single, normal, quiet breath (eupnea). In a healthy young adult at rest, the tidal volume is approximately 500 mL per breath or about 7 mL/kg of body mass. It represents the normal depth of breathing.

Options:

  • Residual volume (RV): This is the volume of air remaining in the lungs after a maximal, forceful exhalation. This air cannot be voluntarily expelled and serves to keep the alveoli open, preventing lung collapse.
  • Inspiratory reserve volume (IRV): This is the additional volume of air that can be forcefully inhaled after a normal tidal inspiration. It represents the reserve capacity for deeper inhalation.
  • Expiratory reserve volume (ERV): This is the additional volume of air that can be forcefully exhaled after a normal tidal expiration. It is the amount of air one can still push out after a quiet breath out.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The bronchi constrict in all of the following situations EXCEPT:
Cool air
Inspiration
Exercise
Expiration

Answer: (B) — Inspiration

Explanation:

Bronchial diameter is dynamically regulated by both mechanical forces and the autonomic nervous system to optimize airflow. During inspiration, the thoracic cavity and the lungs expand. This expansion exerts an outward pulling force on the walls of the bronchi and bronchioles, a phenomenon known as radial traction. This mechanical force physically pulls the airways open, increasing their diameter (bronchodilation) and decreasing airway resistance, which facilitates the inflow of air. This is a passive dilation that occurs with every normal breath.

Options:

  • (A) Cool air: Inhaling cool, and often dry, air is a well-known trigger for bronchoconstriction. The cooling and dehydration of the airway lining (mucosa) can stimulate mast cells to release inflammatory mediators like histamine and leukotrienes. These substances are potent bronchoconstrictors, causing the smooth muscle in the airway walls to contract. This is a protective reflex intended to warm and humidify air before it reaches the delicate alveoli.

  • (C) Exercise: The response of the airways to exercise is complex. In a healthy individual, the sympathetic nervous system is activated, leading to the release of epinephrine, which stimulates β2-adrenergic receptors on bronchial smooth muscle, causing relaxation and bronchodilation to meet the body's increased demand for oxygen. However, the rapid, deep breathing (hyperpnea) during exercise can lead to airway cooling and water loss, which is a powerful stimulus for bronchoconstriction, particularly in individuals with asthma (a condition known as exercise-induced bronchoconstriction). Because exercise involves a potent stimulus for constriction, it is not the correct exception.

  • (D) Expiration: During expiration, the lungs recoil to a smaller volume. This reduces the radial traction that held the airways open during inspiration, allowing them to passively narrow. During forced expiration, the increase in intrathoracic pressure can also physically compress the airways (dynamic airway compression), further increasing airway resistance and causing constriction.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
What is the partial pressure of oxygen in the alveoli when breathing air, if the respiratory quotient is 0.8 and the arterial partial pressure of carbon dioxide is 70mmHg?
66
76
54
42

Answer: (A) — 66

Explanation:

This question requires the use of the Alveolar Gas Equation to calculate the partial pressure of oxygen in the alveoli (PAO2). This equation describes the relationship between the inspired oxygen and the alveolar levels of oxygen and carbon dioxide.

The Alveolar Gas Equation is: PAO2 = [FiO2 × (PB - PH2O)] - (PaCO2 / R)

Let's break down each component: * PAO2: Partial pressure of O2 in the alveoli (what we need to find). * FiO2: Fraction of inspired oxygen. For "breathing air," this is 21% or 0.21. * PB: Barometric pressure. This is not provided, so we assume the standard value at sea level, which is 760 mmHg. * PH2O: Partial pressure of water vapor in the saturated air of the airways at body temperature (37°C). This is a constant value of 47 mmHg. * PaCO2: Partial pressure of CO2 in arterial blood. The question gives this as 70 mmHg. In a healthy state, PaCO2 is an excellent approximation of alveolar PCO2 (PACO2). * R: Respiratory quotient (RQ), which is the ratio of CO2 produced to O2 consumed. The question gives this as 0.8.

Now, we can plug these values into the equation:

  1. Calculate the inspired partial pressure of oxygen (PiO2) in humidified tracheal air: PiO2 = FiO2 × (PB - PH2O) PiO2 = 0.21 × (760 mmHg - 47 mmHg) PiO2 = 0.21 × 713 mmHg PiO2 ≈ 150 mmHg

  2. Calculate the reduction in PAO2 due to CO2 entering the alveoli: Reduction = PaCO2 / R Reduction = 70 mmHg / 0.8 Reduction = 87.5 mmHg

  3. Calculate the final PAO2: PAO2 = PiO2 - (PaCO2 / R) PAO2 = 150 mmHg - 87.5 mmHg PAO2 = 62.5 mmHg

The calculated value is 62.5 mmHg. Among the given options, 66 mmHg is the closest answer. Such small discrepancies in multiple-choice questions can arise from rounding conventions or slight variations in the assumed "standard" values used by the question designer. Given the options, 66 is the intended correct answer.

The high PaCO2 (70 mmHg, normal ≈ 40 mmHg) indicates significant alveolar hypoventilation, which correctly results in a low calculated PAO2 (normal ≈ 100 mmHg).

Options:

  • (A) 66: This is the closest value to our calculated PAO2 of 62.5 mmHg and is the correct choice.
  • (B) 76: This value is incorrect. To get a PAO2 of 76 mmHg, the PaCO2 would need to be approximately 59 mmHg (150 - 76 = 74; 74 × 0.8 = 59.2), which contradicts the given information.
  • (C) 54: This value is too low. It would imply a PaCO2 of about 77 mmHg (150 - 54 = 96; 96 × 0.8 = 76.8), which is significantly different from the given value.
  • (D) 42: This value is far too low and would correspond to an even higher level of CO2 or a lower barometric pressure than standard assumptions.

Confidence: 95% (High confidence in the calculation method; the slight difference between the calculated value and the option is a common feature in exam questions).

196


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A patient presents to ED with supraventricular tachycardia. As part of the treatment, you attempt a valsalva manoeuvre. Which one of the following statements is CORRECT regarding valsalva manoeuvre?
It is followed by a brief period of hypertension and slowing of heart rate
In patients with autonomic insufficiency, the heart rate changes are exaggerated
Sympathectomised patients show no change in heart rate or blood pressure
At the onset, blood pressure falls slightly

Answer: (A) — It is followed by a brief period of hypertension and slowing of heart rate

Explanation:

The Valsalva maneuver is a physiological test that involves forced expiration against a closed glottis. It induces a sequence of changes in heart rate (HR) and blood pressure (BP) mediated by the baroreflex and autonomic nervous system. The maneuver is typically divided into four phases:

  • Phase I (Onset of strain): The increase in intrathoracic pressure compresses the aorta and great vessels, causing a transient rise in arterial blood pressure.
  • Phase II (Maintained strain): The high intrathoracic pressure impedes venous return to the heart, leading to a decrease in cardiac output and a subsequent fall in blood pressure. The baroreflex detects this hypotension and responds by increasing sympathetic outflow, which causes peripheral vasoconstriction and an increase in heart rate (reflex tachycardia).
  • Phase III (Release of strain): The sudden release of intrathoracic pressure causes a brief, transient drop in blood pressure as the external compression on the aorta is removed.
  • Phase IV (Recovery/Overshoot): Venous return and cardiac output rapidly increase into the constricted peripheral vascular bed (from Phase II). This leads to a significant rise in blood pressure, overshooting the baseline level (hypertension). The baroreceptors detect this hypertension and trigger a strong reflex increase in vagal (parasympathetic) tone, resulting in a marked slowing of the heart rate (reflex bradycardia).

This reflex bradycardia in Phase IV is the therapeutic mechanism that can terminate atrioventricular nodal re-entrant tachycardia (AVNRT), a common type of SVT, by increasing refractoriness in the AV node. Therefore, the statement that the maneuver is followed by hypertension and slowing of the heart rate is correct, describing Phase IV.

Options:

  • (B) In patients with autonomic insufficiency, the heart rate changes are exaggerated: This is incorrect. In patients with autonomic failure (e.g., Shy-Drager syndrome), the baroreflex is impaired. As a result, the reflex heart rate changes (tachycardia in Phase II and bradycardia in Phase IV) are blunted or absent. The blood pressure drop in Phase II is often more profound and prolonged because the compensatory sympathetic response is lacking.
  • (C) Sympathectomised patients show no change in heart rate or blood pressure: This is incorrect. A sympathectomized patient would still experience the mechanical effects of the maneuver on blood pressure. However, the compensatory responses would be altered. They would lack the reflex tachycardia and vasoconstriction in Phase II, leading to a more severe drop in blood pressure. They would also lack the hypertensive overshoot in Phase IV, and consequently, the reflex bradycardia would be blunted or absent. Thus, significant changes in BP still occur, just not in the normal pattern.
  • (D) At the onset, blood pressure falls slightly: This is incorrect. At the onset of the strain (Phase I), the increased intrathoracic pressure mechanically compresses the aorta, causing a transient increase in blood pressure, not a fall. A slight fall in blood pressure occurs in Phase III (at the release of strain) and a more significant fall occurs during Phase II (maintained strain).

Confidence: 100%

197


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which one of the following has a positive inotropic effect?
Theophylline
Hypoxia
Hypercapnia
Acidosis

Answer: (A) — Theophylline

Explanation:

A positive inotropic effect refers to an increase in the force of myocardial contraction. Theophylline, a methylxanthine drug, exerts a positive inotropic effect primarily by inhibiting phosphodiesterase (PDE) enzymes, specifically PDE3, within cardiac muscle cells.

The mechanism is as follows: 1. PDE Inhibition: Phosphodiesterase is responsible for the breakdown of cyclic adenosine monophosphate (cAMP). By inhibiting PDE, theophylline causes an increase in the intracellular concentration of cAMP. 2. Protein Kinase A (PKA) Activation: Increased cAMP levels lead to the activation of Protein Kinase A (PKA). 3. Phosphorylation of Key Proteins: PKA phosphorylates several target proteins that enhance contractility: * L-type Calcium Channels: Phosphorylation increases calcium influx into the cell during the plateau phase of the cardiac action potential. * Phospholamban: Phosphorylation of phospholamban removes its inhibitory effect on the sarcoplasmic reticulum Ca²⁺-ATPase (SERCA) pump. This accelerates the reuptake of calcium into the sarcoplasmic reticulum (SR), which not only improves relaxation (lusitropy) but also increases the amount of calcium stored in the SR available for release during the next contraction.

The net result is a greater availability of intracellular calcium for binding to troponin C, leading to more forceful cross-bridge cycling and a stronger myocardial contraction.

Options:

  • (B) Hypoxia: Hypoxia, or a lack of oxygen, has a negative inotropic effect. The heart muscle relies heavily on aerobic metabolism to produce the large amounts of ATP required for contraction and for the function of ion pumps (like SERCA and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase). Insufficient oxygen impairs ATP production, leading to a weaker force of contraction.
  • (C) Hypercapnia: Hypercapnia, an excess of carbon dioxide (CO₂) in the blood, leads to respiratory acidosis. CO₂ readily diffuses into myocardial cells, where it combines with water to form carbonic acid (H₂CO₃), which then dissociates into H⁺ and HCO₃⁻. The resulting increase in intracellular H⁺ concentration (acidosis) has a negative inotropic effect.
  • (D) Acidosis: Acidosis (a decrease in pH or an increase in H⁺ concentration) has a potent negative inotropic effect. The primary mechanism is the competition between H⁺ ions and Ca²⁺ ions for the same binding sites on troponin C. This competition reduces the sensitivity of the myofilaments to calcium, meaning that for a given intracellular calcium concentration, less force is generated.

Confidence: 100%

198


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 24-year-old develops an arrhythmia with a heart rate of about 220. In normal physiological conditions and assuming ample venous return, what is the heart rate at which ventricular filling starts to become compromised?
160
180
200
220

Answer: (180)

Explanation:

The relationship between heart rate (HR), ventricular filling, and cardiac output (CO) is a fundamental concept in cardiac physiology. Ventricular filling, which determines the end-diastolic volume (EDV) and thus the stroke volume (SV) via the Frank-Starling mechanism, occurs almost entirely during diastole (the relaxation phase of the cardiac cycle).

As the heart rate increases, the total duration of the cardiac cycle shortens. However, this shortening is not distributed equally between systole (contraction) and diastole (relaxation). Diastole shortens disproportionately more than systole.

  • At rest (e.g., HR 75 bpm): The cardiac cycle lasts ~800 ms, with diastole taking up a significant portion (~500 ms), allowing ample time for ventricular filling.
  • With increasing HR: The time available for diastole progressively decreases. Initially, the increase in HR leads to a higher cardiac output (CO = HR x SV), as the heart beats more frequently.
  • Critical Threshold: There is a critical heart rate beyond which the diastolic filling time becomes so short that ventricular filling is significantly impaired. This leads to a decrease in EDV and, consequently, a fall in stroke volume. At this point, the decrease in SV is so pronounced that it can no longer be compensated for by the high heart rate, causing the overall cardiac output to decline.

For a healthy young adult, this critical threshold is generally considered to be around 180 beats per minute. Beyond this rate, the diastolic period is too brief for adequate filling, leading to compromised hemodynamics.

Options:

  • (160): Incorrect. At a heart rate of 160 bpm, while diastolic time is reduced, a healthy heart can typically still maintain adequate filling and stroke volume, especially with good venous return (as stipulated in the question). Cardiac output is often at or near its peak at this rate.
  • (180): Correct. This is the widely accepted physiological threshold at which diastolic filling time becomes critically limited, causing ventricular filling to be compromised and leading to a potential fall in stroke volume and cardiac output.
  • (200): Incorrect. At a heart rate of 200 bpm, ventricular filling is already severely compromised, not just starting to be. Cardiac output would likely be significantly reduced compared to its peak.
  • (220): Incorrect. This heart rate, as seen in the patient, represents a state of severe tachyarrhythmia where ventricular filling is critically insufficient, often leading to symptoms like dizziness, syncope, or heart failure due to a sharp drop in cardiac output.

Confidence: 95%

199


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Regarding the normal ECG intervals and events in the heart during those intervals, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
During PR interval, there is ventricular depolarisation
During T wave, there is ventricular depolarisation
During QT interval, there is ventricular repolarisation only
During QRS complex, there is atrial repolarisation

Answer: (D) — During QRS complex, there is atrial repolarisation

Explanation:

The electrocardiogram (ECG) is a recording of the heart's electrical activity. The QRS complex represents the rapid depolarization of the right and left ventricles. This is a large electrical event due to the significant muscle mass of the ventricles. Simultaneously, the atria are undergoing repolarization (recovering electrically from their initial depolarization, which was the P wave). However, the electrical signal of atrial repolarization (sometimes called the Ta wave) is much smaller in amplitude than the massive QRS complex and is therefore obscured or masked by it on a standard ECG tracing. Thus, it is physiologically correct that atrial repolarization occurs during the time of the QRS complex.

Options:

  • During PR interval, there is ventricular depolarisation: This is incorrect. The PR interval represents the time from the start of atrial depolarization (P wave) to the start of ventricular depolarization (QRS complex). It includes the P wave (atrial depolarization) and the PR segment (the delay as the impulse travels through the atrioventricular node). Ventricular depolarization is represented by the QRS complex, which occurs after the PR interval.
  • During T wave, there is ventricular depolarisation: This is incorrect. The T wave represents ventricular repolarization, which is the electrical recovery phase of the ventricles after they have contracted. Ventricular depolarization is represented by the QRS complex.
  • During QT interval, there is ventricular repolarisation only: This is incorrect. The QT interval measures the total duration of ventricular electrical activity. It starts at the beginning of the QRS complex and ends at the end of the T wave. Therefore, it encompasses both ventricular depolarization (the QRS complex) and ventricular repolarization (the ST segment and T wave).

Confidence:

100%

200


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Regarding carotid baroreceptors, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
The afferent fibres pass via the vagus nerve
The receptors are located in the intima of the vessels
The baroreceptors discharge at an increased rate when the blood pressure rises
The efferent fibres pass via the glossopharyngeal nerve

Answer: (C) — The baroreceptors discharge at an increased rate when the blood pressure rises

Explanation:

The baroreceptor reflex is a critical short-term mechanism for regulating arterial blood pressure. Baroreceptors are mechanoreceptors (stretch receptors) located in the walls of the carotid sinus and the aortic arch. Their function is to sense changes in blood pressure by detecting the degree of stretch in the arterial wall.

When arterial blood pressure increases, it stretches the vessel walls more. This increased stretch depolarizes the baroreceptor nerve endings, leading to an increase in the frequency of action potentials (i.e., an increased discharge rate) sent to the brainstem. This information signals the cardiovascular control center (in the medulla oblongata) to initiate a reflex response to lower the blood pressure, which involves decreasing sympathetic outflow and increasing parasympathetic outflow to the heart and blood vessels. Conversely, a drop in blood pressure reduces the stretch, decreases the firing rate, and triggers a reflex to raise the blood pressure.

Options:

  • (A) The afferent fibres pass via the vagus nerve: This is incorrect. The afferent (sensory) nerve fibers from the carotid sinus baroreceptors travel via Hering's nerve, which is a branch of the glossopharyngeal nerve (Cranial Nerve IX). The afferent fibers from the aortic arch baroreceptors travel via the vagus nerve (Cranial Nerve X).
  • (B) The receptors are located in the intima of the vessels: This is incorrect. The baroreceptor nerve endings are primarily located in the tunica adventitia (the outermost connective tissue layer) of the arteries, with some endings extending into the tunica media. They are not found in the tunica intima (the innermost endothelial layer).
  • (D) The efferent fibres pass via the glossopharyngeal nerve: This is incorrect. The glossopharyngeal nerve carries afferent (sensory) signals from the carotid baroreceptors to the brainstem. The efferent (motor) pathways of the baroreflex involve the parasympathetic system (via the vagus nerve to the heart) and the sympathetic nervous system (via sympathetic ganglia to the heart and blood vessels).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Depolarisation initiated in the SA node gets sequentially conducted to the entire heart. All of the following parts of the heart are last to receive depolarisation EXCEPT:
Posterobasal portion of the left ventricle
Pulmonary conus
Apex of the heart
Uppermost portion of septum

Answer: (C) — Apex of the heart Explanation: The cardiac electrical impulse originates in the sinoatrial (SA) node and spreads through the atria to the atrioventricular (AV) node. After a brief delay at the AV node, the impulse travels rapidly down the Bundle of His, which divides into the right and left bundle branches. These branches carry the impulse toward the apex of the heart. The Purkinje fibers then distribute the impulse from the endocardial (inner) surface to the epicardial (outer) surface of the ventricles. The general sequence of ventricular depolarization is: 1. The interventricular septum (depolarizing from left to right). 2. The apex of the heart. 3. The free walls of the ventricles, spreading upwards from the apex towards the base.

Therefore, the last parts of the ventricles to depolarize are the superior and posterior basal regions. The apex of the heart is depolarized relatively early in the sequence, well before the basal portions.

Options: * (A) Posterobasal portion of the left ventricle: This is one of the last regions to be depolarized because the wave of excitation travels from the apex upwards to the base of the heart. * (B) Pulmonary conus: This is the outflow tract of the right ventricle, located at the superior-anterior base of the heart. It is also one of the last parts to be depolarized. * (D) Uppermost portion of septum: While the mid-septum is depolarized first, the wave of activation reaches the superior or uppermost portion of the septum (near the base) late in the cycle, making it one of the last areas to depolarize.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 40-year-old male sustains a laceration over the dorsum of the foot extending to the web space between the third and fourth toes. Nerve block to which one of the following nerves would provide anaesthesia to this region?
Deep fibular nerve
Superficial fibular nerve
Lateral plantar nerve
Medial plantar nerve

Answer: (B) — Superficial fibular nerve

Explanation:

The sensory innervation of the dorsum of the foot is primarily provided by the superficial fibular (or superficial peroneal) nerve. This nerve descends in the lateral compartment of the leg and emerges as a cutaneous nerve in the distal third. It then divides into the medial and intermediate dorsal cutaneous nerves. The intermediate dorsal cutaneous nerve specifically supplies the skin over the dorsum of the foot and the dorsal aspects of the third and fourth toes, including the web space between them. Therefore, a block of the superficial fibular nerve would anesthetize the area of the laceration described.

Options:

  • (A) Deep fibular nerve: This is incorrect. While the deep fibular (peroneal) nerve does provide a small area of sensory innervation on the dorsum of the foot, it is limited to the web space between the first (great) and second toes. Its primary function is motor innervation to the muscles of the anterior compartment of the leg (e.g., tibialis anterior, extensor hallucis longus).

  • (C) Lateral plantar nerve: This is incorrect. The lateral plantar nerve is a terminal branch of the tibial nerve and provides sensory innervation to the sole (plantar aspect) of the foot, specifically the lateral one and a half toes and the corresponding sole area. The injury is on the dorsum.

  • (D) Medial plantar nerve: This is incorrect. The medial plantar nerve is also a terminal branch of the tibial nerve and supplies the sole (plantar aspect) of the foot, specifically the medial three and a half toes and the corresponding sole area. The injury is on the dorsum.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The action potential of skeletal muscle:
Has a prolonged plateau phase
Is longer than the action potential of the cardiac muslce
Spreads inwards to all parts of the muscle via the T tubules
Is not essential for contraction

Answer: (C) — Spreads inwards to all parts of the muscle via the T tubules

Explanation:

The action potential in skeletal muscle is the electrical signal that initiates muscle contraction. This process is known as excitation-contraction coupling. After being generated at the neuromuscular junction, the action potential propagates along the muscle cell membrane, called the sarcolemma. To ensure a rapid and uniform contraction of the entire muscle fiber, this electrical signal must penetrate deep into the cell's interior. This is achieved via the transverse tubules (T-tubules), which are invaginations of the sarcolemma that extend into the sarcoplasm. As the action potential travels down the T-tubules, it triggers the release of Ca²⁺ from the adjacent terminal cisternae of the sarcoplasmic reticulum, leading to muscle contraction.

Options:

  • (A) Has a prolonged plateau phase: This is incorrect. A prolonged plateau phase is a characteristic feature of the cardiac ventricular muscle action potential, not skeletal muscle. The plateau is caused by a slow influx of Ca²⁺ ions, which prolongs the depolarization and the refractory period, preventing tetanus in the heart. Skeletal muscle action potentials are very brief, lasting only about 1-5 milliseconds, and lack a plateau phase.

  • (B) Is longer than the action potential of the cardiac muscle: This is incorrect. The skeletal muscle action potential (1-5 ms) is significantly shorter than the cardiac ventricular muscle action potential (200-300 ms). The long duration of the cardiac action potential is due to the plateau phase described above.

  • (D) Is not essential for contraction: This is incorrect. The action potential is absolutely essential for initiating skeletal muscle contraction. It is the critical "excitation" step in excitation-contraction coupling that leads to the release of calcium and subsequent cross-bridge cycling. Without an action potential, the muscle fiber would not receive the signal to contract.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The diameter of a somatic motor nerve fibre (Aα fibre type) is:
3-5 micrometre
1-2 micrometre
5-12 micrometre
12-20 micrometre

Answer: (D) — 12-20 micrometre

Explanation:

Nerve fibers are classified based on their diameter, myelination status, and conduction velocity. The Erlanger-Gasser classification system is commonly used for this purpose. Somatic motor nerve fibers, which innervate skeletal muscles, are classified as Aα (alpha) fibers.

Aα fibers are the largest and most heavily myelinated nerve fibers in the peripheral nervous system. Their large diameter and thick myelin sheath are crucial for their function, as these properties allow for very rapid conduction of action potentials (saltatory conduction) at speeds of 70-120 m/s. This high speed is essential for the quick and precise control of voluntary muscle movements. The characteristic diameter of Aα fibers is in the range of 12-20 micrometres (µm).

Options:

  • A) 3-5 micrometre: This diameter range is characteristic of Aγ (gamma) fibers, which are motor neurons that innervate intrafusal muscle fibers within muscle spindles, or Aδ (delta) fibers, which are sensory fibers that transmit information about fast, sharp pain and temperature.
  • B) 1-2 micrometre: This small diameter is typical of C fibers, which are unmyelinated and transmit slow, dull pain, temperature, and serve as postganglionic autonomic fibers. It can also include some small, myelinated B fibers (preganglionic autonomic).
  • C) 5-12 micrometre: This diameter corresponds to Aβ (beta) fibers. These are myelinated sensory fibers responsible for transmitting sensations of touch, pressure, and vibration from the skin and joints.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
The normal plasma osmolality is:
270
320
290
310

Answer: (C) — 290

Explanation: Plasma osmolality is a measure of the concentration of all solutes (such as sodium, chloride, bicarbonate, glucose, and urea) dissolved in the plasma portion of the blood. The normal physiological range for plasma osmolality is approximately 285-295 mOsm/kg H₂O. The value of 290 mOsm/kg falls squarely within this range and is the most commonly cited single value for normal plasma osmolality.

The body tightly regulates plasma osmolality, primarily through the actions of antidiuretic hormone (ADH) and the thirst mechanism, to maintain cellular homeostasis. The major contributors to plasma osmolality can be estimated using the following formula:

Calculated Osmolality = (2 x [Na⁺]) + ([Glucose]/18) + ([BUN]/2.8)

Where concentrations are in mg/dL for glucose and BUN, and mEq/L for sodium. Sodium is the primary determinant of plasma osmolality.

Options: * (A) 270: This value is below the normal range and would be considered hypo-osmolar. A state of hypo-osmolality can lead to cellular swelling and is seen in conditions like the syndrome of inappropriate antidiuretic hormone (SIADH) or excessive water intake (water intoxication). * (B) 320: This value is significantly above the normal range, indicating a hyper-osmolar state. This can be caused by dehydration (water loss), hyperglycemia (as in diabetic ketoacidosis or hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state), or uremia (high BUN). * (D) 310: This value is also above the normal range and represents a hyper-osmolar state, which can cause water to shift out of cells, leading to cellular dehydration.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following immunosuppressant agents is nephrotoxic?
Mycophenalate
Tacrolimus
Azathioprine
Cyclophosphamide

Answer: (B) — Tacrolimus Explanation: Tacrolimus is a calcineurin inhibitor, a class of drugs that also includes cyclosporine. Calcineurin inhibitors are cornerstone immunosuppressants, particularly in solid organ transplantation. However, their use is significantly limited by their major adverse effect: nephrotoxicity (kidney damage). This toxicity can be both acute, characterized by reversible afferent arteriolar vasoconstriction leading to a decrease in renal blood flow and glomerular filtration rate (GFR), and chronic, leading to irreversible interstitial fibrosis and tubular atrophy. Due to this well-established risk, patients on tacrolimus require close monitoring of their kidney function and drug trough levels.

Options: (A) Mycophenolate: Mycophenolate mofetil is an inhibitor of inosine monophosphate dehydrogenase, primarily affecting lymphocyte proliferation. Its main side effects are gastrointestinal (e.g., diarrhea, nausea) and hematological (e.g., leukopenia). It is not considered directly nephrotoxic and is often used in combination with calcineurin inhibitors, sometimes allowing for dose reduction of the latter to mitigate kidney damage.

(C) Azathioprine: Azathioprine is a purine antimetabolite. Its primary dose-limiting toxicities are bone marrow suppression (myelosuppression) and hepatotoxicity. It is not known to cause direct nephrotoxicity.

(D) Cyclophosphamide: Cyclophosphamide is a potent alkylating agent used in both oncology and for severe autoimmune diseases. Its most characteristic urological toxicity is hemorrhagic cystitis (inflammation and bleeding of the bladder) due to its toxic metabolite, acrolein. While severe dehydration or tumor lysis syndrome in patients treated with cyclophosphamide can lead to acute kidney injury, the drug itself is not considered a primary direct nephrotoxin in the same way as tacrolimus.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
You have just prescribed rifampicin for family members of a child with meningococcal meningitis. Which one of the following should be included in your advice regarding rifampicin therapy?
Green discoloration of body fluids is a common side effect
The efficacy of oral contraceptive pill is reduced
The efficacy of warfarin is unchanged
Dosage reduction is required in renal failure

Answer: (B) — The efficacy of oral contraceptive pill is reduced

Explanation:

Rifampicin is a potent inducer of the hepatic cytochrome P450 (CYP450) enzyme system, particularly the CYP3A4 isoenzyme. Many drugs, including the estrogen and progestin components of oral contraceptive pills (OCPs), are metabolized by this system. By inducing these enzymes, rifampicin significantly accelerates the breakdown of the hormones in OCPs. This leads to lower circulating levels of the contraceptive hormones, which can result in a loss of contraceptive efficacy and an increased risk of unintended pregnancy. It is crucial to advise patients taking rifampicin to use an alternative, non-hormonal method of contraception (e.g., barrier methods) during and for a period after treatment.

Options:

  • (A) Green discoloration of body fluids is a common side effect: This is incorrect. While rifampicin is well-known for causing discoloration of body fluids (such as urine, sweat, tears, and saliva), the characteristic color is red-orange, not green. Patients should be warned about this harmless but potentially alarming side effect, which can also permanently stain soft contact lenses.

  • (C) The efficacy of warfarin is unchanged: This is incorrect. Similar to its effect on OCPs, rifampicin's induction of CYP450 enzymes (including CYP2C9, which metabolizes warfarin) leads to a more rapid clearance of warfarin. This reduces the anticoagulant effect of warfarin, leading to a lower INR (International Normalized Ratio) and an increased risk of thrombosis. Patients on concurrent therapy require close INR monitoring and often need a significant increase in their warfarin dose.

  • (D) Dosage reduction is required in renal failure: This is incorrect. Rifampicin is primarily metabolized by the liver and excreted in the bile and feces. Only a small fraction is eliminated by the kidneys. Therefore, its clearance is not significantly affected by renal impairment, and dose adjustment is generally not required in patients with renal failure. However, dose reduction should be considered in patients with severe hepatic impairment.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
All of the following antimicrobial agents act by inhibiting bacterial cell wall synthesis, EXCEPT:
Vancomycin
Ceftriaxone
Clindamycin
Meropenem

Answer: (C) — Clindamycin Explanation: Clindamycin is a lincosamide antibiotic that acts by inhibiting bacterial protein synthesis. It binds to the 50S subunit of the bacterial ribosome, thereby preventing the translocation step of peptide chain elongation. This mechanism is distinct from the inhibition of cell wall synthesis.

Options: (A) Vancomycin: Incorrect. Vancomycin is a glycopeptide antibiotic that inhibits cell wall synthesis by binding directly to the D-alanyl-D-alanine terminus of peptidoglycan precursors. This prevents both transglycosylation and transpeptidation, key steps in cell wall formation, particularly in Gram-positive bacteria.

(B) Ceftriaxone: Incorrect. Ceftriaxone is a third-generation cephalosporin, which is a class of beta-lactam antibiotics. Beta-lactams inhibit the final step of peptidoglycan synthesis (transpeptidation or cross-linking) by binding to and inactivating penicillin-binding proteins (PBPs), which are bacterial transpeptidases.

(D) Meropenem: Incorrect. Meropenem is a carbapenem, another class of beta-lactam antibiotics. Like cephalosporins and penicillins, it inhibits bacterial cell wall synthesis by binding to and inactivating penicillin-binding proteins (PBPs), thus preventing the cross-linking of the peptidoglycan cell wall.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following is the primary cause of antimicrobial resistance?
Regular use of more than one antibiotic
Increased use in veterinary medicine
Genetic mutation in bacteria
Non-judicious use by humans

Answer: (D) — Non-judicious use by humans

Explanation:

The primary driver for the development and spread of antimicrobial resistance (AMR) is the selective pressure exerted by the use of antibiotics. Non-judicious use by humans is the most encompassing and accurate term for this driver. When antibiotics are used, they eliminate susceptible bacteria, leaving behind any that have, through random genetic events, developed resistance. These resistant bacteria then multiply without competition, leading to a population of predominantly resistant organisms.

Non-judicious use includes: * Over-prescription: Prescribing antibiotics for viral infections (like the common cold or flu) against which they have no effect. * Inappropriate prescription: Using broad-spectrum antibiotics when a narrow-spectrum agent would be effective. * Patient non-adherence: Patients not completing the full prescribed course of antibiotics, which can allow partially resistant bacteria to survive and multiply. * Widespread agricultural use: Using antibiotics for growth promotion or disease prevention in livestock, which creates a large reservoir of resistant bacteria that can spread to humans.

Options:

  • (A) Regular use of more than one antibiotic: This is incorrect. While inappropriate combination therapy can contribute to resistance, the use of more than one antibiotic (combination therapy) is often a strategy employed to prevent or treat resistant infections by targeting different bacterial mechanisms simultaneously.
  • (B) Increased use in veterinary medicine: This is a major contributor to the overall burden of antimicrobial resistance and falls under the umbrella of "non-judicious use." However, inappropriate use in human medicine is also a massive driver. Therefore, "non-judicious use by humans" is a more comprehensive and primary cause that includes both veterinary and human medical practices.
  • (C) Genetic mutation in bacteria: This is the mechanism by which resistance arises at the molecular level, not the primary cause of its proliferation as a public health crisis. Bacteria constantly undergo random genetic mutations. In the absence of antibiotics, a mutation conferring resistance offers no advantage and may even be detrimental. It is the selective pressure from antibiotic use that makes these mutations advantageous and allows resistant strains to thrive and spread.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
On review of medications, a patient informs you that they are taking St. John’s Wort. Which one of the following statements is CORRECT about St. John’s Wort?
Can suppress autonomic nervous system effects
Inhibition of reuptake of various amine transmitters
Has significantly less adverse effect profile when compared to placebo
Is no better than placebo in treating mild to moderate depression

Answer: (B) — Inhibition of reuptake of various amine transmitters

Explanation:

St. John's Wort (Hypericum perforatum) is an herbal supplement widely used for the treatment of depression. Its primary mechanism of action is believed to be the non-selective inhibition of the synaptic reuptake of several key neurotransmitters, including serotonin (5-HT), norepinephrine (NE), and dopamine (DA). The active constituents, particularly hyperforin, are responsible for this action. By increasing the concentration of these monoamine neurotransmitters in the synaptic cleft, St. John's Wort produces an antidepressant effect, similar in mechanism to some conventional antidepressant medications like SNRIs and TCAs.

Options:

  • (A) Can suppress autonomic nervous system effects: This is incorrect. Due to its serotonergic activity, St. John's Wort can contribute to serotonin syndrome when combined with other serotonergic agents (like SSRIs). Serotonin syndrome is characterized by autonomic hyperactivity, including tachycardia, hypertension, and hyperthermia, not suppression.
  • (C) Has significantly less adverse effect profile when compared to placebo: This is incorrect. While often well-tolerated, St. John's Wort is a pharmacologically active substance and has a greater incidence of adverse effects than a placebo. Common side effects include gastrointestinal symptoms, dizziness, fatigue, and photosensitivity. Most importantly, it is a potent inducer of cytochrome P450 enzymes (especially CYP3A4), leading to numerous clinically significant drug-drug interactions (e.g., reducing the efficacy of oral contraceptives, anticoagulants like warfarin, and immunosuppressants).
  • (D) Is no better than placebo in treating mild to moderate depression: This is incorrect. Multiple meta-analyses and systematic reviews (such as those by the Cochrane Collaboration) have concluded that St. John's Wort extracts are superior to placebo for the treatment of mild to moderate major depression. Its efficacy is often found to be comparable to standard antidepressant medications for this indication, although its use is complicated by the lack of standardization in preparations and its significant potential for drug interactions.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Development of tolerance is minimal or absent for which one of the following adverse effects of opiates?
Analgesia
Constipation
Cough suppression
Nausea and vomiting

Answer: (B) — Constipation Explanation: Tolerance is a pharmacological phenomenon where the effect of a drug diminishes with repeated administration, requiring higher doses to achieve the same effect. With opiates (opioids), tolerance develops to many of their effects, but not uniformly. The constipating effect of opiates, which results from their action on mu-opioid receptors in the myenteric plexus of the gastrointestinal tract, shows very little or no development of tolerance. Therefore, constipation is a persistent adverse effect in patients on long-term opiate therapy and often requires continuous management.

Options: (A) Analgesia: Tolerance to the analgesic (pain-relieving) effects of opiates is a well-established clinical problem. Patients on chronic opiate therapy for pain often require dose escalation over time to maintain the same level of pain control.

(C) Cough suppression: Opiates are effective antitussives (cough suppressants). However, tolerance can develop to this effect with chronic use, reducing its efficacy over time.

(D) Nausea and vomiting: These are common side effects when initiating opiate therapy, caused by the stimulation of the chemoreceptor trigger zone (CTZ). However, tolerance develops relatively quickly to these effects, and they often subside after a few days of consistent use.

Confidence: 100%
