MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following is an example of atypical antipsychotic agent?
Haloperidol
Zuclopenthixol
Olanzepine
Amitriptyline

Answer: (C) — Olanzepine

Explanation:

Antipsychotic medications are broadly classified into two main groups: typical (first-generation) and atypical (second-generation). Olanzapine (spelled "Olanzepine" in the question) is a cornerstone example of an atypical (second-generation) antipsychotic.

Atypical antipsychotics are distinguished from typicals by their mechanism of action and side-effect profile. They act as antagonists at both serotonin type 2A (5-HT2A) and dopamine type 2 (D2) receptors. This dual antagonism, particularly the potent 5-HT2A blockade relative to D2 blockade, is believed to be responsible for their efficacy against the negative symptoms of schizophrenia (e.g., apathy, anhedonia) and their significantly lower risk of causing extrapyramidal symptoms (EPS) like dystonia, parkinsonism, and akathisia compared to typical antipsychotics. However, they are often associated with a higher risk of metabolic side effects, such as weight gain, hyperglycemia, and dyslipidemia.

Options:

  • (A) Haloperidol: This is a high-potency typical (first-generation) antipsychotic. It is a potent antagonist of the D2 dopamine receptor, with little effect on serotonin receptors. This mechanism is highly effective for positive psychotic symptoms but carries a high risk of causing extrapyramidal symptoms.
  • (B) Zuclopenthixol: This is another typical (first-generation) antipsychotic from the thioxanthene class. Similar to haloperidol, it primarily functions as a dopamine (D1 and D2) receptor antagonist and is associated with a significant risk of EPS.
  • (D) Amitriptyline: This drug is a tricyclic antidepressant (TCA), not an antipsychotic. Its primary mechanism is the inhibition of norepinephrine and serotonin reuptake. It is used to treat major depressive disorder, neuropathic pain, and migraine prophylaxis.

Confidence:

100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
What dose would you have administered a patient, if you infiltrated 20ml of 1% Lignocaine?
20mg
200mg
100mg
50mg

Answer: (B) — 200mg

Explanation:

The calculation of a drug dose from a percentage concentration is a fundamental skill in medicine. A percentage solution is defined as the number of grams of a substance per 100 milliliters (ml) of solution.

  1. Understand the Concentration: A 1% Lignocaine solution means there is 1 gram of Lignocaine in every 100 ml of solvent.

    • 1% = 1 g / 100 ml

  2. Convert Grams to Milligrams: Since the dose is required in milligrams (mg), we convert grams to milligrams.

    • 1 gram = 1000 milligrams (mg)
    • Therefore, a 1% solution contains 1000 mg per 100 ml.

  3. Calculate the Concentration in mg/ml: To find the amount of drug in each milliliter, we divide the total milligrams by the total milliliters.

    • Concentration = 1000 mg / 100 ml = 10 mg/ml
    • A simple rule of thumb is to multiply the percentage by 10 to get the concentration in mg/ml (e.g., 1% -> 10 mg/ml; 2% -> 20 mg/ml; 0.5% -> 5 mg/ml).

  4. Calculate the Total Dose: Now, multiply the concentration (in mg/ml) by the volume administered (in ml).

    • Total Dose = Concentration × Volume
    • Total Dose = 10 mg/ml × 20 ml = 200 mg

Options:

  • (A) 20mg: This is incorrect. This dose would result from administering 2 ml of 1% Lignocaine (2 ml × 10 mg/ml), or incorrectly assuming a 1% solution means 1 mg/ml.
  • (C) 100mg: This is incorrect. This would be the correct dose for 10 ml of 1% Lignocaine (10 ml × 10 mg/ml) or 20 ml of a 0.5% solution.
  • (D) 50mg: This is incorrect. This would be the correct dose for 5 ml of 1% Lignocaine (5 ml × 10 mg/ml) or 20 ml of a 0.25% solution.

Confidence: 100%

214


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A patient has open-angle glaucoma. Which one of the following drugs acts to decrease the secretion of aqueous humor?
Epinephrine
Pilocarpine
Latanoprost
Timolol

Answer: (D) — Timolol

Explanation:

Timolol is a non-selective beta-adrenergic antagonist (beta-blocker). The ciliary body, which is responsible for producing aqueous humor, has beta-2 adrenergic receptors. Stimulation of these receptors increases the production of aqueous humor. By blocking these beta receptors, timolol reduces the production (secretion) of aqueous humor by the ciliary epithelium. This decrease in aqueous humor inflow leads to a reduction in intraocular pressure (IOP), making it an effective treatment for open-angle glaucoma.

Options:

  • (A) Epinephrine: Epinephrine is a non-selective adrenergic agonist. It has a dual and complex mechanism in glaucoma. It primarily acts by increasing the outflow of aqueous humor through both the trabecular meshwork (alpha-1 effect) and the uveoscleral pathway. While it can have a transient effect on aqueous humor production (initial increase via beta-stimulation, possible decrease with long-term use), its main therapeutic action is on outflow, and it does not reliably decrease secretion as its primary mechanism.
  • (B) Pilocarpine: Pilocarpine is a direct-acting muscarinic (cholinergic) agonist. It works by causing contraction of the ciliary muscle, which pulls on and opens the trabecular meshwork. This action increases the outflow of aqueous humor through the conventional (trabecular) pathway, thereby lowering IOP. It does not decrease the secretion of aqueous humor.
  • (C) Latanoprost: Latanoprost is a prostaglandin F2-alpha analog. It is a first-line agent for open-angle glaucoma and works by increasing the outflow of aqueous humor, primarily through the unconventional (uveoscleral) pathway. It does not affect the production or secretion of aqueous humor.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following agents does NOT inhibit the peripheral conversion of T4 to T3?
Propylthiouracil
Amiodarone
Propranolol
Carbimazole

Answer: (D) — Carbimazole Explanation: Carbimazole is a thionamide antithyroid drug that functions as a prodrug, being rapidly converted to its active metabolite, methimazole. The primary mechanism of action for methimazole is the inhibition of thyroid peroxidase (TPO) within the thyroid gland. This enzyme is crucial for the organification of iodide and the coupling of iodotyrosines, which are essential steps in the synthesis of thyroid hormones T4 and T3. Unlike propylthiouracil (PTU), carbimazole and methimazole do not have a significant inhibitory effect on the peripheral deiodinase enzymes (specifically 5'-deiodinase) that convert the less active T4 into the more potent T3 in peripheral tissues. Therefore, carbimazole does not inhibit the peripheral conversion of T4 to T3.

Options: (A) Propylthiouracil: Incorrect. Propylthiouracil (PTU) is also a thionamide antithyroid drug. It has a dual mechanism of action: it inhibits thyroid peroxidase (TPO) to reduce thyroid hormone synthesis, and it also inhibits the peripheral 5'-deiodinase enzyme, which decreases the conversion of T4 to the more active T3. This dual action makes it a preferred agent in the acute management of severe hyperthyroidism or thyroid storm.

(B) Amiodarone: Incorrect. Amiodarone is a class III antiarrhythmic drug that is structurally similar to thyroid hormone and contains a high amount of iodine. It is well-known to interfere with thyroid function, and one of its principal effects is the inhibition of peripheral 5'-deiodinase activity. This leads to a decrease in the conversion of T4 to T3, which can result in an increased T4/T3 ratio and potentially cause hypothyroidism.

(C) Propranolol: Incorrect. Propranolol is a non-selective beta-adrenergic blocker used to manage the adrenergic symptoms of thyrotoxicosis (e.g., palpitations, tremor, anxiety). In addition to its beta-blocking effects, high doses of propranolol have been shown to inhibit the peripheral 5'-deiodinase enzyme, thus reducing the conversion of T4 to T3. This provides a secondary therapeutic benefit in treating hyperthyroidism.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A patient with hypoglycemia is given IM glucagon. Which one of the following statements about Glucagon is CORRECT?
Has metabolic effects that antagonize those of adrenaline
Has antiemetic effect.
Physiologically is produced by the beta cells of the pancreas
Glucagon facilitates gluconeogenesis and ketogenesis

Answer: (D) — Glucagon facilitates gluconeogenesis and ketogenesis

Explanation:

Glucagon is a polypeptide hormone secreted by the alpha cells of the pancreatic islets of Langerhans. Its primary physiological role is to counteract the effects of insulin by raising blood glucose levels. When administered, as in the case of treating severe hypoglycemia, glucagon acts predominantly on the liver. It binds to G-protein coupled receptors on hepatocytes, activating adenylyl cyclase and increasing intracellular cyclic AMP (cAMP). This signaling cascade leads to: 1. Increased Gluconeogenesis: Glucagon promotes the synthesis of new glucose from non-carbohydrate precursors like amino acids and lactate. It does this by upregulating key gluconeogenic enzymes (e.g., phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase, fructose-1,6-bisphosphatase). 2. Increased Glycogenolysis: It stimulates the breakdown of stored glycogen into glucose. 3. Increased Ketogenesis: Glucagon promotes the breakdown of fatty acids (beta-oxidation) in the liver. The resulting acetyl-CoA, when produced in excess of the Krebs cycle's capacity, is shunted towards the synthesis of ketone bodies (acetoacetate, beta-hydroxybutyrate).

Therefore, the statement that glucagon facilitates both gluconeogenesis and ketogenesis is correct.

Options:

  • (A) Has metabolic effects that antagonize those of adrenaline: This is incorrect. Both glucagon and adrenaline (epinephrine) are counter-regulatory hormones that work to increase blood glucose levels. They have synergistic, not antagonistic, effects on glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis.
  • (B) Has antiemetic effect: This is incorrect. A very common side effect of glucagon administration is nausea and vomiting. It has an emetic (vomiting-inducing) effect, not an antiemetic (anti-vomiting) effect.
  • (C) Physiologically is produced by the beta cells of the pancreas: This is incorrect. Glucagon is produced and secreted by the alpha (α) cells of the pancreatic islets. The beta (β) cells are responsible for producing and secreting insulin.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which of the following transport channels is an example of exchange transport?

Sodium-Potassium-chloride
Sodium-hydrogen
Sodium-Chloride
Sodium-Lactate

Answer: (B) — Sodium-hydrogen Explanation: Exchange transport, also known as counter-transport or antiport, is a form of secondary active transport where two different ions or molecules are transported across a cell membrane in opposite directions. The Sodium-Hydrogen Exchanger (NHE) is a classic example of this mechanism. It utilizes the favorable electrochemical gradient of sodium ions (Na⁺) moving into the cell to drive the transport of hydrogen ions (H⁺) out of the cell, against their concentration gradient. This process is crucial for regulating intracellular pH and for sodium reabsorption in the proximal tubules of the kidney.

Options: (A) Sodium-Potassium-Chloride: This is an example of cotransport or symport, not exchange transport. The Na-K-2Cl cotransporter (NKCC) moves one sodium ion (Na⁺), one potassium ion (K⁺), and two chloride ions (Cl⁻) across the membrane in the same direction (typically into the cell).

(C) Sodium-Chloride: The Sodium-Chloride cotransporter (NCC) is also a symporter. It transports one sodium ion (Na⁺) and one chloride ion (Cl⁻) together in the same direction across the cell membrane, notably in the distal convoluted tubule of the nephron.

(D) Sodium-Lactate: The Sodium-Lactate transporter is another example of a cotransporter (symporter). It moves sodium ions (Na⁺) and lactate molecules together in the same direction, usually into the cell. This is important for lactate reabsorption in the kidneys and uptake in various other tissues.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
One litre of 3% sodium chloride (hypertonic saline) solution contains approximately:
300mmol/L sodium
600mmol/L sodium
500mmol/L sodium
900mmol/L sodium

Answer: (C) — 500mmol/L sodium x000D x000D

Explanation:

This question requires a calculation to determine the molar concentration of sodium in a 3% sodium chloride solution. Here is the step-by-step breakdown:

  1. Define the concentration: A 3% sodium chloride (NaCl) solution means there are 3 grams of NaCl for every 100 milliliters (mL) of solution.

  2. Calculate mass in one litre: The question asks for the concentration in one litre (1000 mL).

    • Mass of NaCl in 1000 mL = (3 g / 100 mL) × 1000 mL = 30 grams.

  3. Calculate moles of NaCl: To find the number of moles, we use the molecular weight (MW) of NaCl.

    • MW of Sodium (Na) ≈ 23 g/mol
    • MW of Chloride (Cl) ≈ 35.5 g/mol
    • MW of NaCl = 23 + 35.5 = 58.5 g/mol
    • Moles of NaCl = Mass / Molecular Weight = 30 g / 58.5 g/mol ≈ 0.513 moles.

  4. Convert to millimoles (mmol): The options are in mmol/L.

    • 0.513 moles × 1000 mmol/mole = 513 mmol.

  5. Determine sodium concentration: In solution, NaCl dissociates completely into one sodium ion (Na⁺) and one chloride ion (Cl⁻). Therefore, the molar concentration of Na⁺ is equal to the molar concentration of NaCl.

    • The concentration of sodium is approximately 513 mmol/L.

The closest answer choice to the calculated value of 513 mmol/L is 500 mmol/L. x000D x000D

Options:

  • (A) 300mmol/L sodium: This is incorrect. This value is closer to the total osmolarity (not the sodium concentration) of isotonic saline (0.9% NaCl), which contains approximately 154 mmol/L of sodium and 154 mmol/L of chloride, for a total of 308 mOsm/L.
  • (B) 600mmol/L sodium: This is incorrect. This might result from a calculation error, such as using an incorrect molecular weight for NaCl (e.g., rounding down to 50 g/mol).
  • (C) 500mmol/L sodium: This is the correct answer. The calculated value is ~513 mmol/L, which is approximately 500 mmol/L.
  • (D) 900mmol/L sodium: This is incorrect and significantly overestimates the concentration. It may arise from a major calculation error. x000D x000D Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following drugs causes constipation?
Nitroglycerin
Ramipril
Propranolol
Verapamil

Answer: (D) — Verapamil

Explanation:

Verapamil is a non-dihydropyridine calcium channel blocker (CCB). It exerts its effects by blocking L-type calcium channels in various tissues. In the gastrointestinal tract, the smooth muscle cells that are responsible for peristalsis (the wave-like muscle contractions that move food through the digestive tract) depend on calcium influx for contraction. By blocking these calcium channels in the gut, verapamil reduces the contractility of intestinal smooth muscle, which slows colonic transit time and leads to constipation. This is a very common and well-documented side effect of verapamil and, to a lesser extent, other CCBs.

Options:

  • (A) Nitroglycerin: This is a nitrate vasodilator used primarily for angina. Its mechanism involves relaxing vascular smooth muscle. Common side effects include headache, flushing, and hypotension. Constipation is not a characteristic side effect.
  • (B) Ramipril: This is an Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitor used for hypertension and heart failure. Its most characteristic side effects are a dry cough, hyperkalemia, and angioedema. While gastrointestinal upset can occur, constipation is not a prominent or expected side effect.
  • (C) Propranolol: This is a non-selective beta-blocker. By blocking beta-adrenergic receptors, it can affect various systems. While gastrointestinal side effects like nausea, diarrhea, or constipation can occur, they are not as common or mechanistically direct as the constipation caused by verapamil.

Confidence: 100%

The dermatome corresponding to middle finger is:
C5
C8
C7

Answer: (C) — C7 Explanation: A dermatome is an area of skin that is mainly supplied by afferent nerve fibers from a single dorsal root of a spinal nerve. The dermatome for the middle finger is innervated by the C7 spinal nerve. This is a crucial landmark for remembering the sensory distribution of the upper limb. The C7 nerve root also supplies motor function to the extensors of the elbow and wrist (triceps, wrist flexors, and finger extensors).

Options: (A) C5: This dermatome supplies the skin over the lateral aspect of the upper arm (deltoid region) and is associated with the motor function of the deltoid and biceps muscles. (B) C8: This dermatome supplies the skin on the medial aspect of the hand and forearm, including the little finger and the medial half of the ring finger. (D) C6: This dermatome supplies the skin on the lateral aspect of the forearm, the thumb, and the index finger.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following agents used in the treatment of diabetes mellitus does NOT cause hypoglycemia?
Repaglinide
Gliclazide
Acarbose
Insulin

Answer: (C) — Acarbose Explanation: Acarbose is an alpha-glucosidase inhibitor. Its mechanism of action is to inhibit enzymes in the brush border of the small intestine that are responsible for the breakdown of complex carbohydrates into monosaccharides (like glucose). By delaying the digestion and absorption of carbohydrates, acarbose reduces the postprandial (after-meal) rise in blood glucose. Importantly, this mechanism does not stimulate insulin secretion from the pancreas or increase peripheral insulin sensitivity. Therefore, when used as a monotherapy, acarbose does not cause hypoglycemia.

Options: (A) Repaglinide is a meglitinide, a class of drugs known as short-acting insulin secretagogues. It stimulates the pancreas to release insulin by closing ATP-sensitive potassium channels on beta cells. This increase in insulin can lead to hypoglycemia, especially if a meal is missed or delayed after taking the medication.

(B) Gliclazide is a sulfonylurea. Like meglitinides, sulfonylureas act as insulin secretagogues by stimulating the pancreatic beta cells to release insulin. This action is independent of blood glucose levels, making hypoglycemia a significant and well-known side effect of this drug class.

(D) Insulin is a hormone that directly promotes the uptake and utilization of glucose by cells, thereby lowering blood glucose levels. Exogenous insulin administration is a cornerstone of treatment for type 1 diabetes and is often used in type 2 diabetes. An excessive dose, a missed meal, or increased physical activity can easily lead to iatrogenic hypoglycemia, which is its most common and serious adverse effect.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding Buprenorphine and its use in clinical situations, which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
Higher doses cause respiratory depression
It is a short acting partial mu-receptor agonist
It is less effective than methadone for detoxification and maintenance of heroin abusers
Its effects are resistant to naxolone reversal.

Answer: (D) — Its effects are resistant to naxolone reversal.

Explanation:

Buprenorphine is a unique opioid medication with distinct pharmacological properties. It is a partial agonist at the mu-opioid receptor and an antagonist at the kappa-opioid receptor. Its most defining characteristic in the context of reversal is its very high affinity for the mu-receptor and its slow rate of dissociation from it. This means buprenorphine binds very tightly to the receptor and does not let go easily.

Naloxone is a competitive antagonist at the mu-receptor, meaning it works by competing with opioids for the same binding site. Because of buprenorphine's high affinity, naloxone struggles to displace it from the receptor. Consequently, standard doses of naloxone may be ineffective or only partially effective in reversing buprenorphine-induced effects like respiratory depression. Reversal often requires much higher and/or repeated doses of naloxone, and sometimes a continuous infusion, to maintain a sufficient concentration to overcome buprenorphine's tight binding.

Options:

  • (A) Higher doses cause respiratory depression: This statement is misleading. While buprenorphine does cause dose-dependent respiratory depression, it exhibits a "ceiling effect." This means that as the dose increases, the respiratory depression plateaus and does not worsen significantly beyond a certain point. This ceiling effect makes it considerably safer in overdose compared to full mu-agonists (e.g., morphine, heroin, methadone), where respiratory depression is dose-dependent and can be fatal.
  • (B) It is a short acting partial mu-receptor agonist: This is incorrect. Buprenorphine is a long-acting agent. Its high receptor affinity and slow dissociation contribute to a prolonged duration of action, typically lasting 24-72 hours. This long half-life is advantageous for its use in opioid use disorder, allowing for once-daily or even less frequent dosing.
  • (C) It is less effective than methadone for detoxification and maintenance of heroin abusers: This is a debatable and overly simplified statement. Both buprenorphine and methadone are considered first-line, evidence-based treatments for opioid use disorder. While some studies indicate that methadone may be superior in terms of treatment retention for patients with high levels of physical dependence, buprenorphine offers a better safety profile (lower overdose risk) and greater accessibility, as it can be prescribed in office-based settings. For many patients, buprenorphine is highly effective, and its effectiveness is generally considered comparable to methadone in reducing illicit opioid use. Therefore, it cannot be definitively stated as "less effective" for all situations.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A patient with gout is on long-term therapy with allopurinol. Which one of the following is NOT as side effect of allopurinol therapy?
Pruritic maculopapular lesion
Necrotising vasculitis
Peripheral neuritis
Haemolytic anaemia

Answer: (D) — Haemolytic anaemia

Explanation:

Allopurinol is a xanthine oxidase inhibitor used in the long-term management of gout and conditions associated with hyperuricemia. The question asks to identify the condition that is NOT a side effect of allopurinol. While all the listed options have been reported in the literature to varying degrees, one is significantly less associated with allopurinol therapy than the others in a clinical and educational context.

The most prominent side effects of allopurinol are hypersensitivity reactions. * The most common is a pruritic maculopapular lesion (skin rash). * A rare but severe and potentially fatal reaction is Allopurinol Hypersensitivity Syndrome (AHS), also known as DRESS (Drug Reaction with Eosinophilia and Systemic Symptoms). This syndrome can manifest with fever, eosinophilia, organ dysfunction (e.g., hepatitis, nephritis), and severe skin reactions like necrotising vasculitis, Stevens-Johnson syndrome (SJS), or toxic epidermal necrolysis (TEN). * Peripheral neuritis (neuropathy) is also a documented, albeit very rare, neurological side effect.

While extremely rare case reports have linked allopurinol to immune-mediated haemolytic anaemia, it is not considered a characteristic or commonly cited adverse effect. Compared to the other options, which represent a spectrum of well-established (though sometimes rare) hypersensitivity reactions, haemolytic anaemia is the least recognized side effect. For the purposes of a multiple-choice question, it is the most likely intended answer for a condition not typically associated with allopurinol.

Options:

  • (A) Pruritic maculopapular lesion: Incorrect. This is the most common adverse effect of allopurinol, affecting approximately 2% of patients. It requires careful monitoring as it can be a precursor to more severe hypersensitivity reactions.
  • (B) Necrotising vasculitis: Incorrect. This is a known, severe manifestation of the life-threatening Allopurinol Hypersensitivity Syndrome (AHS/DRESS).
  • (C) Peripheral neuritis: Incorrect. Although very rare, peripheral neuropathy/neuritis is a documented adverse effect of allopurinol listed in comprehensive drug databases.

Confidence: 85% (The question is technically imperfect as all options have been reported as side effects. However, in a standard testing environment, haemolytic anaemia is the least associated and most likely intended correct answer).

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which of the following is NOT a prokinetic drug?
Neostigmine
Glucagon
Domperidone
Erythromycin

Answer: (B) — Glucagon

Explanation:

Prokinetic drugs are medications that enhance coordinated gastrointestinal (GI) motility and transit of material in the GI tract. They work through various mechanisms to increase the frequency or strength of contractions. Glucagon is a hormone that has the opposite effect; it is a potent inhibitor of GI smooth muscle activity, causing relaxation of the stomach, duodenum, small bowel, and colon. It is often used therapeutically during endoscopic or radiologic procedures to temporarily reduce bowel motility for better visualization. Therefore, glucagon is not a prokinetic agent but rather an anti-motility agent.

Options:

  • (A) Neostigmine: This is an acetylcholinesterase inhibitor. By preventing the breakdown of acetylcholine, it increases cholinergic stimulation of the muscarinic receptors on GI smooth muscle cells, leading to enhanced contractility and motility. It is used as a potent prokinetic agent, particularly in cases of acute colonic pseudo-obstruction (Ogilvie's syndrome).
  • (C) Domperidone: This is a peripheral dopamine D2-receptor antagonist. Dopamine normally acts as an inhibitory neurotransmitter in the GI tract. By blocking its receptors, domperidone increases esophageal and gastric motility and peristalsis. It is used to treat gastroparesis and nausea/vomiting.
  • (D) Erythromycin: This macrolide antibiotic acts as a motilin receptor agonist at sub-antimicrobial doses. Motilin is a hormone that stimulates the migrating motor complex (MMC), which are waves of electrical activity that sweep through the intestines in a regular cycle during fasting. By activating these receptors, erythromycin powerfully stimulates gastric emptying and upper GI motility, making it a useful prokinetic agent, especially for gastroparesis.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following statements is CORRECT about agents used to treat peptic ulcer disease?
Sucralfate should be taken immediately after meals
Misoprostol is contraindicated in pregancy
Ranitidine is most beneficial if taken early in the morning
Most antacids are completely safe in patients with renal failure

Answer: (B) — Misoprostol is contraindicated in pregancy

Explanation:

Misoprostol is a synthetic analog of prostaglandin E1 (PGE1). Its primary use in peptic ulcer disease is to prevent NSAID-induced gastric ulcers. Prostaglandins play a crucial role in maintaining the integrity of the gastric mucosa by increasing mucus and bicarbonate secretion and enhancing mucosal blood flow. However, prostaglandins also have potent effects on the uterus, specifically causing uterine contractions and cervical ripening. Due to this uterotonic effect, misoprostol can induce abortion, miscarriage, or premature labor. Therefore, it is classified as Pregnancy Category X by the FDA and is absolutely contraindicated in pregnant women or women who may become pregnant.

Options:

  • (A) Sucralfate should be taken immediately after meals

    • This is incorrect. Sucralfate is a mucosal protective agent that requires an acidic environment (pH < 4) for activation. It polymerizes to form a viscous, sticky gel that adheres to the ulcer crater, creating a physical barrier. Taking it with or after a meal would be counterproductive, as food buffers the gastric acid, raising the pH and preventing the drug's activation. It is most effective when taken on an empty stomach, at least one hour before meals.

  • (C) Ranitidine is most beneficial if taken early in the morning

    • This is incorrect. Ranitidine is a histamine H2-receptor antagonist (H2RA) that reduces gastric acid secretion. While it can reduce meal-stimulated acid, it is particularly effective at suppressing nocturnal acid secretion, which is primarily driven by histamine and is a key factor in the pathogenesis of duodenal ulcers. Therefore, for ulcer healing, H2RAs are often prescribed as a single dose at bedtime to achieve maximal suppression of overnight acid.

  • (D) Most antacids are completely safe in patients with renal failure

    • This is incorrect. Antacids containing magnesium, aluminum, or calcium can be hazardous in patients with renal failure. The kidneys are responsible for clearing these cations. In renal impairment, they can accumulate systemically, leading to toxicity:
      • Magnesium: Hypermagnesemia (hypotension, respiratory depression, cardiac arrest).
      • Aluminum: Aluminum toxicity (encephalopathy, osteomalacia).
      • Calcium: Hypercalcemia.
    • Therefore, antacid use must be carefully monitored or avoided in patients with significant renal disease.

Confidence: 100%

226


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following rarely has extrapyramidal adverse effects?
Domperidone
Haloperidol
Risperidone
Metoclopramide

Answer: (A) — Domperidone

Explanation:

Extrapyramidal adverse effects (EPS), such as dystonia, akathisia, parkinsonism, and tardive dyskinesia, are primarily caused by the blockade of dopamine D2 receptors in the nigrostriatal pathway of the central nervous system (CNS).

Domperidone is a peripheral D2 receptor antagonist. It exerts its antiemetic effects by blocking D2 receptors in the chemoreceptor trigger zone (CTZ), which is located in the area postrema of the medulla oblongata. The crucial characteristic of domperidone is that it is a large, polar molecule that does not readily cross the blood-brain barrier (BBB). Because it has poor penetration into the CNS, it does not significantly block D2 receptors in the nigrostriatal pathway, and therefore, the risk of causing extrapyramidal side effects is very low.

Options:

  • (B) Haloperidol: This is a high-potency, first-generation (typical) antipsychotic. Its primary mechanism is potent D2 receptor antagonism within the CNS. It readily crosses the BBB and is well-known for having a high incidence of EPS, making it a classic example of a drug that causes these effects.
  • (C) Risperidone: This is a second-generation (atypical) antipsychotic. While atypical antipsychotics generally have a lower risk of EPS compared to typicals (due to additional serotonin 5-HT2A receptor antagonism), risperidone still acts as a D2 antagonist in the CNS. At higher doses (>6 mg/day), it is one of the atypical antipsychotics most likely to cause dose-dependent EPS.
  • (D) Metoclopramide: This is a prokinetic and antiemetic agent that, like domperidone, is a D2 receptor antagonist. However, unlike domperidone, metoclopramide readily crosses the blood-brain barrier. Consequently, it can cause significant central D2 blockade and is frequently associated with EPS, including a black box warning for tardive dyskinesia with long-term use.

Confidence: 100%

227


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Tolerance does NOT develop for which one of the following effects of diazepam?
None of its effects
Sedation
Anxiolysis
Anticonvulsant

Answer: (C) — Anxiolysis

Explanation:

Tolerance, a state of reduced responsiveness to a drug's effect with repeated administration, is a key characteristic of benzodiazepine use. However, tolerance does not develop equally to all effects of these drugs. With long-term use of diazepam (a benzodiazepine), tolerance develops rapidly to the sedative and hypnotic effects, meaning a patient will feel less sleepy on the same dose over time. Similarly, significant tolerance develops to the anticonvulsant effects, which limits the long-term usefulness of benzodiazepines for managing seizure disorders.

In contrast, tolerance to the anxiolytic (anti-anxiety) effects develops much more slowly and is often incomplete or minimal. This is why benzodiazepines can remain effective for anxiety for longer periods than they do for sedation or seizure control, although their long-term use is still discouraged due to the risks of dependence and withdrawal.

Options:

  • (A) None of its effects: This is incorrect. Tolerance is a well-documented and clinically significant phenomenon with benzodiazepines, particularly for the sedative and anticonvulsant effects.
  • (B) Sedation: This is incorrect. Tolerance to the sedative effects of diazepam develops relatively quickly, often within weeks. Patients who initially experience significant drowsiness find that this effect diminishes with continued use.
  • (D) Anticonvulsant: This is incorrect. Tolerance to the anticonvulsant effects is a major limitation for the chronic treatment of epilepsy with benzodiazepines. Higher doses are required to maintain seizure control, increasing the risk of side effects and dependence.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
(O2 combined with Hb / O2 capacity) x 100  is a measure of:
Alveolar ventilation
Oxygen concentration of blood
Minute ventilation
Oxygen saturation of hemoglobin

Answer: (D) — Oxygen saturation of hemoglobin

Explanation: The formula provided, (O2 combined with Hb / O2 capacity) x 100, is the precise definition of the oxygen saturation of hemoglobin (SaO2). Let's break down the components: * O2 combined with Hb: This represents the actual amount of oxygen currently bound to the hemoglobin molecules in a given sample of blood. This is also known as the oxygen content of hemoglobin. * O2 capacity: This represents the maximum theoretical amount of oxygen that can be bound by the hemoglobin in that same blood sample. This value is determined by the concentration of hemoglobin (e.g., 1.34 mL of O2 can bind to 1 gram of Hb). * The Ratio x 100: By dividing the actual amount of bound oxygen by the maximum possible amount and multiplying by 100, we calculate the percentage of hemoglobin's binding sites that are occupied by oxygen. This percentage is the oxygen saturation.

Options: * (A) Alveolar ventilation: This is a measure of the volume of fresh air that reaches the gas-exchanging areas of the lungs (the alveoli) per minute. It is calculated as (Tidal Volume - Anatomic Dead Space) × Respiratory Rate. This relates to lung function, not a property of blood. * (B) Oxygen concentration of blood: This is the total amount of oxygen carried in the blood, including both the oxygen bound to hemoglobin and the oxygen dissolved in the plasma. The formula is: CaO2 = (1.34 × Hb × SaO2) + (0.003 × PaO2). The question's formula only calculates a component (the saturation) of this total concentration, not the concentration itself. * (C) Minute ventilation: This is the total volume of air inhaled or exhaled from the lungs per minute. It is calculated as Tidal Volume × Respiratory Rate. This is a measure of overall breathing and does not account for the gas exchange efficiency or blood oxygen levels.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding toxidromes, their clinical presentations and differential diagnoses, match the best option with the stems that follow: A 50-year-old man with a history of depression presents with ‘increasingly bizarre behaviour’ according to his wife, including agitation, imagining 'fairies’, and ‘catching flies that are not there’. Examination reveals mumbling speech, mydriasis, Temperature of 38 C, HR 144, RR 28 and GCS 15. He also has dry flushed skin, dry mucous membranes, myoclonus and choreoathetosis.
Salicylism
Anticholinergic syndrome
Serotonin syndrome
Neuroleptic malignant syndrome

Answer: (B) — Anticholinergic syndrome

Explanation:

This patient's presentation is a classic example of the anticholinergic toxidrome. The constellation of symptoms can be remembered by the mnemonic: "Mad as a hatter" (altered mental status, delirium, hallucinations), "Blind as a bat" (mydriasis/dilated pupils), "Red as a beet" (flushed, erythematous skin), "Hot as a hare" (hyperthermia), and "Dry as a bone" (anhidrosis, dry skin and mucous membranes).

The patient exhibits: * Central effects: Agitation, bizarre behaviour, visual hallucinations ('fairies', 'catching flies'), mumbling speech (delirium), myoclonus, and choreoathetosis. * Peripheral effects: Mydriasis, hyperthermia (38°C), tachycardia (HR 144), dry flushed skin, and dry mucous membranes.

Given his history of depression, an overdose of a medication with strong anticholinergic properties, such as a tricyclic antidepressant (TCA) like amitriptyline, is highly probable.

Options:

  • (A) Salicylism: This is incorrect. While salicylism (aspirin overdose) can cause hyperthermia, tachycardia, and altered mental status, it typically presents with tinnitus, diaphoresis (sweating), and a primary respiratory alkalosis followed by a metabolic acidosis. This patient's key features of dry skin, dry mucous membranes, and mydriasis are not characteristic of salicylism.

  • (C) Serotonin syndrome: This is a key differential but incorrect. Serotonin syndrome shares features like altered mental status, autonomic hyperactivity (tachycardia, hyperthermia), and neuromuscular excitation (myoclonus). However, a crucial differentiating sign is that patients with serotonin syndrome are typically diaphoretic (sweaty), whereas this patient has dry skin and mucous membranes. Also, neuromuscular findings in serotonin syndrome often include hyperreflexia and lower extremity rigidity, which are more prominent than choreoathetosis.

  • (D) Neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS): This is incorrect. NMS is an idiosyncratic reaction to dopamine antagonists (antipsychotics). While it presents with fever and altered mental status, its hallmark is severe, generalized "lead-pipe" muscle rigidity. This patient has myoclonus and choreoathetosis, not the profound rigidity seen in NMS. Furthermore, NMS typically develops over days to weeks, whereas this acute presentation is more suggestive of an overdose.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding Penicillin agents and their interactions with microbes, choose the best option to fit the stem that follows: This drug raises the blood levels of all penicillins by impairing renal tubular secretion of weak acids such as β-lactam compounds.
Frusemide
Probenecid
Warfarin
Aspirin

Answer: (B) — Probenecid

Explanation:

The mechanism described in the question involves the competitive inhibition of renal tubular secretion. The kidneys actively eliminate many drugs from the bloodstream, particularly weak acids, through a system of transporters in the proximal convoluted tubule. The primary transporters involved are the Organic Anion Transporters (OATs).

Penicillins are weak acids and are efficiently secreted by these OATs into the tubular fluid for excretion in the urine. This rapid renal clearance contributes to their relatively short half-life.

Probenecid is a uricosuric agent that is also a weak acid. It potently inhibits the OAT system. When co-administered with penicillin, probenecid competes for secretion by the OATs. By occupying the transporter, it effectively blocks the secretion of penicillin from the blood into the renal tubule. This decreases the renal clearance of penicillin, leading to a significant increase in its plasma concentration and a prolongation of its elimination half-life. This interaction is clinically useful, especially for treating infections like neurosyphilis or gonorrhea, where high, sustained levels of penicillin are required.

Options:

  • (A) Frusemide (Furosemide): This is a loop diuretic that acts on the thick ascending limb of the Loop of Henle. While it is also a weak acid secreted by the OAT system and can theoretically compete with penicillin, this is not its primary mechanism of action or a clinically intended use. Probenecid is the classic and most potent agent used for this specific purpose.
  • (C) Warfarin: This is an oral anticoagulant that inhibits Vitamin K epoxide reductase. Its interactions with penicillins are generally not related to renal tubular secretion. Instead, they may involve alterations in gut flora (reducing Vitamin K synthesis) or effects on hepatic metabolism, which can potentiate warfarin's anticoagulant effect.
  • (D) Aspirin: As a salicylate, aspirin is a weak acid that is also secreted by the OAT system. High doses of aspirin can compete with penicillin for tubular secretion. However, probenecid is a much more effective inhibitor of penicillin secretion and is the drug specifically used for this purpose.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding Penicillin agents and their interactions with microbes, choose the best option to fit the stem that follows:

Which agent, when used in a combination, extends activity against β-lactamase-producing strains of S aureus as well as some gram-negative bacteria.

Ampicillin
Benzathine Penicillin G
Tazobactam
Penicillin G

Answer: (C) — Tazobactam

Explanation: The question asks for an agent that, when used in combination with a penicillin, extends its antimicrobial spectrum to include bacteria that produce β-lactamase enzymes. Tazobactam is a β-lactamase inhibitor. It has minimal intrinsic antibacterial activity but functions by irreversibly binding to and inactivating many bacterial β-lactamases. This protects the partner penicillin antibiotic (e.g., piperacillin in the combination piperacillin/tazobactam) from enzymatic degradation, thereby allowing the penicillin to exert its bactericidal effect on otherwise resistant organisms, including many strains of β-lactamase-producing Staphylococcus aureus and various gram-negative bacteria.

Options: (A) Ampicillin is an aminopenicillin, a type of β-lactam antibiotic. While it has a broader spectrum than Penicillin G, it is susceptible to degradation by β-lactamases. It is an agent that needs protection by a β-lactamase inhibitor (e.g., sulbactam in the combination ampicillin/sulbactam), rather than the agent providing the protection.

(B) Benzathine Penicillin G is a long-acting depot formulation of Penicillin G. The benzathine salt slows the absorption of the drug, providing sustained low plasma concentrations for weeks. This formulation affects the pharmacokinetics (duration of action) but does not alter the drug's susceptibility to β-lactamases.

(D) Penicillin G is the original penicillin antibiotic and is the prototype for this class. It is highly effective against susceptible organisms but is very narrow in spectrum and is readily destroyed by β-lactamase enzymes. Therefore, it is ineffective against most modern strains of S. aureus and many gram-negative bacteria.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding toxidromes, their clinical presentations and differential diagnoses, match the best option with the stems that follow: A 44-year-old man on regular risperidone presents with fever, sweating and drowsiness. On examination he has a GCS of 13, Temperature of 40C, BP 172/95, HR 144 and RR 32. He has obvious muscle rigidity, but no clonus or hyperreflexia.
Cholinergic syndrome
Anticholinergic syndrome
Sympathomimetic toxidrome
Neuroleptic malignant syndrome

Answer: (D) — Neuroleptic malignant syndrome

Explanation:

This patient's presentation is a classic example of Neuroleptic Malignant Syndrome (NMS). NMS is a rare but life-threatening idiosyncratic reaction to dopamine-blocking agents, such as antipsychotics (neuroleptics). The patient is taking risperidone, an atypical antipsychotic, which is a known cause. The diagnosis is based on a characteristic tetrad of clinical features, all of which are present in this case:

  1. Mental Status Change: The patient is drowsy with a GCS of 13.
  2. Muscle Rigidity: Described as "obvious muscle rigidity," often termed "lead-pipe" rigidity. This is a hallmark feature.
  3. Hyperthermia (Fever): The temperature is significantly elevated at 40°C.
  4. Autonomic Instability: Manifested by tachycardia (HR 144), hypertension (BP 172/95), tachypnea (RR 32), and diaphoresis (sweating).

The absence of clonus or hyperreflexia further supports NMS over its main differential, Serotonin Syndrome, where these features are prominent.

Options:

  • (A) Cholinergic syndrome: This toxidrome results from excessive stimulation of cholinergic receptors (e.g., organophosphate poisoning). It is characterized by the "DUMBBELS" mnemonic (Diarrhea, Urination, Miosis, Bradycardia/Bronchorrhea, Emesis, Lacrimation, Salivation). The patient's presentation with tachycardia, hypertension, and rigidity is inconsistent with a cholinergic crisis, which typically presents with bradycardia, miosis, and flaccid paralysis or fasciculations rather than rigidity.
  • (B) Anticholinergic syndrome: This is caused by blockade of muscarinic receptors. The classic mnemonic is "mad as a hatter, hot as a hare, red as a beet, dry as a bone, blind as a bat." While there is altered mental status and hyperthermia, patients with anticholinergic toxicity are typically flushed and have dry skin and mucous membranes (anhidrosis). This patient is sweating (diaphoretic), making this diagnosis unlikely.
  • (C) Sympathomimetic toxidrome: This results from excessive stimulation of the sympathetic nervous system by agents like cocaine or amphetamines. It shares features of hyperthermia, tachycardia, and hypertension with NMS. However, patients typically present with agitation and hyperreflexia, and while diaphoresis is common, the profound "lead-pipe" muscle rigidity seen in this case is the key feature that points specifically to NMS.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Regarding toxidromes, their clinical presentations and differential diagnoses, match the best option with the stems that follow: The offending agent acts at presynaptic membranes to allow the release of multiple motor endplate neurotransmitters resulting in delayed onset 10-15 min later with intense pain and pilo-erection, which may progress to severe diaphoresis, hypertension and tachycardia.
Irukandji syndrome
Funnel web envenomation
Blue ringed Octopus envenomation
Latrodectism

Answer: (A) — Irukandji syndrome

Explanation:

The clinical scenario described is a classic presentation of Irukandji syndrome. This toxidrome is caused by envenomation from certain species of small box jellyfish (e.g., Carukia barnesi). The venom contains toxins that act on presynaptic nerve terminals, causing a massive release of endogenous catecholamines (norepinephrine and epinephrine).

This mechanism explains the key features mentioned: * Delayed onset (10-15 min): The initial sting is often minor or unnoticed, with systemic symptoms developing after a delay (typically 5-40 minutes). * Intense pain: Patients experience excruciating, cramping pain, often in the back, chest, and abdomen, and frequently describe a "feeling of impending doom." * Sympathetic storm: The massive catecholamine surge leads to profound piloerection ("goosebumps"), severe diaphoresis (sweating), dangerous hypertension, and tachycardia. This can progress to life-threatening complications like cardiogenic shock, pulmonary edema, and intracerebral hemorrhage.

Options:

  • (B) Funnel web envenomation: This is incorrect. While funnel web spider venom (delta-atracotoxin) also acts presynaptically to cause massive neurotransmitter release, the onset of systemic symptoms is typically much more rapid (within minutes). Furthermore, its presentation often includes prominent early cholinergic features like profuse salivation and perioral muscle fasciculations, which are not described in the question stem.
  • (C) Blue ringed Octopus envenomation: This is incorrect. The venom of the blue-ringed octopus contains tetrodotoxin (TTX), a potent neurotoxin that blocks voltage-gated sodium channels on the postsynaptic membrane. This results in a rapidly progressive, ascending flaccid paralysis and respiratory failure, not a hyperadrenergic state with pain and hypertension.
  • (D) Latrodectism: This is incorrect, although it is a key differential diagnosis. Envenomation by a widow spider (Latrodectus species) also involves a presynaptic toxin (alpha-latrotoxin) that causes massive neurotransmitter release, leading to severe pain and autonomic dysfunction. However, the onset is often slightly longer (30-60 minutes), and a classic feature of latrodectism is intense muscle rigidity, particularly a board-like abdomen, which is a distinguishing feature not mentioned in the question. The clinical picture described is a more quintessential fit for the catecholaminergic crisis seen in Irukandji syndrome.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to platelet function, choose the best option that corresponds with the stems that follow. The agent that causes reversible inhibition of COX-1 (cyclooxygenase)
Clopidogrel
Abciximab
Aspirin
Ibuprofen

Answer: (D) — Ibuprofen

Explanation:

Cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) is a key enzyme in platelet function. It catalyzes the conversion of arachidonic acid to prostaglandin H2, which is then converted to thromboxane A2 (TXA2). TXA2 is a potent promoter of platelet activation and aggregation, as well as a vasoconstrictor. Inhibition of COX-1 is a major therapeutic target for antiplatelet therapy.

Ibuprofen, a non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drug (NSAID), functions by competitively and reversibly inhibiting both COX-1 and COX-2 enzymes. Because the inhibition is reversible, its antiplatelet effect is transient and depends on the concentration of the drug in the plasma. Once ibuprofen is metabolized and cleared, COX-1 activity in platelets is restored.

Options:

  • (A) Clopidogrel: This is an antiplatelet agent, but it does not act on the COX pathway. Clopidogrel is a prodrug that is converted to an active metabolite, which then irreversibly blocks the P2Y12 ADP receptor on the platelet surface, preventing ADP-mediated platelet activation.
  • (B) Abciximab: This is a potent antiplatelet agent that functions as a glycoprotein (GP) IIb/IIIa receptor inhibitor. It is a monoclonal antibody fragment that binds to the GP IIb/IIIa receptor, preventing fibrinogen from binding and cross-linking platelets, which is the final common pathway for platelet aggregation. It has no effect on COX-1.
  • (C) Aspirin: This is the most well-known COX-1 inhibitor used for antiplatelet therapy. However, aspirin causes irreversible inhibition of COX-1 by acetylating a serine residue in the enzyme's active site. Because platelets are anucleated and cannot synthesize new proteins, the effect of a single dose of aspirin lasts for the entire lifespan of the affected platelets (approximately 7-10 days). The question specifically asks for an agent that causes reversible inhibition.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
The dermatome corresponding to thumb is:
C5
C7
C6
C8

Answer: C6

Explanation:

A dermatome is a specific area of skin innervated by sensory fibers from a single spinal nerve root. The dermatomes of the upper limb follow a logical, albeit sometimes overlapping, pattern originating from the cervical spinal cord (C5-T1 nerve roots), which form the brachial plexus.

The C6 spinal nerve root provides sensory innervation to the skin of the lateral aspect of the forearm and the first two digits of the hand. Specifically, the C6 dermatome classically includes the thumb (pollex) and often the index finger. Therefore, testing sensation on the thumb is a key part of the neurological examination to assess the integrity of the C6 nerve root.

A common mnemonic to remember the upper limb dermatomes is to stand in the anatomical position and trace the numbers: * C5 is the lateral shoulder/arm. * C6 runs down the lateral forearm to the thumb (making a "six-shooter" or "thumbs-up" sign with your hand can help you remember C6). * C7 covers the middle finger. * C8 covers the medial two fingers (ring and little finger). * T1 covers the medial forearm/axilla.

Options:

  • C5: Incorrect. The C5 dermatome supplies the skin over the lateral aspect of the shoulder and upper arm, often referred to as the "regimental badge" area over the deltoid muscle.
  • C7: Incorrect. The C7 dermatome typically supplies the skin of the middle finger and the central posterior aspect of the forearm and arm.
  • C8: Incorrect. The C8 dermatome supplies the skin of the medial aspect of the hand, including the little finger, the medial half of the ring finger, and the medial (ulnar) side of the forearm.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to hemostasis and platelet function, choose the best option that corresponds with the stems that follow:

ADP and serotonin lead to

 Platelet secretion
Platelet adhesion
Platelet aggregation
None of the following

Answer: (C) — Platelet aggregation Explanation: Adenosine diphosphate (ADP) and serotonin are potent chemical mediators stored within the dense granules of platelets. When platelets are initially activated (e.g., by adhering to exposed subendothelial collagen), they undergo degranulation, a process also known as platelet secretion. This releases ADP and serotonin into the local environment. These molecules then act as agonists, binding to specific receptors (e.g., P2Y12 for ADP) on the surface of other nearby platelets. This binding triggers further platelet activation and a conformational change in the Glycoprotein IIb/IIIa (GpIIb/IIIa) receptor, enabling it to bind fibrinogen. Fibrinogen then acts as a bridge, linking adjacent platelets together. This process of platelets sticking to one another to build up the platelet plug is known as platelet aggregation. Serotonin also contributes to this process and is a potent vasoconstrictor.

Options: * (A) Platelet secretion: This is the process by which ADP and serotonin are released from activated platelets. It is the preceding step, not the result of ADP and serotonin's action on other platelets. * (B) Platelet adhesion: This is the initial step of platelet plug formation where platelets stick to the injured vessel wall, specifically to exposed collagen. This process is primarily mediated by von Willebrand factor (vWF) and the platelet receptor Glycoprotein Ib (GpIb), not directly by ADP or serotonin. * (D) None of the following: This option is incorrect as platelet aggregation is the correct and primary function of secreted ADP and serotonin.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to hemostasis and platelet function, choose the best option that corresponds with the stems that follow: Patients with GPIa/IIa deficiency are likely to have:
Excessive platelet activation
Abnormal excessive bleeding
Excessive platelet adhesion
Mild bleeding diathesis

Answer: (D) — Mild bleeding diathesis

Explanation:

Glycoprotein Ia/IIa (GP Ia/IIa), also known as integrin α2β1, is a receptor on the surface of platelets. Its primary function is to bind directly to collagen exposed in the subendothelium of a damaged blood vessel. This binding is a critical initial step in platelet adhesion, which is the first stage of forming a primary hemostatic plug.

A deficiency in the GPIa/IIa receptor impairs the ability of platelets to adhere to collagen. This leads to a defect in primary hemostasis and, consequently, a tendency to bleed (a bleeding diathesis). However, the platelet adhesion process is not solely dependent on GPIa/IIa. Platelets also utilize other receptors, most notably Glycoprotein VI (GPVI) which also binds collagen and is crucial for activation, and the GPIb-IX-V complex which binds to von Willebrand factor (vWF) that is itself attached to collagen.

Due to this redundancy in collagen-binding mechanisms, an isolated deficiency of GPIa/IIa results in a bleeding disorder that is typically characterized as mild to moderate. Patients may experience easy bruising, prolonged bleeding from cuts, or mucocutaneous bleeding, but it is generally less severe than the bleeding seen in disorders involving critical, non-redundant pathways, such as Glanzmann thrombasthenia (GPIIb/IIIa deficiency) or Bernard-Soulier syndrome (GPIb deficiency). Therefore, "mild bleeding diathesis" is the most accurate clinical description.

Options:

  • (A) Excessive platelet activation: This is incorrect. GPIa/IIa is an adhesion receptor that contributes to platelet activation upon binding collagen. A deficiency would lead to impaired or reduced platelet activation, not an excessive one.
  • (B) Abnormal excessive bleeding: While a GPIa/IIa deficiency does cause abnormal bleeding, the term "excessive" is very general and typically reserved for more severe bleeding disorders (e.g., Glanzmann thrombasthenia). Option (D) is more precise and clinically accurate in describing the characteristic severity of this specific condition.
  • (C) Excessive platelet adhesion: This is incorrect. A deficiency in a key adhesion receptor like GPIa/IIa would cause defective or impaired platelet adhesion, which is the opposite of excessive adhesion.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to cardiac electrical conduction, choose the option that best matches stem to follow. Acetylcholine released at nerve endings increases the K+ conductance of nodal tissue mediated by M2 muscarinic receptors, which, via the βγsubunit of a G protein, open a special set of K+ channels. The resulting IKAch slows the depolarizing effect of Ih and leads to:
Increased slope of prepotential
Decreased slope of prepotential
Pacemaker cell Impulse generated
Pacemaker cell repolarisation

Answer: (B) — Decreased slope of prepotential

Explanation:

The question describes the mechanism of parasympathetic (vagal) stimulation on the heart's pacemaker cells, primarily those in the sinoatrial (SA) node.

  1. Acetylcholine (ACh) and M2 Receptors: ACh is the neurotransmitter released by the vagus nerve. It binds to M2 muscarinic receptors on the pacemaker cells.
  2. G-Protein Signaling: This binding activates an inhibitory G-protein (Gi). The Gi protein dissociates into αi and βγ subunits.
  3. Potassium Channel Activation: As the stem states, the βγ subunit directly binds to and opens a specific type of potassium channel known as the G-protein-gated inwardly-rectifying K+ channel (GIRK, or K_ACh).
  4. Increased K+ Conductance (IKAch): The opening of these channels increases the cell membrane's permeability to potassium ions (K+). This results in an increased outward flow of positive K+ ions, a current called IKAch.
  5. Effect on Membrane Potential: This outward flow of positive charge (efflux) makes the inside of the cell more negative, an effect known as hyperpolarization. This hyperpolarizing current directly opposes the inward, depolarizing "funny current" (Ih or If), which is responsible for the spontaneous diastolic depolarization (the prepotential or Phase 4).
  6. Slope of the Prepotential: The slope of the prepotential represents the rate of depolarization. By counteracting the depolarizing currents, the IKAch current slows down the rate at which the pacemaker cell's membrane potential drifts towards the threshold for firing an action potential. This slowing is visualized as a decreased (or shallower) slope of the prepotential. This mechanism is the primary reason why parasympathetic stimulation slows the heart rate (negative chronotropy).

Options:

  • (A) Increased slope of prepotential: This is incorrect. An increased slope would mean a faster rate of depolarization, leading to a faster heart rate (tachycardia). This is the effect of sympathetic stimulation via norepinephrine acting on β1 receptors, which increases the Ih funny current.
  • (C) Pacemaker cell Impulse generated: This is an outcome, not the direct effect on the prepotential itself. The process described in the stem actually delays the generation of the impulse by slowing the rate of depolarization. This option is too general and does not accurately describe the change in the prepotential's rate.
  • (D) Pacemaker cell repolarisation: Repolarisation (Phase 3) is the process of the cell's membrane potential returning to its most negative value after an action potential has fired. While the increased K+ conductance does contribute to hyperpolarization (making the maximum diastolic potential more negative), the specific effect mentioned in the stem—slowing the depolarizing effect of Ih—directly relates to the prepotential (Phase 4), not the repolarization phase of the preceding beat.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to cardiac electrical conduction, choose the option that best matches stem to follow. Inactivation of Na+ channels occur during which phase?
Phase 2 Plateau phase in cardiac muscle cell
Phase 1 rapid repolarisation in cardiac muscle
Phase 4 Resting membrane potential in cardiac muscle cell
Phase 3 Slow repolarisation phase in cardiac muscle cell

Answer: (A) — Phase 2 Plateau phase in cardiac muscle cell

Explanation:

The action potential of a ventricular cardiac muscle cell has five distinct phases (0-4). The inactivation of fast voltage-gated Na+ channels is a crucial event that defines the refractory properties of the heart muscle.

  • Phase 0 (Depolarization): Fast Na+ channels open, causing a rapid influx of Na+ and depolarizing the cell.
  • Peak of Phase 0 / Start of Phase 1: As the membrane potential reaches its peak (around +20 mV), the inactivation gates of the fast Na+ channels close. This process is voltage- and time-dependent.
  • Phase 1 (Initial Repolarization) & Phase 2 (Plateau): The Na+ channels remain in this inactivated state throughout Phase 1 and the entire duration of the long Phase 2 plateau. This sustained inactivation is responsible for the long absolute refractory period of the cardiac myocyte. This period is critical because it ensures that the ventricle has enough time to eject blood and refill before the next contraction, thereby preventing tetanus (sustained contraction).
  • Phase 3 (Repolarization): As the cell repolarizes, the Na+ channels begin to recover from inactivation and return to their resting (closed but activatable) state, making the cell excitable again.

While the process of inactivation begins at the end of Phase 0 and initiates Phase 1, the sustained state of inactivation is a key physiological characteristic of the much longer Phase 2 plateau. It is this prolonged inactivation during the plateau that underpins the heart's essential refractory properties. Therefore, Phase 2 is the best answer as it represents the period where Na+ channel inactivation has its most significant functional consequence.

Options:

  • (B) Phase 1 rapid repolarisation in cardiac muscle: Inactivation of Na+ channels is a key event that terminates the upstroke and helps initiate Phase 1. However, this phase is very brief. The question asks for the best match, and the prolonged state of inactivation during the much longer Phase 2 is functionally more significant for cardiac physiology.
  • (C) Phase 4 Resting membrane potential in cardiac muscle cell: During Phase 4, the cell is at rest (~-90 mV). The fast Na+ channels are in their resting state (closed but capable of opening), not their inactivated state. They are ready to open upon receiving a new stimulus.
  • (D) Phase 3 Slow repolarisation phase in cardiac muscle cell: During this phase, the cell is rapidly repolarizing. As the membrane potential becomes more negative, the Na+ channels are recovering from inactivation (de-inactivation) and returning to the resting state. This is the opposite of inactivation.

Confidence: 90%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
The dermatome corresponding to nipple line is:
C4
T2
T6
T4

Answer: (D) — T4

Explanation:

A dermatome is an area of skin that is primarily innervated by a single spinal nerve. These patterns are consistent among people and serve as crucial landmarks in neurology and physical examination to localize lesions within the spinal cord or spinal nerves.

The T4 dermatome corresponds to a horizontal band of skin at the level of the nipple line. This is a key anatomical landmark frequently used in clinical practice, particularly for assessing the sensory level of a spinal cord injury. A helpful mnemonic to remember this is "T4 at the teat-pore" (nipple).

Options:

  • (A) C4: This dermatome is located in the cervical region. It supplies the skin over the base of the neck, the supraclavicular area, and the top of the shoulders, often described as a "collar" or "cape" distribution.
  • (B) T2: This thoracic dermatome is located superior to the nipple line. It supplies the skin of the upper chest, including the axilla (armpit) and the medial aspect of the upper arm.
  • (C) T6: This thoracic dermatome is located inferior to the nipple line. It corresponds to the level of the xiphoid process, the cartilaginous lower tip of the sternum.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to cardiac electrical conduction, choose the option that best matches stem to follow. What event happens when the calcium current (ICa) increases due to opening of T Ca2+ channels?
Pacemaker cell depolarisation and initial prepotential
Pacemaker cell repolarisation
Pacemaker cell prepotential completed
Pacemaker cell Impulse generated

Answer: (C) — Pacemaker cell prepotential completed

Explanation:

The cardiac pacemaker action potential, found in cells of the sinoatrial (SA) and atrioventricular (AV) nodes, is responsible for the automaticity of the heart. This action potential has distinct phases driven by different ion currents. The question focuses on the role of the T-type calcium channel current (ICa-T).

  1. Phase 4 (Pacemaker Potential or Prepotential): This is the slow, spontaneous diastolic depolarization that brings the cell membrane to its threshold potential.

    • It begins with the opening of "funny" channels (HCN channels), which conduct an inward Na+ current (If), causing initial slow depolarization.
    • As the membrane potential becomes less negative (approaching -50 mV), the T-type (Transient) Ca2+ channels open. The resulting inward calcium current (ICa-T) provides the final depolarizing boost to bring the membrane potential to the threshold of approximately -40 mV. Therefore, this event effectively completes the prepotential.

  2. Phase 0 (Depolarization/Upstroke): Once the threshold is reached, voltage-gated L-type (Long-lasting) Ca2+ channels open, causing a large influx of Ca2+ and the rapid upstroke of the action potential. This is the "impulse generation."

  3. Phase 3 (Repolarization): The L-type Ca2+ channels inactivate, and voltage-gated K+ channels open. The resulting efflux of K+ ions repolarizes the cell, bringing the membrane potential back to its most negative value.

The opening of T-type Ca2+ channels is the crucial event that occurs in the latter part of Phase 4, pushing the cell to the threshold and thus completing the prepotential phase.

Options:

  • (A) Pacemaker cell depolarisation and initial prepotential: Incorrect. The main depolarization (upstroke, Phase 0) is caused by L-type Ca2+ channels. The initial prepotential is primarily driven by the funny current (If), not the T-type Ca2+ current.
  • (B) Pacemaker cell repolarisation: Incorrect. Repolarization is a process of the membrane potential becoming more negative, which is caused by the outflow of positive ions (K+), not the inflow of Ca2+.
  • (D) Pacemaker cell Impulse generated: Incorrect. The generation of the impulse, or the rapid upstroke of the action potential (Phase 0), is mediated by the opening of L-type Ca2+ channels once the threshold has been reached. T-type channels contribute to reaching the threshold, not the main impulse itself.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
With regards to cardiac electrical conduction, choose the option that best matches stem to follow. Activation of the M2 receptors by acetylcholine decreases cyclic adenosine 3′,5′-monophosphate (cAMP) in the cells, and this slows the opening of the Ca2+ channels.
Reduced slope of the prepotential
Pacemaker cell prepotential completed
Increased slope of prepotential
Pacemaker cell Impulse generated

Answer: (A) — Reduced slope of the prepotential Explanation: The question describes the parasympathetic stimulation of cardiac pacemaker cells (e.g., in the SA node). Acetylcholine (ACh), released by the vagus nerve, binds to muscarinic M2 receptors on these cells. This action has the following effects: 1. Inhibition of Adenylyl Cyclase: The M2 receptor is coupled to an inhibitory G-protein (Gi). When activated, the alpha subunit of this G-protein inhibits the enzyme adenylyl cyclase. This leads to a decrease in the intracellular concentration of cyclic adenosine 3′,5′-monophosphate (cAMP), as stated in the question. 2. Effect on Ion Channels: * Funny Current (If): cAMP normally stimulates the opening of HCN (hyperpolarization-activated cyclic nucleotide-gated) channels, which carry the "funny" current responsible for the initial part of the pacemaker potential. A decrease in cAMP reduces this inward Na+ current, slowing the rate of depolarization. * Calcium Current (ICa): cAMP also promotes the opening of L-type Ca2+ channels via protein kinase A (PKA) phosphorylation. A decrease in cAMP leads to less PKA activity and thus slows the opening of these Ca2+ channels. This reduces the inward Ca2+ current, further slowing the rate of depolarization towards the threshold. 3. Overall Effect: The combined effect of reducing the inward "funny" current and the inward calcium current is a decrease in the rate of spontaneous diastolic depolarization (Phase 4). Graphically, this is represented as a reduced (flatter) slope of the prepotential. A flatter slope means it takes more time for the membrane potential to reach the threshold for firing an action potential, resulting in a slower heart rate (negative chronotropy).

Options: * (B) Pacemaker cell prepotential completed: This is an event in the cardiac cycle, not a description of the effect of acetylcholine. ACh's action is to slow down the rate at which this completion occurs. * (C) Increased slope of prepotential: This is incorrect. An increased slope is characteristic of sympathetic stimulation (via norepinephrine acting on β1 receptors), which increases cAMP levels, enhances the funny and calcium currents, and thus increases the heart rate (positive chronotropy). * (D) Pacemaker cell Impulse generated: This is the result of the prepotential reaching the threshold. Acetylcholine's effect is to delay the generation of this impulse by slowing the preceding prepotential phase.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to viral infections, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: CNS complications (meningitis, encephalitis, meningoencephalitis) occur in about 1 in 150 infections and results in long-term cognitive and neurologic impair­ment in many survivors. 
Mumps
Enterovirus
West Nile virus
Measles

Answer: (C) — West Nile virus Explanation: West Nile virus (WNV) is a flavivirus transmitted by mosquitoes. The clinical spectrum of WNV infection is broad. While approximately 80% of infections are asymptomatic, about 20% develop a febrile illness (West Nile fever). A small but significant proportion, approximately 1 in 150 infected individuals, will develop severe neuroinvasive disease, which includes meningitis, encephalitis, or acute flaccid myelitis. Among patients who survive neuroinvasive WNV disease, long-term sequelae such as fatigue, memory problems, weakness, and depression are common, affecting a large percentage of them. This combination of a ~1 in 150 incidence for severe CNS disease and significant long-term impairment in survivors is characteristic of West Nile virus.

Options: (A) Mumps: Mumps virus can cause CNS complications, most commonly aseptic meningitis, which can occur in up to 15% of cases but is often mild and resolves without long-term issues. Encephalitis is much rarer (about 1 in 6,000 cases) and can cause severe sequelae, but the overall incidence and outcome profile does not match the question's description as well as WNV does.

(B) Enterovirus: Enteroviruses (e.g., coxsackievirus, echovirus) are the most common cause of viral (aseptic) meningitis. However, the vast majority of enterovirus infections are asymptomatic or cause only a mild illness. While they can cause encephalitis or poliomyelitis-like paralysis, the incidence of severe neuroinvasive disease leading to long-term impairment is much lower than 1 in 150 infections, and most cases of enteroviral meningitis resolve fully.

(D) Measles: Measles virus can cause severe CNS complications. Acute post-infectious encephalitis occurs in approximately 1 in 1,000 cases. A rarer but fatal complication is subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE), which develops years after infection. The incidence of acute encephalitis (1:1000) is significantly lower than the 1:150 figure stated in the question.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to cerebrovascular disease, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: There is an increased incidence of this pathology in individuals with certain Mendelian disorders (e.g., autosomal dominant polycystic kidney disease, Ehlers-Danlos syndrome type IV, neurofibromatosis type 1 [NF1], and Marfan syndrome), fibromuscular dysplasia of extracranial arteries, and coarctation of the aorta.
Saccular aneurysm
Arteriovenous malformation
Hypertensive intracerebral haemorrhage
Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy

Answer: (A) — Saccular aneurysm

Explanation:

The statement describes a set of conditions that predispose individuals to the formation of saccular (or "berry") aneurysms. Saccular aneurysms are thin-walled outpouchings of arteries, most commonly found at arterial bifurcations in the circle of Willis. Their development is attributed to a congenital or acquired weakness in the arterial wall, specifically in the tunica media and internal elastic lamina. The conditions listed in the question all contribute to this arterial wall weakness:

  • Mendelian Disorders:
    • Autosomal Dominant Polycystic Kidney Disease (ADPKD): Associated with a 4-5 times higher risk of saccular aneurysms. The mechanism is multifactorial, involving both systemic hypertension (common in ADPKD) and potential defects in extracellular matrix proteins like polycystins, which affect vessel wall integrity.
    • Ehlers-Danlos Syndrome Type IV (vascular type): A disorder of type III collagen synthesis. Since type III collagen is a crucial structural component of blood vessel walls, its deficiency leads to fragile arteries prone to aneurysm formation and rupture.
    • Neurofibromatosis Type 1 (NF1): Can cause a systemic vasculopathy characterized by abnormal proliferation of cells within the vessel wall, leading to stenosis, occlusion, and aneurysm formation.
    • Marfan Syndrome: Caused by a mutation in the FBN1 gene, leading to defective fibrillin-1, a major component of elastic fibers. This weakens the vessel walls, predisposing them to aneurysm formation (most famously in the aorta, but also in cerebral arteries).
  • Other Conditions:
    • Fibromuscular Dysplasia (FMD): A non-atherosclerotic, non-inflammatory disease that causes abnormal cell growth in arterial walls, leading to weakening and aneurysm formation, particularly in the carotid and vertebral arteries.
    • Coarctation of the Aorta: The congenital narrowing of the aorta causes significant hypertension in the vessels supplying the head and upper extremities. This chronic hemodynamic stress accelerates the formation and potential rupture of pre-existing weaknesses in the cerebral arteries.

Options:

  • (B) Arteriovenous malformation (AVM): Incorrect. AVMs are congenital tangles of abnormal blood vessels where arteries connect directly to veins without an intervening capillary bed. While some genetic syndromes, such as Hereditary Hemorrhagic Telangiectasia (HHT or Osler-Weber-Rendu syndrome), are strongly associated with AVMs, they are not classically linked to the specific constellation of disorders listed in the question (ADPKD, Marfan, etc.).

  • (C) Hypertensive intracerebral haemorrhage: Incorrect. This refers to bleeding into the brain parenchyma caused by the rupture of small, deep penetrating arteries (e.g., lenticulostriate arteries) weakened by chronic hypertension (forming Charcot-Bouchard microaneurysms). While some of the listed conditions (ADPKD, coarctation) cause hypertension, the question's list of primary connective tissue disorders points to a structural defect in larger vessels, which is characteristic of saccular aneurysms, not small-vessel hypertensive disease.

  • (D) Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy (CAA): Incorrect. CAA is a condition characterized by the deposition of amyloid-β protein in the walls of small and medium-sized cerebral and leptomeningeal vessels. This weakens the vessels and is a common cause of lobar hemorrhages in the elderly, often in the absence of hypertension. It is associated with aging and Alzheimer's disease, not the specific genetic and congenital disorders listed.

Confidence: 100%
