MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A 68-year-old suffers from congestive cardiac failure. He has elevated levels of BNP (B-type natriuretic peptide). Regarding BNP, which of the following statements is CORRECT?
BNP increases sodium absorption from renal tubules
BNP stimulates renin secretion
BNP secretion is proportional to the degree to which the ventricles are stretched.
BNP enhances the pressor effect of angiotensin II

Answer: (C) — BNP secretion is proportional to the degree to which the ventricles are stretched.

Explanation:

B-type natriuretic peptide (BNP), along with A-type natriuretic peptide (ANP), is part of the body's counter-regulatory system to the Renin-Angiotensin-Aldosterone System (RAAS). The primary stimulus for the synthesis and release of BNP is myocardial wall stress or stretch. In conditions like congestive cardiac failure, the ventricles are overloaded with volume and pressure, leading to significant stretching of the ventricular muscle cells (myocytes). This stretch triggers the release of BNP into the bloodstream. The level of circulating BNP directly correlates with the severity of ventricular dysfunction and wall tension, making it a crucial biomarker for diagnosing, prognosticating, and monitoring heart failure. The physiological effects of BNP are aimed at reducing this wall stress by promoting vasodilation (reducing afterload), and increasing sodium (natriuresis) and water (diuresis) excretion by the kidneys (reducing preload).

Options:

  • (A) BNP increases sodium absorption from renal tubules: This is incorrect. The name "natriuretic peptide" indicates its function: natriuresis means the excretion of sodium in the urine. BNP acts on the kidneys to decrease sodium reabsorption in the collecting ducts and other tubular segments, leading to increased sodium and water loss.

  • (B) BNP stimulates renin secretion: This is incorrect. BNP is a physiological antagonist of the RAAS. It acts to suppress the RAAS by inhibiting the release of renin from the juxtaglomerular cells in the kidneys. This, in turn, reduces the production of angiotensin II and aldosterone.

  • (D) BNP enhances the pressor effect of angiotensin II: This is incorrect. Angiotensin II is a potent vasoconstrictor (a pressor agent). BNP has the opposite effect; it promotes vasodilation of both arteries and veins, thereby reducing systemic vascular resistance (afterload) and central venous pressure (preload). It directly antagonizes the vasoconstrictive effects of angiotensin II.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
Which of the following is true regarding the action of aldosterone on renal tubular function?
It increases tubular secretion of Na+ in association with reabsorption of K+ and H+
It increases tubular secretion of Na+ and HCO3
It increases tubular reabsorption of Na+ and HCO3
It increases the reabsorption of Na+ in association with secretion of K+ and H+

Answer: (D) — It increases the reabsorption of Na+ in association with secretion of K+ and H+

Explanation:

Aldosterone is a steroid hormone produced by the zona glomerulosa of the adrenal cortex. It plays a crucial role in the long-term regulation of blood pressure by modulating sodium and potassium balance in the body. Its primary site of action is on the principal cells and type A intercalated cells of the late distal convoluted tubule (DCT) and the cortical collecting duct of the nephron.

  1. Action on Principal Cells: Aldosterone increases the synthesis and insertion of:

    • Epithelial Sodium Channels (ENaC) on the apical (luminal) membrane. This enhances the reabsorption of sodium (Na+) from the tubular fluid into the cell.
    • Na+/K+-ATPase pumps on the basolateral membrane. This pump actively transports the reabsorbed Na+ out of the cell into the interstitial fluid (and then into the blood) while simultaneously pumping potassium (K+) from the blood into the cell.
    • Renal Outer Medullary K+ (ROMK) channels on the apical membrane. The increased intracellular K+ concentration and the negative electrical potential in the lumen (created by Na+ reabsorption) drive the secretion of K+ into the tubular fluid for excretion.

  2. Action on Type A Intercalated Cells: Aldosterone stimulates the activity of the H+-ATPase pump on the apical membrane. This leads to increased secretion of hydrogen ions (H+) into the tubular fluid. This process also contributes to the reabsorption of bicarbonate (HCO3−) into the blood, helping to correct acidosis.

In summary, the net effect of aldosterone is the reabsorption of Na+ (and water, which follows osmotically) and the secretion/excretion of K+ and H+.

Options:

  • (A) It increases tubular secretion of Na+ in association with reabsorption of K+ and H+

    • This is incorrect. Aldosterone causes Na+ reabsorption, not secretion. It also causes K+ and H+ secretion, not reabsorption. This option describes the opposite effect.

  • (B) It increases tubular secretion of Na+ and HCO3

    • This is incorrect. Aldosterone causes Na+ reabsorption. It indirectly causes HCO3− reabsorption by promoting H+ secretion.

  • (C) It increases tubular reabsorption of Na+ and HCO3

    • This is partially correct. Aldosterone does increase Na+ reabsorption, and it indirectly leads to increased HCO3− reabsorption. However, this option is incomplete as it omits the critically important and direct effect on K+ and H+ secretion. Option (D) provides a more comprehensive and accurate description of aldosterone's primary functions.

  • (D) It increases the reabsorption of Na+ in association with secretion of K+ and H+

    • This is the correct and most complete answer. It accurately describes the main physiological effects of aldosterone on renal tubular function: retaining sodium while eliminating potassium and hydrogen ions.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Distal radioulnar joint is a
Synovial joint -Plane
Fibrous joint
Secondary cartilaginous joint
Primary cartilaginous joint

Answer: (A) — Synovial joint -Plane

Explanation:

The distal radioulnar joint (DRUJ) is the articulation between the head of the ulna and the ulnar notch of the distal radius. Structurally, it is classified as a synovial joint. This means it is characterized by a joint capsule, a synovial membrane that secretes synovial fluid for lubrication, and articular surfaces covered with hyaline cartilage, all of which facilitate free movement.

Functionally, the DRUJ is a pivot (trochoid) joint. This type of joint allows for rotation around a single axis. In this case, the rounded head of the ulna rotates within the ulnar notch of the radius and a fibrocartilaginous articular disc (part of the Triangular Fibrocartilage Complex or TFCC), enabling the movements of pronation and supination of the forearm.

While the option correctly identifies the joint as synovial, it incorrectly sub-classifies it as a plane joint (which allows for gliding movements). However, among the given choices, this is the most accurate option because the other three describe fundamentally different types of joints. The DRUJ is unequivocally a synovial joint, making this the best possible answer despite the error in the subtype.

Options:

  • (B) Fibrous joint: This is incorrect. Fibrous joints connect bones with dense fibrous connective tissue and allow for little to no movement (e.g., sutures of the skull). The interosseous membrane between the shafts of the radius and ulna is a type of fibrous joint (a syndesmosis), but the distal articulation itself is a mobile synovial joint.
  • (C) Secondary cartilaginous joint: This is incorrect. Also known as a symphysis, this type of joint has bones covered by hyaline cartilage that are then joined by a pad of fibrocartilage (e.g., the pubic symphysis or intervertebral discs). The DRUJ has a complex structure including an articular disc (TFCC), but it is primarily a synovial joint with a joint cavity, not a symphysis.
  • (D) Primary cartilaginous joint: This is incorrect. Also known as a synchondrosis, this joint connects bones via a plate of hyaline cartilage and is typically immobile and often temporary (e.g., the epiphyseal growth plates in long bones). The DRUJ is a permanent and freely movable joint.

Confidence: 95% (The confidence is slightly reduced due to the question's inaccuracy in sub-classifying the joint as "Plane" instead of "Pivot." However, it is the only option that correctly identifies the main joint class).

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Intervertebral disc is a
Primary cartilaginous joint
Synovial joint -Plane
Secondary cartilaginous joint
Fibrous joint

Answer: (C) — Secondary cartilaginous joint

Explanation:

An intervertebral disc forms a joint between the bodies of two adjacent vertebrae. This type of joint is classified as a secondary cartilaginous joint, also known as a symphysis.

The defining features of a secondary cartilaginous joint are: 1. The articular surfaces of the bones are covered by a thin layer of hyaline cartilage. In the vertebral column, these are the vertebral endplates on the superior and inferior surfaces of the vertebral bodies. 2. The bones are united by a strong, resilient pad of fibrocartilage. In this case, that pad is the intervertebral disc itself, specifically its outer layer, the annulus fibrosus. 3. These joints are typically found in the midline of the body and allow for a small amount of movement, contributing to flexibility while providing significant strength and shock absorption. The pubic symphysis is another classic example.

Options:

  • (A) Primary cartilaginous joint: This type of joint (also called a synchondrosis) involves bones united solely by hyaline cartilage. They are typically temporary, such as the epiphyseal growth plates in long bones, and allow for growth rather than significant movement. The first sternocostal joint is a permanent example, but it is largely immobile. This does not describe the intervertebral disc, which is composed of fibrocartilage and allows for movement.

  • (B) Synovial joint - Plane: Synovial joints are characterized by a joint capsule, a synovial membrane producing synovial fluid, and articular cartilage covering the bone ends. The joint formed by the intervertebral disc lacks these features. It is important not to confuse this joint with the zygapophyseal (facet) joints, which are located between the articular processes of the vertebrae and are indeed plane synovial joints. The question specifically asks about the intervertebral disc.

  • (D) Fibrous joint: Fibrous joints (synarthroses) connect bones with dense fibrous connective tissue, allowing for very little or no movement. Examples include the sutures of the skull and the syndesmosis between the tibia and fibula. The intervertebral disc is made of cartilage, not fibrous tissue, so this classification is incorrect.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to cerebrovascular disease, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: The risk factor most commonly associated with lobar haemorrhages and numerous small “microbleeds” is
Hypertensive intracerebral haemorrhage
Arteriovenous malformation
Subarachnoid haemorrhage
Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy

Answer: (D) — Cerebral Amyloid angiopathy Explanation: Cerebral Amyloid Angiopathy (CAA) is a condition characterized by the deposition of amyloid-β protein within the walls of small to medium-sized arteries, arterioles, and capillaries of the cerebral cortex and leptomeninges. This deposition weakens the vessel walls, making them brittle and prone to rupture. Consequently, CAA is a leading cause of spontaneous, often recurrent, intracerebral hemorrhages that are characteristically located in the lobes of the brain (lobar hemorrhages), such as the frontal, parietal, or occipital lobes. Furthermore, the diffuse nature of the vessel wall pathology in CAA leads to the development of numerous small, chronic hemorrhages known as "microbleeds," which are best visualized on gradient-echo or susceptibility-weighted MRI sequences and are typically restricted to the cortical-subcortical regions.

Options: (A) Hypertensive intracerebral haemorrhage: Chronic hypertension is the most common cause of intracerebral hemorrhage overall. However, it typically affects the small, deep penetrating arteries (e.g., lenticulostriate arteries) supplying the basal ganglia, thalamus, pons, and cerebellum. The resulting hemorrhages are therefore typically deep in location, not lobar. While hypertension can also cause microbleeds, they are usually found in these same deep brain structures.

(B) Arteriovenous malformation: An arteriovenous malformation (AVM) is a congenital tangle of abnormal blood vessels that can rupture and cause a large intracerebral hemorrhage, which may be lobar in location. However, an AVM is typically a single, focal lesion and is not associated with the pathological process that causes numerous and widespread microbleeds throughout the brain.

(C) Subarachnoid haemorrhage: This refers to bleeding into the subarachnoid space, the area between the arachnoid and pia mater. The most common cause is the rupture of a saccular (berry) aneurysm, typically located in the Circle of Willis at the base of the brain. While a subarachnoid hemorrhage can sometimes extend into the brain parenchyma, it is a distinct clinical entity and is not the primary cause of multiple microbleeds or a primary lobar hemorrhage.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regard to cerebrovascular disease, match the statement that follows to the option that fits best: This pathology usually is found in the cerebellum, pons or subcortical regions and have 'low flow channels’ of blood without shunting.
Arteriovenous malformation
Cavernous malformation
Mycotic aneurysm
Saccular aneurysm

Answer: (B) — Cavernous malformation

Explanation:

A cavernous malformation (also known as a cavernoma or cavernous angioma) is a type of cerebrovascular malformation characterized by a collection of dilated, thin-walled, abnormal blood vessels. These vessels form "caverns" or sinusoidal spaces that are clustered together, often resembling a mulberry or popcorn. Critically, these lesions are low-flow, meaning blood moves through them slowly, and they lack a direct arteriovenous shunt. There is no significant intervening brain tissue between the vascular channels. Their typical locations include the subcortical white matter, pons, and cerebellum, which perfectly aligns with the description in the question.

Options:

  • (A) Arteriovenous malformation (AVM): This is incorrect. An AVM is a high-flow lesion consisting of a tangled mass of vessels (nidus) that forms a direct connection between arteries and veins, bypassing the normal capillary bed. This direct connection is known as an arteriovenous shunt, which is explicitly ruled out in the question's description.
  • (C) Mycotic aneurysm: This is incorrect. A mycotic aneurysm is a focal dilation of an artery caused by an infection of the vessel wall, typically from septic emboli (e.g., from infective endocarditis). It is not a malformation of vascular channels and does not fit the description of a low-flow system without shunting.
  • (D) Saccular aneurysm: This is incorrect. A saccular (or "berry") aneurysm is a balloon-like outpouching of an artery, most commonly found at bifurcation points in the Circle of Willis. These are high-pressure lesions within the arterial system and are not composed of low-flow channels.

Confidence: 95%

It helps predict prognosis and guide management. The scale is based on two key clinical factors: the Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score and the presence or absence of a major motor deficit (e.g., hemiparesis, hemiplegia).

The WFNS grading system is as follows: * Grade 1: GCS 15, no motor deficit * Grade 2: GCS 13-14, no motor deficit * Grade 3: GCS 13-14, with motor deficit * Grade 4: GCS 7-12, with or without motor deficit * Grade 5: GCS 3-6, with or without motor deficit

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regards to types of brain haemorrhage and brain trauma, match the stem below with the option that fits best. Usually associated with a (temporal) skull fracture in adults and presents with rapidly evolving neurologic symptoms. Does not cross suture lines on CT imaging; the blood is usually arterial. 
Subdural Haemorrhage
Subarachnoid Haemorrhage
Epidural haemorrhage
Cerebral contusions

Answer: (C) — Epidural haemorrhage Explanation: The description provided in the question stem is a classic presentation of an epidural haemorrhage (EDH), also known as an epidural hematoma.

  • Pathophysiology: An EDH is a collection of blood in the potential space between the dura mater and the inner table of the skull. It is most commonly caused by trauma, particularly a fracture of the temporal bone, which lacerates the middle meningeal artery.
  • Clinical Presentation: Because the source of bleeding is arterial, the hematoma can expand rapidly under high pressure. This leads to a swift increase in intracranial pressure (ICP) and rapidly evolving neurologic symptoms (e.g., headache, vomiting, seizure, focal deficits). The classic, though not always present, history is a "lucid interval" where the patient experiences a brief loss of consciousness, recovers for a period, and then rapidly deteriorates.
  • Radiological Features: On a non-contrast CT scan of the head, an EDH appears as a biconvex or lens-shaped (lentiform) hyperdense collection. The dura mater is tightly adherent to the skull at the suture lines. This firm attachment prevents the hematoma from spreading across the sutures, a key diagnostic feature.

Options: * (A) Subdural Haemorrhage: This is incorrect. A subdural haemorrhage (SDH) is typically caused by the tearing of bridging veins that cross the subdural space. The bleeding is venous and therefore lower pressure, leading to a more gradual onset of symptoms (subacute to chronic). On CT, an SDH is crescent-shaped and can cross suture lines, but it is limited by dural reflections (e.g., falx cerebri, tentorium cerebelli). * (B) Subarachnoid Haemorrhage: This is incorrect. A subarachnoid haemorrhage (SAH) is bleeding into the subarachnoid space, where cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) circulates. Traumatic SAH is common, but the classic non-traumatic cause is a ruptured saccular (berry) aneurysm. On CT, the blood appears as hyperdensity within the sulci and basal cisterns, outlining the brain's gyri, rather than forming a discrete, localized collection that doesn't cross sutures. * (D) Cerebral contusions: This is incorrect. Cerebral contusions are bruises of the brain parenchyma itself (intraparenchymal injury), often occurring at the tips of the frontal and temporal lobes. On CT, they appear as areas of edema and hemorrhage within the brain tissue, not as a distinct collection in the epidural space.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
With regards to types of brain haemorrhage and brain trauma, match the stem below with the option that fits best. With increasing displacement of the temporal lobe, the third cranial nerve is compromised, resulting in pupillary dilation; the posterior cerebral artery may also be compressed, resulting in ischemic injury to the primary visual cortex; the contralateral cerebral peduncle may be compressed, resulting in hemiparesis ipsilateral to the side of the herniation.
Transtentorial uncal herniation
Transtentorial central herniation
Tonsillar herniation
Subfalcine herniation

Answer: (A) — Transtentorial uncal herniation

Explanation:

The scenario described is the classic clinical and pathological progression of transtentorial uncal herniation. This occurs when a unilateral expanding supratentorial mass (e.g., epidural hematoma, tumor, abscess) displaces the uncus, which is the most medial part of the temporal lobe, inferiorly and medially over the free edge of the tentorium cerebelli. This displacement compresses several critical structures located at the tentorial notch:

  1. Ipsilateral Cranial Nerve III (Oculomotor Nerve): As the uncus herniates, it directly compresses the oculomotor nerve, which runs between the posterior cerebral artery and the superior cerebellar artery. The parasympathetic fibers responsible for pupillary constriction are located on the periphery of the nerve, making them most vulnerable to compression. This leads to a loss of parasympathetic tone, resulting in an unopposed sympathetic action that causes a fixed and dilated pupil ("blown pupil") on the same side (ipsilateral) as the herniation.
  2. Ipsilateral Posterior Cerebral Artery (PCA): The PCA can also be compressed by the herniating uncus, leading to ischemia in its territory, which includes the primary visual cortex in the occipital lobe. This results in a contralateral homonymous hemianopia (loss of the opposite visual field in both eyes).
  3. Contralateral Cerebral Peduncle: As the herniation progresses, it pushes the entire midbrain against the sharp, rigid edge of the tentorium on the opposite side. This compresses the contralateral cerebral peduncle, which contains the corticospinal tracts. Since these motor tracts decussate (cross over) lower down in the medulla, compression of the contralateral peduncle results in hemiparesis (weakness) on the side ipsilateral to the original lesion and herniation. This false localizing sign is known as Kernohan's notch phenomenon.

Options:

  • (B) Transtentorial central herniation: This involves a symmetric, downward displacement of the diencephalon (thalamus) and brainstem through the tentorial notch, usually due to diffuse increases in intracranial pressure (e.g., cerebral edema). The progression of signs is rostral-to-caudal, affecting consciousness first, followed by bilateral pupillary changes (small, then fixed), and decorticate then decerebrate posturing. It is not initiated by the specific displacement of the temporal lobe as described.
  • (C) Tonsillar herniation: This involves the displacement of the cerebellar tonsils through the foramen magnum. This compresses the medulla oblongata, where the vital cardiorespiratory centers are located. The primary consequence is life-threatening cardiorespiratory arrest, often accompanied by neck stiffness and flaccid paralysis. It does not involve the temporal lobe or the structures around the tentorial notch.
  • (D) Subfalcine herniation: This is the most common type of herniation, where the cingulate gyrus is displaced under the falx cerebri to the opposite side. The main clinical significance is the potential compression of the anterior cerebral arteries (ACA), which can lead to ischemic injury of the medial aspects of the frontal and parietal lobes, classically causing contralateral leg weakness. It does not produce the CN III or brainstem signs described in the stem.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Regarding visual pathways and corresponding structures, this part of the brain receives dual supply from posterior cerebral artery as well as collateral supply from the middle cerebral artery. This partly explains why some sparing of Visual field defects occurs when it is affected.  
Thalamus
Parietal lobe
Temporal lobe
Occipital lobe

Answer: (D) — Occipital lobe

Explanation:

The occipital lobe, specifically the primary visual cortex (Brodmann area 17) located along the calcarine sulcus, is the primary destination for visual information. The vast majority of the visual cortex is supplied by the posterior cerebral artery (PCA). However, the most posterior part of the occipital lobe, the occipital pole, which corresponds to the cortical representation of the macula (central vision), often has a dual blood supply. It receives blood from the PCA and also frequently receives collateral anastomotic branches from the distal middle cerebral artery (MCA).

This dual supply is clinically significant. In the event of a PCA occlusion (stroke), which would typically cause a contralateral homonymous hemianopia (loss of the opposite visual field), the occipital pole may be spared due to its collateral supply from the MCA. This results in the classic finding of contralateral homonymous hemianopia with macular sparing, where central vision is preserved while the peripheral visual field is lost.

Options:

  • (A) Thalamus: The thalamus, specifically the lateral geniculate nucleus (LGN), is a critical relay station in the visual pathway. It is primarily supplied by thalamogeniculate and posterior choroidal branches of the PCA. It does not typically receive a significant collateral supply from the MCA that would lead to the described sparing phenomenon.
  • (B) Parietal lobe: The parietal lobe is involved in the dorsal ("where") visual stream, processing spatial awareness. It is primarily supplied by the MCA. While lesions can cause complex visual deficits (e.g., optic ataxia, visual neglect), it is not the site of the primary visual cortex, nor does it have the specific PCA/MCA dual supply that explains macular sparing.
  • (C) Temporal lobe: The temporal lobe contains a portion of the optic radiations (Meyer's loop) and is involved in the ventral ("what") visual stream for object recognition. It is primarily supplied by the MCA. A lesion in Meyer's loop causes a contralateral superior quadrantanopia ("pie in the sky" defect), which is different from the pattern described.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Distal tibiofibular joint is a
Fibrous joint
Synovial joint -Plane
Secondary cartilaginous joint
Primary cartilaginous joint

Answer: (A) — Fibrous joint

Explanation:

The distal tibiofibular joint is structurally classified as a fibrous joint, specifically a syndesmosis. In a syndesmosis, two adjacent bones are linked by strong ligaments or an interosseous membrane. The distal tibia and fibula are held together firmly by the anterior tibiofibular ligament, the posterior tibiofibular ligament, and the interosseous tibiofibular ligament (a thickened, distal continuation of the interosseous membrane). This arrangement allows for very slight movement, which is crucial for the normal mechanics of the ankle joint, particularly during dorsiflexion when the wider anterior part of the talus wedges between the tibia and fibula.

Options:

  • (B) Synovial joint - Plane: This is incorrect. A plane synovial joint allows for gliding or sliding movements and is characterized by a joint capsule and synovial fluid. While the proximal tibiofibular joint is a plane synovial joint, the distal tibiofibular joint is a fibrous syndesmosis.
  • (C) Secondary cartilaginous joint: This is incorrect. A secondary cartilaginous joint, or symphysis, is a joint where the bones are covered by hyaline cartilage and joined by a pad of fibrocartilage. Examples include the pubic symphysis and the joints between vertebral bodies. The distal tibiofibular joint lacks a fibrocartilaginous pad.
  • (D) Primary cartilaginous joint: This is incorrect. A primary cartilaginous joint, or synchondrosis, is a joint where bones are united by a plate of hyaline cartilage. These joints are typically immovable and are often temporary, such as the epiphyseal growth plates in long bones. The distal tibiofibular joint is connected by ligaments, not hyaline cartilage.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHYSIOLOGY
A person at high altitude breathes air at an atmospheric pressure of 500 mmHg. Assuming normal water vapour pressure, the partial pressure of inspired O2 is:
105
85
75
95

Answer: (4) — 95

Explanation:

The partial pressure of inspired oxygen (PᵢO₂) is calculated using the following formula, which accounts for the fact that inspired air becomes fully saturated with water vapor in the upper airways.

Formula: PᵢO₂ = FᵢO₂ × (Pₐₜₘ - P₋ₕ₂ₒ)

Where: * PᵢO₂ is the partial pressure of inspired oxygen. * FᵢO₂ is the fraction of inspired oxygen. This is constant at approximately 21% (or 0.21) regardless of altitude. * Pₐₜₘ is the total atmospheric pressure. In this case, it is 500 mmHg at high altitude. * P₋ₕ₂ₒ is the partial pressure of water vapor in the airways. At body temperature (37°C), air is saturated with water vapor, which exerts a constant pressure of 47 mmHg.

Calculation: 1. Subtract the water vapor pressure from the total atmospheric pressure to find the pressure of the dry gases: * 500 mmHg - 47 mmHg = 453 mmHg 2. Multiply this pressure by the fraction of oxygen in the air: * PᵢO₂ = 0.21 × 453 mmHg * PᵢO₂ = 95.13 mmHg

The calculated value is approximately 95 mmHg.

Options:

  • (1) 105: This value is incorrect. It is calculated by incorrectly multiplying the FᵢO₂ by the total atmospheric pressure without first subtracting the water vapor pressure (0.21 × 500 mmHg = 105 mmHg). This mistake fails to account for the humidification of air in the respiratory tract.
  • (2) 85: This value is incorrect. It does not correspond to the correct calculation for inspired PO₂. It may be a distractor value, potentially closer to what the alveolar PO₂ (PₐO₂) might be after gas exchange, but it is not the correct inspired PO₂.
  • (3) 75: This value is incorrect and does not result from the standard calculation. It is likely a distractor.
  • (4) 95: This is the correct answer, derived by accurately applying the formula for the partial pressure of inspired, humidified air.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 25-year old presents to ED upper abdominal pain and jaundice. Investigations reveal deranged LFTs with elevated tranaminases. The increase in serum enzyme levels most likely results from which of the following changes in the hepatocytes?
Swelling of mitochondria
Defects in the cell membrane
Clumping of nuclear chromatin
Dispersion of ribosomes

Answer: (B) — Defects in the cell membrane Explanation: The patient's presentation with upper abdominal pain, jaundice, and elevated transaminases (like ALT and AST) is classic for acute hepatocellular injury (hepatitis). Transaminases are enzymes that are normally located within the cytoplasm of hepatocytes. For these enzymes to appear in high concentrations in the serum, they must be released from the damaged liver cells. This release occurs when the hepatocyte cell membrane loses its integrity and becomes permeable or ruptures. This loss of plasma membrane integrity is a hallmark of irreversible cell injury and necrosis. The damaged membrane can no longer maintain the concentration gradient between the cell's interior and the exterior, leading to the leakage of intracellular enzymes into the bloodstream.

Options: (A) Swelling of mitochondria: This is an early and reversible sign of hypoxic or toxic cell injury. It occurs due to failure of ATP-dependent ion pumps, leading to an influx of water and ions. While it indicates the cell is under stress, it does not, by itself, cause the release of large cytoplasmic enzymes into the blood. Significant enzyme leakage requires a more severe injury that compromises the outer cell membrane. (C) Clumping of nuclear chromatin: This change, known as pyknosis, is a feature of irreversible cell injury and cell death (necrosis or apoptosis). While it occurs in cells that are also leaking their contents, the clumping of chromatin itself is not the direct cause of enzyme release. The enzyme release is due to the concurrent damage to the cell membrane. (D) Dispersion of ribosomes: The detachment of ribosomes from the rough endoplasmic reticulum is an early, reversible change seen in cell injury. This leads to a decrease in protein synthesis but does not cause the leakage of existing enzymes out of the cell.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 24-year old female presents with chronic headaches. You plan to perform a lumbar puncture to exclude benign intracranial hypertension. Regarding cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) which one of the following statements is CORRECT?
The hydrostatic pressure of CSF is unchanged by increases in venous pressure
CSF prevents compression of cerebral blood vessels and cranial nerve roots
CSF is produced at a rate of 1000ml per day
CSF is absorbed through the choroid plexus

Answer: (B) — CSF prevents compression of cerebral blood vessels and cranial nerve roots Explanation: One of the primary functions of cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) is to provide mechanical protection and buoyancy for the brain and spinal cord. The brain, with an average weight of about 1400 grams in air, has an effective weight of only about 50 grams when suspended in CSF. This buoyancy prevents the brain from compressing the delicate blood vessels and cranial nerve roots at its base against the inner surface of the skull. Without this support, the brain's own weight would lead to ischemia and nerve damage.

Options: (A) The hydrostatic pressure of CSF is unchanged by increases in venous pressure This statement is incorrect. The Monro-Kellie doctrine states that the intracranial volume is fixed and contains three components: brain parenchyma, blood, and CSF. An increase in the volume of one component must be compensated by a decrease in another. An increase in central venous pressure (e.g., from a Valsalva maneuver or heart failure) impedes venous drainage from the dural sinuses. This increases intracranial blood volume and also reduces the pressure gradient for CSF absorption into the arachnoid granulations, leading to an increase in CSF pressure.

(C) CSF is produced at a rate of 1000ml per day This statement is incorrect. CSF is produced at a rate of approximately 20 ml

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 20-year-old injures his knee while skiing. On examination, the patient experiences pain on medial rotation of tibia on the femur. Which structure of the knee is most likely to be injured?
Lateral collateral ligament
Medial meniscus
Lateral meniscus
Medial collateral ligament

Answer: (C) — Lateral meniscus

Explanation:

The knee joint allows for a small degree of rotation of the tibia relative to the femur, primarily when the knee is in a flexed position. Provocative maneuvers that involve rotating the tibia are used to diagnose meniscal injuries by compressing or trapping the torn portion of the meniscus between the femoral condyle and the tibial plateau.

  • Medial (Internal) Rotation of the Tibia: During this motion, the lateral femoral condyle glides over the posterior horn of the lateral meniscus. This action compresses the lateral meniscus. If there is a tear in the lateral meniscus, this compression and shearing force will elicit pain. This is the principle behind diagnostic tests like the McMurray test for the lateral meniscus.

  • Lateral (External) Rotation of the Tibia: Conversely, this motion stresses the medial compartment of the knee, compressing the medial meniscus. Pain with lateral rotation would suggest a medial meniscal injury.

Given that the patient experiences pain specifically on medial rotation of the tibia, the injury is most likely located in the structure being stressed by that movement, which is the lateral meniscus.

Options:

  • (A) Lateral collateral ligament (LCL): The LCL is a primary stabilizer against varus stress (a force from the medial side). It also becomes taut with external rotation of the tibia. It is relatively relaxed during medial rotation, so an isolated LCL injury would not typically cause pain with this specific maneuver.

  • (B) Medial meniscus: The medial meniscus is located in the medial compartment of the knee. It is stressed and compressed during lateral (external) rotation of the tibia. Therefore, an injury to the medial meniscus would cause pain on lateral, not medial, rotation.

  • (D) Medial collateral ligament (MCL): The MCL is a primary stabilizer against valgus stress (a force from the lateral side). It also becomes taut with external rotation of the tibia. Pain would be elicited with valgus stress or lateral rotation, not medial rotation.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 45-year-old female presents with swelling to her right lower limb. An ultrasound shows thrombosis affecting some of the veins. Which one of the following is a superficial vein of the lower limb?
Profunda femoris vein
Anterior tibial vein
Great saphenous vein
Posterior tibial vein

Answer: (C) — Great saphenous vein

Explanation:

The venous system of the lower limb is functionally and anatomically divided into two main systems: the superficial veins and the deep veins.

  • Superficial Veins: These veins are located within the subcutaneous tissue (superficial fascia), above the deep muscular fascia. The two major superficial veins are the great saphenous vein and the small saphenous vein. The great saphenous vein is the longest vein in the body, originating from the medial side of the dorsal venous arch of the foot, ascending along the medial aspect of the leg and thigh, and draining into the femoral vein at the saphenofemoral junction in the groin.
  • Deep Veins: These veins are located deep to the muscular fascia and typically accompany the major arteries of the lower limb (venae comitantes). Thrombosis in these veins is known as deep vein thrombosis (DVT), a more serious condition than superficial thrombophlebitis due to the risk of pulmonary embolism.

The great saphenous vein is the principal superficial vein of the lower limb, making it the correct answer.

Options:

  • (A) Profunda femoris vein: This is the deep femoral vein, a large deep vein in the thigh that drains the thigh muscles and accompanies the profunda femoris artery. It is part of the deep venous system.
  • (B) Anterior tibial vein: This is a deep vein that runs alongside the anterior tibial artery in the anterior compartment of the leg. It drains into the popliteal vein and is part of the deep venous system.
  • (D) Posterior tibial vein: This is a deep vein that runs alongside the posterior tibial artery in the posterior compartment of the leg. It joins the anterior tibial vein to form the popliteal vein and is part of the deep venous system.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 20-year-old sustains fracture of his tibia and fibula. He has ongoing pain and you suspect a compartment syndrome affecting the lateral compartment. Which one of the following is likely to be associated with a compartment syndrome of the lateral compartment?
Loss of dorsalis pedis pulse
Weakness of ankle inversion
Weakness of fibularis tertius
Loss of sensation over most of the dorsum of foot

Answer: (D) — Loss of sensation over most of the dorsum of foot

Explanation:

Compartment syndrome is a medical emergency caused by increased pressure within a fascial compartment, leading to compromised blood flow and damage to nerves and muscles. The lateral compartment of the lower leg contains the fibularis (peroneus) longus and fibularis (peroneus) brevis muscles, which are responsible for foot eversion. The nerve that runs through and supplies this compartment is the superficial fibular (peroneal) nerve.

Increased pressure in the lateral compartment will compress the superficial fibular nerve. This nerve provides motor innervation to the fibularis muscles and sensory innervation to the skin over the anterolateral aspect of the distal leg and the majority of the dorsum of the foot (except for the first webspace, which is supplied by the deep fibular nerve). Therefore, a key neurological sign of lateral compartment syndrome is sensory loss or paresthesia over the dorsum of the foot.

Options:

  • (A) Loss of dorsalis pedis pulse: The dorsalis pedis artery is a continuation of the anterior tibial artery, which runs within the anterior compartment. Loss of this pulse is a late and ominous sign of anterior compartment syndrome, not lateral.
  • (B) Weakness of ankle inversion: Ankle inversion is primarily performed by the tibialis posterior (in the deep posterior compartment) and the tibialis anterior (in the anterior compartment). The lateral compartment muscles perform eversion. Therefore, weakness of inversion is not a feature of isolated lateral compartment syndrome.
  • (C) Weakness of fibularis tertius: The fibularis (peroneus) tertius muscle is located in the anterior compartment of the leg and is innervated by the deep fibular (peroneal) nerve. Weakness of this muscle would be associated with anterior compartment syndrome.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
You are administering tetanus immunisation to a patient who needs a booster dose. The safe area in the gluteal region for this injection is:
Superomedial quadrant
Superolateral quadrant
Inferolateral quadrant
Inferomedial quadrant

Answer: (B) — Superolateral quadrant

Explanation:

The gluteal region is a common site for intramuscular (IM) injections, but precise landmarking is crucial to avoid injury to underlying structures, most importantly the sciatic nerve. For this purpose, the buttock is divided into four quadrants by two imaginary lines: a vertical line from the highest point of the iliac crest downwards, and a horizontal line that bisects the vertical line.

The superolateral quadrant (the upper, outer area) is considered the safest location for a dorsogluteal injection. This area primarily contains the gluteus medius and gluteus minimus muscles, which are thick and well-vascularized, allowing for good absorption of the medication. Critically, this quadrant is farthest from the sciatic nerve and major blood vessels (like the superior and inferior gluteal arteries), which are located more medially and inferiorly. An injection here minimizes the risk of nerve damage, pain, paralysis, or hemorrhage.

It is also worth noting that the ventrogluteal site is now often recommended over the dorsogluteal site as it is considered even safer, with less subcutaneous fat and no major nerves or vessels. However, when using the traditional dorsogluteal site, the superolateral quadrant is the correct choice.

Options:

  • Superomedial quadrant: This quadrant is incorrect and unsafe. It lies close to the superior gluteal nerve and artery. Injecting here poses a risk of damaging these structures.
  • Inferolateral quadrant: This quadrant is also unsafe. The sciatic nerve, after emerging from the pelvis, travels inferiorly and can pass through or very close to this area, creating a significant risk of nerve injury.
  • Inferomedial quadrant: This is the most dangerous quadrant for an injection. The sciatic nerve runs directly through this area. An injection here has a very high probability of causing direct trauma to the sciatic nerve, potentially leading to severe pain (sciatica), foot drop, and permanent disability.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 40-year old presents to ED after falling onto her face with swollen right cheek and double vision. A CT scan confirms a fracture to the roof of the maxillary sinus. This might result in
Loss of nasolabial fold.
Sensory loss of upper incisors and canine teeth
Weakness of the upper lip movement
Sensory loss over the zygomatic bone

Answer: (B) — Sensory loss of upper incisors and canine teeth

Explanation:

The roof of the maxillary sinus forms the floor of the orbit. A fracture in this location is commonly known as an orbital "blowout" fracture. The infraorbital nerve, a major branch of the maxillary division of the trigeminal nerve (V2), runs through the infraorbital groove and canal, which are located within the floor of the orbit (i.e., the roof of the maxillary sinus).

Before exiting the skull at the infraorbital foramen, the infraorbital nerve gives off the anterior superior alveolar nerve (ASAN) and sometimes the middle superior alveolar nerve. The ASAN provides sensory innervation to the upper incisors, canine teeth, their associated gingiva, and the mucous membrane of the maxillary sinus. A fracture of the orbital floor can directly injure or cause compression (due to swelling or a bone fragment) of the infraorbital nerve and its branches, such as the ASAN. This leads to anesthesia or paresthesia (numbness or tingling) in the areas they supply, including the upper incisors and canines. The patient's presenting symptom of double vision (diplopia) is also classic for this type of fracture, caused by entrapment of the inferior rectus muscle in the fracture site, restricting eye movement.

Options:

  • (A) Loss of nasolabial fold: This would be caused by paralysis or weakness of the muscles of facial expression, such as the zygomaticus major, which are innervated by the facial nerve (Cranial Nerve VII). The facial nerve does not pass through the floor of the orbit and would not be affected by this specific fracture.
  • (C) Weakness of the upper lip movement: Movement of the upper lip is controlled by muscles of facial expression (e.g., levator labii superioris), which are also innervated by the facial nerve (CN VII). This fracture involves a sensory nerve (trigeminal), not the motor facial nerve.
  • (D) Sensory loss over the zygomatic bone: Sensation to the skin over the prominence of the cheek (zygomatic bone) is provided by the zygomaticofacial nerve. This is a branch of the zygomatic nerve (also from V2), but it travels along the lateral wall of the orbit, not the floor. Therefore, it is not typically injured in an isolated orbital floor fracture.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 25-year old male presents to ED with acute urinary retention. You plan to place an indwelling urinary catheter. The narrowest part of the male urethra is
The Prostatic part of urethra
The Intramural part of urethra
The membranous urethra
The penile or spongy urethra

Answer: (C) — The membranous urethra

Explanation:

The male urethra is divided into four anatomical parts. When considering the passage for a urinary catheter, the membranous urethra is the narrowest and least distensible segment. It passes through the deep perineal pouch and is surrounded by the external urethral sphincter. Its fixed position and surrounding muscular sphincter make it the most constricted part of the urethral canal, and therefore a common site for iatrogenic injury during catheterization or instrumentation. While the external urethral meatus is technically the narrowest point of the entire urethra, it is an orifice, not a segment. Among the given options representing the parts of the urethra, the membranous urethra is the narrowest.

Options:

  • (A) The Prostatic part of urethra: This is incorrect. The prostatic urethra, which passes through the prostate gland, is the widest and most dilatable part of the male urethra.
  • (B) The Intramural part of urethra: This is incorrect. Also known as the pre-prostatic urethra, this segment passes through the bladder wall. While relatively short and surrounded by the internal urethral sphincter, it is not the narrowest part.
  • (D) The penile or spongy urethra: This is incorrect. This is the longest part of the urethra, passing through the corpus spongiosum. It is relatively wide and contains two dilatations: the intrabulbar fossa and the navicular fossa. It is not the narrowest segment.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 30 year old presents with one day history of right lower abdominal pain. You suspect appendicitis. The second most common position of appendix is:
Post-ileal
Pelvic
Retroceacal
Sub-cecal

Answer: (B) — Pelvic

Explanation:

The vermiform appendix is a blind-ended tube connected to the cecum. Its position is highly variable, which can alter the clinical presentation of appendicitis. The most common position of the appendix is retrocecal, lying behind the cecum. The pelvic position, where the appendix hangs down into the true pelvis, is the second most common.

The approximate frequencies of the different appendiceal positions are: 1. Retrocecal: ~65-70% 2. Pelvic: ~25-30% 3. Subcecal: ~2% 4. Post-ileal: ~1% 5. Pre-ileal: <1%

This anatomical variability is clinically significant. For example, a pelvic appendix may irritate the bladder or rectum, causing suprapubic pain, urinary frequency, or tenesmus, which can mimic a urinary tract infection or pelvic inflammatory disease.

Options:

  • (A) Post-ileal: This position, where the appendix lies behind the terminal ileum, is relatively rare, accounting for only about 1% of cases.
  • (C) Retrocecal: This is the most common position of the appendix, not the second most common. In this position, pain may be less localized or present more in the flank or back.
  • (D) Sub-cecal: In this position, the appendix is located inferior to the cecum. It is less common than the retrocecal and pelvic positions, occurring in approximately 2% of individuals.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Tracheal bifurcation occurs at the level of:
T4
T5
T5-T6 intervertebral disc
T4-T5 intervertebral disc

Answer: (D) — T4-T5 intervertebral disc

Explanation:

The trachea, or windpipe, descends from the larynx into the superior mediastinum, where it terminates by dividing into the right and left main (primary) bronchi. This point of division is known as the carina.

The bifurcation of the trachea occurs at the level of the transverse thoracic plane, also known as the plane of Ludwig or the sternal angle. This is an imaginary horizontal plane that passes through the sternal angle (the articulation between the manubrium and the body of the sternum) anteriorly and the intervertebral disc between the T4 and T5 vertebrae posteriorly.

This plane is a crucial anatomical landmark for several other structures and events: * The beginning and end of the aortic arch. * The bifurcation of the pulmonary trunk. * The division between the superior and inferior mediastinum. * The azygos vein arching over the root of the right lung to enter the superior vena cava.

Options:

  • (A) T4: This option refers to the vertebral body of T4. The bifurcation occurs slightly inferior to this, at the level of the disc between T4 and T5.
  • (B) T5: This refers to the vertebral body of T5, which is inferior to the level of the tracheal bifurcation.
  • (C) T5-T6 intervertebral disc: This level is too inferior (caudal). The carina can descend to this level during deep inspiration, but its standard anatomical position is higher.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 69-year old woman presents with central chest pain. Her ECG shows ST elevation in inferior leads. You suspect ischemia to the region supplied by the right coronary artery. The right coronary artery usually supplies
Anterior third of the interventricular septum
The left atrium
AV node in 50% of people
Diaphragmatic surface of the left ventricle

Answer: (D) — Diaphragmatic surface of the left ventricle

Explanation:

The clinical presentation of an inferior wall myocardial infarction (MI), characterized by ST elevation in leads II, III, and aVF on an ECG, is most commonly caused by an occlusion of the right coronary artery (RCA). The RCA courses along the right atrioventricular (coronary) sulcus and gives off several important branches. In a right-dominant circulation (present in ~85% of individuals), the RCA gives rise to the posterior descending artery (PDA), which runs in the posterior interventricular sulcus. The PDA, along with other branches of the RCA, supplies the inferior or diaphragmatic surface of the heart, which includes portions of both the right and left ventricles. Therefore, ischemia in the RCA territory directly affects the diaphragmatic surface of the left ventricle.

Options:

  • (A) Anterior third of the interventricular septum: This is incorrect. The anterior two-thirds of the interventricular septum is supplied by the septal perforating branches of the left anterior descending (LAD) artery, which is a branch of the left coronary artery. The posterior one-third is typically supplied by the PDA, which usually arises from the RCA.
  • (B) The left atrium: This is incorrect. The left atrium is primarily supplied by branches of the left circumflex artery (LCx), which is a terminal branch of the left coronary artery. The RCA primarily supplies the right atrium.
  • (C) AV node in 50% of people: This is incorrect. The atrioventricular (AV) node is supplied by the AV nodal artery, which arises from the vessel that crosses the crux of the heart (the junction of the interatrial and interventricular septa). In right-dominant circulation (~85% of people), the AV nodal artery is a branch of the RCA. In left-dominant circulation (~8-10%), it arises from the LCx. Therefore, the RCA supplies the AV node in a vast majority of people, significantly more than 50%.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 69-year old woman presents with central chest pain. Her ECG shows ST elevation in the anterior leads. You suspect ischemia to the region supplied by the left anterior descending (LAD) artery. The left anterior descending (LAD) artery usually supplies all of the following EXCEPT
Right ventricle
Left ventricle
Left atrium
SA node in approximately 20-40% of people

Answer: (C) — Left atrium

Explanation:

The Left Anterior Descending (LAD) artery, a branch of the left coronary artery, is crucial for the blood supply to the front of the heart. Its course along the anterior interventricular sulcus dictates its primary territories of perfusion. An occlusion of the LAD, as suggested by ST elevation in the anterior ECG leads (V1-V4), typically causes ischemia or infarction of the following structures:

  • Anterior wall of the left ventricle: This is the main area supplied by the LAD.
  • Anterior two-thirds of the interventricular septum: Supplied by the LAD's septal perforator branches. This is critical as this region contains the bundle of His and the bundle branches of the cardiac conduction system.
  • Anterolateral papillary muscle.
  • Apex of the heart.
  • A small adjacent portion of the anterior wall of the right ventricle.

The left atrium is primarily supplied by atrial branches originating from the left circumflex (LCx) artery, which is the other main branch of the left coronary artery. The LCx artery courses posteriorly in the atrioventricular groove, making it ideally positioned to supply the left atrium. Therefore, the left atrium is not usually within the territory of the LAD.

Options:

  • (A) Right ventricle: This is incorrect. The LAD supplies the anterior two-thirds of the interventricular septum. As the septum forms the medial wall of the right ventricle, the LAD contributes to its blood supply. Small epicardial branches may also supply the adjacent free wall of the right ventricle.
  • (B) Left ventricle: This is incorrect. The LAD is the primary blood supply for the anterior wall and apex of the left ventricle. An LAD occlusion is the classic cause of an anterior wall myocardial infarction.
  • (D) SA node in approximately 20-40% of people: This statement is misleading and describes a territory not supplied by the LAD. The sinoatrial (SA) node is supplied by the SA nodal artery, which arises from the right coronary artery (RCA) in ~60% of people and the left circumflex (LCx) artery in ~40%. The LAD does not typically supply the SA node. While this option is also an exception, "Left atrium" represents a larger, more consistently defined anatomical territory that is clearly outside the LAD's domain, making it the best answer.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 20-year-old injures his knee while skiing. Examination shows that the tibia slides anteriorly on a fixed femur. Which structure of the knee is most likely to be injured?
Posterior cruciate ligament
Lateral meniscus
Medial meniscus
Anterior cruciate ligament

Answer: (D) — Anterior cruciate ligament

Explanation:

The clinical finding described is excessive anterior translation of the tibia relative to the femur. This is the classic sign of an Anterior Cruciate Ligament (ACL) tear. The ACL is the primary ligamentous restraint that prevents the tibia from sliding forward on the femur. The physical examination maneuver that elicits this sign is known as the Anterior Drawer Test or, more sensitively, the Lachman test. Skiing injuries, particularly those involving sudden stops, twisting, or hyperextension of the knee, are a common mechanism for ACL tears.

Options:

  • (A) Posterior cruciate ligament: The Posterior Cruciate Ligament (PCL) is the primary restraint to posterior translation of the tibia on the femur. An injury to the PCL would result in a positive posterior drawer sign, where the tibia sags or can be pushed backward on the femur.
  • (B) Lateral meniscus: The menisci (lateral and medial) are C-shaped fibrocartilage structures that act as shock absorbers and improve the congruity of the knee joint. While a meniscal tear can occur with an ACL injury, an isolated meniscal tear does not cause the gross anterior instability described. Its injury typically causes pain, clicking, locking, or a sensation of the knee "giving way" due to pain or a displaced fragment, not ligamentous laxity.
  • (C) Medial meniscus: Similar to the lateral meniscus, the medial meniscus serves to absorb shock and stabilize the joint. An isolated tear would not lead to significant anterior tibial translation. It is frequently injured along with the ACL and Medial Collateral Ligament (MCL) in what is known as the "unhappy triad," but the ACL is the structure responsible for preventing anterior instability.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - DKA
A 30 year old woman, known type I diabetic, presents with vomiting and abdominal pain for two days. You suspect diabetic ketoacidosis. Hormones have various responses on various organs and tissues. The intracellular response to hormones is mediated by:
Ceruloplasmin
Magnesium
First messengers
Second messengers

Answer: (D) — Second messengers Explanation: The intracellular response to many hormones, particularly those that cannot cross the cell membrane (like peptide hormones and catecholamines), is mediated by second messengers. When a hormone (the "first messenger") binds to its specific receptor on the cell surface, it triggers a conformational change in the receptor. This activation leads to the production or release of small, non-protein intracellular molecules called second messengers. These molecules, such as cyclic AMP (cAMP), cyclic GMP (cGMP), inositol trisphosphate (IP3), diacylglycerol (DAG), and calcium ions (Ca²⁺), then diffuse within the cell to relay and amplify the signal, activating downstream effector proteins like protein kinases. This cascade ultimately leads to the physiological response of the cell.

In the context of DKA, there is an excess of counter-regulatory hormones like glucagon and epinephrine. Both of these hormones bind to G-protein coupled receptors, leading to the activation of adenylate cyclase, which increases the intracellular concentration of cAMP (a second messenger). cAMP then activates Protein Kinase A (PKA), which phosphorylates key enzymes to promote glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis, contributing to the severe hyperglycemia seen in DKA.

Options: * (A) Ceruloplasmin: This is incorrect. Ceruloplasmin is a copper-carrying protein found in the plasma. It also functions as a ferroxidase, which is important for iron metabolism. It is not involved in mediating intracellular hormone signaling. * (B) Magnesium: This is incorrect. While magnesium (Mg²⁺) is a crucial cofactor for many enzymes involved in signal transduction (e.g., it is required for the function of adenylate cyclase and kinases like PKA), it is not the messenger itself. It facilitates the enzymatic reactions but does not carry the primary signal from the receptor to the effector proteins in the way a second messenger does. * (C) First messengers: This is incorrect. First messengers are the hormones themselves (e.g., glucagon, insulin, epinephrine). They are the extracellular signals that initiate the process by binding to cell surface receptors. The question asks for the mediator of the intracellular response, which occurs after the first messenger has bound to its receptor.

Confidence: 100%

297


CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - DKA
A 30 year old woman, known type I diabetic, presents with vomiting and abdominal pain for two days. You suspect diabetic ketoacidosis. Which one of the following is the most likely mechanism for the development of type I diabetes mellitus?
Type II Hypersensitivity
Type I Hypersensitivity
Type IV Hypersensitivity
Type III Hypersensitivity

Answer: (C) — Type IV Hypersensitivity

Explanation:

Type I Diabetes Mellitus (T1DM) is an autoimmune disease characterized by the selective destruction of insulin-producing beta cells in the islets of Langerhans of the pancreas. This destruction is primarily a T-cell-mediated process, which is classified as a Type IV hypersensitivity reaction.

In T1DM, autoreactive T-lymphocytes recognize specific antigens on the surface of pancreatic beta cells (e.g., glutamic acid decarboxylase [GAD65], insulinoma-associated antigen 2 [IA-2]). The process involves: 1. CD4+ helper T-cells (Th1 subtype), which become activated and secrete cytokines (like IFN-γ and TNF-α). These cytokines recruit and activate macrophages, leading to local inflammation within the islets (a process called "insulitis"). 2. CD8+ cytotoxic T-lymphocytes (CTLs), which directly recognize and kill the pancreatic beta cells.

This cell-mediated cytotoxicity, orchestrated by T-cells rather than antibodies, is the hallmark of a Type IV hypersensitivity reaction. While autoantibodies are often present and used as diagnostic markers, the primary mechanism of beta-cell destruction is cellular.

Options:

  • (A) Type II Hypersensitivity: This is an antibody-mediated cytotoxic reaction where IgG or IgM antibodies bind to antigens on a cell's surface, leading to its destruction (e.g., autoimmune hemolytic anemia). While autoantibodies exist in T1DM, they are not the primary cause of beta-cell destruction; T-cells are.
  • (B) Type I Hypersensitivity: This is an immediate, IgE-mediated reaction involving mast cells and basophils, responsible for allergic reactions like anaphylaxis and asthma. This mechanism is not involved in the pathogenesis of T1DM.
  • (D) Type III Hypersensitivity: This reaction is caused by the deposition of antigen-antibody (immune) complexes in tissues, leading to complement activation and inflammation (e.g., systemic lupus erythematosus, post-streptococcal glomerulonephritis). This is not the primary mechanism of beta-cell loss in T1DM.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - DKA
A 30 year old woman, known type I diabetic, presents with vomiting and abdominal pain for two days. You suspect diabetic ketoacidosis. What will happen if insulin alone is given rapidly to patients in diabetic ketoacidosis?
Hyperphosphatemia
Hypernatremia
Hypokalemia
Hypocalcemia

Answer: (C) — Hypokalemia

Explanation:

Diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA) is a state of absolute or relative insulin deficiency, leading to hyperglycemia, ketosis, and metabolic acidosis. While patients with DKA have a total body deficit of potassium due to osmotic diuresis and vomiting, their initial serum potassium levels can be normal or even elevated. This paradoxical finding is due to two main factors:

  1. Acidosis: In a state of acidosis, excess hydrogen ions (H+) move from the bloodstream into cells to be buffered. To maintain electrical neutrality, potassium ions (K+) move out of the cells and into the bloodstream.
  2. Insulin Deficiency: Insulin normally promotes the uptake of potassium into cells by stimulating the Na+/K+-ATPase pump. In DKA, the lack of insulin impairs this process, causing potassium to remain in the extracellular fluid.

When insulin is administered, it reverses these processes. Insulin stimulates the Na+/K+-ATPase pump, causing a rapid shift of potassium from the bloodstream back into the cells. This, combined with the correction of acidosis (which also encourages potassium to move back into cells), can lead to a sudden and severe drop in serum potassium levels, resulting in hypokalemia.

Severe hypokalemia (<2.5 mEq/L) is life-threatening and can cause cardiac arrhythmias (e.g., ventricular tachycardia, fibrillation), muscle weakness, paralysis, and respiratory failure. For this reason, it is critical to check the patient's potassium level before initiating insulin therapy. If the initial potassium is <3.3 mEq/L, potassium repletion should begin before insulin is administered.

Options:

  • (A) Hyperphosphatemia: This is incorrect. Similar to potassium, patients in DKA have a total body phosphate deficit. Insulin administration drives phosphate into the cells, which can cause or worsen hypophosphatemia, not hyperphosphatemia.
  • (B) Hypernatremia: This is incorrect. Patients in DKA often present with hyponatremia due to the osmotic effect of severe hyperglycemia, which pulls water from the intracellular to the extracellular space, diluting the serum sodium. As insulin lowers the blood glucose, water shifts back into the cells, and the serum sodium concentration will rise towards its true value. However, the most acute and life-threatening electrolyte change from insulin alone is hypokalemia.
  • (D) Hypocalcemia: This is incorrect. Insulin administration does not have a direct, rapid, or clinically significant effect on serum calcium levels in the management of DKA. While changes in pH and albumin can affect ionized calcium levels, insulin-induced hypocalcemia is not a recognized major complication.

Confidence: 100%

299


CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - DKA
A 30 year old woman, known type I diabetic, presents with vomiting and abdominal pain for two days. You suspect diabetic ketoacidosis. Insulin increases the entry of glucose into
All tissues
Skeletal muscle
Renal tubular cells
The mucosa of small intestine

Answer: (B) — Skeletal muscle

Explanation:

Insulin's primary role in glucose metabolism is to facilitate the uptake, utilization, and storage of glucose in specific tissues. It achieves this mainly by promoting the translocation of a specific glucose transporter, GLUT4, to the cell surface.

GLUT4 is an insulin-sensitive glucose transporter found predominantly in skeletal muscle, cardiac muscle, and adipose tissue. In the absence of insulin (the "basal" or "fasting" state), most GLUT4 transporters are sequestered within intracellular vesicles. When insulin binds to its receptor on these cells, it initiates a signaling cascade that causes these vesicles to fuse with the plasma membrane. This dramatically increases the number of GLUT4 transporters on the cell surface, thereby increasing the rate of glucose uptake into the cell.

In the context of the clinical scenario, a patient with Type 1 Diabetes and DKA has an absolute deficiency of insulin. This means glucose cannot efficiently enter skeletal muscle and adipose tissue, leading to severe hyperglycemia (high blood sugar) despite the cells effectively "starving" for glucose.

Options:

  • (A) All tissues: This is incorrect. Many vital tissues have insulin-independent glucose transporters to ensure a constant supply of glucose, regardless of hormonal status. For example, the brain primarily uses GLUT1 and GLUT3, and red blood cells use GLUT1. These tissues must have access to glucose even during fasting.
  • (C) Renal tubular cells: This is incorrect. The proximal tubules of the kidneys reabsorb glucose from the glomerular filtrate primarily via sodium-glucose cotransporters (SGLT2 and SGLT1) on the apical membrane and GLUT2 transporters on the basolateral membrane. These are insulin-independent. In DKA, the massive amount of glucose in the blood overwhelms these transporters, leading to glucose in the urine (glucosuria) and subsequent osmotic diuresis.
  • (D) The mucosa of small intestine: This is incorrect. The absorption of glucose from the digested food in the small intestine into the enterocytes is mediated by SGLT1 (apical membrane), and its transport out of the enterocytes into the bloodstream is mediated by GLUT2 (basolateral membrane). Both processes are insulin-independent.

Confidence: 100%

301


CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - ASTHMA
A 5 year old boy presents with an acute exacerbation of asthma. Which one of the following statements about Lung compliance is TRUE?
Falls as the lung ages
Is the area under the pressure volume curve
Falls if the lung remains unventilated for long periods
Rises if the pulmonary venous pressure is increased

Answer: (C) — Falls if the lung remains unventilated for long periods

Explanation:

Lung compliance is a measure of the lung's ability to stretch and expand in response to a change in pressure. It is calculated as the change in volume divided by the change in pressure (C = ΔV/ΔP). A high compliance indicates that the lungs are easy to inflate, while low compliance means the lungs are stiff and difficult to inflate.

The correct statement is that compliance falls if the lung remains unventilated for long periods. This phenomenon is primarily due to atelectasis (alveolar collapse) and the loss of surfactant. Surfactant is a substance produced by type II alveolar cells that reduces surface tension within the alveoli, preventing their collapse and increasing compliance. The production and spread of surfactant are stimulated by the regular stretching of the alveoli during ventilation. When a lung or a portion of it is not ventilated, surfactant is not replenished, surface tension increases, and the alveoli tend to collapse. A collapsed, atelectatic lung is very stiff and requires significantly more pressure to re-inflate, hence its compliance is low.

Options:

  • (A) Falls as the lung ages: This is incorrect. With normal aging, there is a loss of elastic recoil in the lung tissue due to the degradation of elastin fibers. This makes the lungs easier to inflate, thus increasing lung compliance. However, chest wall compliance decreases with age, but the question specifically asks about lung compliance.
  • (B) Is the area under the pressure volume curve: This is incorrect. Lung compliance is represented by the slope of the pressure-volume (P-V) curve (ΔV/ΔP). The area within the P-V loop represents the work of breathing required to overcome resistive forces.
  • (D) Rises if the pulmonary venous pressure is increased: This is incorrect. An increase in pulmonary venous pressure (e.g., in left-sided heart failure) leads to pulmonary congestion and edema. The accumulation of fluid in the lung interstitium and alveoli makes the lung tissue stiff and "heavy," which significantly decreases lung compliance.

Confidence: 95%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - ASTHMA
A 5 year old boy presents with an acute exacerbation of asthma. Salbutamol may cause all of the following EXCEPT:
Nervousness
Low pO2 initially
Hyperkalaemia
Skeletal muscle tremor

Answer: (C) — Hyperkalaemia

Explanation:

Salbutamol (albuterol) is a short-acting beta-2 adrenergic agonist (SABA). Its primary therapeutic effect is bronchodilation via relaxation of the smooth muscle in the airways. However, stimulation of beta-2 receptors in other parts of the body leads to several side effects.

One of the key metabolic effects of beta-2 agonists is the stimulation of the Na+/K+-ATPase pump on cell membranes, particularly in skeletal muscle. This pump actively transports potassium from the extracellular fluid (blood) into the intracellular space. This shift of potassium results in a decrease in serum potassium levels, a condition known as hypokalaemia. Therefore, salbutamol causes hypokalaemia, not hyperkalaemia (high potassium). In fact, high-dose nebulized salbutamol is an established treatment for acute hyperkalaemia.

Options:

  • (A) Nervousness: This is a common side effect of salbutamol. Adrenergic stimulation can lead to central nervous system effects such as anxiety, restlessness, and nervousness.

  • (B) Low pO2 initially: This is a known, though often transient, paradoxical effect. In an asthma exacerbation, some areas of the lung are poorly ventilated. Salbutamol causes bronchodilation (improving ventilation, V) but also causes pulmonary vasodilation (increasing perfusion, Q). If it dilates blood vessels in poorly ventilated lung segments, it can temporarily worsen the ventilation/perfusion (V/Q) mismatch, leading to a transient drop in arterial oxygen partial pressure (pO2) until the bronchodilatory effects improve overall ventilation.

  • (D) Skeletal muscle tremor: This is one of the most common side effects of salbutamol. It is caused by the direct stimulation of beta-2 receptors located on skeletal muscle.

Confidence: 100%

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - ASTHMA
A 5 year old boy presents with an acute exacerbation of asthma. All of the following cause direct bronchodilation EXCEPT:
Theophylline
Atropine
Adrenaline
Chromoglycate

Answer: (D) — Chromoglycate

Explanation

The question asks to identify the medication that does not cause direct bronchodilation. Direct bronchodilation refers to the direct action of a drug on the bronchial smooth muscle to cause relaxation and widening of the airways.

Chromoglycate (Sodium Cromoglicate/Cromolyn Sodium) is a mast cell stabilizer. Its primary mechanism of action is to prevent the degranulation of mast cells when they are exposed to allergens or other triggers. By inhibiting mast cell degranulation, it prevents the release of inflammatory and bronchoconstrictive mediators such as histamine, leukotrienes, and prostaglandins. Because its action is to prevent the cascade that leads to bronchoconstriction, it has no direct effect on relaxing already constricted bronchial smooth muscle. Therefore, it is used for prophylaxis (prevention) of asthma attacks, not for the treatment of an acute exacerbation.

Options

  • (A) Theophylline: This is a methylxanthine drug that causes bronchodilation primarily by inhibiting phosphodiesterase (PDE) enzymes. This inhibition leads to increased intracellular levels of cyclic adenosine monophosphate (cAMP), which promotes smooth muscle relaxation and thus, bronchodilation. It has a direct relaxant effect on the airway smooth muscle.
  • (B) Atropine: This is a non-selective muscarinic antagonist (an anticholinergic drug). The parasympathetic nervous system causes bronchoconstriction via acetylcholine acting on M3 muscarinic receptors on airway smooth muscle. By blocking these receptors, atropine and its derivatives (like ipratropium) prevent bronchoconstriction and cause

CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - ASTHMA
A 5 year old boy presents with an acute exacerbation of asthma. Which one of the following statements about bronchial asthma is CORRECT?
Primary mediators include eosinophilic and neutrophilic chemotactic factors
Bronchial wall smooth muscle is atrophic
Extrinsic asthma is initiated by diverse non-immune mechanisms
Sub-epithelial vagal receptors in respiratory mucosa are insensitive to irritants

Answer: (A) — Primary mediators include eosinophilic and neutrophilic chemotactic factors

Explanation:

Bronchial asthma is a chronic inflammatory disorder of the airways characterized by bronchial hyperresponsiveness and reversible airflow obstruction. The underlying pathophysiology involves a complex interplay of inflammatory cells and mediators.

In atopic (extrinsic) asthma, which is common in children, exposure to an allergen triggers a Type I hypersensitivity reaction. This process begins with the cross-linking of IgE antibodies on the surface of mast cells. This activation causes the degranulation of mast cells, releasing pre-formed (primary) mediators. These mediators include histamine, proteases, and crucial chemotactic factors like Eosinophil Chemotactic Factor of Anaphylaxis (ECF-A) and Neutrophil Chemotactic Factor (NCF). These factors recruit eosinophils and neutrophils to the airway, which then release their own secondary mediators, perpetuating the inflammatory cascade and contributing to the late-phase reaction and airway remodeling.

Options:

  • (B) Bronchial wall smooth muscle is atrophic: This is incorrect. Chronic inflammation in asthma leads to airway remodeling. A key feature of this remodeling is the hypertrophy (increase in cell size) and hyperplasia (increase in cell number) of bronchial smooth muscle. This increased muscle mass contributes to the exaggerated bronchoconstriction seen in asthma. Atrophy is the wasting or shrinking of muscle, which is the opposite of what occurs.
  • (C) Extrinsic asthma is initiated by diverse non-immune mechanisms: This is incorrect. Extrinsic asthma (also known as atopic or allergic asthma) is, by definition, initiated by an immune mechanism. It is a classic IgE-mediated Type I hypersensitivity reaction to an external allergen (e.g., pollen, dust mites). It is intrinsic (non-atopic) asthma that is often triggered by non-immune stimuli such as viral infections, cold air, exercise, or stress.
  • (D) Sub-epithelial vagal receptors in respiratory mucosa are insensitive to irritants: This is incorrect. A hallmark of asthma is bronchial hyperresponsiveness. The sub-epithelial vagal receptors (irritant receptors) are actually hypersensitive to various stimuli like dust, cold air, and chemical irritants. Stimulation of these receptors triggers a reflex arc via the vagus nerve, leading to the release of acetylcholine, which causes potent bronchoconstriction and mucus secretion, thereby worsening asthma symptoms.

Confidence: 100%
