CLINICAL SCENARIO QUESTION - ASTHMA
A 5 year old boy presents with an acute exacerbation of asthma. All of the following are effects of hypoxia except:
Pulmonary vasodilation
Increased HR
Increase in respiratory minute volume
Increase sensitivity to pCO2

Answer: (A) — Pulmonary vasodilation

Explanation:

Hypoxia, a state of low oxygen levels in the tissues, triggers several compensatory physiological responses. In the context of an acute asthma exacerbation, poor ventilation in parts of the lung leads to localized alveolar hypoxia. The body's response to alveolar hypoxia is hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction (HPV). This is a crucial, protective reflex where the small pulmonary arteries constrict in poorly ventilated areas of the lung. This shunts blood away from these areas and redirects it toward better-ventilated alveoli, thereby optimizing the ventilation/perfusion (V/Q) match and improving overall gas exchange. Therefore, pulmonary vasodilation (the widening of blood vessels) is the opposite of what occurs in the pulmonary circulation in response to localized hypoxia.

Options:

  • (B) Increased HR: This is incorrect because hypoxia is a potent stimulator of the sympathetic nervous system. Peripheral chemoreceptors (in the carotid and aortic bodies) detect low PaO2 and signal the brainstem, leading to an increased sympathetic output. This causes tachycardia (increased heart rate) and increased cardiac contractility, which serve to increase cardiac output and improve oxygen delivery to the body's tissues.
  • (C) Increase in respiratory minute volume: This is incorrect. Hypoxia is a primary driver of respiration. The stimulation of peripheral chemoreceptors by low oxygen levels sends strong signals to the respiratory centers in the medulla and pons, causing an increase in both the rate (tachypnea) and depth of breathing. This results in an increased respiratory minute volume (Tidal Volume x Respiratory Rate) in an attempt to increase oxygen intake.
  • (D) Increase sensitivity to pCO2: This is incorrect. The ventilatory drive is primarily controlled by PaCO2 levels, which are sensed by central chemoreceptors. However, hypoxia significantly enhances the respiratory system's sensitivity to carbon dioxide. When peripheral chemoreceptors are stimulated by hypoxia, they potentiate the response of the central chemoreceptors to a given level of PaCO2. This means that for any increase in CO2, the corresponding increase in ventilation is much greater when hypoxia is also present.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 65 year old presents with an injury to the upper arm. On examination the patient has loss of sensation to the hypothenar eminence. Which part of the humerus is likely to have fractured?
Medial epicondyle
Surgical neck
Distal end
Shaft

Answer: (A) — Medial epicondyle

Explanation: The hypothenar eminence, which is the muscular bulge on the ulnar (medial) side of the palm at the base of the little finger, receives its cutaneous (sensory) innervation from the ulnar nerve. The ulnar nerve travels down the arm and passes posteriorly to the medial epicondyle of the humerus in a groove known as the cubital tunnel. Due to this close anatomical relationship, a fracture of the medial epicondyle can easily damage or compress the ulnar nerve, leading to sensory deficits (like numbness or paresthesia) in its distribution, which includes the hypothenar eminence and the medial one and a half digits.

Options:

  • (B) Surgical neck: A fracture of the surgical neck of the humerus is most commonly associated with an injury to the axillary nerve and the posterior circumflex humeral artery. An axillary nerve injury would result in paralysis of the deltoid muscle and sensory loss over the "regimental badge" area of the shoulder, not the hypothenar eminence.
  • (C) Distal end: This is a general term. While the medial epicondyle is part of the distal humerus, this option is less precise. A supracondylar fracture (also at the distal end) most commonly injures the median nerve or brachial artery.
  • (D) Shaft: A fracture of the shaft of the humerus, particularly at the level of the radial (spiral) groove, is classically associated with an injury to the radial nerve. This would lead to "wrist drop" (inability to extend the wrist and fingers) and sensory loss on the posterior aspect of the forearm and hand.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 50-year-old male felt a snap followed by a sudden development of swelling in his mid arm. You suspect a rupture of biceps muscle. Regarding the biceps muscle of the arm, which one of the following statements is correct?
The short head of biceps arises from the acromion
Approximately 10% of people have a third head
The nerve supply arises from the medial cord
The long head of biceps arises from the infraglenoid tubercle

Answer: (B) — Approximately 10% of people have a third head

Explanation:

The biceps brachii muscle, located in the anterior compartment of the arm, typically has two heads: a long head and a short head. However, it is one of the most variable muscles in the human body. The most common variation is the presence of a third head. This supernumerary head is present in approximately 10% of the population, with variations based on ethnicity. It most commonly arises from the superomedial aspect of the brachialis muscle on the humerus and inserts distally with the common biceps tendon. The clinical scenario describes a "Popeye sign," which is characteristic of a long head of biceps tendon rupture, a common injury in this age group.

Options:

  • (A) The short head of biceps arises from the acromion: This is incorrect. The short head of the biceps brachii arises from the coracoid process of the scapula, along with the coracobrachialis muscle. The acromion serves as an attachment point for the deltoid and trapezius muscles.
  • (C) The nerve supply arises from the medial cord: This is incorrect. The biceps brachii is innervated by the musculocutaneous nerve (C5, C6, C7), which is a terminal branch of the lateral cord of the brachial plexus. The medial cord gives rise to the ulnar nerve and the medial root of the median nerve.
  • (D) The long head of biceps arises from the infraglenoid tubercle: This is incorrect. The long head of the biceps brachii arises from the supraglenoid tubercle of the scapula. The tendon of the long head of the triceps brachii arises from the infraglenoid tubercle.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
A 25 year old IV drug user presents to ED after concerns that he accidentally injected into his artery at the elbow. Regarding the anatomical structures in this region, which one of the following statements in CORRECT?
The brachial artery ends as it divides into radial and ulnar arteries superior to the bicipital aponeurosis.
Pronator teres forms the lateral boundary of the cubital fossa.
The brachial artery lies between the biceps tendon and median nerve
The median cubital vein lies underneath the deep fascia

Answer: (C) — The brachial artery lies between the biceps tendon and median nerve

Explanation:

The cubital fossa is a triangular space on the anterior aspect of the elbow. Understanding its contents and their relationships is crucial. The contents, from lateral to medial, can be remembered by the mnemonic TAN: Tendon (of biceps brachii), Artery (brachial), Nerve (median). Therefore, the brachial artery is situated medially to the biceps tendon and laterally to the median nerve, meaning it lies between these two structures. The bicipital aponeurosis, a sheet of fascia from the biceps tendon, passes superficially to the brachial artery and median nerve, offering them some protection. This anatomy is clinically relevant in the context of the question, as the brachial artery is vulnerable to accidental puncture during venipuncture or IV drug injection in the cubital fossa.

Options:

  • (A) The brachial artery ends as it divides into radial and ulnar arteries superior to the bicipital aponeurosis. This is incorrect. The brachial artery bifurcates into the radial and ulnar arteries at the apex of the cubital fossa, at the level of the neck of the radius. This bifurcation occurs deep (or inferior) to the bicipital aponeurosis, which crosses over the artery.

  • (B) Pronator teres forms the lateral boundary of the cubital fossa. This is incorrect. The pronator teres muscle forms the medial boundary of the cubital fossa. The lateral boundary is formed by the brachioradialis muscle.

  • (D) The median cubital vein lies underneath the deep fascia. This is incorrect. The median cubital vein is a superficial vein located within the subcutaneous tissue (superficial fascia), overlying the deep fascia and the bicipital aponeurosis. It is the most common site for venipuncture precisely because it is superficial and accessible.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - ANATOMY
Common dominant myotome corresponding to elbow flexion and wrist extension
C5
C6
C8
C7

Answer: (B) — C6 Explanation: The C6 spinal nerve root is the primary myotome responsible for both elbow flexion and wrist extension. A myotome is a group of muscles innervated by a single spinal nerve root. Clinically, testing the strength of these two movements is a key part of assessing the integrity of the C6 nerve root and spinal cord segment.

  • Elbow Flexion: The C6 nerve root innervates key elbow flexors, including the brachioradialis and contributing significantly to the biceps brachii and brachialis (which are also supplied by C5).
  • Wrist Extension: The C6 nerve root innervates the primary wrist extensors, the extensor carpi radialis longus and brevis.

Therefore, weakness in both elbow flexion and wrist extension points strongly to a C6 radiculopathy.

Options: * (A) C5: The C5 myotome is primarily responsible for shoulder abduction (deltoid muscle) and also contributes to elbow flexion (biceps brachii, brachialis). It is not associated with wrist extension. * (C) C8: The C8 myotome is primarily responsible for finger flexion (flexor digitorum profundus and superficialis) and thumb extension/adduction. It is not involved in elbow flexion or wrist extension. * (D) C7: The C7 myotome is primarily responsible for elbow extension (triceps brachii), wrist flexion (flexor carpi radialis), and finger extension. It is essentially the antagonist to the movements described in the question.

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following agents crosses the blood brain barrier?
Dopamine
Gentamicin
Glycopyrrolate
Propranolol

Answer: (D) — Propranolol

Explanation:

The ability of a drug to cross the blood-brain barrier (BBB) is primarily determined by its physicochemical properties, such as lipid solubility (lipophilicity), molecular size, and ionization state. Propranolol is a non-selective beta-blocker that is highly lipophilic (fat-soluble). This high lipid solubility allows it to readily diffuse across the lipid membranes of the endothelial cells that form the BBB. Consequently, propranolol achieves significant concentrations in the central nervous system (CNS), which explains its use in treating conditions like essential tremor and migraine prophylaxis, as well as its potential to cause CNS side effects such as fatigue, sedation, and nightmares.

Options:

  • (A) Dopamine: Dopamine is a catecholamine that is highly polar and does not cross the BBB. This is the pharmacological basis for using its precursor, Levodopa (L-DOPA), to treat Parkinson's disease. L-DOPA can cross the BBB via an amino acid transporter and is then converted into dopamine within the CNS.
  • (B) Gentamicin: Gentamicin is an aminoglycoside antibiotic. It is a large, highly polar, and water-soluble molecule. These properties severely limit its ability to penetrate the BBB, resulting in very low concentrations in the cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) after systemic administration. It is generally only effective for CNS infections if given directly into the CSF (intrathecally or intraventricularly).
  • (C) Glycopyrrolate: Glycopyrrolate is an anticholinergic agent. It is a quaternary ammonium compound, which means it carries a permanent positive charge. This charge makes it highly polar and lipid-insoluble, preventing it from crossing the BBB. This is why it is preferred over other anticholinergics (like atropine, a tertiary amine) when only peripheral effects (e.g., reducing secretions) are desired without CNS side effects.

Confidence:

100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following sedatives has its hepatic clearance decreased in a 75-year old?
Nitrazepam
Oxazepam
Diazepam
Lorazepam

Answer: (C) — Diazepam

Explanation:

The hepatic clearance of drugs can be significantly altered in the elderly due to age-related physiological changes, particularly in liver metabolism. Drug metabolism in the liver occurs via two main types of reactions: Phase I (oxidation, reduction, hydrolysis) and Phase II (conjugation, e.g., glucuronidation).

In the elderly, Phase I metabolic pathways, which are often mediated by the cytochrome P450 (CYP) enzyme system, tend to decline in efficiency. In contrast, Phase II conjugation reactions are generally well-preserved.

Diazepam is metabolized extensively by Phase I oxidative reactions (N-demethylation and hydroxylation) via CYP2C19 and CYP3A4 enzymes. Since this metabolic pathway is impaired in the elderly, the clearance of diazepam is significantly decreased, and its elimination half-life is prolonged (from ~20 hours in young adults to ~80 hours or more in the elderly). This leads to drug accumulation and an increased risk of adverse effects like excessive sedation, falls, and cognitive impairment.

Options:

  • (A) Nitrazepam: Incorrect. Nitrazepam is metabolized primarily by nitroreduction, which is a Phase I reaction. Therefore, its clearance is also decreased in the elderly. However, diazepam is the most classic and pronounced example of a benzodiazepine with age-dependent clearance due to its reliance on oxidative metabolism and its long-acting active metabolites, making it the best answer in this context.
  • (B) Oxazepam: Incorrect. Oxazepam is a short-to-intermediate acting benzodiazepine that is metabolized primarily via Phase II glucuronidation. This pathway is not significantly affected by age, so its clearance remains relatively stable in older adults, making it a safer choice.
  • (D) Lorazepam: Incorrect. Similar to oxazepam, lorazepam is metabolized almost exclusively by Phase II glucuronidation into an inactive metabolite. Its clearance is minimally affected by aging, making it another preferred benzodiazepine for use in the elderly.

Mnemonic: A helpful way to remember the benzodiazepines that are safer for the elderly (and those with liver disease) because they bypass Phase I metabolism is "Lots of Old Thinkers" or "Outside The Liver" for Lorazepam, Oxazepam, and Temazepam.

Confidence: 95%

319


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following is the first change to occur after a fracture of a typical long bone?
Development of soft tissue callus
Capillary proliferation from viable marrow
Ossification of persistent fibrin clot
Reconstruction of a periosteal sheath

Answer: (B) — Capillary proliferation from viable marrow

Explanation:

The healing of a long bone fracture is a sequential process with four overlapping stages:

  1. Hematoma Formation and Inflammation (First hours to days): Immediately after the fracture, blood vessels within the bone, periosteum, and surrounding soft tissues are torn, leading to hemorrhage and the formation of a fibrin-rich blood clot, known as a fracture hematoma. This hematoma fills the fracture gap and provides a scaffold for healing. It also initiates an acute inflammatory response, recruiting neutrophils and macrophages to clear necrotic debris.

  2. Soft Callus Formation (First days to weeks): Following the initial inflammatory phase, the reparative process begins. The fracture hematoma is organized into granulation tissue. A critical and initiating step in this process is neovascularization, which involves the proliferation and ingrowth of new capillaries from the undamaged marrow, endosteum, and periosteum. These new vessels are essential for bringing in mesenchymal stem cells, fibroblasts, and osteoprogenitor cells. These cells then produce a fibrocartilaginous "soft callus" that bridges the fracture ends, providing initial stability. Therefore, capillary proliferation is the first step in the formation of the soft callus.

  3. Hard Callus Formation (Weeks to months): The soft callus is gradually replaced by woven (immature) bone through endochondral and intramembranous ossification, forming a "hard callus." This provides much greater strength to the fracture site.

  4. Bone Remodeling (Months to years): The hard callus of woven bone is slowly remodeled into stronger, more organized lamellar bone. Osteoclasts and osteoblasts work to restore the bone's original cortical structure and medullary canal.

Based on this sequence, capillary proliferation is the earliest event listed among the options, as it marks the beginning of the reparative phase (soft callus formation) that follows the initial hematoma.

Options:

  • (A) Development of soft tissue callus: This is the entire second stage of healing. Capillary proliferation is the initial step that allows the soft callus to form. Therefore, capillary proliferation precedes the full development of the soft callus.
  • (C) Ossification of persistent fibrin clot: Ossification (bone formation) occurs during the third stage (hard callus formation), which happens much later than the initial capillary proliferation. The fibrin clot (hematoma) is replaced by granulation tissue and then cartilage, not directly ossified.
  • (D) Reconstruction of a periosteal sheath: The periosteum is crucial for healing as it provides blood supply and osteoprogenitor cells. However, its complete reconstruction is a late event that occurs during the final remodeling phase.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 50-year old male has glucocorticoid excess. An important site of steroid synthesis in steroid secreting cells is the:
Golgi apparatus
Mitochondria
Centrosome
Smooth endoplasmic reticulum

Answer: (D) — Smooth endoplasmic reticulum

Explanation:

The synthesis of steroid hormones, such as glucocorticoids (e.g., cortisol), is a complex enzymatic process that occurs within specific organelles in steroidogenic cells (e.g., cells of the adrenal cortex). This process involves a coordinated effort between the mitochondria and the smooth endoplasmic reticulum (SER).

The smooth endoplasmic reticulum (SER) is the principal site for the synthesis of steroid hormones from cholesterol. Cells that are specialized for steroid production, such as those in the adrenal cortex, testes (Leydig cells), and ovaries (theca and luteal cells), are characterized by having an extensive and prominent SER. The SER contains a majority of the enzymes required for the steroidogenic pathway, including key enzymes like 17α-hydroxylase, 21-hydroxylase, and 3β-hydroxysteroid dehydrogenase, which are essential for converting pregnenolone into glucocorticoids, mineralocorticoids, and androgens.

While the SER is a major site, it is important to note that the mitochondria also play a crucial, indispensable role. The very first and rate-limiting step of steroidogenesis (the conversion of cholesterol to pregnenolone by the enzyme cholesterol side-chain cleavage, or P450scc) and the final step in glucocorticoid synthesis (the conversion of 11-deoxycortisol to cortisol by 11β-hydroxylase) both occur within the mitochondria. Therefore, substrates are shuttled between the mitochondria and the SER during the synthesis process. However, given the options, the SER is the organelle that houses the bulk of the synthetic machinery and is most characteristically hypertrophied in steroid-secreting cells.

Options:

  • A) Golgi apparatus: The Golgi apparatus is primarily responsible for modifying, sorting, and packaging proteins and lipids for secretion or delivery to other organelles. It does not synthesize steroid hormones.
  • B) Mitochondria: This is a very strong distractor as it is a critical site for steroid synthesis. It contains the enzymes for the initial rate-limiting step and some final steps of the pathway. However, the SER contains the majority of the intermediate enzymes and is considered the primary organelle whose abundance defines a steroid-secreting cell. In the context of a single-best-answer question, the SER is the more encompassing choice.
  • C) Centrosome: The centrosome is the main microtubule-organizing center of the cell and plays a vital role in cell division (mitosis) by forming the spindle apparatus. It has no function in steroid synthesis.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Based on epidemiological studies, which one of the following has been found to be most protective against colon cancer?
High fibre diet
High carbohydrate diet
Low protein diet
Low fat diet

Answer: (A) — High fibre diet Explanation: A diet high in fibre has been consistently shown in numerous epidemiological studies to be protective against the development of colorectal cancer. The proposed mechanisms for this protective effect are multifactorial: 1. Increased Stool Bulk and Dilution of Carcinogens: Fibre increases the weight and size of the stool and softens it. This dilutes the concentration of potential carcinogens within the colon. 2. Decreased Transit Time: By increasing bulk, fibre reduces the time it takes for waste to move through the colon. This decreases the contact time between the colonic mucosa and potential carcinogens like secondary bile acids. 3. Production of Short-Chain Fatty Acids (SCFAs): Gut bacteria ferment dietary fibre into SCFAs, most notably butyrate. Butyrate is the preferred energy source for normal colonocytes (the cells lining the colon) and has anti-neoplastic properties, including promoting cancer cell apoptosis (programmed cell death) and inhibiting their proliferation. 4. Binding of Carcinogens: Some types of fibre can bind to carcinogenic substances, preventing them from interacting with the colonic epithelium and facilitating their excretion.

Options: * (B) High carbohydrate diet: This option is incorrect because the term "carbohydrate" is too broad. While a diet high in complex carbohydrates (which are rich in fibre) is protective, a diet high in refined carbohydrates and simple sugars is associated with an increased risk of colon cancer. This is thought to be mediated through obesity, insulin resistance, and chronic inflammation, all of which are risk factors for cancer. * (C) Low protein diet: This is not the most accurate answer. The risk for colon cancer is not linked to protein intake in general but specifically to high consumption of red and processed meats. These meats contain heme iron and can lead to the formation of N-nitroso compounds and other carcinogens during processing or high-temperature cooking. Protein from other sources like poultry, fish, and plants is not associated with the same level of risk. Therefore, reducing red meat intake is protective, but a general low-protein diet is not the primary recommendation. * (D) Low fat diet: The evidence linking total dietary fat intake to colon cancer risk is inconsistent. Early hypotheses suggested that high-fat diets increase bile acid secretion, which could be converted to carcinogenic secondary bile acids. However, large prospective studies have not consistently supported this. The type of fat may be more important than the total amount, with some evidence suggesting that omega-3 fatty acids may be protective, while saturated fats and omega-6 fatty acids might increase risk. Overall, the evidence for a low-fat diet is less robust than that for a high-fibre diet.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following statements about first order kinetics is correct?
Fixed amount of drug is eliminated
Half-life increases with dose
Clearance decreases as dose increases
Clearance remains constant

Answer: (D) — Clearance remains constant

Explanation:

First-order kinetics describes a process where the rate of drug elimination is directly proportional to the drug's plasma concentration. This means that a constant fraction or percentage of the drug is eliminated per unit of time. For the vast majority of drugs at therapeutic concentrations, elimination follows first-order kinetics.

The key characteristics of first-order kinetics are: * Constant Clearance (CL): Clearance is the volume of plasma from which the drug is completely removed per unit of time. It is the proportionality constant that relates the rate of elimination to the plasma concentration (Rate of elimination = CL × Concentration). In first-order kinetics, this value does not change with the drug concentration or dose. * Constant Half-life (t½): The half-life is the time it takes for the plasma drug concentration to decrease by 50%. In first-order kinetics, the half-life is constant and independent of the dose. * Variable Elimination Rate: The absolute amount of drug eliminated per unit of time changes with the concentration. When the concentration is high, a larger amount of drug is eliminated; when the concentration is low, a smaller amount is eliminated.

Therefore, the statement that clearance remains constant is a defining feature of first-order kinetics.

Options:

  • (A) Fixed amount of drug is eliminated: This statement describes zero-order kinetics. In zero-order kinetics, the elimination pathway is saturated, and a constant amount (e.g., 10 mg/hour) of the drug is eliminated per unit of time, regardless of the drug concentration.
  • (B) Half-life increases with dose: This is characteristic of zero-order kinetics. In first-order kinetics, the half-life is constant and independent of the dose. In zero-order kinetics, it takes longer to eliminate half of a larger dose compared to half of a smaller dose.
  • (C) Clearance decreases as dose increases: This describes saturable (or Michaelis-Menten) kinetics as it approaches the zero-order range. When the dose is high enough to begin saturating the elimination mechanisms, the body's ability to clear the drug becomes less efficient, causing clearance to decrease. In pure first-order kinetics, clearance is constant.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
You want to commence antihypertensive therapy for a 75-year-old man who suffers from diabetes mellitus and bilateral renal artery stenosis. The agent of choice is:
Verapamil
Chlorthiazide
Atenolol
Perindopril

Answer: (A) — Verapamil

Explanation:

This patient presents with hypertension and two significant comorbidities: diabetes mellitus and bilateral renal artery stenosis. The choice of antihypertensive agent must take these conditions into account.

In patients with bilateral renal artery stenosis, the glomerular filtration rate (GFR) is critically dependent on the constriction of the efferent arterioles, a process mediated by angiotensin II. Administering an ACE inhibitor (like perindopril) or an Angiotensin II Receptor Blocker (ARB) would block this compensatory mechanism. This leads to marked dilation of the efferent arterioles, a precipitous drop in intraglomerular pressure and GFR, and can precipitate acute renal failure. Therefore, ACE inhibitors and ARBs are absolutely contraindicated in this patient.

Among the remaining options, a calcium channel blocker (CCB) like Verapamil is the most appropriate choice. CCBs lower blood pressure through systemic vasodilation and are not contraindicated in renal artery stenosis. They are also considered a good choice for older adults and are metabolically neutral, making them safe for patients with diabetes mellitus.

Options:

  • (A) Verapamil: Correct. As a calcium channel blocker, verapamil is an effective antihypertensive that does not rely on the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS) for its primary effect. It is safe in renal artery stenosis and does not adversely affect glucose metabolism, making it the agent of choice in this clinical scenario.
  • (B) Chlorthiazide: Incorrect. Thiazide diuretics are a first-line option for hypertension but can cause adverse metabolic effects, including hyperglycemia, which is undesirable in a patient with diabetes. Furthermore, their efficacy is reduced in patients with significant renal impairment (GFR < 30 mL/min), which can be a consequence of renal artery stenosis. They can also cause volume depletion, potentially worsening renal perfusion.
  • (C) Atenolol: Incorrect. Beta-blockers are no longer considered first-line therapy for uncomplicated hypertension, particularly in older adults. More importantly, in patients with diabetes, non-selective beta-blockers and even cardioselective ones like atenolol can mask the adrenergic symptoms of hypoglycemia (e.g., tachycardia, tremors), which can be dangerous.
  • (D) Perindopril: Incorrect. This is an ACE inhibitor. As explained above, ACE inhibitors are absolutely contraindicated in patients with bilateral renal artery stenosis due to the high risk of inducing acute renal failure.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Nitrates decrease myocardial oxygen consumption by all of the following mechanisms EXCEPT:
By direct reduction of oxygen consumption of the myocardial cell
By dilation of the capacitance vessels
By decreasing the left ventricular end diastolic pressure
By decreasing the size of the heart

Answer: (A) — By direct reduction of oxygen consumption of the myocardial cell

Explanation:

Nitrates, such as nitroglycerin, are potent vasodilators that primarily act to decrease myocardial oxygen consumption (MVO2) by altering the hemodynamic load on the heart, not by directly affecting the metabolism of individual myocardial cells. Their main mechanism involves relaxing vascular smooth muscle, which leads to:

  1. Venodilation (Primary Effect): Nitrates are more potent on veins (capacitance vessels) than on arteries. Dilation of veins increases their capacity to hold blood, leading to venous pooling. This reduces the amount of blood returning to the heart (decreased venous return).
  2. Preload Reduction: The decrease in venous return leads to a reduction in the volume of blood in the ventricle at the end of diastole (Left Ventricular End-Diastolic Volume, LVEDV). This, in turn, decreases the Left Ventricular End-Diastolic Pressure (LVEDP), which is the definition of reducing preload.
  3. Decreased Wall Tension: According to the Law of Laplace (Wall Tension ∝ Pressure × Radius), a reduction in preload (which decreases both ventricular pressure and radius) significantly reduces myocardial wall tension. Since wall tension is a major determinant of MVO2, this is the principal way nitrates reduce the heart's oxygen demand.
  4. Afterload Reduction (Secondary Effect): At higher doses, nitrates also cause some dilation of arteries, which reduces systemic vascular resistance and blood pressure. This reduction in the pressure the heart must pump against is called a reduction in afterload, which also contributes to a decrease in MVO2, albeit to a lesser extent than preload reduction.

Nitrates do not have a direct metabolic effect on the cardiomyocyte that would make it consume less oxygen for a given amount of work. Their effect is entirely mediated by changing the mechanical workload of the heart.

Options:

  • (B) By dilation of the capacitance vessels: This is incorrect because it is the primary mechanism of action for nitrates. Dilation of capacitance vessels (veins) reduces preload, which is key to decreasing MVO2.
  • (C) By decreasing the left ventricular end diastolic pressure: This is incorrect because it is a direct and intended consequence of nitrate-induced venodilation. Decreasing LVEDP (preload) is how nitrates reduce myocardial wall tension and oxygen demand.
  • (D) By decreasing the size of the heart: This is incorrect because by reducing preload (LVEDV), nitrates effectively decrease the diastolic volume or "size" of the heart chamber. This reduction in cardiac radius contributes to lower wall tension and, consequently, lower MVO2.

Confidence: 100%

329


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
In which of the following circumstances, the toxicity of digoxin is aggravated?
Hyperkalemia
Hypocalcemia
Hypokalemia
Hypermagnesemia

Answer: (C) — Hypokalemia

Explanation:

Digoxin exerts its therapeutic and toxic effects by inhibiting the sodium-potassium ATPase (Na+/K+-ATPase) pump in the myocardial cell membrane. Potassium (K+) and digoxin compete for the same binding site on this pump.

In a state of hypokalemia (low serum potassium), there is less extracellular potassium to compete with digoxin for binding to the Na+/K+-ATPase pump. This leads to increased binding of digoxin to the pump, resulting in greater inhibition. Excessive pump inhibition causes a significant rise in intracellular sodium, which in turn leads to a marked increase in intracellular calcium via the sodium-calcium exchanger. This calcium overload is responsible for the classic signs of digoxin toxicity, such as cardiac arrhythmias (e.g., ventricular bigeminy, AV block).

Options:

  • (A) Hyperkalemia: This is incorrect. High levels of serum potassium increase the competition with digoxin for binding to the Na+/K+-ATPase pump. This reduces the binding and effect of digoxin, thereby lessening its toxicity. In fact, moderate hyperkalemia can be protective against digoxin toxicity, and potassium administration is sometimes used to treat certain digoxin-induced arrhythmias.
  • (B) Hypocalcemia: This is incorrect. The toxic effects of digoxin are mediated by an increase in intracellular calcium. Hypocalcemia (low serum calcium) would tend to counteract this effect, potentially reducing the risk of toxicity. Conversely, hypercalcemia can potentiate digoxin's effects and aggravate toxicity by further increasing intracellular calcium levels.
  • (D) Hypermagnesemia: This is incorrect. High levels of magnesium may have a protective effect against digoxin-induced arrhythmias. In contrast, hypomagnesemia is a condition that aggravates digoxin toxicity, as it can promote potassium wasting (leading to hypokalemia) and increase cardiac irritability.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
A patient diagnosed with acute coronary syndrome is given a dose of clopidogrel. Which one of the following is the mechanism of action of clopidogrel?
Inhibits ADP-induced platelet aggregation
Inactivates antithrombin III
Inhibits adenosine uptake and cGMP phosphodiesterase activity.
Inhibits thromboxane A2

Answer: (A) — Inhibits ADP-induced platelet aggregation

Explanation:

Clopidogrel is an antiplatelet medication belonging to the thienopyridine class. It is a prodrug that requires bioactivation in the liver, primarily by the cytochrome P450 enzyme CYP2C19, to form its active metabolite. This active metabolite then irreversibly binds to the P2Y12 subtype of adenosine diphosphate (ADP) receptors on the surface of platelets.

The binding of ADP to its P2Y12 receptor is a crucial step in platelet activation and aggregation. By blocking this receptor, clopidogrel prevents ADP-mediated activation of the glycoprotein IIb/IIIa complex, which is the final common pathway for platelet aggregation. Consequently, platelets are unable to bind to each other via fibrinogen bridges, thus inhibiting thrombus formation. This effect lasts for the entire lifespan of the platelet (approximately 7-10 days).

Options:

  • (B) Inactivates antithrombin III: This is the mechanism of action for heparin (both unfractionated and low-molecular-weight heparin). Heparin binds to and potentiates the activity of antithrombin III, which then inactivates thrombin (Factor IIa) and Factor Xa, thereby preventing fibrin clot formation.
  • (C) Inhibits adenosine uptake and cGMP phosphodiesterase activity: This describes the mechanism of action of dipyridamole, another antiplatelet agent. By inhibiting adenosine uptake, it increases local adenosine levels, which stimulates platelet adenylyl cyclase and raises cAMP, inhibiting aggregation. It also inhibits cGMP phosphodiesterase, leading to increased cGMP levels, which also contributes to its antiplatelet and vasodilatory effects.
  • (D) Inhibits thromboxane A2: This is the mechanism of action of aspirin. Aspirin irreversibly inhibits the cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) enzyme in platelets, which prevents the conversion of arachidonic acid to thromboxane A2 (TXA2). TXA2 is a potent vasoconstrictor and promoter of platelet aggregation.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
If 1mg of adrenaline is present in 1 L of solution, the dilution is:
1:10000
1:100000
1:1000
1:1000000

Answer: (D) — 1:1000000

Explanation:

A dilution ratio in pharmacology, such as 1:X, represents 1 gram of the drug dissolved in X milliliters (mL) of solvent. To solve this problem, we need to convert the given quantities (1 mg and 1 L) into the standard units of grams (g) and milliliters (mL).

  1. Convert Mass: The mass of adrenaline is 1 milligram (mg).

    • Since 1 gram (g) = 1000 milligrams (mg), then 1 mg = 1/1000 g or 0.001 g.

  2. Convert Volume: The volume of the solution is 1 liter (L).

    • Since 1 liter (L) = 1000 milliliters (mL).

  3. Calculate the Ratio: Now we have the concentration expressed as grams per milliliter.

    • Concentration = 0.001 g / 1000 mL

  4. Simplify to 1:X Format: To find the dilution ratio in the form 1:X, we need to determine how many mL of solution would contain 1 gram of the drug. We can set up a proportion:

    • (0.001 g) / (1000 mL) = (1 g) / (X mL)
    • Cross-multiply: 0.001 * X = 1000 * 1
    • Solve for X: X = 1000 / 0.001
    • X = 1,000,000

Therefore, 1 gram of adrenaline is present in 1,000,000 mL of solution, which corresponds to a dilution of 1:1,000,000.

Options:

  • (A) 1:10000: This dilution means 1 g in 10,000 mL. This is equivalent to 1000 mg in 10,000 mL, or 100 mg per 1000 mL (1 L). This is a common dilution for intravenous (IV) adrenaline during resuscitation.
  • (B) 1:100000: This dilution means 1 g in 100,000 mL. This is equivalent to 1000 mg in 100,000 mL, or 10 mg per 1000 mL (1 L). This dilution is often used when adrenaline is added to local anesthetic solutions.
  • (C) 1:1000: This dilution means 1 g in 1000 mL. This is equivalent to 1000 mg in 1000 mL, or 1 mg per 1 mL. This is the standard concentration of adrenaline supplied in ampules for intramuscular injection (e.g., for anaphylaxis).

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Which one of the following is NOT an adverse effect of salbutamol?
Hypoglycemia
Hypokalemia
Tolerance
Tachycardia

Answer: (A) — Hypoglycemia

Explanation:

Salbutamol (albuterol) is a short-acting beta-2 adrenergic agonist. Its primary therapeutic use is to cause bronchodilation in conditions like asthma and COPD. The adverse effects of salbutamol are a direct consequence of stimulating beta-adrenergic receptors throughout the body.

Beta-2 receptor stimulation has several metabolic effects, including an increase in blood glucose levels. This occurs through two main mechanisms: 1. Increased Glycogenolysis: Stimulation of beta-2 receptors in the liver and skeletal muscle promotes the breakdown of glycogen into glucose. 2. Increased Gluconeogenesis: Beta-2 stimulation in the liver also increases the synthesis of new glucose.

Therefore, salbutamol tends to cause hyperglycemia (high blood sugar), not hypoglycemia (low blood sugar). Hypoglycemia is not a recognized adverse effect of this medication.

Options:

  • (B) Hypokalemia: This is a well-known adverse effect. Beta-2 receptor stimulation activates the Na+/K+-ATPase pump in skeletal muscle cells, which drives potassium from the extracellular fluid into the cells. This leads to a decrease in serum potassium levels.
  • (C) Tolerance: With frequent or chronic use, the number of beta-2 receptors on cell surfaces can decrease (downregulation). This leads to a reduced response to the same dose of the drug, a phenomenon known as tolerance or tachyphylaxis.
  • (D) Tachycardia: This is one of the most common side effects. It can occur as a reflex response to peripheral vasodilation (also mediated by beta-2 receptors) or, at higher doses, due to direct stimulation of cardiac beta-1 receptors, as salbutamol's selectivity for beta-2 receptors is not absolute.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PHARMACOLOGY
Monteleukast is used as maintenance treatment for chronic asthma. Which one of the following is its mechanism of action?
Beta-receptor agonist action
Inhibition of leukotriene production
Inhibits cholinergic receptors
Phosphodiesterase inhibition

Answer: (B) — Inhibition of leukotriene production

Important Clarification: The most precise mechanism of action for Montelukast is leukotriene receptor antagonism, which is not an option in the provided list. The option "Inhibition of leukotriene production" technically describes a different drug, Zileuton. However, among the choices given, this is the only one that correctly identifies the leukotriene pathway as the therapeutic target, and may be considered the intended answer in a poorly formulated question.

Explanation:

Montelukast is a drug used for the maintenance treatment of asthma and allergic rhinitis. Its true mechanism is as a selective antagonist of the cysteinyl leukotriene 1 (CysLT1) receptor.

  1. Pathophysiology: In asthma, the body releases inflammatory mediators called cysteinyl leukotrienes (LTC₄, LTD₄, LTE₄).
  2. Leukotriene Action: These leukotrienes bind to CysLT1 receptors located on airway smooth muscle cells and inflammatory cells (like eosinophils). This binding triggers potent bronchoconstriction, increased mucus secretion, airway edema, and recruitment of inflammatory cells.
  3. Montelukast's Role: Montelukast works by physically blocking the CysLT1 receptor. By occupying the receptor, it prevents the body's own leukotrienes from binding and causing their pro-inflammatory and bronchoconstrictive effects. This leads to a reduction in asthma symptoms.

Options:

  • (A) Beta-receptor agonist action: This is incorrect. This is the mechanism of bronchodilators such as albuterol (short-acting) and salmeterol (long-acting). They stimulate beta-2 adrenergic receptors to cause relaxation of the airway smooth muscle.
  • (B) Inhibition of leukotriene production: This is technically incorrect for Montelukast but refers to the correct pathway. This mechanism belongs to the drug Zileuton, which inhibits the 5-lipoxygenase enzyme, thereby preventing the synthesis of leukotrienes from arachidonic acid. Montelukast blocks the action of leukotrienes, it does not inhibit their production.
  • (C) Inhibits cholinergic receptors: This is incorrect. This is the mechanism of antimuscarinic agents like ipratropium and tiotropium. They block the action of acetylcholine on muscarinic receptors in the airways to prevent bronchoconstriction.
  • (D) Phosphodiesterase inhibition: This is incorrect. This is the mechanism of drugs like theophylline. By inhibiting the PDE enzyme, it increases intracellular cAMP, which leads to bronchodilation and has some anti-inflammatory properties.

Confidence: 100% (Confidence in the pharmacological principles. The question itself is flawed as it omits the correct specific answer.)

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
An elderly patient presents with chronic cough and x-ray findings suggestive of re-activation of pulmonary tuberculosis. What is the most common location for re-activation of pulmonary tuberculosis?
Subplueral
Apex
Base
Around the main bronchus

Answer: (B) — Apex

Explanation:

Re-activation, or secondary, pulmonary tuberculosis occurs when a previously dormant latent infection with Mycobacterium tuberculosis becomes active again. This typically happens when the host's immune system is weakened (e.g., due to old age, malnutrition, HIV infection, or immunosuppressive therapy).

Mycobacterium tuberculosis is an obligate aerobe, meaning it requires a high-oxygen environment to thrive and multiply. The apices (singular: apex) of the lungs have the highest ventilation-to-perfusion (V/Q) ratio. This physiological characteristic means there is more air (and thus oxygen) relative to blood flow, creating a high-oxygen-tension environment. This makes the lung apices the most favorable location for the reactivation and proliferation of dormant mycobacteria. The classic radiological finding of re-activation TB is a cavitary lesion in the apical or posterior segments of the upper lobes.

Options:

  • Subpleural: A subpleural location (specifically in the lower part of the upper lobe or the upper part of the lower lobe) is the characteristic site for the initial lesion of primary tuberculosis, known as the Ghon focus. This is not the typical site for re-activation.
  • Base: The lung bases have a low V/Q ratio, meaning there is more perfusion than ventilation, resulting in lower oxygen tension. This environment is less favorable for the growth of the aerobic M. tuberculosis, making it an uncommon site for re-activation.
  • Around the main bronchus: This refers to the hilar region. Hilar lymph node involvement (lymphadenopathy) is a hallmark of primary tuberculosis, where it combines with the Ghon focus to form the Ghon complex. While there can be some lymph node involvement in re-activation TB, the primary site of parenchymal disease is characteristically in the apex.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
An 8-year-old presents with a cut to her foot after she accidently stepped on a piece of broken glass. The bleeding stops with firm local pressure in 10 minutes. Which of the following substances is most likely to limit thrombus formation to the local area of injury by inhibiting platelet activation?
Prostacyclin
Tissue-type plasminogen activator
Phospholipid
Thrombomodulin

Answer: (A) — Prostacyclin

Explanation:

The question asks to identify the substance that limits thrombus formation locally by inhibiting platelet activation. Following an injury, such as a cut, hemostasis is initiated to form a clot and stop bleeding. However, this process must be tightly regulated to prevent the clot from growing excessively and occluding the vessel or spreading to uninjured areas.

Prostacyclin (PGI2) is synthesized by healthy, intact endothelial cells adjacent to the site of injury. It is a potent vasodilator and a powerful inhibitor of platelet activation and aggregation. It achieves this by binding to platelet receptors, which increases intracellular levels of cyclic AMP (cAMP). Elevated cAMP levels sequester intracellular calcium, thereby preventing platelet degranulation, shape change, and aggregation. This action effectively creates an "anti-thrombotic halo" around the injury site, ensuring the platelet plug is confined only to the area where the endothelium is damaged.

Options:

  • (B) Tissue-type plasminogen activator (t-PA): Incorrect. t-PA is a key component of the fibrinolytic system, not an inhibitor of platelet activation. It is released by endothelial cells and converts plasminogen to plasmin. Plasmin's function is to break down the fibrin mesh of an already-formed clot. While this limits the ultimate size and duration of the thrombus, it does so by dissolving the clot, not by preventing initial platelet activation.
  • (C) Phospholipid: Incorrect. Phospholipids, specifically the negatively charged phospholipids exposed on the surface of activated platelets (formerly known as platelet factor 3), play a pro-coagulant role. They provide a critical surface for the assembly and concentration of coagulation factor complexes (e.g., the tenase and prothrombinase complexes), thereby accelerating the generation of thrombin and promoting clot formation.
  • (D) Thrombomodulin: Incorrect. Thrombomodulin is a protein on the surface of intact endothelial cells that acts as an anticoagulant, but its primary mechanism is not the inhibition of platelet activation. It binds to thrombin, changing thrombin's function from a procoagulant to an anticoagulant. The thrombin-thrombomodulin complex activates Protein C, which, along with its cofactor Protein S, inactivates coagulation factors Va and VIIIa. This downregulates the coagulation cascade, thus limiting clot growth, but it does not directly inhibit platelet activation in the way prostacyclin does.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 12-year old boy has a history of markedly decreased factor VIII activity. He presents frequently with multiple soft tissue hematomas and hemarthroses. Which of the following statements best describes the underlying pathological process?
Failure of platelet aggregation
Decrease in coagulation cascade component
Failure of fibrin polymerization
Inability to neurtralize antithrombin III

Answer: (B) — Decrease in coagulation cascade component

Explanation:

The clinical presentation of a 12-year-old boy with markedly decreased factor VIII activity, soft tissue hematomas, and hemarthroses (bleeding into joints) is classic for Hemophilia A. Hemostasis (the process of stopping bleeding) involves two main stages: primary hemostasis (platelet plug formation) and secondary hemostasis (coagulation cascade).

Factor VIII is a crucial protein that acts as a cofactor for Factor IXa in the intrinsic pathway of the coagulation cascade. This complex (Factor VIIIa-Factor IXa) is responsible for activating Factor X, a key step in amplifying the production of thrombin. Thrombin, in turn, converts soluble fibrinogen into an insoluble fibrin mesh, which stabilizes the initial platelet plug to form a durable clot.

A deficiency in Factor VIII, as seen in this patient, leads to a significant impairment of the coagulation cascade. Without sufficient Factor VIII, thrombin generation is severely reduced, resulting in poor and delayed fibrin clot formation. This leads to the characteristic clinical signs of deep tissue bleeding, such as hematomas and hemarthroses, which are hallmarks of defects in secondary hemostasis.

Options:

  • (A) Failure of platelet aggregation: This describes a defect in primary hemostasis, where platelets fail to stick to each other. This is characteristic of disorders like Glanzmann thrombasthenia (deficiency of GpIIb/IIIa receptors). Patients with Hemophilia A have normal platelet function.
  • (C) Failure of fibrin polymerization: This refers to the inability of fibrin monomers to form a stable polymer mesh. This is seen in conditions like dysfibrinogenemia, where the fibrinogen molecule itself is structurally abnormal. In hemophilia, the problem is not the quality of fibrin but the insufficient quantity produced due to the upstream defect in the cascade.
  • (D) Inability to neutralize antithrombin III: Antithrombin III is a natural anticoagulant that inactivates thrombin and other clotting factors. An inability to neutralize it is not a recognized pathological mechanism. A deficiency of antithrombin III would lead to a hypercoagulable state (thrombosis), not a bleeding disorder. The patient's problem is a lack of a pro-coagulant factor, not an issue with an anti-coagulant.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 44-year old female presents to ED with a laceration to her lower leg. She is on long-term steroids for rheumatoid arthritis. Which of the following aspects of wound healing is most likely to be deficient in this patient?
Fibroblast growth factor elaboration
Collagen deposition
Neutrophil infiltration
Re-epithelialization

Answer: (B) — Collagen deposition

Explanation:

Wound healing is a complex process divided into four main phases: hemostasis, inflammation, proliferation, and remodeling. Long-term use of corticosteroids, such as in this patient with rheumatoid arthritis, significantly impairs this process.

Corticosteroids exert potent anti-inflammatory effects. They inhibit the inflammatory phase by suppressing the function of key inflammatory cells, particularly macrophages. Macrophages are crucial for orchestrating the healing process as they release numerous growth factors, such as Transforming Growth Factor-beta (TGF-β).

The primary mechanism by which steroids impair wound healing is by inhibiting TGF-β production. TGF-β is essential for stimulating fibroblast migration, proliferation, and, most importantly, the synthesis and deposition of collagen. Collagen provides the structural framework for granulation tissue and is ultimately responsible for the tensile strength of the healed wound.

Therefore, in a patient on long-term steroids, the reduced inflammatory response and subsequent lack of growth factor stimulation lead to a marked deficiency in fibroblast activity and collagen deposition. This results in poor granulation tissue formation, delayed wound closure, and a weak scar that is prone to dehiscence (reopening).

Options:

  • (A) Fibroblast growth factor elaboration: This is also inhibited by corticosteroids as part of their broad anti-inflammatory and immunosuppressive effects. However, this is more of an upstream mechanism. The most significant and direct consequence on the structural integrity of the healing wound is the resulting failure of collagen deposition.
  • (C) Neutrophil infiltration: Corticosteroids do suppress the initial inflammatory response, which includes dampening neutrophil chemotaxis and infiltration. However, the most clinically significant and lasting deficit in wound healing from long-term steroid use is not the blunting of the initial inflammation but the failure of the subsequent proliferative and remodeling phases, which are dependent on collagen.
  • (D) Re-epithelialization: This process, involving the migration and proliferation of keratinocytes to cover the wound surface, can also be slowed by corticosteroids. However, the most profound and well-established deficiency caused by steroids is the impairment of dermal connective tissue (scar) formation, which is fundamentally a problem of collagen deposition.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 6-year-old presents with high fever and cough for the last 2 days. Which one of the following mediators is most likely responsible for the fever?
Nitric oxide
Histamine
Tumour necrosis factor
Leukotrienes

Answer: (C) — Tumour necrosis factor Explanation: Fever is a systemic manifestation of inflammation, most commonly caused by infection. The pathogenesis of fever involves the production of endogenous pyrogens, which are cytokines that act on the hypothalamus to reset the body's thermoregulatory set-point to a higher level. Tumour necrosis factor (TNF), along with Interleukin-1 (IL-1) and Interleukin-6 (IL-6), are the most important endogenous pyrogens. They are typically produced by macrophages and other immune cells in response to pathogens or tissue injury. These cytokines stimulate the production of prostaglandins (specifically PGE2) in the hypothalamus, which directly mediates the increase in the temperature set-point. In a 6-year-old with fever and cough, an infection is the likely cause, leading to the production of these pro-inflammatory cytokines.

Options: (A) Nitric oxide is primarily a potent vasodilator and is involved in neurotransmission and microbial killing. While it is an important mediator of inflammation, it is not a primary cause of fever; in some contexts, it may even have antipyretic (fever-reducing) effects.

(B) Histamine is a key mediator of type I hypersensitivity reactions (allergies) and acute inflammation. It causes vasodilation and increased vascular permeability, but it is not considered an endogenous pyrogen and does not directly induce fever by acting on the hypothalamus.

(D) Leukotrienes are lipid mediators derived from arachidonic acid. They are potent chemotactic agents for neutrophils, and they cause bronchoconstriction and increased vascular permeability. While crucial in inflammatory responses like asthma, they are not primary mediators of the febrile response.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
A 48-year old presents with 3-day history of high fever, cough and yellow sputum. A Chest X-ray shows bilateral consolidation and unilateral pleural effusion. Which one of the following inflammatory cells is most likely to be seen in large numbers in the patient’s sputum specimen?
Neutrophils
Mast cells
Lymphocytes
Macrophages

Answer: (A) — Neutrophils

Explanation: The patient's clinical presentation—acute onset of high fever, productive cough with yellow sputum, and chest X-ray findings of consolidation and pleural effusion—is highly suggestive of acute bacterial pneumonia.

Acute bacterial infections trigger a robust innate immune response characterized by the rapid recruitment of neutrophils to the site of infection. These phagocytic cells are the first line of defense against extracellular bacteria. They engulf and destroy the pathogens, and their accumulation, along with dead bacteria, cellular debris, and fluid, forms pus. The yellow or greenish color of the sputum (purulent sputum) is a direct result of the large number of neutrophils and the enzymes they contain (like myeloperoxidase). Therefore, a sputum sample from a patient with acute bacterial pneumonia will show a predominance of neutrophils.

Options:

  • (B) Mast cells: These cells are primarily involved in type I hypersensitivity reactions (allergies) and defense against parasites. They release granules containing histamine and other inflammatory mediators. They are not the predominant cell type in the inflammatory exudate of acute bacterial pneumonia.
  • (C) Lymphocytes: Lymphocytes (T cells and B cells) are the key players in the adaptive immune response. A lymphocytic infiltrate is characteristic of chronic inflammation (e.g., tuberculosis) and most viral infections. The patient's presentation is acute and bacterial, not chronic or typically viral.
  • (D) Macrophages: Alveolar macrophages are resident immune cells in the lungs and are crucial for initiating the inflammatory response and for cleaning up debris during the resolution phase of pneumonia. While they are present, they are significantly outnumbered by the massive influx of neutrophils during the height of an acute bacterial infection.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following sites of cancer is responsible for more deaths in men than women?
Lymphoma
Pancreas
Colon and rectum
Lung

Answer: (D) — Lung

Explanation:

Lung cancer is responsible for significantly more deaths in men than in women on a global scale. The primary reason for this disparity is the historical difference in smoking prevalence between the sexes. Smoking, particularly of cigarettes, is the leading cause of lung cancer. Historically, men began smoking in large numbers several decades before women, leading to a much higher incidence and mortality rate from lung cancer in men. While the gap has narrowed in many developed countries as smoking rates in men have declined and rates in women have increased, a substantial difference in mortality persists worldwide. According to global cancer statistics (GLOBOCAN 2022), lung cancer is the leading cause of cancer death in men, with mortality rates being approximately 1.8 times higher in men than in women.

Options:

  • (A) Lymphoma: While the mortality rate for lymphomas (both Hodgkin and Non-Hodgkin) is higher in men than in women, the absolute number of deaths and the magnitude of the difference between the sexes are considerably smaller than for lung cancer. The male-to-female mortality ratio is approximately 1.3-1.6.

  • (B) Pancreas: Pancreatic cancer has a slightly higher mortality rate in men than in women, but the difference is minimal. The incidence and mortality rates are nearly equal between the sexes, with a male-to-female mortality ratio of about 1.1.

  • (C) Colon and rectum: Colorectal cancer also causes more deaths in men than in women. However, similar to lymphoma, the disparity is not as pronounced as it is for lung cancer. The male-to-female mortality ratio for colorectal cancer is approximately 1.2.

Conclusion: Among the given options, lung cancer exhibits the most significant and well-documented difference in mortality between men and women, making it the correct answer.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following environmental factors increases the risk of developing idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis several fold?
Organic dusts
Toxic gases – chlorine, sulphur dioxide
Inorganic dusts
Inhalation of tobacco smoke

Answer: (D) — Inhalation of tobacco smoke Explanation: Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis (IPF) is a chronic, progressive, fibrosing interstitial pneumonia of unknown cause. While the etiology is by definition "idiopathic," several genetic and environmental risk factors have been strongly associated with its development. Among the environmental factors listed, cigarette smoking is the most well-established and significant risk factor. Epidemiological studies have consistently shown that a history of smoking increases the risk of developing IPF by approximately 1.6 to 2.9 times (several fold) compared to non-smokers. The proposed mechanism involves chronic epithelial cell injury, oxidative stress, and an aberrant wound healing response in genetically susceptible individuals, leading to fibroblast proliferation and excessive extracellular matrix deposition.

Options: (A) Organic dusts: Inhalation of organic dusts (e.g., moldy hay in farmer's lung, avian proteins in bird fancier's lung) typically causes hypersensitivity pneumonitis. While chronic hypersensitivity pneumonitis can lead to pulmonary fibrosis, it is considered a separate disease entity from IPF because the causative agent is known.

(B) Toxic gases – chlorine, sulphur dioxide: Acute high-level exposure to toxic gases like chlorine or sulphur dioxide can cause acute lung injury and acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS). Fibrosis can be a long-term consequence (sequela) of ARDS, but this is a post-injury fibrotic process, not the chronic, insidious development characteristic of IPF. It is not considered a primary risk factor for developing IPF itself.

(C) Inorganic dusts: Inhalation of inorganic dusts leads to a group of diseases called pneumoconioses. For example, silica dust causes silicosis, and asbestos fibers cause asbestosis. These are distinct forms of interstitial lung disease with known causes and are therefore not "idiopathic." While the histological pattern of asbestosis (usual interstitial pneumonia or UIP) can be identical to that seen in IPF, the known exposure to asbestos classifies it as a separate disease.

Confidence: 99%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Atypical pneumonia can be caused by all of the following microbial agents EXCEPT:
Mycoplasma pneumoniae
Human metapneumovirus
Klebsiella pneumoniae
Legionella pneumoniae

Answer: (C) — Klebsiella pneumoniae

Explanation:

Pneumonia is broadly classified into "typical" and "atypical" syndromes based on clinical presentation, radiographic findings, and the causative microorganisms.

Atypical pneumonia is characterized by a more gradual onset, a non-productive cough, and constitutional symptoms like headache, myalgia (muscle aches), and low-grade fever. Chest X-rays often show diffuse, patchy, or interstitial infiltrates rather than dense lobar consolidation. The causative organisms are "atypical" because they cannot be easily isolated on standard bacterial media or visualized with a Gram stain.

Klebsiella pneumoniae is a classic cause of typical pneumonia. This type of pneumonia presents acutely with high fever, chills, and a productive cough with thick, purulent, often blood-tinged sputum (classically described as "currant jelly" sputum). Radiographically, it typically causes lobar consolidation, often in the upper lobes, and has a propensity to form abscesses and cavities. It is a gram-negative rod that is readily identified on Gram stain and culture. Therefore, it is the exception in a list of atypical pneumonia agents.

Options:

  • (A) Mycoplasma pneumoniae: This is the most common cause of atypical pneumonia, often referred to as "walking pneumonia." It is particularly prevalent in children and young adults. As a bacterium lacking a cell wall, it does not respond to beta-lactam antibiotics and is a classic atypical pathogen.
  • (B) Human metapneumovirus: Viruses are a primary cause of atypical pneumonia. Human metapneumovirus (hMPV), along with influenza virus, respiratory syncytial virus (RSV), and adenoviruses, causes a clinical syndrome consistent with atypical pneumonia, characterized by interstitial inflammation.
  • (D) Legionella pneumophila (causative agent of Legionnaires' disease): This is a key cause of severe atypical pneumonia. It is an intracellular bacterium that stains poorly with Gram stain and requires special culture media. The disease often presents with prominent extrapulmonary symptoms, such as diarrhea, confusion, and hyponatremia, alongside the pneumonia.

Confidence: 100%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
The heart of a 90-year-old patient is likely to have which one of the following changes?
Increased left ventricular cavity size
Sigmoid shaped ventricular septum
Decreased myocardial mass
Yellow atrophy

Answer: (B) — Sigmoid shaped ventricular septum

Explanation:

The aging heart undergoes several characteristic morphological and functional changes. One of the most common gross anatomical findings in the heart of an elderly individual is a sigmoid shaped ventricular septum. This change results from a combination of factors, including: 1. A decrease in the anteroposterior diameter of the chest. 2. Elongation and tortuosity of the ascending aorta, which displaces the base of the heart. 3. A decrease in the left ventricular cavity size at the base.

These changes cause the basal portion of the interventricular septum to bulge into the left ventricular outflow tract, creating an "S" or sigmoid shape. This is generally considered a benign, age-related finding but can sometimes contribute to dynamic left ventricular outflow tract obstruction.

Options:

  • (A) Increased left ventricular cavity size: This is incorrect. In the normal aging process, the left ventricular cavity size typically remains unchanged or may even decrease slightly. An increased cavity size is characteristic of dilated cardiomyopathy, a pathological condition, not a normal age-related change.
  • (C) Decreased myocardial mass: This is incorrect. While there is a loss of individual myocytes (cardiac muscle cells) through apoptosis with age, the remaining myocytes often undergo compensatory hypertrophy (increase in size). This, combined with an increase in interstitial fibrous tissue, usually results in a net increase or no significant change in overall myocardial mass, particularly of the left ventricle.
  • (D) Yellow atrophy: This is incorrect. The term refers to a combination of atrophy (decrease in cell/organ size) and a yellow/brown discoloration. While the heart does accumulate lipofuscin pigment (a wear-and-tear pigment) which gives it a brownish color ("brown atrophy"), the term "atrophy" is misleading for the heart as a whole. As explained above, the overall myocardial mass does not typically decrease. Therefore, while lipofuscin accumulation is a feature of aging, significant "atrophy" of the heart is not, making sigmoid septum a more accurate and characteristic gross finding.

Confidence: 95%

MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
An 80-year old female with a background history of heart failure presents with acute severe dyspnea. Her temperature is 36, RR 34, spo2 – 90% on 6L oxygen, HR 120, BP 190/90. Auscultation shows bilateral crepitation. CXR shows marked interstitial opacities and small bilateral pleural effusions. Which one of the following is the most common pathological mechanism for her current condition?
Reduced colloid osmotic pressure
Elevated hydrostatic pressure
Elevated lymphatic pressure
Increased permeability of alveolar capillaries

Answer: (B) — Elevated hydrostatic pressure

Explanation:

The clinical presentation is classic for acute cardiogenic pulmonary edema, a life-threatening complication of acute decompensated heart failure (ADHF). The patient's history of heart failure, coupled with acute severe dyspnea, tachypnea, tachycardia, hypoxia, hypertension, bilateral crepitations, and CXR findings of interstitial edema and pleural effusions, all point to this diagnosis.

The underlying pathological mechanism for cardiogenic pulmonary edema is an increase in pulmonary capillary hydrostatic pressure. Here's the sequence of events: 1. The left ventricle fails to effectively pump blood into the systemic circulation. 2. This leads to a backup of blood and pressure into the left atrium. 3. The elevated left atrial pressure is transmitted backward into the pulmonary veins and, subsequently, the pulmonary capillaries. 4. This rise in hydrostatic pressure within the capillaries (reflected by an elevated pulmonary capillary wedge pressure) exceeds the opposing force of the colloid osmotic pressure. 5. According to the Starling equation, this pressure imbalance forces a net movement of fluid (a transudate) from the capillaries into the pulmonary interstitium and, when the capacity of the interstitium and lymphatics is overwhelmed, into the alveoli, causing pulmonary edema. The patient's hypertension (BP 190/90) further increases the afterload against which the failing heart must pump, exacerbating the situation.

Options:

  • (A) Reduced colloid osmotic pressure: This can cause pulmonary edema but is typically associated with conditions leading to hypoalbuminemia, such as nephrotic syndrome, severe liver disease, or malnutrition. While an elderly patient may have low albumin, it is not the primary, acute driver of pulmonary edema in the context of a heart failure exacerbation.
  • (C) Elevated lymphatic pressure: The lymphatic system is responsible for draining excess fluid from the lung interstitium. In pulmonary edema, lymphatic flow increases significantly to compensate. However, the primary problem is the massive rate of fluid filtration from the capillaries, which overwhelms the lymphatic drainage capacity. Elevated lymphatic pressure is a secondary consequence, not the initial cause.
  • (D) Increased permeability of alveolar capillaries: This is the hallmark of non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema, also known as Acute Respiratory Distress Syndrome (ARDS). ARDS is caused by direct lung injury (e.g., from pneumonia, sepsis, aspiration) that damages the alveolar-capillary membrane, making it "leaky" to protein-rich fluid (an exudate). This patient's presentation is more consistent with a cardiac cause (cardiogenic edema) rather than primary lung injury, especially given the history and afebrile status.

Confidence: 100%

357


MULTIPLE CHOICE QUESTION - PATHOLOGY
Which one of the following is most sensitive to radiation?
Gastrointestinal epithelium
Endocardium
Epithelium of bladder
Corneal epithelium

Answer: (A) — Gastrointestinal epithelium

Explanation:

The sensitivity of a tissue to radiation is primarily determined by its cellular characteristics, a principle summarized by the Law of Bergonié and Tribondeau. This law states that radiosensitivity is directly proportional to the rate of cell division (mitotic activity) and inversely proportional to the degree of cell differentiation. Therefore, tissues with rapidly dividing, poorly differentiated cells are the most sensitive to radiation damage.

The gastrointestinal epithelium, particularly the stem cells in the crypts of the small intestine, has one of the highest rates of cell turnover in the body (approximately 3-5 days). These crypt cells are constantly dividing to replenish the epithelial lining. Due to this high mitotic activity, the GI tract is extremely sensitive to radiation. Damage to these cells leads to mucosal breakdown, malabsorption, diarrhea, and electrolyte imbalances, which are hallmark features of the gastrointestinal syndrome of acute radiation sickness.

Options:

  • (B) Endocardium: The endocardium is the inner lining of the heart chambers. It is composed of a single layer of endothelial cells overlying connective tissue. These are stable, well-differentiated cells with a very low rate of division. Consequently, the endocardium is relatively radioresistant.
  • (C) Epithelium of bladder: The transitional epithelium (urothelium) of the bladder is a stable cell population with a much slower turnover rate compared to the gastrointestinal epithelium. While it can be damaged by high doses of radiation (causing radiation cystitis), it is significantly less sensitive than the GI lining.
  • (D) Corneal epithelium: The corneal epithelium does have a relatively high turnover rate (complete replacement in about 7-10 days) to maintain the ocular surface. However, the rate of proliferation is still less than that of the small intestinal crypt cells, making it comparatively less radiosensitive than the gastrointestinal epithelium.

Confidence: 100%
